LENGTH: 1714 words

HEADLINE: The Art Market

BYLINE: By Holland Cotter

 BODY:

Being in the Bronx
In the last decade, multi-culturalism has become a buzz word in the art world. That concept has affected art institutions in different ways. Manhattan's major museums, like the Metropolitan Museum of Art and the Museum of Modern Art, have tried to diversify their programs and collections, but at their own pace. The Bronx Museum of the Arts -- situated in an area where minorities are now in the majority -- doesn't have this option. Multi-culturalism isn't theoretical; it's right outside the door.
The museum was conceived in the 1960's by a group of Riverdale residents, in part to bring mainstream art to the Bronx. It was officially founded in 1971 with a show of Impressionist paintings on loan from the Met and displayed in the rotunda of the Bronx County Courthouse. Since then, it has moved to its own building and grown as a community-based, grass-roots institution, with all the liabilities that status implies. It doesn't have a reliable financial base in the form of a powerful board of trustees or wealthy patrons. ("Simply put, we can't throw a party and clear $100,000," says the museum's president, Elizabeth Cooke.) And it doesn't have a long-established permanent collection like the Brooklyn Museum's to give it an academic pedigree. 
 What it has had is a strong and specific ethnic identification with Hispanic art. When the museum moved to a converted synagogue on the Grand Concourse at 165th Street in 1983, it inaugurated its new home with one of the first major local exhibitions of contemporary Latin American art. It has a curator of Latin American art, the only staff member with an ethnic specialization, and with a Ford Foundation grant, it has developed a new Latin American art research department, one of the first of its kind in the city.
 
Seeing a Wider World
 But does this identity jibe with the notion of multi-cultural diversity? The museum's director, Grace Stanislaus, who came to her job just eight months ago, thinks not. As she points out, the Bronx Museum was established with no ethnic alignment but was created to serve the whole Bronx community, a community that is "constantly shifting," she says. "Right now we have new Russian and Mexican populations coming in to join the Hispanic, Irish, Italian, black, Jewish and Asian populations already here in strength. This is the ideal setting for talking about multi-culturalism, and I want to reposition the museum as a truly multi-cultural institution."
In many ways, Ms. Stanislaus, a 33-year-old native of the island of Carriacou in the West Indies, is suited to the task. She comes to the Bronx Museum from the Studio Museum in Harlem, another grass-roots institution, where she worked after earning a master's degree in 20th-century art from Columbia University in 1982. At the Studio Museum, she organized the influential exhibition "Contemporary African Artists: Changing Traditions," a version of which traveled to the 1990 Venice Biennale. The show had its problems -- the individual work was not uniformly strong -- but virtually for the first time it placed contemporary non-Western art on a continuum with its Western counterparts, and demonstrated the increasingly international and synthetic flavor of art worldwide.
This feel for cultural breadth in today's art is likely to be the strong suit of Ms. Stanislaus's still untested tenure at the Bronx. (The museum is scheduled to reopen in October after being closed for renovations, and Ms. Stanislaus has yet to mount an exhibition there.) She is well aware that her plans for diversity within the museum won't necessarily guarantee an audience, diversified or otherwise. "We don't want to be perceived as a provincial museum," says Ms. Cooke. Yet like many small institutions scattered throughout the other boroughs, the Bronx Museum gets relatively light foot-traffic from Manhattan. A plan to attract more visitors by adding a restaurant, a theater and a parking garage, financed by the city and the borough, was scheduled for completion this year but is now on hold.
At the same time, people from the immediate neighborhood also tend to keep their distance. "They stand at the bus stop and look in, trying to figure out what we're doing in here," Ms. Stanislaus says. "In this community, drugs, violence and survival are big concerns. Many of the adults and kids have never traveled outside the neighborhood. Obviously, these people aren't typical museumgoers. We have to teach them what art is, how you value it, how you integrate it into your life." One approach is the museum's free after-school art programs, which, Ms. Stanislaus says, "offer at least a temporary sanctuary from the streets and from often difficult home situations."
She added: "In general, I look at this museum as a human-services institution, a place where life and art intersect."
 
It's All in the Plans
 The attempt to combine life and art, downtown and uptown sensibilities, art appreciation and scholarship, and in general to shape an institution which by its very nature has contradictory needs and impulses, is reflected in exhibition plans for the next few years.
When the museum reopens, the season is to include two traveling shows of Hispanic art, both scheduled before Ms. Stanislaus's arrival and both historical in nature. The first, organized by the Archer M. Huntington Art Gallery in Austin, Tex., will be "The School of the South: El Taller Torres-Garcia and Its Legacy," devoted to a workshop and school opened in Uruguay by the painter Joaquin Torres-Garcia in 1943. A utopian art community, it had far-reaching impact on Latin American artists of succeeding generations. "Learning about it may give a new perspective to people who don't think of Latin American art as being formal or structured," Ms. Stanislaus adds.
That show is to be followed in March by "Chicano Art: Resistance and Affirmation, 1965-1985." A traveling multi-media exhibition that originated at the Wight Art Gallery at the University of California at Los Angeles, its lively, politically loaded work may well find a responsive audience among the Bronx's Hispanic residents. Next summer, the museum plans to present historical material of a somewhat different kind when, in conjunction with the New York City Health and Hospital Art Program, it mounts an exhibition of recently conserved W.P.A. murals painted in New York City. The show will be supplemented with mural studies, photographs and other historical materials.
Of interest, too, are plans for theme shows that will include an ethnically wide spectrum of participants. One exhibition, scheduled for 1994, is to examine artists whose work Ms. Stanislaus describes as "urban and rural-based folk art," primarily from salvaged and recycled materials. Listed among the artists are David Hammons, Tyree Guyton, Alison Saar, Jesse Lott, Leonardo Drew, Nick Quijano and Catalina Paira. "Some of these people might not consider themselves folk artists," Ms. Stanislaus says. "But they all recognize that there is a spiritual power inherent in objects, and they know that the material they choose has meanings for the community they live in. They aren't, in other words, 'naive,' as they are often portrayed as being."
For 1995, the museum plans a historical reassessment of feminist art, with an exhibition and a series of seminars focusing on feminist scholarship in art criticism. The museum's 25th anniversary in 1996 will bring a show devoted to the art of contemporary Asian-Americans (an ethnic group with whom, Ms. Stanislaus admits, the museum has so far had little contact), and another titled "Black Artists in Britain." And for 1997 she has scheduled what may end up being the first major show of contemporary American Indian art.
 
Expanding the Permanent
 Finally, Ms. Stanislaus says, she intends to expand the Bronx Museum's still-young permanent collection. Six years ago it was decided that the museum would buy only works on paper, mostly by 20th-century Latin American and black artists, although Ms. Stanislaus says she would like to begin collecting in other media. "Building the collection is a way of preserving a culture," she says, "but above all, it's great for the individual artists, who get a museum credential even though they may never be represented in other major collections."
A show of selections from the permanent collection scheduled for spring 1993 will also provide an incentive for doing the kind of scholarly documentation that has so far been neglected. Ms. Stanislaus has made finding money for publications a priority. "It's just a basic fact that there isn't a lot of interest in publishing material about these artists," she says. "So we have to do it."
Is she ever discouraged about having to swim upstream? "No," she says. "I have real faith in our artists. They haven't been exhausted by success, chewed up and spit out, at least not yet. We're not trying to satisfy a market. We're just working and struggling. And that means something fresh is still going on out here at the periphery."
 
Photo Gallery
 Photography galleries seem to have fared better than most over the last several years, but it still might appear risky to open a new gallery in the middle of a recession. Next month, though, Robert Mann, a private photography dealer for seven years, will go against the trends and open a Manhattan gallery under his own name. It will be in the same building he's currently in, at 42 East 76th Street. "When the space next door became available, I just saw it as a blessing of conveniences," Mr. Mann says of his decision to go public. "I outgrew the space here and I outgrew being a private dealer as well."
The new gallery will open with an exhibition of work by Aaron Siskind, who achieved fame for his close-up abstractions of peeling paint and torn posters on walls. Included in the show, Mr. Mann says, will be an album from 1930 to 1932 that Siskind made with his first camera, given to him as a wedding present. "It's fascinating," Mr. Mann says of these early pictures. "He could have gone in any one of a number of directions: into architectural photography, or portrait, or documentary."
A few of the pictures, Mr. Mann says, were even made on the artist's honeymoon.

HEADLINE: TV Weekend;
Tom Brokaw's Pop Quiz for America's Schools

BYLINE: By WALTER GOODMAN

 BODY:
In "America's Schools: Pass or Fail?," Tom Brokaw visits three innovative schools that are working to reverse the downward course of American education. In Leadville, Colo., a school known as the Center is open from early morning till after dark, offering educational, health and social services to students and their families. A Philadelphia high school is managing to get more inner-city youths interested in learning. Magnet schools in Los Angeles seem to be giving off sparks.
Mr. Brokaw maintains a respectful yet skeptical attitude as he questions teachers, principals and other educators. The big problem for all the schools, most agree, begins at home, where parents are too busy or ignorant or troubled to give children the sort of start that most had a generation or two ago. Can programs that engage these youngsters also teach them the facts and skills they will need to hold good jobs? Mr. Brokaw predicts change but sensibly resists making any grand promises.
 
Egypt
Beyond the Pyramids
TBS
Sunday at 9 P.M.
"National Geographic Explorer" starts its new season with a visit to the Great Pyramids on the Giza Plateau to investigate, with the help of archeologists, the mystery of who built the pyramids. The main news here is that the workers are now believed by some to have been not slaves, but conscripted laborers.
After this dose of scholarship, the host, Robert Urich, relaxes with a report on Lucy, who is described as the world's premier belly dancer. An ancient art, it says here, but Lucy looks thoroughly modern. And the final segment of this undemanding two-hours reports on Thor Heyerdahl's theory that Egyptian merchants crossed the Atlantic to the Americas 2,000 years before Columbus. Then how come the West had to wait so long for belly dancing?
 
The Sunday Talkies
In recognition of the fact that the next two months of Sunday talk shows will be largely devoted to Presidential politics, here are a few tips on how to listen to the language of the partisan guests:
"Let's move on." This is a signal that the guest would rather talk about something other than the question asked. Democrats like to move on from the Congressional bank and post-office embarrassments. Republicans prefer to move on from the Administration's policy toward Iraq before the invasion of Kuwait and, in general, from questions about the economy. They will often get away with it by turning abruptly to the next questioner.
"Frankly." This word, as used by politicians, invariably precedes a statement that comes right out of their prepared package. From a Republican: "Frankly, George Bush has an economic program." From a Democrat: "Frankly, Bill Clinton is a family man."
"The American people." This phrase is waved each week by representatives of both parties like warriors who have run out of ammunition. Frankly, it signals the moment to hit the remote and move on.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

The Economy
The gross domestic product grew at a puny 1.4 percent annual rate in the second quarter, the Government said, citing consumers' reluctance to spend. But a record drop in applications for unemployment insurance in mid-August and better corporate profits offered glimmers of hope for improved growth later. [Page D1.]
Battalions of insurance adjusters visited South Florida, trying to reach the tens of thousands of home owners who need cash for emergency supplies, food and shelter. But so overwhelming is the devastation from Hurricane Andrew that they are having great difficulty getting together with policyholders. [A15.] 
 The dollar's tumble has been an alarm bell in the world's currency markets in recent weeks. But in Washington, no one seems to be listening. Low interest rates, which greased its slide, have far more political appeal than a sound currency. [D2.]
A new incinerator is to be built in the Brooklyn Navy Yard under a long-range rubbish plan voted by the New York City Council. It also calls for increased recycling. [A1.]
 
Companies
Continental Airlines got a $400 million bid from Air Canada and two Texas financiers. The offer may touch off another bidding war for the bankrupt carrier. The Texans are associated with Robert M. Bass, who is not involved in the offer. [D1.]
Apple plans inexpensive home computers based on its Macintosh line, hoping to snare first-time buyers away from cheap I.B.M. clones, which now sell for well below $1,000. Analysts said the machines would offer an optional built-in CD-ROM player. [D3.]
The U.A.W. struck a General Motors metal fabricating plant in Lordstown, Ohio, over job security, leading G.M.'s Saturn plant in Spring Hill, Tenn., to plan an early stop to car assembly today. More G.M. assembly plants could shut within days. [D3.]
G.M. closed the last car plant in Southern California, a once busy factory that had operated for 45 years in Van Nuys. [D3.] Mycogen agreed to form a venture with Lubrizol to buy Lubrizol's seed and plant science unit in a transaction valued at about $135 million. The companies plan to develop genetically engineered biological crop pesticides and seed products. [D4.]
A bid for Canary Wharf was cut by $227.7 million, to $465.3 million, by an investors group that includes Paul Reichmann, the head of Olympia & York, which started the London project. [D4.]
 
International
The Japanese Government prepared to announce a huge financial rescue package aimed at stimulating the economy, shoring up the banking system and returning confidence to the sagging stock and real estate markets. [D1.]
Mexican toy manufactures are disgruntled at the competition expected from the North American Free Trade Agreement. [D1.]
 
 Markets
The Dow average gained 7.83 to close at 3,254.64, lifted by a stronger dollar and upswings in overseas markets. [D6.]
Prices of Treasury securities edged up after the Fed reported much lower money supply growth than expected. [D5.]
The dollar edged up to 1.4085 marks, and rose against most other currencies as well. Gold prices also rose. [D13.]
Sugar futures settled sharply higher in reaction to reports of extensive hurricane damage to the Louisiana sugar crop. [D13.]
 
Today's Columns
Bond mutual funds are attracting near record amounts of money. Much of it is going into funds that buy foreign bonds. But stock fund sales have weakened this month, reflecting growing caution among investors. Floyd Norris: Market Place. [D1.]
Tri-Star Pictures' decision to move up Woody Allen's film, "Husbands and Wives," and broaden its release was endorsed by executives involved in advertising, marketing and promoting movies and other entertainment. Stuart Elliott: Advertising. [D6.]

HEADLINE: New Ball Game for Mexican Toys

BYLINE: By TIM GOLDEN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MONTERREY, Mexico

 BODY:
"Nobody likes this Free Trade Agreement," said Eduardo Iga Saade. He was speaking as a Mexican toy maker. "I was very happy making my lousy products. People bought them, I took my vacations, and everything was fine."
Yet five years after a first reduction of trade barriers turned the manufacture of tea sets, plastic trucks and the like into one of Mexico's more perilous industrial ventures, Mr. Iga and the family-run company he heads are just the sort of survivors that the Government hopes will emerge from the North American Free Trade Agreement with Canada and the United States that was announced on Aug. 12. 
 Like Plasticos Iga S.A., the Mexican toy industry as a whole seems to sketch an unusually stark picture of the fate of some light manufacturing in transforming an elaborately protected, developing economy into a more modern, open and service-oriented one.
A week after the accord was announced, the president of the Mexican Toy Industry Association, Maria Teresa Kassuga, said she was still trying to find out how the trade agreement would affect tariffs on toys. What seems clear, however, is that in continuing to adjust from big profit margins, low productivity and a captive clientele, the toy industry will be even more disadvantaged by scant information about its foreign competitors and limited capital for new investment. Unlike many larger Mexican industrialists, toy makers did not have the clout to lobby trade officials for more time to scale back barriers to imports.
Although President Carlos Salinas de Gortari has insisted that the new trade pact will not "damage our industry or our countryside," economists contend that industries like toys, textiles, paper, furniture, petrochemicals and automotive parts will suffer especially from greater foreign competition because of the cost of becoming more efficient.
"From the time they started to talk about free trade, what we had to do was clear," said Carlos Iga Saade, the head of purchasing for the company founded in 1964 by his father, Carlos Iga Canavati. "We either become more efficient or we close down."
Toy makers have already had a considerable taste of the difficulties of competing on a global scale, having gone through spasms since Mexico entered the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade five years ago.
With that first opening, a wall to foreign imports dropped to a tariff of 40 percent. And as the tariff has since fallen to 20 percent, smugglers have taken advantage of the opening to flood the market with new, used and defective toys that are priced to reflect only the premiums of corrupt officials. Just since last year, 80 of the 265 members of the trade association have disappeared, victims of Barbie Dolls, Cabbage Patch Dolls, Ninja Turtles and the like.
Previously, one Mexican company, Grupo Auriga, had dominated the Mexican toy market in a joint venture with Mattel Inc. of El Segundo, Calif. Plasticos Iga and a half-dozen other Mexican companies had divided up much of the remainder.
Toys would sometimes be sold for twice the cost of manufacturing, advertising, royalties and overhead, Eduardo Iga said. Profit margins of 30 percent were taken for granted. And so was the expense, he added, "of all sorts of absurd things."
"We had our own construction engineer up here on salary in case we needed to fix any of the bathrooms or remodel an office," he recalled. "All of the big companies -- us included -- did whatever we wanted with the buyers. We did what we wanted with the price, with the quality. We got very cocky."
After years of making and distributing different foreign branded toys, Iga bought a license from Hasbro Inc. of Pawtucket, R.I., to make and sell its Transformers toys. The item became Mexico's hit toy of 1986, and Iga grew even cockier. Like other companies, Iga decided it would only sell distributors its Transformers and other popular items if they also bought a package of less popular toys. The sales of the company zoomed to about $15 million, while its market share grew to about 10 percent.
 
A Shift in Strategy
 But within barely two years, the Transformers' novelty had worn off. In picking other foreign toys to make and distribute, the company acknowledges that it chose badly. Legal and contraband imports flooded the market.
With its sales plummeting and debts of about $6 million to a handful of Mexican banks, Iga was forced into bankruptcy proceedings. Workers were laid off by the hundreds, executives by the handful. From 2,100 workers in 1986, the payroll shrunk to fewer than 900. The company closed divisions, sold off most of its property and struggled to imagine how it could survive the new waves of foreign competition, Eduardo Iga said.
"By the time people saw it coming, it was smashing them in the face," he said of freer trade.
Given a choice between shutting down or hanging on, Plasticos Iga began doing business differently.
It bought licenses for foreign products more selectively. It started to make and assemble pieces for foreign companies, including Mattel, though the profit on such work is typically around 5 percent. And the Igas and their engineers began learning more about the intricacies of engineering plastics, like the television cabinets they are now starting to make for Zenith. They courted their Mexican clients more aggressively, too, offering them better credit and lowering prices.
 
Problems Persist
 Yet with all of Iga's sense of renewal comes little sense of security.
Unlike smaller members of Mexico's small-business association, Iga cannot qualify for small, cut-rate credits. Because it no longer owns the land in Monterrey on which its plant sits, it can borrow from commercial banks only at annual rates of between 25 percent and 31 percent. So to buy new plastic-injection machines, Iga needed credit from the manufacturer. And to keep clients, the company said it must offer distributors not only higher quality toys at better prices, but finance terms as well.
Although the cost of plastic has come down in Mexico, the expense of everything else the company uses -- from labels and packing materials to telephone services and tiny screws -- conspires against Iga's new push to become competitive with American and Canadian companies.
Iga looks at Processed Plastics Inc. of Montgomery, Ill., a company from which Iga has bought toy molds for a decade, and marvels at its scale and efficiency. With $40 million in annual sales last year, Processed Plastics is about four times Iga's current size. The thought that it and other American companies will seize on the free trade agreement to push harder into Mexico fairly terrifies the Igas.
"They make better molds," Eduardo Iga noted. "They have higher volume. Their assembly and production lines are much more efficient because they can automate so much. And their supplies are much cheaper."
Walking around the Iga brothers' bustling plant, one comparative advantage is especially obvious: On the lines of workers assembling plastic trucks, toy motorcycles and sets of tiny dishes, the young women said they were paid as little as the local minimum wage of $3.97 for a 10-hour day of intense, almost uninterrupted work.
Nor do such hazards as the plastic cement fumes that rise from open vats raise concerns about liability suits by the workers. "That doesn't happen here," Eduardo Iga said.
Mrs. Kassuga, the industry association president, said that when she invited buyers from Mattel and Hasbro to meet with Mexican toy manufacturers, the American executives reported that Mexican labor costs were high compared with those of Asian countries like China, Malaysia and Indonesia.
Although Mexican trade officials have released scant details about the new agreement, the United States Commerce and Treasury departments said that Mexico's 20 percent tariffs on foreign-made dolls would be phased out over five years. The country's tariff of 10 to 20 percent on toys with wheels would be eliminated over 10 years. One-quarter of the remaining nonrubber toys would begin moving into Mexico duty-free as soon as the trade accord goes into effect, another quarter after five years and the rest after 10 years, the officials said.
In the absence of information about the new rules, Eduardo Iga said he could only assume that his competition would stiffen. "Sooner or later, they are going to come at us, so we are better off trying to join them," he said.

HEADLINE: Market Place;
Bond Funds Attracting Surge of Cash

BYLINE: By FLOYD NORRIS

 BODY:
With interest rates down and the dollar falling, Americans are pouring money at a near record pace into mutual funds that invest in bonds, and much of that money is going into funds that buy foreign bonds, fund managers said yesterday.
But stock fund sales, which have been strong for all of 1992, weakened this month as some investors grew more cautious. 
 Investors searching for higher yields have increasingly been fleeing banks and money market funds, and much of the money has gone into bond funds. Figures released yesterday by the Investment Company Institute, a trade group, showed that a net $11.8 billion flowed into bond funds last month, 31 percent more than in June and the second-largest monthly figure ever.
 
Looking Back to 1987
 The record month for bond fund cash flow was set in January 1987, when $12.8 billion was invested. That was near the end of the last bull market in bonds, which drove long-term rates down to levels not seen again until this month.
The cash flow figures include both new sales and redemptions, as well as transfers among various fund groups. But the figures exclude reinvested dividends.
In July, stock prices were generally higher, and the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index reached a record high. That spurred investors to put a net $6.8 billion into stock funds, up 46 percent from a depressed June figure of $4.6 billion. The June figure, which the trade group revised downward from $5.3 billion, was the lowest for any month since November.
Over all, the cash flow into long-term funds, both those specializing in stocks and bonds, came to $18.63 billion in July. That figure was also the second highest ever, just trailing the $18.68 billion figure for January 1987.
The stock market has weakened this month, in part because of concern over the dollar, and fund managers said some stock fund owners had decided to take profits. "Our stock fund activity is down 64 percent from July, but remember that July was one of our strongest months," said Steve Norwitz, a spokesman for T. Rowe Price funds.
At Fidelity Investments, the largest mutual fund company, there was an increase in new sales of stock funds in August, said Michael Hines, a spokesman. But he added that this increase was offset by the fact that some investors had moved money from stock funds to other funds in an attempt to time market movements.
Such investors are often fickle, and one of the most striking facts about the surge into stock funds this year has been that the market timers have stayed positive. New transfers into stock funds were positive for four straight months, from April through July. Records going back to 1984 show no comparable string.
That the public is not yet very nervous about the stock market, despite the reports of slowing sales of stock funds in August, was reflected by a University of Michigan survey of investor attitudes, done for Fidelity. Preliminary data for August showed the most positive figures for plans to buy individual stocks since the survey began in 1990, as well as strong intentions to buy stock mutual funds.
The professionals who manage stock funds had been less bullish than their customers and have pushed cash levels in their funds to record levels. That continued in July, with net stock purchases by funds of only $4.5 billion, well under the funds' cash flow of $6.8 billion, and cash available in stock funds rose to $40.2 billion from $38.6 billion. But with stock prices up, the percentage of cash in funds slipped to 9.5 percent of assets from 9.6 percent.
With the dollar trading late this month at record lows against the German mark, and with interest rates significantly higher in much of Europe than in this country, the lure of international bond funds has been great. T. Rowe Price's best-selling fund this month was a new one, started at the beginning of the month, that specializes in short-term investments overseas. So far, it has taken in $35 million.
A similar fund at Scudder, Stevens & Clark had net cash flow in August of about $265 million, a spokesman, Gavin Quill, reported. That fund, which began this year with assets of $348 million, now boasts assets of $1.25 billion.
Such funds benefit from high foreign interest rates and have been able to report even better performance because the dollar has fallen, raising the dollar value of foreign bond portfolios. Their performances will suffer if the dollar begins to rise, but it appears that more and more investors are willing to bet that will not happen.

GRAPHIC: Graph: "Growing Enthusiasm" shows the amount of net monthly cash flow into stock and bond mutual funds from Aug. 1991-July 1992. (Source: Investment Company institute)

HEADLINE: Tokyo Sets Fiscal Package To Revive Ailing Economy

BYLINE: By ANDREW POLLACK, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TOKYO, Friday, Aug. 28

 BODY:
The Japanese Government prepared to announce a huge financial rescue package today aimed at stimulating growth in the nation's staggering economy and shoring up a banking system that has been endangered by the rapid fall in stocks and real estate prices.
One part of the double-barreled plan is expected to include roughly 10 trillion yen, or about $80 billion, in measures to stimulate growth through extra spending on public works and tax incentives to encourage businesses to invest in new equipment. 
 This package, known as a supplemental budget, is the largest ever and far exceeds the six trillion yen that was contemplated only two months ago, an indication of the growing sense of crisis here as the economy shows few signs of recovering.
The second part of the plan is aimed at reviving banks and other financial institutions, in part by providing tax incentives to help them write off bad loans.
The ruling Liberal Democratic Party agreed this morning on a plan that would then be presented to Government ministers for final approval this evening.
Economists have said the package will come too late to help the Government meet its target of 3.5 percent economic growth this fiscal year, which ends next March, although it should contribute to an economic recovery next year. Economists expect growth to be about 2 percent, which is very low by Japanese standards.
There was some speculation that the Government would have to take additional steps, like cutting the Bank of Japan's discount rate again from its current level of 3.25 percent.
Although economists have said they still harbor some doubts about the practical effects of the policies, the Tokyo stock market has soared over the last week in the belief that the Government plans to do whatever is necessary to shore up the financial system.
The market had its most spectacular day on Thursday, with the Nikkei index of 225 issues rocketing up 1,013.35 points, or 6.13 percent, to 17,555.00. In today's trading, the index closed with a gain of 415.79 points, or 2.37 percent, to 17,970.79.
The Nikkei has now jumped more than 25 percent in the last eight trading sessions, one of the most powerful rallies ever, after falling to a six-and-a-half-year low on Aug. 18.
 
Rising Trade Deficit
 The United States Government has been urging Japan to adopt such measures to help stimulate growth in the world economy and to provide a larger market for American exports to Japan. After years of shrinking, the politically troublesome American trade deficit with Japan has been rising again this year as Japan has cut back on imports.
The pump-priming measures agreed upon today by the ruling party center on an additional expenditure of some three trillion yen, or about $24 billion, for Federal public works projects, plus an addtional 1.8 trillion yen, or $14.4 billion, for local projects.
The plan also aims at reviving the dormant real estate market. Money will be allocated to help local governments buy land for future public works projects and to a Government agency that helps finance housing construction.
The plan will provide low-interest loans to small businesses and tax cuts to encourage companies to invest in energy-saving equipment.
 
A Forced Rescue
 The basic outline of the financial elements of the Government's package were announced last week, just as the stocks took a steep plunge, forcing the Finance Ministry to rescue the stock market.
The financial rescue focuses on easing a crisis of confidence in the banking system, which has been struggling under a mounting toll of bad loans.
The Finance Minister, Tsutomu Hata, said the Government would offer a series of tax concessions and ease other rules to enable banks to write off or make provisions for their bad loans. In addition, he said, banks would not be required to report the losses on their stock holdings for the six months ending Sept. 30, in the hope that what investors did not know would not hurt them.
Mr. Hata also issued a strong request that banks and other big financial institutions not unload those stocks on which they still showed a profit before Sept. 30.

HEADLINE: Slow Growth In 2d Quarter Is Confirmed

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 27

 BODY:
The economy stumbled in the second quarter as consumers showed reluctance to spend, the Government said today. But a record drop in applications for unemployment insurance in mid-August and an increase in corporate profits offered glimmers of hope for improved growth later this year.
The nation's gross domestic product, the broadest measure of economic health, inched up at a 1.4 percent annual pace, unchanged from an advance estimate released last month, the Commerce Department said. 
 That was less than half the 2.9 percent rate of the first quarter. Most of the deterioration came because consumer spending decreased two-tenths of 1 percent in the second quarter after surging 5.1 percent in the first three months of the year.
 
An Encouraging Sign
 Meanwhile, the Labor Department said first-time claims for unemployment benefits plunged to 382,000 during the week ended Aug. 15, down from 474,000 a week earlier. It was the largest decrease on record since the department began tracking claims in 1967 and the first time claims had fallen below 400,000 in 22 months.
Economists were encouraged and said the drop, if sustained, could give consumers the buying power and confidence needed to fuel more robust economic growth. But they cautioned against reading too much into the big decline, noting that the previous week's number was inflated by a temporary shutdown of General Motors factories.
"At the moment, I'm admiring the drop but I'm not reaching any strong conclusions about it," said Robert G. Dederick, an economist at the Northern Trust Company in Chicago. "We have to wait for more evidence."
On another bright note, the Commerce Department said profits of American corporations rose 2.1 percent to an annual rate of $234.6 billion in the second quarter, the highest level ever. The rise was built on a 10.8 percent increase in the first quarter, the best in four years.
Higher profits should eventually give businesses the ability to hire more workers and increase purchases of productivity-improving capital equipment. But so far increases have been generated chiefly by cost-cutting rather than strong sales, analysts said.
"The reason profits are doing so well is because employment is being held down," said the economist Laurence H. Meyer. "The question is: Can you continue to earn profits if that doesn't generate increases in employment? The answer is: No.'
 
A Worrisome Turn
 Although second-quarter G.D.P. growth was unchanged from the estimate announced last month, its composition took a worrisome turn, economists said. More of the growth than first thought came from an accumulation of inventories, and the nation's trade performance proved to be a bigger negative than initially believed.
The larger inventory buildup could reduce the need for production in the third quarter, detracting from growth. And now Hurricane Andrew looks as if it will depress the third-quarter figures as well.
Many analysts are looking for third-quarter results similar to the second quarter and do not foresee a pickup until late in the year, too late to benefit President Bush's re-election effort.
The nation's unemployment rate, at 7.7 percent in July, just off its eight-year peak of 7.8 percent, will probably not improve more than slightly, they said.
"Later in the year, growth could pick up and be sustained into 1993," said Bruce Steinberg, an economist with Merrill Lynch. "But Bush won't get any help from that. No one is going to notice before the election that any improvement is taking place in the economy."
The second quarter marked the 13th consecutive quarter of subpar growth or outright decline in the G.D.P. Although the last five quarters have been positive, the expansion has not been strong enough to provide any substantial number of jobs for the growing labor force.

GRAPHIC: Graph: "Real Economic Growth" shows the annual rate of change in the gross domestic product from 1988-'92. (Source: Commerce Department)

HEADLINE: A Risky Stance on the Falling Dollar

BYLINE: By STEVE LOHR

 BODY:
The sharp slide of the value of the United States dollar to all-time lows recently is an alarm bell going off in the world's currency markets. But in Washington, no one seems to be listening.
With the American economy weak and a Presidential race in its homestretch, the Federal Reserve Board and the Bush Administration want to avoid raising interest rates. An increase in rates would prop up the dollar, but also raise the cost of borrowing on everything from business loans to home mortgages. 
 Still, a dollar policy of benign neglect is risky. The dollar has steadied in nervous trading in the last three days, and the stock market has rallied somewhat in sympathy. But currency traders warn that the forces behind the dollar's fall remain, notably the big gap in policies between the United States, where policy makers want low interest rates to encourage growth, and Germany, where they worry about inflation and keep rates high.
The value of the American currency has dropped some 16 percent in four months against the German mark, the linchpin European currency, with three percentage points of the shrinkage in the last two weeks. An unchecked free fall of the dollar could touch off a panicky crash in stock markets, similar to those of 1987, and hurt economic growth worldwide.
"The situation is very fragile right now, and any bit of bad news could send the dollar tumbling," said C. Fred Bergsten, a former senior Treasury official in the Carter Administration, who is the director of the Institute for International Economics. "The Administration and the Fed ought to be terrified. They're flirting with disaster."
Yet short of disaster, the fate of the dollar affects far fewer Americans, in a less immediate way, than interest rate movements. "The votes are in lower interest rates," said David Wyss, chief financial economist for DRI/McGraw-Hill, a consulting firm in Lexington, Mass.
Many homeowners, for example, have benefited from the repeated lowering of interest rates by the Fed in the last two years as it has tried to revive the sluggish economy. In 1989, homeowners in their late 30's and early 40's, the peak mortgage-paying age, paid an average of $4,000 a year in house payments, according to DRI/McGraw-Hill. By this year, the same homeowners, assuming they had floating-rate mortgages or refinanced their fixed-payment loans, are paying $800 less, or $3,200 a year.
In his Houston speech accepting the Republican nomination last week, President Bush eagerly took credit for the low American rates. "Interest rates are the lowest they've been in 20 years, and millions of Americans have refinanced their homes," he said.
The Federal Reserve, not the Administration, sets monetary policy and American interest rates, and the Fed is an independent body. Yet few analysts believe the Fed is entirely immune to political pressure.
The Banc One Corporation, based in Columbus, Ohio, has refinanced thousands of mortgages at its 1,200 offices in Ohio, Michigan, Indiana, Wisconsin, Kentucky and Texas. Its chairman, John B. McCoy, agrees that it is interest rates, not the dollar, that affect most Americans. "The American consumer basically couldn't care less about the dollar," he said.
To be sure, there are exceptions. American tourists traveling abroad have been stunned by how little their dollars buy. More important to the economy, a weak dollar helps American exporters, whose goods are cheap to foreign buyers.
 
Help for Exports
 Since the dollar peak of 1985, the fairly steady decline in the value of the American currency has aided exports considerably, with merchandise exports nearly doubling in five years, to $422 billion last year. Since 1989, the lift from surging sales of exports of both goods and services more than accounted for all the growth in the American economy, estimates Edward McKelvey, a senior economist at Goldman, Sachs & Company. In short, without exports there would have been no growth at all during the Bush years.
The Hurco Companies has seen the overseas sales of its specialized machine tools more than double in five years to $43 million last year, representing roughly half of the Indianapolis company's business. "A low dollar helps you break into foreign markets against formidable competition," said Brian McLaughlin, Hurco's president. "And if the dollar goes a bit lower, as a U.S. manufacturing exporter, that's fine with me."
But the recent slide in the dollar's value, economists agree, will not help the American economy in the form of increased export sales for six to nine months, well after the November election, given the usual lag between currency-driven price changes and additional foreign orders.
 
Rate Changes Felt Quickly
 The effects of interest-rate movements, however, are felt in the domestic economy more quickly in the form of payments made by businesses and consumers on the money they borrow.
The pressure to raise American rates is not home-grown. Inflation is low in the soft economy, so there is little danger that a weak dollar, which makes imported goods more costly, poses an inflationary threat anytime soon.
Instead, the pressure on the dollar, and American rates, comes from Germany. Specifically, it is the gap between high German rates and the low American rates that makes buying the mark and selling the dollar such an attractive bargain for currency traders.
Like the policy makers in Washington, the Germans are focused on domestic pressures. The steep costs of German unification have fueled Government spending and inflation in a country whose bankers and Government officials, recalling the hyperinflation of the 1920's and the period immediately after World War II, have a visceral antagonism toward inflation. The Bundesbank, the German central bank, has raised rates, despite protests from other industrial nations, which want lower rates to stimulate growth.
 
Germany Cannot Be Ignored
 But in an increasingly interwoven global economy, the policies of a unified Germany, the economic giant of Europe, cannot be ignored. The impact is most severe in Europe, where the currencies of Britain, France and other nations are directly linked to the German mark via the European Monetary System.
But as the sobering slide of the dollar shows, the pressures are mounting in Washington as well. "This, in many ways, is a sign of the new world order -- a shock imposed on the U.S. economy by Germany," said Robert Z. Lawrence, an economist at Harvard University's Kennedy School of Government.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
EXXON'S PRESIDENT AND OTHER OFFICERS SELL SHARES

BYLINE: Bloomberg Business News

 BODY:
The Exxon Corporation's president, Lee Raymond, sold 45,999, or 43 percent, of his shares in the Irving, Tex., oil giant. Mr. Raymond sold the shares Aug. 5 for $64.76 each and now holds 59,627 shares. In addition, other company officials sold about 151,000 shares in the last month for $55 to $64.76 each, Securities and Exchange Commission filings show. The sales come as Exxon stock has reached highs and as falling United States interest rates have weakened the dollar and driven overseas operating expenses up. This could push down earnings for an international company like Exxon. "The stock price by some people's standards is close to the asset valuation," said Benjamin Rice, an analyst at Brown Brothers Harriman in New York. Exxon officials declined to comment.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
Last Auto Factory in Southern California Closed by G.M.

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES, Aug. 27

 BODY:
A red Chevrolet Camaro Z-28 rolled off the line today to end 45 years of production at the General Motors Corporation's Van Nuys plant, the last auto factory in the world's biggest car market.
Camaros and Pontiac Firebirds that once rolled out in huge numbers -- 257,000 were built in 1978 by 5,100 workers -- will now be assembled near Montreal. Canada's national health-care system reduces the price per car by $600 to $800 in lower benefit costs, said Raymond Knudson, a union official. 
 The closing, eliminating 2,600 jobs, is another blow to manufacturing employment in Southern California. The area, once rich in high-paying factory and construction work, has been buffeted by military cutbacks, business flight from high costs and a building-industry recession.
This shutdown was more bitter than most, ending auto building in a region where 14 million people are bound to their cars by lack of public transit, the far-flung freeways and a personal devotion matched nowhere else.
It also ended years of uncertainty at the aging plant, where parts of the roof were unsafe to walk, water pipes regularly burst, and morale had flagged since a 1980's battle over Japanese manufacturing techniques that cut job classifications from 170 to 2: team member and team leader.
In the 1960's, G.M. had a second Los Angeles-area factory, building Buicks, Pontiacs and Oldsmobiles in South Gate. Nearby, Fords were built in Pico Rivera, Chryslers in Commerce.
Then, British and German workers flocked to skilled trade jobs at local auto plants. Managers looked the other way as illegal aliens from Mexico took dirty jobs, Mr. Knudson said.
Now it takes two salaries to buy a condominium, and the state's only car factory is near San Francisco, a joint venture of G.M. and Toyota where the Corollas and Geo Prisms are the result of strict Japanese-style team-concept production.
Mr. Knudson said workers who had made $18 an hour are looking at $8-an-hour jobs fixing air conditioners or cars.
Under their contract with G.M., employees who return to school will get full pay and benefits through September 1993; pay is 85 percent for those who do not seek retraining.
They also can get on the hiring lists for G.M. plants in other states. Mr. Knudson said skilled workers should find work fairly quickly, but it could take years for assembly line workers.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Treasury Bond Yield Falls Slightly

BYLINE: By JONATHAN FUERBRINGER

 BODY:
The Treasury market did not go far yesterday as it got a dose of perplexing economic news and a small rally in the dollar faded by the end of the day.
But the market got a last-minute lift, just as many traders were heading home, when the Federal Reserve reported that two key measures of the nation's money supply grew much less than expected in the week ending Aug. 17. 
 In late trading in the secondary market, the 7.25 percent 30-year Treasury bond was offered at 98 7/32, up 6/32, to yield 7.39 percent, compared with 7.41 percent on Wednesday.
But trading was still generally thin and indecisive, and that did not help the Wall Street firms that underwrote the $1 billion in Tennessee Valley Authority three-year notes, which were moving sluggishly.
The money supply numbers showed a $1.5 billion decline in M-2, which includes currency in circulation, checking accounts, savings accounts and certificates of deposit, and a $1 billion rise in M-3, which includes M-2 and money market accounts.
 
Money Supply Sluggish
 Ward McCarthy, a managing director with Stone & McCarthy Research Associates in Princeton, N.J., said that M-2 had been expected to rise $3 billion to $4 billion and M-3 had been expected to rise more than $1 billion. He said the current figures left the Federal Reserve $24 billion below the bottom of its money-supply growth targets for the year.
At the end of a day with little action, the report may have made some traders just react instinctively and buy on data that reflect a slow economy and would normally spur speculation on another easing of rates by the Fed.
But with the dollar hovering just above its record low against the German mark, the Fed's hands may be tied in the near term because cutting rates here would weaken the dollar further. So the money supply data may not be as supportive, for now, as they seemed late yesterday.
Two other economic reports may have held the market back a little yesterday because they gave some signs, albeit weak ones, that the economy may just have a tiny bit more oomph in it. But with the economy moving so slowly a little bit of lift does not mean that much.
One report showed a rise in the Mortgage Bankers Association indexes of mortgage applications and new purchases. And the Labor Department reported that initial claims for unemployment compensation dropped 92,000 to 382,000 in the week ending Aug. 15. That was the largest drop since the survey began in 1967 and the first time that the number of new claimants has fallen below 400,000 since October 1990. But these numbers were distorted by the return of furloughed workers to General Motors plants in Michigan.
 
Corporate Bonds
 The Tennessee Valley Authority sold $1 billion in three-year notes yesterday that will reduce its interest costs and add some flexibility to its financial planning.
The new three-year 4.75 percent notes were priced to the public to yield 4.88 percent, 15 basis points, or hundredths of a percentage point, over the yield on three-year Treasury notes. They will be used to retire an existing $1 billion in five-year bonds sold in 1998. They have an 8.25 percent coupon and mature on Oct. 1. 1994.
Although those bonds are not callable for refinancing, the T.V.A. can effectively retire them from their books by establishing a trust from the proceeds of the new note sale. The cash is used to buy enough two-year Treasury securities to cover the principal and interest due on the old five-year notes.
William F. Malec, the chief financial officer of the T.V.A., said the new deal would save $2 million a year in interest expenses. In addition, the new three-year bonds are callable after one year, so the T.V.A. has some additional flexibility if interest rates do move lower over the next several years.
But while the sale was good for the T.V.A., traders involved said it was not going well for the underwriters. While the first few notes sold quickly, a trader said "they have gone very slowly this afternoon" because investors are not comfortable with the volatility in the market right now.
 
Foreign Bonds
 In foreign bond markets, a small rebound was also erased when the dollar gave up some earlier gains. In addition, a rumor in the bond markets that the Italian Government was going to raise its official interest rates to defend the weakening lira knocked the British pound lower and pulled down bonds in London.
The 10-year bond future in London was down about a half a point as the rumor of an Italian rate increase made one seem more likely in Britain, since its currency is also near the bottom of its trading range in the European Monetary System. In France, a rally of a quarter to a half of a point was wiped out and the 10-year bond future finished unchanged.
The Italian Government denied that it was considering a rate increase to defend its weak currency. And the French Finance Minister said the French were not considering any realignment in the European Monetary System. But these comments did not provide much comfort. Investors are worried that the French will reject the Maastricht Treaty in a Sept. 20 referendum. The treaty outlines the steps to a single currency by the end of the century and rejection by the French would create chaos in European financial markets.

HEADLINE: Dollar and Foreign Stocks Help Dow Advance by 7.83

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
Stock prices rode out a wave of afternoon selling to post a moderate gain yesterday, encouraged by a stronger dollar and upswings in overseas markets.
Building-related stocks with interests in Florida were some of the day's standout performers in the aftermath of Hurricane Andrew. 
 The Dow Jones average of 30 industrials, up more than 25 points at its peak during the day, closed with a gain of 7.83, to 3,254.64.
Advancing issues outnumbered declines by more than 3 to 2 on the New York Stock Exchange. Big Board volume came to 178.6 million shares, up from 171.9 million in the previous session.
 
Some Losses Recouped
 The dollar rose in foreign exchange trading, recouping some of the losses it had suffered lately amid worries about the American economic and political outlook.
Analysts said that helped to ease fears that the Federal Reserve might be forced to shift toward a more restrictive credit policy in defense of the dollar.
Stock prices also climbed in the major foreign markets, reversing a recent slide that had unsettled Wall Street.
Home building and construction materials stocks drew buying interest from traders anticipating a major rebuilding effort in Florida after this week's hurricane caused billions of dollars in damages.
Home Depot, operator of building-supply and home-improvement stores, climbed 1 1/2, to 52 1/4; Lennar, a residential-community developer, gained 1 3/4, to 30 1/8; Elcor, which makes roofing shingles, rose 1 3/8, to 11 1/4, and Hughes Supply, a distributor of construction materials, added 1 1/8, to 14 3/8.
Home Depot said it had been holding the line on lumber prices in South Florida in the face of heavy customer demand and sharp increases in wholesale prices.
 
Home Maker Rises
 Fleetwood Enterprises, a producer of manufactured housing, rose 1 1/4, to 30 7/8. The stock jumped 3 1/4 points Wednesday after the company reported a bigger-than-expected earnings gain for the fiscal first quarter that ended July 26.
Elsewhere yesterday, Philip Morris, which announced a dividend increase on Wednesday, gained 5/8, to 81 1/4, in active trading.
Other blue chips contributing to the Dow's rise were Walt Disney, up 1/2 at 34 3/8; Allied-Signal, up 1 1/8 at 53 5/8; International Business Machines, up 1 at 87 3/8, and J. P. Morgan, up 3/8 at 59 3/4.
Auto issues, by contrast, came under renewed pressure amid worries about economic-recovery prospects. General Motors, whose operations may be hurt by a strike at a metal fabricating plant, lost 3/8, to 33 3/4; Ford Motor slipped 1/8, to 40 3/8, and Chrysler dropped 1/8, to 19 1/2.
Among leading technology stocks in the Nasdaq over-the-counter market, Microsoft gained 2, to 73 5/8; Novell 1 1/4, to 50 3/4; Intel 3/4, to 57 3/4; Adobe Systems 1 1/8, to 33 1/4, and Seagate Technology 3/8, to 13 5/8.
The Nasdaq composite index rose 4.47, to 563.27. At the American Stock Exchange, the market value index was up 1.35 at 381.00.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Ends Slightly Higher On Mixed Data on Economy

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
The dollar ended slightly higher against the German mark and most other currencies yesterday after Government reports provided mixed news on the economy's health. Gold prices rose.
"The dollar had a little bit of a good tone to it this morning when the jobless claims data came out, but it drifted off as the day went on," said Joe Volpicella, chief currency trader at the Westpac Banking Corporation in New York. 
 The number of Americans filing first-time claims for unemployment fell 92,000, to 382,000, in the week ending Aug. 15, the Labor Department said yesterday.
A separate report that the gross domestic product, the broadest measure of economic health, rose at only a 1.4 percent annual pace, softened the dollar, traders said.
In New York, the dollar closed at 1.4085 marks, up from 1.4065 on Wednesday. In Europe, the dollar rose as high as 1.4190 marks before easing to 1.4110 marks late in the day. This compared with 1.4070 marks late Wednesday in Europe.
Earlier this week, the dollar hit a new closing low of 1.4015 marks in New York. Low interest rates and a sluggish economy have discouraged investment in the dollar.
Traders said the dollar was supported yesterday by a statement by Economics Minister Michel Sapin of France that the Group of Seven industrialized nations did not want the dollar to weaken further.
Meanwhile, Germany's Finance Minister, Theo Waigel, said Germany needed lower interest rates, and that statement began some weakening in the mark, easing pressure on the dollar and European currencies.
In New York, the British pound closed at $1.9800, down from $1.9860 on Wednesday. In London, the pound was quoted at $1.9770, down from $1.9865 late Wednesday.
In New York trading, the dollar was quoted at 124.75 Japanese yen, down from 124.85 late Wednesday. In Tokyo, the dollar closed at 124.95 yen, unchanged from Wednesday. Later in London, the dollar was quoted at 124.85 yen.
Other late dollar rates in New York, compared with late Wednesday's rates, included: 1.2605 Swiss francs, up from 1.2580; 4.8015 French francs, up from 4.7950; 1,077.00 Italian lire, up from 1,074.50, and 1.19575 Canadian dollars, up from 1.19175.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

International
Japan announced the largest economic rescue package in its history. Public works spending is to be doubled to $86 billion to stimulate growth. The plan is also intended to strengthen a banking system that has been jeopardized by falling land and stock prices. The market soared in response. [Page 1.]
 
The Economy
United Technologies pled guilty to fraud in military contracts and agreed to pay $6 million in penalties in a five-year Federal investigation that has snared six other companies. [33.] 
 Economic forecasters, seeing the goal of recovery receding, have reversed themselves for the third time in three years, and now do not expect it to happen until winter, if then. [33.]
Americans dipped into their savings in July to maintain living standards, the Commerce Department reported. Spending rose three-tenths of 1 percent, to $4.08 trillion, last month, while incomes gained just two-tenths of 1 percent, to $5.05 trillion. [33.]
 
Companies
Edward S. Finkelstein and R. H. Macy have agreed on a severance package for the bankrupt retailer's former chairman. He is to get $750,000 a year through Dec. 31, 1994, and will have a secretary and health benefits, but will not be released from liability for Macy's financial problems. [33.]
G.M. plans to issue 25 million new class H shares to pay for the acquisition of General Dynamics' missile business, and possibly to generate fresh capital for itself. The company also said it would try to sell its entire bearing manufacturing operation. [35.]
Atari reported a $39.75 million loss for the second quarter. The computer and video game maker had an inventory write-down. [35.]
Nissan said it expected to report its first loss in 42 years, and that it would halve its dividend and cut its work force by 4,000. [35.]
Al Johnson plans to retire as chief executive of Wal-Mart's rapidly expanding Sam's Wholesale Club. Dean L. Sanders, head of operations at the Wal-Mart stores division, will succeed him. [35.]
Digital Equipment filed with the S.E.C. to offer $1 billion in debt securities, a departure from it practice under Kenneth H. Olsen, its founder and president, of avoiding debt. [35.]
 
Markets
Stocks drifted higher with the Dow industrials up 12.97 points at 3,267.61. A steady dollar in recent days helped the market reverse last week's retreat. For the week, the Dow gained 13.51 points. [36.]
Treasury bond prices slipped in quiet trading, as investors kept an eye on the course of the dollar. [36.]
The yen bounced up in reaction to Japan's economic package, but the dollar rose against the mark. Gold prices also rose. [42.] Cotton futures tumbled, grains were lower and energy gained. [42.]
 
Today's Columns
The next couple of weeks are crucial for job seekers. They should research positions and put together resumes and cover letters. And most importantly, they should look at their own skills and stay optimistic. Jan M. Rosen: Your Money. [34.]
Inventors need to understand the needs of corporations that can license, manufacture and market their inventions, says Richard C. Levy, a full-time independent inventor who makes a good living inventing toys. Edmund L. Andrews: Patents. [34.]

HEADLINE: Dow Is Up for a 4th Day, Rising 12.97

BYLINE: By Reuters

 BODY:
Stocks drifted higher yesterday, extending the recovery from last week's sharp selloff to four straight sessions.
A steady dollar in the last few days lent support to the stock market, which began retreating last week when it fell to record lows against the German mark. 
 The Dow Jones industrial average closed 12.97 points higher at 3,267.61. Stocks that rose outnumbered losers by 955 to 749 on the New York Stock Exchange. For the week, the Dow gained 13.51 points.
Volume was only 152.3 million shares, compared with 178.6 million shares on Thursday.
"I think the stability in the foreign exchange markets has helped the stock market in the last three days recover some, but it's a very tentative recovery," said Hugh Johnson, chief investment officer of the First Albany Corporation.
 The dollar closed at 1.4090 marks, little changed from the Thursday close of 1.4085.
Analysts said a rally in Japanese stocks gave Wall Street a sense of relief. Shares jumped 2.37 percent yesterday in Tokyo, and were up 11 percent for the week.
The Japanese Government approved the largest fiscal spending package since World War II yesterday to try to jolt the economy back to life. The package, which followed months of talks on how to end the economic slowdown, amounts to an injection of $86 billion into the economy. The Tokyo stock market has embraced the package.
"The continued rally in Tokyo has lessened people's fears for the short-term of any meltdown," said Bradley Turner, chairman of the investment policy committee at McDonald & Company.
Still, there were persistent worries about the course of the economy and the Presidential election.
 Mr. Turner said, "On any given day, the dollar may play a role, but I think that from now through the end of the third quarter, the focus is largely going to be on politics."
With Gov. Bill Clinton and President Bush in a close race, it is far from certain what economic policy will take shape after the November election. In the meantime, Wall Street is keeping a close watch.
The Standard & Poor's composite index of 500 stocks closed up 1.31 points at 414.84. The American Stock Exchange index gained 0.18, to 381.18.
The New York Stock Exchange composite index closed up 0.70 point at 228.43. The average share gained 11 cents.
The Nasdaq index gained 0.29 at 563.56. The Wilshire Associates Equity Index -- the market value of Big Board, American and over-the-counter issues -- was up 11.74, or three-tenths of a percent, to 3,991.83.

HEADLINE: Consumer Groups Attack Regulations Freeze

BYLINE: Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 28

 BODY:
In a joint report, two public-interest groups have strongly disputed the Bush Administration's statement that up to $20 billion will be saved under its current moratorium on new regulations.
"The Administration's claims of cost savings to the economy are pure voodoo accounting," Joan Claybrook, president of Public Citizen, said in issuing the report on Thursday. Public Citizen, founded by the consumer advocate Ralph Nader, worked on the report with O.M.B. Watch, a group that monitors the White House Office of Management and Budget. 
 Rather than saving money, the report said, the moratorium has damaged the public health and the environment. More than 75 regulations have been halted or weakened by the moratorium.
The White House has said that $15 billion to $20 billion has been saved in the first eight months of the moratorium, an estimate, it said, that was taken from figures submitted by two dozen Federal agencies.
"We stand by that number," said Jeffrey A. Nesbit, a spokesman for Vice President Dan Quayle. Mr. Quayle heads the White House Council on Competitiveness, which administers the effort to curb regulations.
The President declared a moratorium on all new regulations in January, making exceptions only for those rules deemed necessary because of imminent danger to the public health or those required by deadlines set in law.

LENGTH: 425 words

HEADLINE: One-Fisted Strength on China

 BODY:
Whether the issue is intellectual property rights or intellectuals jailed for their beliefs, the same facts apply. The U.S. has vast trade leverage with China and can use it to induce better Chinese behavior.
The Bush Administration now threatens China with sweeping tariff penalties for trade infractions. But at the same time, indifferent to the obvious contradiction, it insists that linking tariff penalties to human rights would jeopardize reform and hurt the wrong Chinese. The Administration cannot, honorably, have things both ways. 
 U.S. trade negotiators are right to warn Beijing that "we cannot let a country with this level of trade play by its own rules." Among the infractions cited are outright bans and quotas, discriminatory licensing requirements and failure to publish relevant laws and regulations. The Administration has given China until Oct. 10 to open its markets or face 100 percent tariffs on $4 billion of imports, including shoes, silks, luggage and electronic goods.
The express goal of this ultimatum is not to stop trade but to push Beijing into timely concessions. Last year, similar U.S. pressure worked; China agreed to respect intellectual property rights like patents and copyrights. Why does the Administration find such pressure unacceptable when directed at political prisoners, respect for human rights conventions and honoring arms agreements?
A bill overwhelmingly passed by the House and now pending in the Senate would make China's access to low tariffs conditional on "significant progress" in these areas. Noncompliance would bring tariff increases only for products of state industry, sensibly shielding the budding entrepreneurial sector.
Incredibly, the Administration threatens to veto this bill on principle. What principle? The only difference between the trade ultimatum and the human rights legislation is which goals the U.S. hopes to achieve. In fact, many see the Administration's trade stance as designed to draw Senate votes away from the human rights bill by flaunting toughness on economic issues.
Under normal circumstances, trade legislation should not be a vehicle for routine political concerns. But China is already in a special statutory category because of its Communist system and emigration restrictions. Gross violations of international rights standards are not routine politics.
The sad truth seems to be that the Bush Administration is prepared to lean hard on China where dollars and cents are concerned, but on human rights it doesn't care enough to try.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

HEADLINE: Cacucaco Journal;
'This Is Our Land' Has Angola Astir

BYLINE: By KENNETH B. NOBLE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: CACUACO, Angola, Aug. 24

 BODY:
The problem is simple, Jonas Savimbi told supporters at a rally here recently. For four centuries foreigners have ruled Angola -- first its Portuguese colonizers, then Soviet military advisers and eventually soldiers from Cuba, who backed the ruling party in this country's brutal 16-year-long civil war.
When he becomes President, Mr. Savimbi promised, Angola will be ruled by Angolans. 
 A month before Angolans vote in this country's first multiparty elections, Mr. Savimbi, the leader of the National Union for the Total Independence of Angola, or Unita, has made the anti-foreigner message a central theme in his presidential campaign.
The message is deceptive in its simplicity, political analysts here say, and has potentially explosive implications in a continent where tribal and racial tensions across artificial frontiers haunt many governments. "Every foreigner who comes to occupy Angola will be fought against," the former guerrilla fighter told the 10,000 to 15,000 people who had gathered in a dusty square to hear him speak.
 
Hypocrisy, Opponents Say
 "People get upset," he acknowledged, when he insists that "Angolans must come first, second and third."
But he said that those who feel that way are "outsiders" who are mainly concerned with maintaining their tight economic and political grip on the country. Those days will soon be over, Mr. Savimbi asserted.
The issue has focused attention on how the ruling Popular Movement for the Liberation of Angola brought in tens of thousands of Soviets and Cubans to help them hold power after Portugal granted its African colonies independence in 1975.
But supporters of the ruling party accuse Mr. Savimbi of hypocrisy because he eagerly sought the support of foreigners -- namely the United States and South Africa -- in his guerrilla war against the Luanda Government. And when Mr. Savimbi talks about the future role of "foreigners" in Angola, many political analysts and Western diplomats here say he is also alluding to Angola's large Portuguese and mixed-race population.
"Savimbi considers anyone who isn't a pure African a foreigner," said a young Angolan businessman whose father is Portuguese and his mother Angolan. "This is a very frightening time here."
Venacio de Moura, the Deputy Foreign Minister and one of the country's most seasoned politicians, declared that Mr. Savimbi was "a racist and a tribalist." Mr. de Moura added that he knew of Portuguese and mixed-raced families, many of whom have spent decades in Angola before and during the civil war, who had fled the country because they feared a pogrom if Mr. Savimbi won.
 
'His Worst Enemy'
 A Western political consultant who has advised both the Angolan Government and opposition parties about the elections said that Mr. Savimbi's fiery nationalism had alienated many better-educated urban voters who might have felt tempted to vote for change. "Savimbi is proving to be his worst enemy in this campaign," the consultant said.
Savimbi aides say there is nothing threatening about his message. Those who have found a racist or tribalist meaning in Mr. Savimbi's campaign declarations have "a guilty conscience," said Jardo Mulekalia, Unita's chief Washington representative.
"We have said time and time again that we consider anybody who was born here, whether white or black, Angolan," Mr. Mulekalia said. "If they feel that by referring only to Angolans we only mean black, then that's their problem."
Unita officials are quick to point to the ethnically and racially broad range of Mr. Savimbi's senior advisers. The core group is drawn mainly from Mr. Savimbi's ethnic group, the Ovimbundu, who come from Angola's mostly rural and agrarian central highlands, but they also consist of people from the Kimbundu and Luanda-Chowde groups in the northeast, the Bakongo in the northwest and a number of Portuguese.
Mr. Savimbi himself made a similar point at a recent rally in Baia Farta. "I would like to stress here that foreigners who want to come and work in Angola in order to develop this country are welcome," he told the crowd. But then he warned: "We do not want, however, people coming here to enslave us. This is our land."
 
The Message Is Clear
 The debate hinges in part on the personality and style of Mr. Savimbi himself. While professing that he is a staunch believer in the democratic process, the goateed 57-year-old rebel leader has often been accused of having an authoritarian streak and of encouraging a cult of personality.
Another reason that Mr. Savimbi's anti-foreigner theme has caused such a stir is that many Angolans feel that the upper levels of the ruling party are disproportionally filled with mixed-race Angolans, or mesticos as they are called in Portuguese.
Whether Mr. Savimbi is intentionally trying to foment dissent among Angola's ethnic and racial groups is open to dispute. What is clear, however, is that many people have no trouble understanding the message.
A businessman of Portuguese ancestry recently recalled sitting in a restaurant when a group of Unita soldiers struck up a conversation.
"They weren't hostile or anything like that," the businessman said, "but after we had a couple of drinks, they said -- and it was said in a casual and non-threatening tone -- that people like me were no longer going to control this country when Savimbi takes over."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Jonas Savimbi's promise that Angola will be ruled by Angolans when he becomes President has caused a stir among many citizens in the country. (Alain Morvan/Gamma-Liason)
