HEADLINE: Daniel Ludwig, Billionaire Businessman, Dies at 95

BYLINE: By ERIC PACE

 BODY:
Daniel K. Ludwig, the self-made American shipowner and real estate man who was for years one of the world's richest men, died Thursday afternoon at his midtown Manhattan apartment. He was 95 years old.
The cause of his death was heart failure, and he had been in poor health for several years, said R. Palmer Baker Jr., a lawyer who is Mr. Ludwig's executor. 
 Mr. Baker said Mr. Ludwig had owned about 60 ocean-going vessels years ago, at the height of his shipping career. He said that over the years, Mr. Ludwig was also "a major participant on an international scale in the production of forest products, oil and gas, coal and other minerals, in hotels and other real estate development programs, and in a number of cattle-ranching and agricultural ventures."
The son of a Michigan real-estate man, Mr. Ludwig was best known in his later years for his unsuccessful billion-dollar development project in a Brazilian jungle enclave the size of Connecticut. In 1982 he made arrangements to give it up after problems that included Brazilian nationalism and bureaucracy and his own volatile executive style. Mr. Baker said Mr. Ludwig retained no land holdings in Brazil at the time of his death.
Mr. Ludwig (pronounced LUDD-wig) was secretive and had assets that were difficult to appraise. Forbes magazine estimated his wealth at $1.2 billion late last year, but the current issue of Fortune magazine does not include him on a list of billionaires. In the early 1970's he gave "substantially all" of his foreign assets to the Ludwig Institute for Cancer Research in Zurich, Mr. Baker said.
In a telephone interview yesterday, Mr. Baker said the current endowment of the institute was about $700 million. Asked what Mr. Ludwig's own net worth was, Mr. Baker declined to reply, but he said Mr. Ludwig's holdings in the United States at the time of his death included a substantial amount of real estate. He declined to elaborate, but he said Mr. Ludwig had no posts in any companies or charitable organization.
 
Shipping Empire Built on Credit
 New York businessmen familiar with Mr. Ludwig's holdings said that in the summer of 1990 ill health had left him incapacitated. They said parts of his business empire had been sold off, with the proceeds going to the cancer institute.
In earlier decades, Mr. Ludwig built a huge shipping empire mainly on credit, then embarked on a variety of large-scale, long-term undertakings, lavishly investing the cash earned by his ships and using them as collateral for more loans. His interests extended to more than a score of countries.
Mr. Ludwig went into shipping at the age of 19, using a borrowed $5,000. In 1968, when he was 71, Fortune magazine said he was one of eight Americans worth $500 million or more, and in 1976 the magazine said he and the insurance executive John D. MacArthur were the country's only billionaires or near-billionaires.
Mr. Ludwig's epic Brazilian development project, known as Jari, was in an isolated inland enclave in the Amazon region in the north of the country. He bought the jungle-covered land for $3 million in 1967, planning to reforest much of it with fast-growing Burmese melina trees to meet a world fiber shortage that he -- correctly -- thought was coming in the 1970's and 1980's.
The project, whose operations ranged from forestry and agriculture to mining and cattle-ranching, drew criticism from conservationists. .
But Mr. Ludwig and the foreign experts he brought in persevered, bulldozing existing forest, tapping deposits of kaolin and bauxite and building a 26-mile railroad, 3,000 miles of trails and roads and settlements that had 30,000 inhabitants by early 1982. Equipment imported to the enclave included a $200 million pulp mill.
Eventually, after more than a decade, Mr. Ludwig warned a Brazilian Government leader, Gen. Golbery do Couto e Silva, that he would close Jari if the Government did not ease stubborn bureaucratic problems he was facing, and provide him with financial relief.
The Brazilian leadership withheld the aid Mr. Ludwig sought, and he eventually set about abandoning Jari. His failing health was officially given as the reason.
 
$863 Million Gamble Showed Philosophy
 By early 1982 an arrangement had been worked out whereby a Brazilian state commercial bank, the Banco do Brazil, other companies and a Brazilian industrialist, Augusto Azevedo Antunes, were to come up with $280 million by the mid-1980's to take over the project. A new Jari company was to be formed, and its prospectus reported that Mr. Ludwig had invested $863 million in the undertaking since buying the land. The prospectus said his outlay had been the equivalent of $1.15 billion at 1981 prices.
That colossal, unsuccessful gamble by Mr. Ludwig reflected some of the same qualities that made him so extraordinarily successful in other enterprises: his tireless entrepreneurial drive -- he started his first business venture when he was 9 years old -- coupled with an unusual amount of imagination and foresight and with the constant use of what he called his antennae for sensing a potential profit.
"Nearly everyone has those antennae; most people just don't use them," he once told an interviewer. "I spend my time putting projects together."
For decades Mr. Ludwig's main corporate vehicle was a New York-based shipping concern, National Bulk Carriers Inc., which he owned outright and built into one of the world's largest private multinational corporations. The corporation once said in a brochure that it had more than 20,000 employees and "several billion dollars in assets."
But the businessmen familiar with Mr. Ludwig's holdings said in 1990 that the interests and operations of National Bulk Carriers had very greatly diminished. And Forbes magazine reported in October 1990 that his shipping interests had been permitted to dwindle.
Mr. Ludwig emphasized patience, discernment and creativity in his business affairs. "We may look at 20 possible ventures before we find one that suits our thinking," he once told an interviewer. "The raw materials of a deal, before it is assembled, look very different from the final arrangement."
In the 1940's a process for welding rather than riveting tankers was developed at a Virginia shipyard that Mr. Ludwig ran, and in the 1950's he went into shipbuilding in Japan to take advantage of low labor costs and other favorable conditions. There he turned out a succession of bulk carriers and ever-larger tankers, pioneering the development of what came to be known as the supertanker.
 
Hard Businessman, Casual Stroller
 Mr. Ludwig concentrated on his business and was casual and unpretentious about much of the rest his life.
A tall, lean man with a pronounced stoop, he used to be seen strolling in midtown Manhattan. He wore the same plastic raincoat for years. He liked to lunch in midtown restaurants, often alone. He used to make and receive many telephone calls himself. He often flew economy class. And for recreation he liked to watch television reruns of old movies starring his friend Clark Gable.
"Clark was all man," he once recalled crustily. "He stood head and shoulders above all those two-bit phonies in Hollywood today."
Mr. Ludwig was often profane and short-tempered in his business dealings. In 1938, after he made indirect use of one of his ships as security for a loan, the vessel went astray in a hurricane. When a worried banker called and asked, "How's my collateral?" Mr. Ludwig said, "When we find the goddamn thing I'll let you know."
And in the 1950's, cables used to arrive at the Ludwig shipyard offices in Japan with such blunt messages as: "Mr. Ludwig wants to know what the hell is holding you up."
Despite his crustiness, Mr. Ludwig was firmly loyal to some old associates. "I like to do business with friends," he told an interviewer in 1957. "Why the hell should I deal with somebody who isn't my friend?"
Mr. Ludwig was an economic conservative, and some of his famous friends and acquaintances were on the conservative side. They included Richard M. Nixon, whom Mr. Ludwig and his second wife, Gertrude Virginia Ludwig, had to dinner before he became President, and Ronald Reagan, whose photograph was said to be displayed in Mr. Ludwig's apartment.
 
Early Investment In a Sunken Boat
 Boating and shipping were in Daniel Keith Ludwig's blood. Four uncles were captains of vessels plying the Great Lakes, and he was born, on June 24, 1897, on the shore of Lake Michigan, in the little port of South Haven, Mich., near the site of a local landmark, Ludwig's Pier. It had been built by his grandfather.
As a 9-year-old boy, the story goes, Daniel Ludwig earned money by selling popcorn and shining shoes, and he paid $75 for a sunken small boat that he then repaired and rented out for more than $150.
He left school after the eighth grade, became a salesman for a ship supplies firm, went to night school and worked for a time at a marine engine manufacturing company.
It was then, at the age of 19, that Mr. Ludwig went into business on his own, using the $5,000, which had been borrowed mainly on his father's signature. He spent the money on an old paddle steamer, converted it into a barge and used it as the nucleus for a fleet of freight vessels, which he used for hauling molasses and wood. But business was slow and he looked around for ways to improve it.
"I had to hit on something or I was busted," he later recalled.
He went into the tanker business, starting by chartering a single tanker. In 1926, he hurt his back badly in an explosion on board a tanker. Undaunted by the years of pain that followed, he persisted in the shipping business.
During the 1930's, despite the Depression, Mr. Ludwig managed to expand his operations. A watershed in his career was a 1936 Chemical Bank loan. He used it to buy several freight vessels that he made over into tankers, substantially expanding his operations.
 
Extended Holdings Beyond Shipping
 By the end of World War II, Mr. Ludwig was the owner of the United States' fifth-largest tanker fleet. He set about building more and bigger tankers at the shipyard he leased in 1951 in the naval base city of Kure in southwestern Japan.
In the 1960's, having become one of the richest shipowners, he began branching out into many other fields, and by the mid-1970's, his interests included the United Pocahontas Coal Company in West Virginia, with an annual output of a million tons of low-sulfur, high-grade coal; mining in Australia (in 1976 he sold half a coal mine there to British Petroleum for $200 million); savings and loan associations; half-interest in an Australian insurance company, and a private merchant banking firm, European-American Securities Inc.
In October 1990, Forbes magazine reported that Mr. Ludwig's investments had included several savings and loan associations that it said were then operated by Resolution Trust. Forbes also said a large-scale development undertaking by Mr. Ludwig in New Jersey had become "stalled."
Mr. Ludwig was divorced in 1937 from his first wife, Gladys Madeline Ludwig, after 9 years of marriage, a biographer of Mr. Ludwig, Jerry Shields, wrote in a 1986 book, "The Invisible Billionaire: Daniel Ludwig." Mr. Ludwig married his second wife later in 1937.
Mr. Ludwig's executor, Mr. Baker, said the businessman's only survivor was his wife.
Mr. Baker said a daughter, Patricia Ludwig, was born to Mr. Ludwig's first wife during the marriage and survives, but that Mr. Ludwig did not acknowledge her as his child.

HEADLINE: 45 Are Charged In Drug Fraud Of Medicaid

BYLINE: By RONALD SULLIVAN

 BODY:
In what Federal officials said was the largest number of Medicaid-fraud indictments ever filed in New York State, 45 people have been charged by a Federal grand jury in Manhattan with the illegal sale and redistribution of prescription drugs.
The indictments against 3 ringleaders, 32 pharmacists and 10 other defendants were filed on Thursday and announced yesterday by Otto G. Obermaier, the United States Attorney for the Southern District of New York. The 45 defendants were among 69 people arrested in raids conducted throughout the New York City metropolitan region on June 30 by the Federal Bureau of Investigation. 
 Thirteen pharmacies were closed and 46 searched in what the F.B.I. said was part of a national crackdown on the criminal diversion of prescription drugs for illicit sale on the streets and resale to drug stores.
Federal officials described a black market in which Medicaid recipients, whose medications are paid for by the state and Federal government, sold their prescriptions or the drugs to distributors, who would sell them back to druggists with large Medicaid clienteles at reduced prices.
Stanley Jacobson, of Northport, L.I., who was charged with being a ringleader, was said by prosecutors to have kept such a large inventory of the diverted drugs in a warehouse in Farmingdale, L.I., that he needed a computer to keep track of them.
Two others, Martin Bobb, of Port Jefferson, L.I., and Donald Solof, of Howard Beach, Queens, were said by prosecutors "to have amassed enormous wealth from the scheme, including a private airplane, an ambulette business, a real estate business, numerous bank accounts and substantial quantities of cash."

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: Senate Race;
In Bible Belt, Underdog Runs On Backing Abortion Rights

BYLINE: By CLIFFORD KRAUSS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: OKLAHOMA CITY, Aug. 25

 BODY:
For the Bible Belt, it was a political caricature from hell: Steve Lewis, the Democratic candidate for the Senate, distributing bumper stickers from the window of an abortion clinic.
In a normal election year that would not be the cartoon image a politician wants to see in The Daily Oklahoman, the largest-circulation newspaper in a state where religion often mixes with politics like barbecue and beans. 
 But running at least 25 points behind in the polls against Senator Don Nickles, the far better financed two-term Republican incumbent, Mr. Lewis has little to lose by striking out on the risky strategy of featuring his support for abortion rights.
He is trying to use the most controversial of family issues to drive a wedge between Mr. Nickles and the voters. The tactic could easily backfire by November, however, and even some of Mr. Lewis's own campaign workers admit they are afraid that will happen.
Some out-of-state abortion-rights advocates have expressed concern that the race could become viewed nationally as a referendum on the issue, a problem for them because Oklahoma is a state where their advocates are not nearly as well organized as the Christian fundamentalist right.
"It's a definitional issue that helps define whose side you're on," said Mr. Lewis, 49 years old, who is known around the state for an unsuccessful bid for Governor in 1990.
Mr. Lewis has told voters that unlike Mr. Nickles he would have voted against confirming Clarence Thomas to the Supreme Court and that as a Senator he would support only Court nominees who promise to uphold Roe v. Wade, the 1973 Supreme Court decision legalizing abortion.
Mr. Nickles became nationally identified with uncompromising opposition to abortion during the Republican National Convention last week, when the platform committee he headed included a plank calling for a constitutional amendment banning all abortions, even in cases of incest and rape.
But the 43-year-old Republican drew notice at home when he decided not to mention the issue at all in a speech presenting the platform before the convention. In its coverage of the first day of the Houston convention, the lead story in the daily Tulsa World noted that Mr. Nickles never mentioned the "A-word" during his speech nor in remarks to the state delegation. The headline of the article read: "Nickles' Speech Shows Party Trying to Mute Abortion Issue."
Mr. Lewis's campaign staff and the Democratic State Committee tried to exploit the impression of uncertainty conveyed by the headline, releasing a flurry of news releases promoting abortion rights during the convention. In a tour of eastern Oklahoma on Saturday, Mr. Lewis proclaimed his support for abortion rights at every campaign event.
Speaking before customers at a restaurant in Ponca City, Mr. Lewis asked, "Are you for Don Nickles having a vote in deciding whether your wife or your daughter has a choice when it comes to a very personal matter of conscience?"
Some customers burst out cheering, but his remarks seemed to cut both ways. "He's talking about choice, but he's not talking about the choice of the child who is destroyed," said Emma Walcher, 66, a retired school teacher and a registered Democrat.
In contrast to his opponent, Mr. Nickles did not mention abortion in two speeches on Friday in Ponca City and Oklahoma City. Instead, he stressed less controversial topics like his desire to cut taxes and regulations on the oil and gas industry.
Local and national Democrats say Mr. Nickles ought to be vulnerable because their surveys show that few voters associate him with any legislative accomplishments. His homespun charm helped him prevail in a tough race six years ago when the Oklahoma economy was weaker than it is today.
Still, in a year when voter discontent has thrown every political certainty into question, the abortion issue has added an unpredictable new twist for Mr. Nickles and the rest of the Republican ticket.
"I'm a registered Republican but I don't feel very Republican these days," said Margaret Johnson, 39, a legal secretary in Oklahoma City. "I'm pro-choice."

HEADLINE: New $100 Million Sensor System To Direct New York Traffic Flow

BYLINE: By CLIFFORD J. LEVY

 BODY:
After years of delay, New York City is installing an elaborate and costly system of sensors under its roadways to enable the Transportation Department to monitor congestion, identify trouble spots and control the flow of traffic by quickly changing the duration of red lights.
Spanning a city whose crowded streets and highways often seem to be little more than a gridlocked maze, the sensors and "smart" traffic lights are the major components of a planned $100 million traffic-tracking network. 
 The first phase of the system is to begin operating next summer in Manhattan, transportation officials say. A total of 1,100 electromagnetic sensors are to be embedded in the asphalt of roadways in the borough. The sensors will detect vehicles as they pass over and transmit information on their number, speed and type to a central computer in Long Island City, Queens.
 
Identifying Problems
 Traffic officials will use the data to identify tie-ups from ordinary congestion or from extraordinary events like an accident on the West Side Highway, a water main break in Herald Square or a protest on lower Broadway.
Aided by pictures from 50 closed-circuit television cameras to be mounted on light poles across the borough, the traffic officials will be able to alert traffic agents and the police to help clear the congestion. They will also be able to use the computer to adjust the borough's 3,000 traffic lights to move vehicles from the affected areas and keep others away. Now, the timing and duration of most traffic lights can be changed only at their control boxes on the street -- one at a time.
At least 400 sensors have been installed so far in Manhattan. An estimated 883,000 vehicles enter the borough on an average day.
A traffic-control system similar to that planned for New York is already in use in Los Angeles and Tokyo. Sensors alone are used on the New Jersey Turnpike and the Long Island Expressway. The New York system would be one of the most expensive and elaborate in the world, transportation experts said.
New York's methods of surveying congestion have advanced little since the days of the tail-finned Cadillac, and transportation officials hope that by reducing congestion, the new system will cut pollution and bolster the city's economy. A study by the Texas Transportation Institute of traffic congestion in major metropolitan areas estimated that New York loses $7 billion a year from traffic delays. The study took into account the cost of pollution controls, delays in transporting goods to merchants, accidents and auto insurance.
But some transportation experts and officials warn that the new network relies on advanced technology operating in a city with extremely complex traffic patterns. "These systems are so complicated that they are hard to model in a laboratory," said Anthony F. Hotz, executive director of the Intelligent Vehicle Highway Systems Research Program at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology. "There's never ever any real guarantee that the technology is going to work the way you'd like it to."
Others, including the president of the New York City Transit Authority, Alan F. Kiepper, criticize city officials for pumping so much money into a computer system as its roadways continue to deteriorate and financing for mass transit is reduced.
"The biggest problem in New York City," Mr. Kiepper said, "is too many autos and too few highways and underutilized capacity on public transit."
The city's Transportation Department began designing the network, which is financed largely with money from the state and Federal Governments, in the early 1980's. At that time, officials said they expected to have it installed in Manhattan by the late 1980's.
 
Cable Problems
 But Raman K. Patel, chief of systems engineering at the department, said recently that the city scrapped its original plan in the mid-80's after discovering that the cables it had expected to use to link the sensors to the control center would have been unable to transmit enough data. The problem was caught before any contracts had been let so the city had spent relatively little, Mr. Patel said. He could not provide a specific figure.
After that initial delay, the department ran into problems in 1988 when it began embedding the 1,100 sensors and connecting one million feet of underground cable in Manhattan, Mr. Patel said. Each sensor is 36 square feet, and takes two workers 8 to 10 hours to install. A sensor is embedded under each lane at an intersection.
When the network of sensors and "smart" lights is operating in Manhattan, it is also expected to enable engineers to control the traffic lights to increase the speed of inbound traffic during the morning rush and outbound at the end of the workday. The central computer will also feed the data from the vehicle sensors to government agencies and companies that provide traffic reports to radio and television stations. Both have long relied almost exclusively on traffic spotters in helicopters and on drivers and traffic agents on the ground.
"The problem now is that we just don't know enough about the traffic flow," said the Transportation Commissioner, Lucius J. Riccio, "This is a way to provide us with far greater information about the use of our roads and bridges. It will enable us to make smarter strategies."
When the sensors detect street congestion, the Transportation Department will be able to intervene directly by manipulating traffic lights. But transportation officials say the system will also assist them greatly in monitoring and controlling traffic on the highways of Manhattan, even though they have no traffic lights. The officials say that when the sensors detect congestion on a roadway like the West Side Highway, they will be able to send traffic agents to clear the tie-up or even close entrances more swiftly than they can now.
After spending $45 million in Manhattan, the city plans to spend $50 to $60 million to expand the network to the Bronx, Brooklyn and Queens by 1996, Mr. Patel said. Those three boroughs already have some sensors and some controlled traffic lights, but that system is far more primitive than the one being installed in Manhattan. The city has already installed a small network on Staten Island and does not plan to upgrade it.
Mr. Patel declined to estimate exactly how much the system would reduce congestion or pollution.
"I do not want to speculate," he said. "But we will make an impact across the board."
City officials hope that by the end of the decade, motorists may use computers in their vehicles, homes and offices to tap into the information from the sensors to help them plan the fastest routes around the city.
The department says its new system will help it respond better to crises like the one touched off by the aborted landing and fire aboard a Trans World Airlines jet at Kennedy International Airport during the thick of the evening rush hour on July 30. The incident forced the police to close the Van Wyck Expressway, a major highway in Queens, from Rockaway Boulevard to the airport to allow for passage of emergency vehicles.
 
In the Control Center
 That move caused gridlock on nearby streets and roads and extensive delays that stretched across the city and into Nassau County. At the Transportation Department's nerve center at 40 Worth Street in lower Manhattan, engineers and operators used a maze of computers and radios to monitor traffic and coordinate the city's response.
As radios squawked updates from traffic agents on the road and traffic reporters in the sky, Nelson Castillo, the center's chief of communications operations, pored over a map of southeastern Queens, trying to pinpoint the best routes for channeling traffic away from the Van Wyck and the airport.
The Grand Central Parkway seemed a good candidate for cars, but Mr. Castillo had no way of knowing how congested it was. No traffic agents were nearby and no traffic reporters from radio stations had mentioned the parkway in the last few minutes.
By chance, Leon Heyward, a Transportation Department official, was driving on the Grand Central Parkway, and Mr. Castillo learned from him over the department's radio frequency that congestion was moderate. "If we had the new sensors," Mr. Castillo said, "we could see where the traffic is backed up and how far it is backed up."
Officials in Los Angeles say their system of sensors, which first began operating for the 1984 Summer Olympics, has worked well. The Los Angeles Transportation Department has spent $60 million to install 3,200 sensors linked by computer to 800 traffic signals, said Edwin Rowe, the department's general manager. Downtown Los Angeles receives about half as many vehicles as Manhattan on an average day.


GRAPHIC: A traffic management system that is to be completerd next summer will allow Transportation Department engineers to speed the flow of traffic in Manhattan. Engineers can also control the pacing of traffic lights. That will help them to speed traffic into the city in the mornings and out in the late afternoones. The system is based on a network of electromagnetic sensors under the street. Here is how it would work.
Chart/Diagram: "Transportations: Speeding Up the Flow of Traffic"
Electromagnetic sensors under Fifth Avenue detect that traffic has come to a halt near 19th Street. The Transportation Department uses cameras to determine that a serious accident has occurred. It radios traffic agents to the scene; it also alerts companies that provide traffic reports to radio stations, which then tell motorists to avoid the intersection.
A control center in Long Island City, Queens, increases the duration of green lights on Broadway to speed traffic away from the accident and ensure that the delays do not spread. Traffic control officers direct vehicles to Broadway. (pg. 22)

HEADLINE: Bond Prices Drop on Worries About Dollar

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
Treasury bond prices were mostly lower yesterday in quiet trading.
The price of the 30-year bond fell 11/32 point, or $3.44 cents per $1,000 in face amount. Its yield, which rises when prices fall, rose to 7.42 percent, from 7.39 percent late Thursday. 
 Some traders sold bonds as the dollar dropped against some foreign currencies after tumbling to record lows earlier in the week.
A decline in the dollar hurts the bond market by decreasing the likelihood that the Federal Reserve will lower interest rates. A lowering of rates would enhance the value of fixed-income securities like bonds.
 
Acting on Profits
 Yesterday's selling followed a rise in bond prices Thursday, indicating that traders were taking some of their profits.
The market appeared uncertain about the course of the dollar, including whether it would continue to decline and what effect it would have on the economy.
In late New York trading, the dollar fell against the Japanese yen, while rising slightly against the German mark.
Elliott Platt, research director at the Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette Securities Corporation, said the market was affected in early trading by the University of Michigan's consumer confidence report for August. He said traders had anticipated a decline, but the report showed a moderate increase.
In the secondary market for Treasury bonds, short-term maturities were unchanged to down 1/32 , intermediate maturities were off by a range of 1/16 to 7/32 , and long-term issues were down 11/32 , the Telerate Inc. financial information service reported.
The movement of a point is equivalent to a change of $10 in the price of a bond with a $1,000 face value.
The Lehman Brothers Daily Treasury bond index, which measures price movements on all outstanding Treasury issues with maturities of a year or longer, fell 0.89, to 1,266.30.
 
Rates on Bills Are Up
 Yields on three-month Treasury bills rose to 3.22 percent as the discount rose a basis point, to 3.16 percent. Yields on six-month bills rose to 3.34 percent as the discount rose a basis point, to 3.25 percent. Yields on one-year bills rose to 3.45 percent as the discount rose a basis point, to 3.33 percent.
A basis point is one-hundredth of a percentage point. The yield is the annualized return on an investment in a Treasury bill. The discount is the percentage at which bills are selling below the face value, which is paid at maturity.
The Federal funds rate, the interest on overnight loans between banks, was quoted at 35/16 percent, down from 3 3/8 percent late Thursday.
In the tax-exempt market, the Bond Buyer index of 40 actively traded municipal bonds closed at 97 3/8, up 3/32 . The average yield to maturity was unchanged from late Thursday, at 6.36 percent.

HEADLINE: Japan Plans to Spend $86 Billion To Stimulate Its Lagging Economy

BYLINE: By ANDREW POLLACK, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TOKYO, Aug. 28

 BODY:
Saying the economy here is in severe straits, Japan's Government tonight announced the largest economic rescue package in the nation's history to try to stimulate growth and strengthen a banking system that has been jeopardized by falling land and stock prices.
The centerpiece of the plan, aimed at jolting the economy, consists of a one-time increase in Government spending of 10.7 trillion yen, or about $86 billion. That money would be used to more than double public works spending for the current fiscal year, which ends next March 31, and to provide new incentives to encourage business investment.
 
U.S. Seeks a Bigger Market

 A second part consists of measures to stabilize banks and other financial institutions by making it easier for them to dispose of bad loans and by propping up land and stock prices. 
 The announcement is giving an immediate psychological lift to business confidence and the stock market. The Nikkei index, after soaring more than 1,000 points on Thursday in anticipation of the plan, rose 415 more points today to close at 17,970.
The United States has been pushing Japan to adopt measures to stimulate growth, in part because it believes a growing Japanese economy will provide a larger market for American exports. Because Japan imports so few products, its politically nettlesome trade surplus with the rest of the world is expected to reach a record of at least $120 billion this year.
The value of the measures is more than 50 percent higher than the amount contemplated two months ago and appears to have been increased by more than 1 trillion yen, or $8 billion, just in the last day.
The rapid increase reflects the growing concern of policy makers here as Japan's mighty electronics and automobile manufacturers begin to report losses, the country's weakened banks become reluctant to make loans for corporate investment and the unemployment rate of 2.2 percent, though relatively low compared with other countries, threatens to begin climbing.
"The Ministry of Finance and other Government agencies have finally accepted the view that the Japanese economy is very weak," said Yoshihisa Kitai, senior economist with the Long Term Credit Bank of Japan.
Government officials estimated that the measures, representing 2.3 percent of last year's gross national product, would increase economic growth by 2.4 percentage points in the next 12 months. In other words, if the G.N.P. would have grown 2 percent in that period, it should now grow 4.4 percent.
Other economists said that such a large package would indeed spur growth, but that the effect would be gradual. Japan's Parliament is not expected to meet until October to approve a supplementary budget to cover the increased public spending. After that, it will take several months for the money to actually be spent.
"Its real effect is only going to be on 1993," said Kenneth S. Courtis, strategist for Deutsche Bank in Asia, adding that Japan will not achieve its growth target of 3.5 percent for this fiscal year. Nevertheless, he said: "We've never seen a supplemental budget as big as this before. These are very big numbers."
Some business leaders said they were disappointed that the measures did not include tax cuts for individuals. Despite assertions by Japan that it wants to encourage consumer spending, the economic system here is heavily oriented toward helping manufacturers.
 
Purchase of Foreign Goods
 Japanese officials said they could not yet estimate how much imports would increase over all because of the economic package. But they said the Government itself would buy $250 million of foreign goods, mostly scientific and medical equipment, in connection with the extra public works spending this year. In addition to relying on general economic growth to spur imports, the plan announced today includes some special incentives, like low-interest loans for companies that want to sell foreign products in Japan.
The heart of the plan calls for increased public spending of 8.6 trillion yen, or $69 billion. Most of that will be used by both national and local governments to build roads, sewers, schools and other public works.
The remainder will be used to help the sluggish real estate market. That includes an additional 800 billion yen, or $6.4 billion, for housing assistance and 1.55 trillion yen, or $12.4 billion, to buy land for use in future public projects.
In addition to increased public spending, there will be loans and tax incentives valued at 2.1 trillion yen, or $17 billion, to help small businesses and to encourage companies to invest in energy-saving equipment and automation.
Government officials said Japan would probably be able to handle the increased spending without running a budget deficit or spurring inflation.
A second set of measures is aimed at shoring up the financial system and the stock market. Some of these were announced earlier this month, while some are new.
The economic downturn has hobbled banks with huge amounts of bad loans. And the fall in stock prices by almost 60 percent from the peak at the end of 1989 has made it difficult for banks to meet international requirements for their capital levels.
The Government said today that it would provide tax breaks to help banks write off bad loans. It also said that by the end of the year it would come up with concrete proposals to relieve banks of some of the land they have seized when borrowers failed to meet loan payments.
Analysts have likened this idea to the Resolution Trust Corporation in the United States, which was set up to help resolve the savings and loan crisis. But Japanese officials say that unlike in the United States, they do not want to use taxpayer money in this effort.
As for the stock market, the Government said it would make changes that would allow more money in public pension funds and savings programs to be invested in stocks. It will also postpone plans to sell stock in former Government monopolies including Nippon Telegraph and Telephone and Japan Tobacco. And it will reduce the minimum number of shares that must be bought or sold at one time to make it easier for individuals to buy stocks.
This 10.7 trillion yen rescue effort, equal to 2.3 percent of the G.N.P., is the biggest ever in Japan not only in absolute amount but also relative to the size of the economy. The last big pump-priming effort, announced in May 1987, amounted to 6 trillion yen and 1.8 percent of G.N.P.
To be sure, what seems like a weak economy here might be envied elsewhere. The gross national product is expected to grow by 2 percent or so this year while in other recession-bound nations it shrinks. And, officially at least, there is still a labor shortage, although it is about to evaporate.
According to figures released by the Labor Ministry today, there were 104 jobs being offered for each 100 people seeking jobs. But that figure has dropped from 147 jobs for each 100 job seekers in March 1991. Moreover, many large corporations have announced that they will trim their work forces by thousands of people over the next few years and reduce hiring of new college graduates. That could lead to a rise in the unemployment rate.

HEADLINE: Some Recall the Apple Sellers and Hoovervilles

 BODY:
To the Editor:
David Shipley's attempt to resurrect the reputation of Herbert Hoover (Editorial Notebook, Aug. 10) is a valiant effort. Unfortunately, I remember Hoover. Granted, he was a successful mining engineer. We also heard a great deal about his success in the World War I relief effort, especially during his campaign. 
 It is noteworthy that he wrote his own speeches -- unless you had to listen to them. If there were good ideas buried in that deluge of verbiage, they were obscured by his dreary monotone. When he took office, the unemployment rate was 4.4 percent. When he left it was 23.6. If "he did his best to end the Depression," he was a dismal failure. Like most Republican Presidents, he appointed many commissions to study these problems, while the economy and the average citizen suffered. The reports were generally shelved, and little was tried.
This is not dull history to me. I was there. I saw the apple sellers on the street corners. I saw the empty, pleading eyes of unemployed men wearing sandwich boards asking for a job -- any job. And there were the shacks made of old cartons for the homeless on many empty city lots. These were dubbed Hoovervilles.
Hoover probably did save the banking business with the Reconstruction Finance Corporation, but note that it was created in the final year of his term. It took the bank holiday imposed by Franklin D. Roosevelt as soon as he took office to prevent serious runs on the banks. And it was the quick action of the Roosevelt Administration in presenting a series of "must pass" emergency bills to Congress that prevented further debacle.
I do not wish to demean Hoover's character. He was sincere, honorable, but believed only business could grow us out of crisis. That seems like the "trickle down" theory, which could more properly be called the "trickle on" theory.
 
MILTON SLATER
North Tarrytown, N.Y., Aug. 10, 1992

HEADLINE: Hard Times Indeed for Forecasters

BYLINE: By LOUIS UCHITELLE

 BODY:
For the third time in three years, the nation's most prominent economic forecasters have reversed themselves, and they now say the recovery they had expected by late summer will not happen until this winter -- if then.
With new blows to the American economy announced almost daily, some economists even acknowledge that their forecasting techniques might not be adequate to foresee accurately when the stagnation that has afflicted the United States for more than three years will come to an end. 
 That view shows up in public comments by Alan Greenspan, chairman of the Federal Reserve. It is reflected in the August survey of 50 forecasters polled monthly by Blue Chip Economic Indicators. And it showed up in interviews this week with economists who had predicted earlier this year that the economy by now would clearly be on its way to recovery.
 
'Structural Change' Cited

 
 "I am far more wary today than I was a few months ago of predicting how an economy in the midst of structural change is going to perform," said Stephen S. Roach, a senior economist at Morgan Stanley & Company. He had said in the spring that by now a recovery would be under way.
The prolonged stagnation is an issue in the Presidential election campaign, of course. But in all their economic proposals neither President Bush nor Gov. Bill Clinton has embraced either of the two principal solutions offered by economists. One is to let the stagnation run its course; once consumers and corporations work off their huge debts, they will borrow and spend again, and that process cannot be artificially rushed.
The other argues for an artificial stimulant: Government should spend billions on public-works projects, driving up the deficit in the process, but putting people to work with new wages to spend. Companies, in turn, would invest and expand to satisfy the growing demand.
The problem with the first solution is that the wait could be long. The business cycle is not functioning as it has since World War II -- lifting the economy, then easing it into recession and then lifting it again in a familiar pattern of alternating, and more or less predictable, waves and troughs. Since mid-1989, the gross domestic product, measuring the nation's new wealth, has risen each quarter at an annual rate of less than 2 percent.
 
Postponing the Rebound
 Not since World War II has the economy been so weak for so long. The forecasts of recovery have mostly said that in six to nine months the economy would be growing at a job-creating annual rate of 2.5 percent to 3 percent. But with each disappointment, the recovery date has been pushed back at least six months.
The forecasters in the Blue Chip survey, for example, had predicted in January that the G.D.P. would be growing by 2.5 percent in the second quarter -- not the 1.4 percent growth actually reported. They had also said growth would break 3 percent in the current quarter, but in the August survey, they pushed back this achievement until early next year.
"There is a tendency among forecasters to be too optimistic," said Robert J. Eggert, publisher of the Blue Chip surveys. "If you look at the long-range trend, it is upward: growth with periods of recession. So there is a tendency to look at this trend and to project more growth, and we fail to recognize the forces that will cause the economy to dip below the trend."
The weak growth, in turn, has made the recession that began in July 1990 and apparently ended in the spring of 1991 -- a period in which the G.D.P. shrank -- hard to distinguish from the periods of meager growth. Most economists say the G.D.P. must grow at an annual rate of more than 2 percent to make incomes rise and to shrink unemployment, which rose to 7.7 percent in July from 5.2 percent in April 1989.
 
2 Pieces of Bad News
 Two recent events upset the forecasters. The first was the announcement that the unemployment rate had jumped in June to 7.8 percent from 7.5 percent in May, just when many forecasters thought the economy was on the verge of revival. The second was the announcement that economic growth in the March-through-June quarter fell to an annual rate of 1.4 percent from 2.7 percent in the first quarter.
In hindsight, Federal Reserve officials, including Mr. Greenspan, have concluded that the actual growth rate this year has been just under 2 percent, a level of weakness that for many Americans has become indistinguishable from recession.
"There is a loss of confidence that we understand why the stagnation is continuing, and that has led to a loss of confidence that it will end shortly," said Albert T. Sommers, a senior economist at the Conference Board.
The great hope for months had been that sharply falling interest rates would prompt consumers and companies to resume borrowing and spending, not only for store purchases but also for new machinery, appliances and housing. But that stimulant to the economy has not occurred.
Until recently, economists in the Clinton camp had thought that while the economy would not revive on its own before Election Day in November, damaging Mr. Bush's re-election efforts, it would turn up by early next year, giving a Clinton Administration easier sailing. Now the growing possibility of economic stagnation in the opening months of a Clinton Administration "is a definite point of discussion," said Gene Sperling, economic policy director for Mr. Clinton.
While Mr. Bush advocates tax cuts to stimulate the economy, Mr. Clinton calls for more spending on public works. But both candidates stop short of advocating a higher budget deficit to pay for their proposals, bowing to arguments that rising deficits would be inflationary and wasteful.
 
Taxing and Spending
 Mr. Bush's tax-cut proposals -- including a $5,000 tax credit for first-time home buyers -- would be offset by cutbacks in Government spending, while Mr. Clinton would balance public-works spending with tax increases on the wealthy and other measures, one being the diversion of military spending to public works.
"Mr. Clinton would not advocate a solution where he could be seen as using an anti-recession solution as an excuse for not worrying about the deficit," Mr. Sperling said.
Mr. Greenspan says the protracted stagnation is primarily a result of the debts piled up by households and companies in the 1980's, and many economists share this view.
Both sectors are slowly paying off debt, and Mr. Greenspan says he does not know when they will feel ready to borrow and spend again. "The degree of debt retrenchment has turned out to be much greater than experience since World War II would have suggested," he told the Senate Banking Committee last month.
Mr. Greenspan argues that once consumers and corporations regain confidence, healthy economic growth will resume on a surer footing than in the 1980's, particularly since Corporate America is steadily improving its efficiency, largely by laying off thousands of workers and increasing the output of those who remain.
He wants to wait out the process. But Robert M. Solow and James Tobin, both Nobel laureates in economics, argue that the economy should not be allowed to stagnate until the debt problem works itself out. "Economic policy is not a spectator sport," Mr. Solow said. He and Mr. Tobin are the leaders of a group of more than 100 economists calling for an increase in job-creating public spending, financed through a temporary rise in the deficit.
Aside from the debt problem, many economists say some blame for the protracted hard times can be assigned to the economic slowdowns in Europe and Japan, which are big customers for American products; to the cutback in military spending, and to numerous other factors, among them the sharp decline in the formation of small companies, a prime source of jobs in the 1980's.

HEADLINE: Claremont Journal;
Where Tax Bills Rival the Mortgages

BYLINE: By WILLIAM CELIS 3d, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: CLAREMONT, N.H.

 BODY:
Owen and Camilla Wadleigh were proud owners of a gabled stone house that had been in Mr. Wadleigh's family since the mid-1800's, a house where Mr. Wadleigh's father grew up, as did his grandfather before him.
But in a matter of weeks, the Wadleighs will lose their much-beloved home at 43 High Street. Squeezed by rising local property taxes, the Wadleighs, who borrowed from a local bank to buy the house from the estate of Mr. Wadleigh's grandmother, aredeeding the house to the bank to avoid the additional costs of foreclosure proceedings. 
 "In six years' time, our taxes nearly doubled," to $2,000 a year from just over $1,000, said Mrs. Wadleigh, whose job as a legal secretary is the family's main source of income. "It's real difficult when you can't even afford to stay in your home, but when your taxes are as high as your mortgage, you have to wonder. There's just no way."
The Wadleighs' dilemma is unfolding with distressing frequency in this placid town of 15,000 people, where "for sale" signs on front lawns increasingly compete with "garage sale" signs. In a state that has neither a state income tax nor a state sales tax, Claremont's town leaders have leaned heavily on homeowners like the Wadleighs to pay bills, for everything from education, police and fire protection to parks and libraries.
The result is a local property tax rate in Claremont of $33.39 for every $1,000 in assessed value, the second-highest rate in the state after the $34.16 levied by Greenville. Although Alaska similarly has no state sales or income tax, it has oil wealth. All other states provide cities with some financial assistance that helps pay a municipality's bills so local property taxes are not prohibitively high.
Not so in New Hampshire, and in places like Claremont the consequences have been mounting. This year the city estimates it will assume ownership of 16 properties in lieu of taxes, more of them single-family homes than four years ago, when the city took over just eight properties.
"We certainly are not in the business of owning property," said Normand G. Bernaiche, the city assessor, but "the reliance on property taxes puts a heavy burden on the community."
Residents and business owners worry about the high tax rate and its effect on their town. "A lot of people are meeting their limit and have to sell their homes," said Clara Homiak, the owner of Harry's Shoe Barn in downtown Claremont.
Down the street at the Tremont House of Pizza, the co-owner, Neil Petersen, said property taxes on his house have jumped to $3,100 a year from $1,600 two years ago. "Taxes are too high," he said. "There should be a state sales tax."
The Wadleighs can only shake their heads at the tax climate in their state and their own situation.
"It's not easy for anyone to lose their home and certainly under these circumstances because it is a family home and it has a lot more sentimental value," said Mrs. Wadleigh, who is 28 years old and has a 5 1/2-year-old son, Kyle.
The couple moved into the two-story house on a large corner lot six years ago. As taxes increased, at a clip of $150 to $200 a year, the Wadleighs imposed a savings program on themselves to pay the twice-a-year tax bill.
At one point, Mrs. Wadleigh says, they were socking away $200 a month for taxes alone, a sum she says strained the family budget.
There were other circumstances that undermined the family's home ownership. Mr. Wadleigh, 31 years old, lost his job in January 1991 and for eight months could not find work; the stress, his wife said, led to a heart attack in November of that year.
Mr. Wadleigh is in better health now and is working as a manager at a Claremont restaurant. But the stretch of unemployment did not help the family's finances. And when the Wadleighs finally conceded the battle to high taxes a year ago, by putting their home on the market at $69,000, they were hurt again. Like much other real estate, it didn't sell.
The Wadleighs now live in a rented apartment in a nearby town, Lempster, that is cheaper, Mrs. Wadleigh said. But she lamented, "If it wasn't for my job, we would have left New Hampshire a long time ago."

HEADLINE: Income Rise Is Outpaced By Spending

BYLINE: Reuters

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 28

 BODY:
Faced with slight income growth, Americans dipped into their savings in July to maintain their standard of living, a Commerce Department report showed today.
The department said spending increased by three-tenths of 1 percent, to $4.08 trillion, last month, while incomes rose by just two-tenths of 1 percent, to $5.05 trillion. The pattern was similar in June, when a four-tenths of 1 percent spending rise also outpaced a minimal income gain of one-tenth of 1 percent. 
 Economists warned that as consumers reached into savings to keep up their spending habits, they were reducing the pool of capital available for investment.
"A lot of people are being forced to use their credit cards to borrow money, and so savings are falling." said Marco Babic, an economist with Evans Economics Inc. in Washington.
Savings fell to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of $226.7 billion in July from $228.8 billion in June and $240.7 billion in May. That means that only 5.1 cents out of each dollar earned after taxes was put in savings last month, compared with 5.2 cents in June and 5.5 cents in May.
The savings figure is closely watched by economists not only as a barometer of consumers' economic welfare but also as a measure of capital available for productive investment.
Other than in January, when incomes were flat, incomes have risen by only fractional amounts each month this year. Gains in personal income -- wages and salaries as well as income from dividends and interest -- are essential for consumer purchases, which fuel two-thirds of national economic activity.
"The economic recovery will remain subpar as long as the consumer recovery remains subpar," said Ed Yardeni, an economist with C. J. Lawrence Securities Inc. in New York.
Private-sector wages and salaries were up by $4.5 billion in July from June, the department said, but goods-producing industries' payrolls dropped by $1.5 billion after decreasing $3.2 billion in June.
Separately, the department said the nation's trade deficit during the second quarter increased to an 18-month high of $24.4 billion in the second quarter from $17.2 billion in the first three months of 1992.

HEADLINE: A Twisting Walk to Inner Peace on a Painted Purple Canvas

BYLINE: By NATALIE ANGIER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: SAN FRANCISCO, Aug. 28

 BODY:
Stretched across the nave floor of the majestic Grace Cathedral like a tarpaulin hauled in from a football field, the 35-foot-wide labyrinth is nothing more than a bright purple pattern painted on an expanse of bleached canvas.
Yet the design has an extraordinary pedigree: it is a replica of a labyrinth built into the stone floor of Chartres Cathedral in France, and it inspires in the people who have come to walk along its sinuous, painted path a palpable mix of solemnity and joy. 
 A woman wearing glasses, a peasant skirt and a small, secret smile walked through the maze design one recent evening practically on tiptoe, her gaze turned toward the cathedral's gothic vaulting, her arms lifted away from her sides as if she were about to fly.
Two 9-year-old girls holding hands scurried along with swift, mincing steps, goading each other on and scooting impatiently around any adults in their way; and when they finished following the tightly coiled design, an intricate series of loops and switchbacks that measures a third of a mile long, the pair started it all over again.
 
Finding a Sense of Calm
 They were among more than 100 people gathered at Grace Cathedral on Nob Hill, perhaps the most photographed cathedral in the city, to take part in a ceremony that is at once ancient and New Age mysticism, called "walking the labyrinth."
Two evenings a month, the seven pieces of the makeshift labyrinth are pulled out of duffle bags, stuck together with Velcro and laid out for ritualized walking by anyone who cares to experience the maze, to stroll along its surprising torques and curves and, in so doing, to find a sense of calm.
The labyrinth design painted on the canvas, the only one to be seen in any American church, also evokes a grand sense of history. Back in the 13th and 14th centuries, visitors to Chartres and other soaring cathedrals like Rheim and Amiens would walk the labyrinth designs built into the naves as the spiritual culmination of their lengthy pilgrimages.
The tradition of meditative walking has long since lapsed among Westerners, and chairs now cover the labyrinths of the great French cathedrals so completely that even many devoted Chartres lovers are not even aware the stone maze exists.
But at Grace, the Rev. Lauren Artress, canon pastor of the Episcopal cathedral, is trying to revive the tradition as a tool for encouraging introspection and spiritual transformation, whatever one's religion.
"When I'm walking the labyrinth, I have the chance to think about all the people in my life who are very sick right now," said Joanna DeVito Larson, an unemployed employee counselor. "A friend has ovarian cancer, my brother is addicted to drugs, my sister is falling apart psychologically. It makes me very sad, but being here at Grace gives me the focus that helps me deal with my troubles."
The labyrinth, Ms. Artress said, is a profound archetype seen in most cultures throughout history. The ancient Greeks had the labyrinth of Minos, whose serpentine corridors on the island of Crete led to the Minotaur, a creature with the head of a bull, the body of a man, and a bottomless appetite for sacrificial young men and maidens. Hindus and Buddhists have the mandala, a circular design symbolizing the universe and totality. For medieval Christians, the labyrinth became a manageable substitute for a pilgrimage to Jerusalem.
 
Act of Celebration
 All good Christians vowed to visit Jerusalem during their lives, but most had to settle for a pilgrimage to great cathedrals like Chartres. Walking the labyrinth served as the final stage of the pilgrim's journey.
In the new rendition of the labyrinth rite, walking is less an act of penance than of celebration. Walkers are gently tutored on how to get the most out of the maze.
Going into the center, a walker is supposed to shed the solipsistic obsessions of everyday life. At the center of the labyrinth, a six-petal design, the pilgrim looks for illumination. "I call it clarity," Ms. Artress said. "You get insight into yourself."
On the way out from the center, the walker should feel a sense of communion with the cosmos, God, or some sort of higher, healing power. One woman at Grace, a retired lawyer who now writes for a living, described the experience of walking the labyrinth as "a dance I do with my soul."
 
Revealing Exercise
 A high school math teacher said he found the journey toward the center much harder than the journey back out. "I practically stumbled while I was going in," he said. "Going out was much more peaceful."
But even for the spiritually tone-deaf, walking the labyrinth is a revealing exercise. The turns never take the walker where the thinking brain had expected; the center of the maze remains elusive for an astonishing length of time, and one gains new appreciation for how an enormous amount of material can be compressed into a small space simply through artful winding.
Over the next few months, a permanent stone labyrinth will be built in the cathedral garden, and Ms. Artress will be taking the canvas labyrinth on the road, to show churches and other groups in Southern California, New York, North Carolina and elsewhere how to design one.
"You have to get the mystical figure in the center right," she said. Underneath the center is a 13-pointed star. "When you lay that star right," she said, "then all the paths are perfectly balanced."

Two evenings a month, the canvas replica is assembled for ritualized walking by those who wish to experience the maze. (Marc Geller for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: A Heaping Plate of Ventures for Chili's Impresario

 BODY:
On any given day or night, Norman Brinker, a tall, balding man who comes off as a slightly befuddled tourist, can be found wandering the parking lots of America's restaurants. "How was the food?" he asks patrons as they leave.
So far, at least, there has been nothing befuddled about Norman Brinker. The parking lots are probably his, or a competitor's, and what he is doing is his version of market reseach. 
 On Wall Street, Mr. Brinker, who heads the Chili's Grill and Bar restaurant chain, is regarded as a visionary on the order of Ray Kroc, the man who built McDonald's. Mr. Brinker has built Chili's, based in Dallas, into one of the fasting-growing restaurant chains in America. He expanded it from 23 stores in 1984 to 276 now, increasing revenues to almost $600 million, from $40 million. Over the last five years, both revenues and earnings of Brinker International, the publicly owned company of which he is now chairman and chief executive, have been growing at a rate of nearly 30 percent compounded annually. Chili's has $40 million in cash on hand -- and no debt.
 
 
5 Chains at Once
 Now Mr. Brinker is beginning a new venture that some say is both rare and risky: he is trying to run as many as five restaurant chains at once.
In so doing, Mr. Brinker, who is 61 years old, is leading a direct assault on his chief competitors, General Mills Inc., which owns Red Lobster and Olive Garden, the two largest full-service restaurant chains; the McDonald's Corporation, and Pepsico Inc., the country's largest restaurateur, which owns Pizza Hut, Taco Bell and Kentucky Fried Chicken.
In the last year, Mr. Brinker has added 31 new Chili's across the country. The restaurants serve a full range of moderately priced foods. He has recently acquired two other Southern chains and plans to expand them nationally: Romano's Macaroni Grill, an Italian-food chain that now has 13 units, and Grady's, which serves traditional American dishes, with 17 units. Beyond that, he has begun work on a new Italian chain called Spageddies, which recently opened its first unit, and a Mexican-food chain. His goal is to double sales by 1995.
"It is very rare for a company to successfully manage more than one restaurant chain" and do it well, said Steven A. Rockwell, restaurant analyst with Alex. Brown & Sons in Baltimore. But, he added, "if there is an individual who can do it -- set up the systems and attract the people -- it's Brinker."
That seems to be the consensus. "Norman truly is a great entrepreneur and a great innovator," said Joe R. Lee, chief financial officer of General Mills. "And he takes care of his investors really well." Scott Allmendinger, editor of Restaurant Business, a trade publication, said, "At every company he's been with, he's had the Midas touch."
 
 
Founded Bennigan's
 Before Chili's, Mr. Brinker ran the Pillsbury Company's restaurant group, which then owned Burger King and was the nation's second-largest restaurateur, behind McDonald's. At Pillsbury, he also founded Bennigan's.
In the last year, Chili's has moved into third place, behind Red Lobster and Olive Garden, in the category known as casual dining. In the meantime, Bennigan's has slipped to fifth place, from third, behind T.G.I. Friday's.
Casual dining, a field in which Mr. Brinker was a pioneer, refers to a sort of middle range between fast food and fine dining: full-menu table service in a restaurant environment, but at prices that may be only a few dollars more than a dinner at McDonald's. Dress is weekend casual, though waiters may be in black tie.
The time does seem right for expansion in casual dining, which is quickly replacing fast food as the industry growth leader. Even with the recession, customer traffic in the casual segment rose 8.4 percent in the 1989 fiscal year, 4.6 percent in the 1990 fiscal year and 1.5 percent in the 1991 fiscal year, while fast food rose only 1.2 percent, 2.4 percent and 1.8 percent in the same periods. Casual dining revenues rose 15 percent in 1991, compared with about 5 percent for fast food.
Fast food, for all of its growth, was aimed almost exclusively at young consumers at a time -- the 1960's -- when 15 percent of all Americans were between 13 and 20 years old. It took a number of years for fast food to grow beyond those demographics.
Casual dining, by contrast, is taking business away from all segments, from fast food, coffee shops and, especially, fine restaurants.
Throughout the 1988-91 period, customer traffic at fine-dining establishments fell 7.5 percent, then 7 percent, then another 11.6 percent, according to the National Restaurant Association.
 
Aging Customers
 "With the aging of America," said Mr. Lee of General Mills, "you'll get about 20 million additional people between the ages of 40 to 60 by the year 2000. Those people happen to be the highest frequenters of casual-dining restaurants."
Mr. Lee figures that at least 184 million more meals will be eaten out annually by 2000. The 45-to-54 age bracket will rise 47 percent, with the number of people from 55 to 65 rising 14 percent.
And younger Americans can be expected to switch from fast food as they mature, analysts say. As a rule, people who have come of age since the 1950's tend to eat out more often than earlier generations. And Mr. Brinker is one of those responsible.
"Prior to the 60's," he said, "people were looking at restaurants as places to eat, a place to get food," and that was it.
 F. Lane Cardwell Jr., planning chief of Brinker International, said Mr. Brinker "kind of rewrote that script" at Steak and Ale, which he ran for Pillsbury, "and said what people are looking for is some fun and good food."
 
Eliminating theGeneric Waiter
 It was really with Steak and Ale, he said, that Mr. Brinker introduced Americans to relatively inexpensive dinners in a pleasant but casual setting, a salad bar and waiters who introduced themselves by saying, "Hello, I'm Michael and I'll be your waiter this evening."
Mr. Brinker credits his shoe-leather market research, the endless polling of departing customers -- his and competitors' -- with helping him to keep up with changing consumer tastes.
He said he tried to introduce fixes, tiny and large, to keep the menu and decor at just the right point on what he calls the "life style curve."
"The difference between winning and losing is such a tiny little fraction," Mr. Brinker said. "If you're 'off life style' even a little bit, you won't be successful."
 Two other trends he tried to keep up with were people's concern for their health and their taste, so to speak, for adventure.
"From 1980 to 1985," he said, "the tons of spices sold in the U.S. went up five or six times."
And the increased seasoning of foods allowed restaurants to come up with healthier foods that were still tasty. No matter how healthy a food is, he said, "if it doesn't taste good it won't work."
Despite the hesitancy of others toward his plans for a huge expansion in five or more chains, Mr. Brinker seems confident. He reminds people that he has already done it.
At Pillsbury, he briefly ran four chains -- Steak and Ale, Bennigan's, Burger King and Poppin' Fresh -- and he says earnings tripled by the time he left the company.
The trick, he said, is to operate the chains as separate entities, under separate, stable management, with separate styles and ambiance.
"We've got a good shot at it," he said.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Dollar Gains on the Mark But Drops Against the Yen

BYLINE: By The Associated Press

 BODY:
The dollar finished trading mixed yesterday as the yen bounced higher after Japan released details of a large fiscal package to stimulate that nation's economy. Gold prices rose.
Investors were encouraged by the $86.3 billion-plus plan to fix Japan's economy through increased public works and investment, said Paul Stancs, a trader at Dai-Ichi Kangyo Bank Ltd. in New York. 
 "It really gave the yen a boost that it sustained throughout the session," he said.
Heavy selling of German marks for yen helped the dollar against the mark but the mark-yen cross trading also aggravated the American currency's decline against the yen, traders said.
In late New York trading, the dollar was quoted at 123.05 Japanese yen, down from 124.75 yen Thursday. In Tokyo, the dollar dropped 1.35 yen to close at 123.60 yen. Later in London, the dollar was quoted at 123.20 yen.
In New York, the dollar continued to drift higher after hitting a record low of 1.4015 marks early this week. The dollar edged upward to 1.4090 marks, from 1.4085 late Thursday, in New York trading.
But the dollar reversed course in Europe, slipping to 1.4060 marks in late trading from 1.4110 marks late Thursday. Low interest rates in the United States and a sluggish economy have discouraged investment in the dollar.
The meeting of deputy ministers from the Group of Seven major industrialized nations ended yesterday with no public statements on the dollar.
Ministers discussed the currency's recent weakness and interest rates. Traders were waiting to see if the Group of Seven might intervene on the dollar's behalf.
In New York, the British pound was quoted at $1.9825, up from $1.9800 late Thursday. In London, the pound was quoted at $1.9815, up from $1.9770 late Thursday.
Other late dollar rates in New York, compared with late Thursday's rates, included: 1.2610 Swiss francs, up from 1.2605; 4.800 French francs, down from 4.8015; 1,077.50 Italian lire, up from 1,077.00, and 1.19655 Canadian dollars, up from 1.19575.
Gold prices rose around the world.
On the New York Commodity Exchange, gold bullion for current delivery settled at $339.90 an ounce, up 70 cents from $339.20 on Thursday.
The Republic National Bank said gold rose to a late bid price of $339.75 an ounce, up 80 cents from Thursday.
Gold traded in London late yesterday at $339.95 an ounce, up from $339 bid late Thursday. In Zurich, the bid was $340 an ounce, up from $339 late Thursday. In Hong Kong, gold rose $1.57 an ounce to close at $340.59.
On New York's Comex, silver bullion for current delivery settled at $3.674 an ounce, down from $3.705 on Thursday. Silver traded in London at a bid of $3.70 an ounce, unchanged from Thursday.

HEADLINE: Strike by Professors Is Settled At the University of Bridgeport

BYLINE: By GEORGE JUDSON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BRIDGEPORT, Conn., Aug. 28

 BODY:
A two-year strike by professors at the University of Bridgeport has been settled, the administration and faculty union announced Thursday, with the union likening the settlement to a divorce.
As part of the settlement, both sides agreed not to disclose its terms. But people familiar with the agreement said the 66 professors and librarians who have remained on strike since September 1990 are dropping all claims against the university in return for buyouts of as much as a year's salary. They will not get their jobs back. 
 "We're getting a divorce," said Professor Donna Wagner, the president of Bridgeport's chapter of the American Association of University Professors. "After two years, most of the people on strike are really tired and needing to get on with their lives."
For the administration, the settlement eliminates some of its most scathing critics, as well as their claims on any new faculty positions that the university might create. The university has been restored to financial health by the Professors World Peace Academy, an affiliate of the Unification Church.
The university president, Dr. Edwin G. Eigel Jr., would not comment except for a statement saying, "We are working very hard to do whatever is necessary to bring an end to all our differences."
The strike was one of the bitterest at an American university, and the first in which the administration hired instructors to replace the strikers, effectively breaking the strike.
The cost for the financially struggling university was high, however. While the administration was seeking to eliminate nearly a third of the 158 faculty positions at the time and cut salaries as much as 30 percent, the strike and ensuing publicity drove away more than 1,000 students.
 
Academy Offer Causes Uproar
 Little more than a year later, trustees considered an offer of aid from the Professors World Peace Academy, setting off a new uproar. As the financial crisis deepened, trustees made plans to eliminate the university's liberal-arts programs, sought a merger with another institution and finally set a date for closing entirely.
This summer, however, the university did reach an agreement with the Professors World Peace Academy, which promised to invest as much as $50 million in the next several years in return for choosing a majority of the university's trustees. Classes will resume next week for an estimated 1,300 full-time and part-time students.
"This settlement closes the door on the past for the striking faculty," Dr. Wagner said. "Given the changes taking place at UB, this is necessary. We are relieved to put the dispute behind us."

HEADLINE: Heady Opportunities for Home Buyersw

BYLINE: By SHAWN G. KENNEDY

 BODY:
Since the economy started to sputter a few years ago, home prices, which soared during the 1980's, have leveled off or declined nearly everywhere. Buyers have also benefited from steadily falling mortgage-interest rates.
As a result, homes throughout the country are more affordable now than they have been since the mid-1970's. Nationwide, mortgage interest rates have fallen about 2.5 percent since 1989. But even in a favorable market, the task of finding and paying for a house can be a daunting one. And when the price is right, there is often a temptation to overlook flaws or to rush the deal. 
 "My main piece of advice for anyone looking for a home today is to look for long-term value, not just a good price," said John Tucillo, chief economist of the National Association of Realtors in Washington. "A buyer who bases his purchasing decision on price alone might get a tremendous deal in terms of the dollars, but no appreciation potential."
Buyers should ask themselves, he said, how long they expect to live in their homes and should try to anticipate the neighborhood's long-term appeal.
"Buyers should not take on more than they can afford," Mr. Tucillo said. "But today they have the luxury of stretching it a bit, because interest rates are low."
In some areas, bargain hunters have additional opportunities in the discounted prices of homes sold in bank foreclosures, through builder-sponsored auctions or repossessions by public agencies like the Resolution Trust Corporation or the Federal Housing Administration. Depending on location, condition and the degree of desperation of the seller, the discount on distressed properties can range from 20 to 40 percent.
 
Special Sales
 But real estate professionals say that getting the most for your money means, above all, knowing what you are buying. "I tell buyers who are in the market for bargains to seek out vacant properties," said Michael Teer, a broker in Riverside, Calif. "When the home is empty, it is usually a sign that the seller, whether it is a bank or an owner who has moved on, is willing to be flexible."
But at the same time, Mr. Teer cautioned that vacant homes or those bought in special sales often come with problems. "A buyer will need to be particularly diligent when it comes to the inspection process," he said. "Finding a reputable structural engineer is critical."
Emma and Joe Benson bought a three-bedroom home in Riverside earlier this summer from the Resolution Trust Corporation, the Federal agency set up to dispose of properties owned by failed savings and loan institutions. The $85,000 that the Bensons paid for the house was well below the market price for homes of its size and age. Mr. Benson is a skilled carpenter and can handle most home-repair jobs, so the couple was not put off by the structure's rotting roof, tattered kitchen flooring, cracked cement and soiled carpeting.
The Bensons will surely see a rise in the value of their home when their renovation is finished. But the same purchase could have been a risky investment for a buyer lacking Mr. Bensons' home-repair skills. If the cost of correcting flaws in a fixer-upper pushes the total investment beyond the structure's appraised value, the buy is no bargain.
 
Leasing With Option to Buy
 House hunters should not overlook rental listings. Often, a prospective seller who has not yet been able to make a sale but who is under pressure to move -- in cases of divorce or job relocation, for example -- will accept a short-term lease. "Lease with option to buy" contracts have spread as the market has slowed. The benefit to the tenants is that they get to try out the residence, and often the owner will agree to take part of the rent off the purchase price if there is a sale.
While an atmosphere of declining home prices can be a boon to buyers, lenders have tightened their mortgage-qualification standards in the face of falling values and mounting foreclosures. Still, mortgage money has not dried up. Bankers have rolled out the welcome mat for mortgage applicants with sufficient income, good credit ratings and job stability.
 
Prequalifying for a Mortgage
 Marie H. Roller, a senior vice president of Ridgefield Bank in Ridgefield, Conn., said house hunters could save time and avoid the trauma of seeing their deals fall apart by prequalifying for their mortgages. Ridgefield Bank is among the lenders that will give buyers letters certifying their ability to qualify for loans.
"The prequalification gives the buyers an idea of how much they can afford," Ms. Roller said. "Another advantage is that in a soft market, sellers are often nervous about whether potential buyers can get loans. The prequalifying letter gives the seller some comfort and can make the process move faster."
Gregory T. Barmore, president of the General Electric Capital Mortgage Corporation, a national mortgage lender based in Raleigh, N.C., said a common misconception is that buyers must make down payments of 15 to 20 percent to qualify for mortgages.
With mortgage insurance, usually about six-tenths of a percent of the mortgage amount, buyers can qualify for loans with as little as 5 percent down. Mr. Barmore said that nationwide, one-fourth of his company's mortgage loans are made to home buyers with down payments of 10 percent or less.

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
FORD TO SHUT TWO VAN PLANTS FOR A WEEK

BYLINE: AP

 BODY:
The Ford Motor Company will shut down operations for Econoline vans in its Lorain and Avon Lake, Ohio, assembly plants for a week because of slow sales, a company official said yesterday. About 1,500 workers at Lorain and 1,400 workers at Avon Lake will be laid off during the shutdown from Monday to Sept. 6, said Frank Sopata, spokesman for the Ford's North American automotive operations in Dearborn, Mich. "They should all be back at work after that week," he said. 
 The Lorain plant, where Thunderbird and Mercury Cougar autos are also assembled, has 4,100 hourly and 330 salaried employees. Avon Lake, where Villager mini-vans are also assembled, has 1,900 hourly and 250 salaried workers.
