HEADLINE: New Housing At Lowest Since '85

BYLINE: By NICK RAVO

 BODY:
THE once frenetic pace of housing construction in New York City has slowed to a virtual halt over the last three years, impeded by the lingering recession, a dearth of Federal funds and a bureaucratic bog of rent and land-use regulations.
Housing experts are unsure exactly how many new units are needed today. They contend, though, that despite attempts by the administrations of Mayors Edward I. Koch and David N. Dinkins to stimulate both construction and rehabilitation, vast numbers of affordable homes for low- and middle-income residents need to be built, particularly in the outer boroughs. 
 "The issue, once you get beyond the absolute numbers, is price, and there is a substantial segment of the population that can't afford to pay rents that cover the costs of housing," said Charles Brecher, director of research at the Citizen's Budget Commission. "It's an income problem as much as a housing problem."
New housing production, according to the Department of City Planning, has been declining for each of the last three years, falling to 7,639 units in 1991, the lowest level since 1985. Manhattan had the most housing starts last year: 2,595. It was followed by Queens, which had 1,996, and Staten Island, which had 1,628. Brooklyn and the Bronx had the fewest, 764 and 656, respectively.
The decline is not a short-term aberration. The annual average of 10,276 units completed between 1981 and 1991 was only a third of the average of 34,800 new units built from 1961 to 1970, when substantial Federal aid was available.
The Planning Department figures on housing are based on final certificates of occupancy. Buildings under construction or occupied under a temporary certificate of occupancy are not counted. The figures also do not include units that have become occupied because of conversions from other uses or rehabilitation, the method by which much of what little new housing there is today is being created in the city. Indeed, in 1991, more apartments -- 7,800 -- were rehabilitated by the city's Department of Housing Preservation and Development than were newly built in the entire city.
"If you look at housing starts in the city, it is frightening," said Will L. Zeckendorf, vice president of the Zeckendorf Company, one of the largest residential developers in the city. "There is virtually nothing being built now. In 1991, we built fewer units than Peoria, Ill."
The slowdown in construction has hit not only housing for low- and moderate-income residents, but also the segment of the market that developed the most feverishly during the 80's -- Manhattan luxury units, particularly condominiums. In 1991, there were 950 condominiums and 725 rental apartments built in Manhattan. This year, that number should drop to 300 condominiums and 700 rentals.
"It is getting down to nonexistent," said Yale Robbins, president of Yale Robbins, a real estate publishing and consulting concern. "There are buyers of units out there. There are also enough apartments to satisfy these buyers."
The lack of new construction is not limited to the city. Rosemary Scanlon, the chief economist for the Port Authority of New York and New Jersey, said that the 18,000 units built last year in the 17-county metropolitan region including the city was about 70 percent lower than in 1985.
"WE have had six consecutive years of decline," she said. "To build 18,000 units for an area of 15.5 million households is a minuscule amount."
"In Los Angeles, they built 112,000 units for an area with a population of 11 million," Ms. Scanlon added. "And our best year in the last 20 was 1985, when we built 64,000."
No studies have been done since 1985 to determine the size of the city's housing gap -- the difference between the number of existing units and the number needed to house its population and maintain a 5 percent vacancy rate. That 1985 report, sponsored by the Pratt Institute Center for Community and Environmental Development, estimated the gap at 622,600 units, 391,600 to be obtained through moderate rehabilitation of existing units and 231,000 through new construction or gut rehabilitation. There are 2.9 million existing housing units in the city, according to 1990 census figures.
A report in 1984 by the New York State Division of Housing and Community Renewal found the city needed 630,000 more units, 446,000 through moderate rehabilitation and 184,000 in new construction.
The next projection, part of the city's triennial vacancy report, will not be released until the end of the year.
"There are still severe problems," said Michael A. Stegman, chairman of the Department of City and Regional Planning at the University of North Carolina at Chapel Hill, who conducts the study.
At least 10,000 units should be built each year merely to replace older units, said Mr. Zeckendorf. "Even if we don't need more units, a healthy city should be replacing its older housing stock," he said. "We have a lot of older housing in the city, and out of our total we should be replacing 1 to 2 percent every year."
Aside from the issue of the number of units needed, there is also an affordability gap. "The issue is the allocation and the cost of housing relative to people's ability to pay, as much as it is the sheer quantity of housing," Mr. Stegman said.
Though the New York metropolitan area's median family income rose 76 percent, to $36,900, in 1990 from $20,919 in 1981, the median price of an existing home rose by almost twice as much -- by 133 percent -- to $172,000 in 1991, from $73,800 in 1981.
Even with today's low interest rates, a family with the 1991 median income would need to make at least a $17,200 down payment on the median-priced home, which would be equal to 47 percent of its annual income. It would also have to pay 41 percent of its income to meet mortgage payments on a 9 percent, 30-year, fixed-rate mortgage, not including taxes and other costs.
"The affordability gap is probably widening at this point," said Felice Michetti, Commissioner of the city's Department of Housing Preservation and Development. "We are seeing fewer and fewer low-rent units, and there is tremendous pressure on the rents. Where there have been increases in supply, it is just feeding the high end."
Most optimistic analysts lay the construction slowdown to the recession and the difficulty of getting credit and look forward a comeback sparked by a revival of the economy.
"The drop in housing production in New York City in the last two years reflects the slowdown in the local and national economies," said Richard L. Schaffer, the director of the Department of City Planning. "However, as the city emerges from the recession, falling interest rates may provide an incentive for new housing construction."
Others, though, point to entrenched structural problems, not only in the economy but also with the housing that is built and allocated in New York.
Peter D. Salins, a professor of urban planning at Hunter College, cited, in particular, the city's decades-old system of rent-regulation. He said that although housing shortages often motivate the impulse to regulate, regulation only exacerbates a shortage.
"Rent regulation discourages the developer because it may build housing that falls under its regulation," he said.
Mr. Salins added that rent regulation in New York reduces the number of people who trade up to homeownership or better apartments because rent-regulated apartments are priced too low to leave.
"You need a vigorous market of people looking for new housing to spur construction," he said. "You have to create a market for the stock by getting people to move from wherever they are right now."
HE also said that rent regulation, besides underhousing New Yorkers in quality and size, unjustly misallocates housing by creating a two-tier system that provides inexpensive regulated apartments for the vested, or long-term resident of the city, and higher priced units for newcomers.
The lack of a means test for rent-regulated apartments also means that some low- and middle-income residents pay more for housing in the open market than affluent residents living in rent-controlled or rent-stabilized apartments.
"New York has plenty of low-income housing," Mr. Salins said. "It's just that high-income people live in it."
Angelo J. Aponte, the New York State Director of Housing, disagreed that rent-regulation was a signifcant factor in the construction slowdown. He noted that rent-regulation does not apply to buildings built after 1974, unless they take part in a city subsidy or tax-abatement program, which many do. Instead, he cited the regional economic downturn and the drying up of commercial credit.
"There are no new buildings going up in New York City," he said. "We are in a real estate depression -- not a recession, a depression."
Apart from rent regulation, the city also has created a web of zoning constraints, environmental review procedures and site assemblage laws that hinder housing construction.
"WE have such a crippling regulatory atmosphere," said Kathryn Wylde, president of the New York City Housing Partnership, a 10-year-old nonprofit collaboration of government officials, builders, bankers and business leaders. "The costs have really offset the falling interest rates and the increased demand."
Local officials, though, note that direct Federal assistance for housing declined by 60 percent between 1981 and 1989. Further, Federal tax shelters available in the early and mid-1980's for those who built or financed real estate projects, have largely disappeared.
The tax breaks, which let people sharply reduce their taxes by declaring passive losses, were largely eliminated with the revision of the tax codes in 1986, but not before drawing projects into development that were not completed until later.
"For a very long time before the weak rental market came in, we had a buildup of supply at the same time there was diminishing demand," Ms. Michetti said. "And that slows down or halts the construction of new units."
During the 1980's, the city responded to the dwindling amount of Federal assistance by creating several programs. Most of them, however, have not been as successful as hoped.
For example, the city's 421a program, which offered tax abatements to developers of new housing and was started early in the decade, was eliminated below 96th Street in Manhattan in December 1985, although developers could transfer abatements to lower Manhattan from affordable projects elsewhere in the city.
The restriction of the program also created a construction spurt from 1986 to 1989 by builders who raced to put in their foundations before the end of 1985. Unfortunately, many of the projects were completed just as the city's residential market began to slump after the stock market collapse in 1987.
Another example is the city's Battery Park City program, which was unveiled by Mayor Koch in 1986. The program was supposed to rehabilitate 24,000 derelict buildings by using $1 billion in profits from some of the luxury units built in Battery Park City in lower Manhattan. Thus far, though, the program has raised only $210 million, $143 million of which has been used to rehabilitate 1,122 completed units.
An additional $220 million was raised in the bond market in 1990, but the Dinkins administration used the money to close a budget shortfall. Moreover, investors are also uneasy about buying Battery Park City Authority bonds because of the financial problems associated with the project's largest tenant, Olympia & York.
MORE successful has been the city's $5.1 billion capital plan, which was announced in 1986 and is projected to have ready for occupancy 250,000 rental and owned units through gut rehabilitation of vacant and abandoned units, new construction and the moderate rehabilitation of existing units. About half of the total number of units have been completed, including 45,000 of the 53,000 gut rehabilitations.
The state has also contributed financial and administrative assistance to spur construction in the city. Between April 1, 1989, and April 1, 1991, 2,878 new housing units were completed using state money or Federal funds administered by the state.
Even so, the current shortage of new construction represents what Louis Winnick, a housing analyst with the Fund for the City of New York, called "a genuine bust" in housing. "The last one was in the 1960's," he said.
"This is a down cycle in a down trend," he added. "And the combination is fairly lethal."

HEADLINE: POSTINGS: Ferry Terminal Design;
6 Chosen for Competition

 BODY:
Six firms have been chosen to compete for the commission of designing the Whitehall Ferry Terminal -- better known as the Staten Island ferry terminal -- in lower Manhattan. The existing terminal was badly damaged by fire last year; its replacement will cost $112 million
The competitors, selected by city officials from a field of 54, are Hardy Holzman Pfeiffer Associates; James Stewart Polshek & Partners; Aldo Rossi Studio di Architettura; Skidmore, Owings & Merrill; Rafael Vinoly Architects and a joint venture of Venturi, Scott Brown & Associates and Anderson/Schwartz Architects. All of the teams are based in New York or have New York offices. 
 "Any one of them would be able to design an extraordinary new terminal which will provide a major new architectural statement," said Carl Weisbrod, president of the city's Economic Development Corporation, sponsors of the competition and managers of the construction project, which is expected to take five years. A winner is to be picked in November by a jury headed by Arthur Levitt Jr., chairman of the development agency. Other jury members are city officials, design professionals, a museum director, a civic leader and an architecture critic.

HEADLINE: Nonprofits Did Many of the Rehabilitations

BYLINE: By NICK RAVO

 BODY:
MANY of the rehabilitated housing units completed within the last few years in New York City were the result of efforts by nonprofit community development groups like the Mid-Bronx Desperados and organizations like the New York Equity Fund, the Local Iniataives Support Corporation and the Enterprise Foundation.
Five years ago, the Crotona Park East section of the South Bronx was perhaps best known for its blocks and blocks of bombed-out buildings. Since then, however, virtually all of the abandoned buildings in the area have been renovated into affordable apartments for the working poor and the homeless -- largely because of the efforts of the Desperados and the Equity Fund, the largest private provider of financing for low-income housing in the city. 
 "A neighborhood that was a symbol of devastation is now a neighborhood that has been brought back to life," said Marc Jahr, the New York program director for the Local Initiatives Support Corporation, a national non-profit group, which along with the Enterprise Foundation of Columbia, Md., created the Equity Fund.
Earlier this summer, the Desperados began renovating the last city-owned abandoned buildings in their neighborhood. "We are doing 65 units in four buildings," said C. Ralph Porter, the group's president.
Part of the financing -- $2.4 million -- for the $7.5 million project will come from $35 million in investment capital the Equity Fund received from four New York-based banks to help rehabilitate city-owned vacant buildings in low-income areas. Three banks, Bankers Trust J.P. Morgan & Company and Republic National Bank are investing $10 million each. The Bank of New York is contributing $5 million.
THE $35 million, more than half the Equity Fund's 1992 goal of $60 million, will be used by the Desperados and 12 other community-development groups to create 702 apartment units for 14 projects in Ocean Hill-Brownsville, Bedford-Stuyvesant, East New York and Crown Heights in Brooklyn; East Harlem and Manhattan Valley in Manhattan and Morissania, Crotona Park and Longwood in the Bronx.
The Equity Fund operates by exchanging tax credits for equity under the five-year-old Federal Low-Income Housing Tax Credit program, the nation's primary source of Federal aid for producing low-income housing. In New York City alone, the program, also used by other organizations, has helpedcreate more than 12,000 low-income apartments.
Since the fund's inception, it has raised $153 million, $46 million of it last year. The fund uses this equity to leverage additional public and private financing. Working with nonprofit developers, the state's Division of Housing and Community Renewal and the city's Department of Housing Preservation and Development, it has helped create more than 3,250 units of affordable housing.
"The future of our city depends on these public-private partnerships," said Stephen M. Porras, fund manager of the Equity Fund. "Part of the frustration out in L.A. clearly had to do with the lack of opportunity."

HEADLINE: Commercial Property: Manhattan Supermarkets;
Space Shoppers Discover Bargains on the Shelves

BYLINE: By CLAUDIA H. DEUTSCH

 BODY:
EVERYONE agrees on the barebones fact: John A. Catsimatides, chairman of Red Apple Companies, has renewed the lease on the 8,000-square-foot space used by his Sloan's supermarket at 1343 Lexington Avenue, at 89th Street. But as for the details -- well, its Rashomon, real estate style.
Koeppel & Koeppel, which owns the building, says it is getting $55 a square foot, as well as 45 percent of any increase in real estate taxes. "In Manhattan, good supermarket space is rare, and the rarer the commodity, the better the rent," said William W. Koeppel, a partner. 
 Mr. Catsimatides, who is also the chairman of the ailing First New York Bank for Business, says he gained enough givebacks to bring the actual rent "substantially under $30" over the next five years, and to keep it below $40 for the 15-year life of the lease. "This is a good time to negotiate with landlords," he said.
Probably neither side feels particularly fortunate right now. New York landlords are having little trouble finding supermarkets to occupy cavernous (by Manhattan standards, upwards of 8,000 square feet) spaces -- but at rents well below what they are asking. And many of the hard-hit supermarket chains say that even $40 a square foot -- the average rent for supermarket space in Manhattan -- is beyond what they can pay. Some have put expansion plans on hold, while others have rented basements, garages and mezzanines, all in hopes of keeping rents down.
"Landlords are still asking $65 and $70 a square foot, but they're agreeing to formulas that bring it down to a lot less," said Linda Sue Wolff, a store leasing specialist at New Spectrum Realty.
In flusher times, many residential co-ops spurned supermarkets in favor of so-called "dry" operations that yielded fewer objectionable odors and vermin and a lot more prestige. And co-ops and commercial landlords alike discovered that, by cutting huge spaces into smaller parcels, they could replace one supermarket paying, say, $38 a square foot in rent with four quality stores willing to pay $70.
Late in 1986, for example, a small store adjoining an 8,000-square-foot A. & P. on Madison Avenue and 87th Street came up for rent. The A. & P. wanted to expand, and offered the landlord $50 a square foot for the space, ten times what they were paying for the market. "He simply laughed," recalled Michael Rourke, an A. & P. vice president. He had reason to: Mr. Rourke said a shoe store rented the space for roughly $90 a foot.
Those $90 rents are history in most areas, and landlords are again looking to supermarkets to pick up the slack. But many of the spaces have been carved up beyond repair -- Mr. Koeppel, for one, guesses that half the supermarket space in Manhattan disappeared in the 80's. And supermarkets cannot pay top dollar for the spaces that are left.
THE supermarkets are facing a killing recession of their own. Although cash-strapped consumers are eating home more, they are eating cheaper. That means pasta instead of steak, private labels instead of premium brands, fewer second helpings, and maybe forget the dessert. The Food Marketing Institute says that the national average per-customer supermarket receipt in fourth-quarter 1991, the most recent figure available, was $17.79, 5 percent below fourth quarter 1990's $18.64.
Supermarkets, which operate on margins of less than 1 percent even in the best of times, simply cannot absorb this new spartanism. Small chains, like Sloan's Supermarkets, are being forced to sell stores. That's how Mr. Catsimatides was able to buy the Sloan's on Lexington Avenue at 89th Street for his company six months ago. Larger chains are scaling back. At one time the Great Atlantic & Pacific Tea Company had more than 50 stores in Manhattan. Today, it has 28.
Nonetheless, vacant supermarkets remain a rare sight. Sloan's had no trouble unloading 20 of its 31 supermarkets in Brooklyn and Manhattan, to Red Apple and other more prosperous chains that wanted the long-term leases or the locations. "People aren't buying up Sloan's for the business or the customers, but for the real estate," said Edward A. Friedman, president of Newmark Retail Services. "There are not enough affordable big spaces for supermarkets in this town."
New York is a mirror, albeit a distorted one, of the supermarket situation throughout the country. Nationally, only 6.7 percent of stores were remodeled last year, compared to 9.1 percent the year before, according to the Food Marketing Institute. And new stores accounted for only 2.9 percent of the 30,670 supermarkets extant last year, compared with 4 percent in 1990. So clearly, the recession has had a dampening effect on supermarket expansion everywhere.
Still, analogies between the rest of the nation and New York, where rents for cramped basements are nearly four times the national rate of $8 to $12 for typical 40,000-square-foot stores, can go only so far.
New York City shoppers expect markets to be within walking distance, so supermarkets can safely locate within a few blocks of each other. And, with large chains like A. & P. and Red Apple operating popular-priced and premium-priced stores under different names (A. & P. owns Food Emporium and Waldbaum's, Red Apple owns Gristedes), supermarkets that might appear to be competing are often working hand in hand to serve different income groups in the same area. Selling to what is pretty much a captive audience, they can charge prices high enough to compensate for their swollen rents.
All those factors made New York supermarkets willing to gamble on locations in prerecession days. A few years ago, Mr. Catsimatides opened a Red Apple and a cramped Gristedes in Battery Park City, expecting that the huge upscale development would grow by three or four more buildings and at least 4,000 more families. It didn't, and the stores' revenues are far below expectations. "We're a lot more cautious now," said Mr. Catsimatides. "We wouldn't open stores today in a location that didn't look like it would pay off right away."
Supermarkets have gotten pickier on types of space, too. For a time, it looked as though basements would be a good compromise for supermarkets hungry for new stores at bargain rents. But that trend was predicated on rents for existing street-level spaces remaining very high, and on lots of new buildings going up, built with basement stores in mind.
NOW supermarket executives have rethought the basement idea. "Basements only made sense when street-level space cost $100 a foot," said Mr. Catsimatides, who pays about $30 a foot rent for a Red Apple basement store on West 86th Street. Basements have lost their allure for more subtle reasons, too. "We get the feeling that New Yorkers would rather walk a few extra blocks than shop in a basement," said Jules F. Rose, Sloan's president.
Supermarkets are also less willing to shoehorn themselves into tiny spaces. For one thing, they need to stock more frozen food, reduced-calorie items and fresh produce to attract a convenience-minded, health-conscious populace. For another, the many bank consolidations and large store closings have made more large spaces available. "We won't even look at anything less than 8,000 square feet today," said Mr. Rose of Sloan's.
For now, the question of where supermarkets will or will not locate is moot. Supermarket executives say that, if their receipts are any indication, the recession shows no sign of abating any time soon. And when it does, many chains may have to use any improvement in their cash flow to remodel or maintain existing stores before they think about building new ones.
Still, there are isolated signs of expansion, particularly outside of Manhattan. A. & P. just opened a 55,000-square-foot Waldbaum's, complete with parking, two blocks from Yankee Stadium. And it opened a 60,000-square-foot Waldbaum's in Queens Village, in an old Macy's furniture warehouse.
And considering the continuing scarcity of supermarkets in Manhattan, activity, albeit on a smaller scale, will probably pick up there soon, too. "If you have an underserved market you get stores opening up," said Debra Levin, a supermarket analyst at Morgan Stanley. "It's that simple."

HEADLINE: ART;
Politics and Artists, Then and Now

BYLINE: By VIVIEN RAYNOR

 BODY:
THE perspective provided by a show at the Katonah Museum of Art should prove valuable to voters "coming down" from the conventions. Titled "In Good Conscience: The Radical Tradition in 20th-Century American Illustration," the exhibition proposes in the clearest possible terms that while times change the issues remain the same.
It was organized for the museum by Bernard Reilly Jr., head curator of prints and photographs at the Library of Congress. 
 Imperialism, war, capital punishment, unemployment, destitution, racism -- all the ills are here (except drug addiction, abortion and AIDS) that artists have been covering all century long. In 1927, for example, Hugo Gellert did a collage for a New Masses cover that involved a map of Mexico, a compass in the form of a skull and crossbones and the legend, "Is oil thicker than blood?"
If there is a difference between then and now it is in the more impassioned styles of earlier artists. John Sloan comments on the massacre that ended a strike at the John D. Rockefeller Fuel and Iron Company in Ludlow, Colo., with a brisk 1914 sketch in black chalk of a miner who stands in a wrecked house grasping a dead child and firing a handgun. Reflecting similar outrage, Robert Minor sums up the Pittsburgh steelworkers strike of 1916 with an equally swift chalk sketch of a man impaled on the bayonet of a National Guardsman.
Compared with these, Sue Coe's "War" (1991) is tame stuff, being a neat design of a woman with head bowed and a sea of gas-masked faces under a sky black with warplanes. Nor does the pulse beat faster at the sight of Marshall Arisman's "Old Man on Park Bench Eating Dog Food" (1977); the artist has devoted too much care to the can opener lying beside the man.
Since industrialization and the population growth it fosters are its chief prerequisites, the art of political and social comment developed later in the United States than in Europe. Daumier, a caricaturist who was jailed for his pains, and Gustave Dore had long since made their marks by the time Thomas Nast began contributing to Harper's Weekly. Mr. Reilly notes in his brochure introduction that the German-born Nast was the first social critic in American art, but that he was "less a champion of the common man than a loyal advocate of republican ideology."
It was left to Robert Henri and the Ashcan painters to focus on the ever-widening gap between rich and poor, and they, in turn, passed the baton to The Masses, which had Sloan for art editor and artists like Minor, George Bellows and the young Stuart Davis for contributors. The Masses died in 1917, but the dissent continued -- notably in The Nation, which employed Adolf Dehn and Louis Lozowick, among others, and of course in The New Masses.
Not surprisingly, the vintage period for dissent in this show extends from the mid-1920's to the mid-1930's. Reginald Marsh reports sedately on bread lines (for a New Yorker of 1928), Philip Evergood on derelicts (1932), James Daughterty on police brutality (1932) and Paul Cadmus on lynching (1925). More interesting esthetically, however, is Philip Guston's 1930 drawing of a Ku Klux Klan hanging, because it all but proves the artist's 1970's departure from Abstract Expressionism to figuration to have been a reversion.
Jacob Lawrence speaks for the 1950's with a large gouache of a window that has flies and a dead mouse on the inside and gives on to a view of tenements packed tighter than sardines in a can. Ben Shahn does the same with a revised pen-and-ink version of his famous 1931-32 painting, "Nicola Sacco and Bartolomeo Vanzetti."
After this, and despite fine drawings by Antonio Frasconi and David Levine, the show loses momentum. It would appear that the most eloquent commentators of the last 30 years have been photographers.
After closing here on Sept. 27, the show travels to the Hood Museum of Art at Dartmouth College.
Also at the museum is a retrospective by Alan Cober, who is dubbed "The Artist as Illustrator, the Illustrator as Journalist." The guest curator for this is Steven Heller, who is editor of the A.I.G.A. Journal of Graphic Design and Design and Style, as well as a senior art director at The New York Times.
The recipient of numerous graphic arts awards, Mr. Cober is currently a professor of art and distinguished visiting artist at the State University of New York at Buffalo. His other accomplishments include covers for Time and Newsweek, posters, a mural for the National Museum of American History and a book on Willowbrook, the state mental hospital, that is titled "The Forgotten Society." He covered the 1980 Presidential election.
Mr. Heller calls him a "maverick" who "played a crucial role in changing the practice and perception of commercial and editorial illustration." To an outside observer, however, Mr. Cober is part of the figural trend that has its roots in Picasso, Goya, the German New Objectivity Movement and the sculpture of Marino Marini. Named the "Outsider Movement" by the historian Selden Rodman, the mode took on in the United States after World War II only to be shunted aside by Abstract Expressionism.
It is a heroic form of realism that seems always to be pointing to a moral and it has attracted artists from Jose Luis Cuevas to Rico Lebrun and Leonard Baskin and may now be attracting a new generation of painters and sculptors. Nevertheless, its influence seems to have been strongest in the graphic world.
Whether working with pencil and watercolor, pen and ink or an etching needle, Mr. Cober is ever the rigorous draftsman but with a highly subjective approach. The 1975 portraits of the aging Joe Louis in pen and colored inks are evidently faithful to life; but the "villain" of the 1980's, Michael Milken, is depicted as a junkie mainlining money.
Mr. Cober frequently departs without warning into his own Surrealist imagination, as in the profile of a man whose nose is metamorphosing into crab's legs and in the portrait of himself wearing the shell of a horseshoe crab. There are few images not tinged with the bizarre, whether it is the beautifully worked portrait heads on shriveled bodies or the men wearing hats, tight overcoats and masks who are forcing Martin Luther King away.
The artist, however, can produce amazingly cool drawings of machinery. Indeed, the key to understanding Mr. Cober's mind may well be in the labyrinthine hand-colored etchings of engines.
This show remains on view through Sept. 27. Call 232-9555 for information.
"Warnings," at Nardin Fine Arts in Cross River, was to have been a companion to the Cober show, but for reasons unclear has metamorphosed into an anthology of paintings, drawings, prints, sculpture and photographs, and the same title serves. As with the "Conscience" exhibition at Katonah, the time span is considerable, but the artists are contemporary, most of them living in Westchester.
Anthony Toney is represented by a watercolor or two homeless women, dated circa 1937; his daughter, Anita, by a fairly recent Rouaultish-looking lithograph of three men titled "Highlights 3, Age." Paul Pascarella takes issue with humanity in 1990's paintings of a predatory bird, one titled "Desert Storm." Hananiah Harari, on the other hand, mocks it with his circa 1950 vision of an old man in evening dress doing a "Miss Haversham" in a room cluttered with mementos, including a giant Victrola.
Also noteworthy in the fine art department are the delicate watercolor of a wood thrush lying dead in tall grass, by Donald Mackay, and Leonard Beall Smith's bird carried in black basalt, both from the early 1970's.
Claire Yaffa, a Social Realist photographer in black and white, focuses on infant burials in Potter's Field. Geoff Kerrigan is more the moderate in his black-and-whites of prison visitors, but he tries for a concentration camp effect in the print of a woman holding a baby up to a barbed-wire fence. The photographer who scores highest is Barbara Brundage, with two black-and-whites, one of a crack baby no bigger than the hand of the nurse holding it and with the face of an old man, the other of a car speeding past a dead deer.
All the photographs seem to be recent, likewise Bill Delaney's "Pipe Dream," boxed under glass. An assemblage that intimates warnings of a wittier kind, it consists of a pipe that emits a cutout reproduction of an Ingres nude instead of smoke.
The show remains through Sept. 1. Call 763-8451 for information.

HEADLINE: MAKING A DIFFERENCE;
Gambling on the Gulf Coast

BYLINE: By VERONICA BYRD

 BODY:
When he starts his job Tuesday as the new chief of the Mississippi Gaming Commission, Lorenzo D. Creighton says, his first assignment will be to listen.
By hearing what his staff and state gambling officials have to say, "I will have a much better feel for what is needed in order to make the administration run smoothly," Mr. Creighton said. "I want people to feel comfortable. I want to hear their feedback." 
 Riverboat gambling along the Mississippi River started in the spring of 1991. Dockside gambling began Aug. 1 on the Gulf Coast.
While gambling "is not the cure-all" for the state's economic ills, Mr. Creighton said, "when you look at the various businesses that spring up around riverboat gambling operations, it's clear gaming can be a strong economic force in the community."
He acknowledges that not everyone has warmly embraced the return of riverboat gambling and says there is a delicate balance in regulating the industry.
"A lot of people out there are anti-gambling, but as I'm involved, gaming will be conducted with the utmost integrity," said Mr. Creighton.
As deputy director of the Iowa Gaming and Racing Commission, Mr. Creighton, who has a law degree from Drake University, worked with casino and riverboat gambling, as well as parimutuel betting on horses and dogs.
Having held jobs from prison corrections officer to high school teacher to bank officer before beginning a career in gambling regulation, Mr. Creighton, 39 years old, said his diverse background would come in handy: "It prepared me with a pretty eclectic view of how an administration operates."

HEADLINE: Business Diary/August 23-28

BYLINE: By Joel Kurtzman

 BODY:

THE ECONOMY

 
One Theory Says Andrew Will Help the Economy 

The first cost of Hurricane Andrew was a human one: at least 20 people killed, 30 injured and as many as 230,000 left homeless. And then there is the economy. Estimates are imprecise, but Andrew may have caused $30 billion in destruction. "The rule of thumb is that one-third of losses is insured," said Bob Bannon, a financial economist with I.D.E.A. Inc., a research and forecasting concern in New York. A further rule, Mr. Bannon said, is that when insured losses reach a certain point, insurance companies, banks and individuals are forced to liquidate assets. "We may see $8 billion to $10 billion worth of assets put up for sale, which could put severe downward pressure on the markets," he said. But when the sweep-up begins, the economy is expected to feel a little bounce. Housing is expected to pick up, and with it durable goods and government spending. "It could create a little boomlet," Mr. Bannon said.
 
The Job-Training Bandwagon
 
Last week, President Bush wandered into Clinton territory. Against the backdrop of the Lincoln Technical Institute in blue-collar Union, N.J., Mr. Bush proposed expanding Federal job-training programs by $10 billion over five years. (Gov. Bill Clinton made an expansion of training and apprenticeship programs a centerpiece of his platform.) But while Mr. Clinton said he would pay for his $3 billion-a-year program with a payroll tax of 1.5 percent to 2 percent on companies with more than 50 employees, Mr. Bush said he believed that "we can pay for this new job-training offensive without raising taxes on people or increasing overall Government spending." To do that, Mr. Bush said he would cut other domestic programs. Which ones? Mr. Bush did not say.

Stricter Diets
 
The Federal Government has been investigating the advertisements and claims of commercial diet centers for years. Battling the bulge is a $33 billion business. Last week, the five largest companies in that market -- Jenny Craig International, Nutrisystem, Physician's Weight Loss Center, Diet Center and Weight Watchers -- joined to petition the Federal Trade Commission to establish industrywide standards and regulations for advertising and claims. These companies, which offer weight-loss programs without medical supervision, said in their petition they want their competing businesses to operate under a uniform set of rules. But some people familiar with the industry are skeptical of the companies' motives. "This is a classic effort to stall the Federal process," said Representative Ron Wyden, an Oregon Democrat. "It's a strategy to evade hard-nosed settlements and evade providing a factual basis for their advertising claims."

'Dark Forces' in the Economy
For months, Alan Greenspan, chairman of the
Federal Reserve, has been saying the economy is sailing into headwinds. More evidence came last week when the Commerce Department confirmed the earlier estimate that gross domestic product increased by just 1.4 percent in the second quarter, after growing by 2.9 percent the quarter before. But for Allen Sinai, chief economist at the Boston Company, the economic problems are more hurricane than headwind. "The forces of darkness are arrayed against the forces of light," he said. On the light side are low interest rates, low inflation and a low dollar -- which should spark growth. Opposing them are such forces as "the real estate depression, a weak world economy, the switch from a wartime economy to peace, and businesses trying to raise profits by keeping costs down." Together, Mr. Sinai said, these factors have created the "most aberrant business cycle in history."

Queasy Consumers

The first line of defense against recession is the consumer. Consumers account for two-thirds of all economic activity, but they spend freely only when they feel confident about the future. And lately, they have been feeling uneasy. The Conference Board's index of consumer confidence slipped to 58 in August from 61.2 in July. The index was an ebullient 100 in 1985. "The promise of business recovery, which was suggested by the markedly improved confidence readings recorded in the spring, has all but withered away," said Fabien Linden, director of the survey.

 
 
COMPANIES

 
When the Publicity Is Free
 
Hollywood has never been known for its subtlety or sensitivity. So it was no surprise last week when Tri-Star Pictures said it would advance the release date of Woody Allen's new movie, "Husbands and Wives," and increase the number of cities where it would open. The movie -- about the relationship between an older man and younger woman -- stars Mr. Allen and Mia Farrow. Hollywood's marketing mavens think the publicity surrounding Mr. Allen's own troubled relationship with Ms. Farrow will help the film. "Because what's going on so closely mirrors the film," said Leonie de Picciotto, senior vice president of the Samuel Goldwyn Company, "there's going to be a lot of interest in seeing it."

D.E.C.'s Second Chance
 
Though it tried once before -- with poor results -- the Digital Equipment Corporation, the nation's No. 2 computer maker, said last week it would make another attempt to become a force in the world of personal computers. Digital said it would manufacture in Taiwan a low-priced line of computers called DECpc LP (for low profile), which would sell for as little as $899. Digital, which is big in mainframes and minicomputers, currently has just 1 percent of the PC market. But analysts are optimistic about its plans. "Right now, Digital isn't even a slice on the personal-computer pie chart," said Jack Karp, an analyst with the Meta Group in Westport, Conn. "With these systems, it ought to become an identifiable slice in the next 12 months."

Hard Times for Mentor
 
The latest victim of the slowdown in the computer industry is the Mentor Graphics Corporation, once a high-flying company. Mentor, which makes scientific work stations and writes software, said last week it would eliminate 100 to 200 jobs, freeze salaries and streamline its operations. The company will take a charge of $15 million to $25 million for the restructuring. Mentor posted a $5.66 million loss for the first half of 1992 on sales of $189.2 million.
 
Toward a New T.W.A.
 
The privately held Trans World Airlines took a major step toward reorganizing itself into an employee-owned airline last week when its creditors committee, the International Association of Machinists and the Air Line Pilots Association reached an agreement on wages similar to one previously worked out with the Independent Federation of Flight Attendants. The agreement, which still must be approved by the Federal Bankruptcy Court and the unions' membership, calls for a 15 percent wage reduction in exchange for a 45 percent equity stake in the newly reorganized airline. The reductions will save the airline $350 million a year.

Faraway Events Stall Saturn
 
The Saturn division is the General Motors Corporation's shining light. Its sales are sprinting, its quality is high and its dealers are rated among the best. But in the world of just-in-time inventory, Saturn's plant in Spring Hill, Tenn., had to shut down when a strike by members of the United Automobile Workers closed G.M.'s Lordstown, Ohio, metal fabrication plant. Workers at Lordstown are striking over job security, health and safety issues and a proposal to eliminate 240 jobs. Other plants could also be struck. Even though no cars are being built, Saturn employees are scheduled to show up for work tomorrow for training classes.
 
Continental, the Popular Airline
 
Last week, Air Canada and Air Partners L.P., bid $400 million for the bankrupt Continental Airlines, with $100 million in equity. It's beginning to look like a bidding war. A month ago, Charles E. Hurwitz, the Houston investor, bid $350 million for Continental with $25 million in equity. And earlier this month, Alfredo Brener, a Houston investor whose family owns a major stake in Mexicana Airlines, bid $385 million for Continental.
 
 
 
INTERNATIONAL

 
The Dollar's Bleak Future
 
After being buffeted for weeks and falling to a 47-year low against the German mark, the dollar finally regained a little of its composure, settling at 1.4105 marks on Friday. But with German short-term interest rates hovering at 9.8 percent and American short term rates at 3.25 percent, traders do not expect the dollar to regain its ground anytime soon. "We aren't going to see an end to this pressure until the Bundesbank gives some indication of when it's going to lower interest rates," said Alan Chase, senior trader at National Westminster Bank in London
 
Yeltsin's Spirit of Cooperation
 
When he first asked for aid, President Boris N. Yeltsin of Russia reluctantly agreed to the conditions of the International Monetary Fund. But with $24 billion in Western aid held up pending a formal agreement between Russia and the I.M.F., Mr. Yeltsin's Government has turned enthusiastic. Moscow now agrees to trim spending to less than 5 percent of gross domestic product and to keep monthly inflation in the single digits, as the I.M.F. requested. Russia and the I.M.F., are working out a slew of economic targets, including those relating to the ruble exchange rate. The I.M.F. said it was pleased with the progress -- a prelude to freeing the aid.
 
Economic Tonic in Tokyo
 
To rouse its slumbering economy from recession and add a little life to its stock market, the Japanese Government announced a new emergency spending package worth $86.3 billion. The package includes money for public works, for bailing out ailing banks and for investing in the stock market. The Nikkei 225 stock index rose 2.37 percent on the news.

HEADLINE: The Executive Computer;
A Fast Way to Discover Patterns in Vast Amounts of Data

BYLINE: By Peter H. Lewis

 BODY:
In their quest for an edge in the financial markets, a number of traders have turned to a computer system called the Market Information Machine, created by Logical Information Machines Inc. of Austin, Tex.
MIM, as its fans call it, combines a powerful desktop computer with an extremely fast method of searching data bases to sort through current and historical market data, looking for patterns or correlations. Once a pattern is discovered, it can be used to increase the chances of making a profitable trade -- in theory, at least. 
 "It's one of those things that you might not use for several days, and then something comes up and you say, 'Hey, what's the answer?' " said Frank J. Marinaro, vice president of research for the Gelber Group of Chicago, a bond trading office and commodity trading adviser. "Like yesterday, the bond market was down a full point. So we ask, 'Show me the bond market the week before and the week after, when bonds are down a point on the day.' That's a simple request. We can tack on other conditions, like, 'and when X happens.' " X might be a sudden rise in foreign currency movements, or a shift in a particular company's stock price.
Jeffrey Li, vice president of the Duich Investment Corporation of San Francisco and another MIM enthusiast, said: "If you look at correlation of interest rate levels versus the price of stocks, you know that as interest rates go lower the market is going to go up. But everybody knows that and will be on that trade." So the hard part, he said, is coming up with original ideas. "Usually it's been done before, and it's hard to make money when everyone is doing the same thing," he said.
Using MIM, traders can concoct ad hoc queries with multiple variables. For example:
* When the Consumer Price Index was up less than 5 percentage points over a 12-month period, and the price-earnings ratio of the Standard & Poor's 500 index was 10 or less, was the S.&P. up in the following 12 months? If so, by how much?
* Show United States bond prices from, say, the first hour of trading to 1 P.M. on days when a share of International Business Machines is down more than 10.
* The last time bond prices were up 1 percent and so was the German mark against the dollar, what happened in the bond and currency markets in the next three days?
MIM has a relatively easy-to-use method that allows traders to create such questions using point-and-click commands, or by typing the questions in plain English. In other data base systems, queries are usually constructed in a programming language. Few traders have the time to learn to be programmers, or to wait for staff programmers to prepare and process the request.
Anthony D. Kolton, president of Logical Information Machines and, by no coincidence, a former stock and commodity trader in Chicago, said he thought MIM would eventually prove itself in many uses outside the financial markets.
"Our technology will apply wherever there are vast quantities of data, and you want to ask complex questions," he said. Insurance companies might want to use the system for risk analysis, for example. "We're currently in the design phase to analyze consumer scanner data at grocery stores," Mr. Kolton said. Supermarkets generate thousands of megabytes of data, in areas ranging from inventory control and special pricing to sales at the checkout counter, but as with the stock market there is no convenient way to ask complex questions of it.
As he explained it, a manager might ask MIM: "Show me the sales of this certain product in this region when it's on sale on Saturday between 9 A.M. and noon, when the competitor is not running a coupon and when it's displayed on an endcap," the prime display area at the ends of aisles. "The way it is now, if you want the knowledge you have to do custom programming. Using this system, you can get quality research a lot quicker."
The great strength of the MIM seems to be its speed, which derives in part from the proprietary way the system takes advantage of a computer's high-speed memory, said Eugene Lowenthal, a former director of the advanced computer architecture program at the Microelectronics and Computer Corporation of Austin and now a director of Logical Information Machines. MIM traces its origins to research done at the corporation.
In the financial markets particularly, all the data needed for research are generally publicly available, but few traders have the luxury of enough time to spend researching data, poring over charts and reading reams of analysis. And often they are not willing to commit the time or money needed to have a researcher check out a convoluted hunch -- especially if the hunch requires going back through 70 years of stock market data and economic indicators.
Mr. Li of Duich Investment said he was impressed with how quickly MIM can sift through years of data and display the results as graphs. His system is loaded with about 1,500 megabytes of market data -- about 1.5 billion characters. "It retrieves data almost instantaneously," Mr. Li said. And, of course, "as traders, we really, really like speed," he said.
Duich Investment leases the MIM software, which includes the data base search engine and the historic market data, and runs it on a Unix work station. The software costs the company about $2,500 a month, depending on options.
Logical Information Machines said more than two dozen companies, including some of the biggest names on Wall Street, use its system. Financial and insurance companies are notoriously secretive about the equipment and technologies they use in competitive forecasting, and several companies declined to acknowledge that they use MIM. Those that did praised MIM despite its expense.
"One of our traders said it paid for itself after the first trade we did on it, and that was for the full year, hardware and software," said Mr. Marinaro of the Gelber Group.
Clearly, MIM is not for the casual investor, although no doubt some individuals would like to have instant access to decades of market data.
Because it is a one-stop shop, combining market data and the search engine, MIM may prove to be a model for future tools for the investor, once prices come down, as they inevitably have in the computer business.
Many traders have turned to powerful computers to take over the research chores. "Speed and accuracy are two of the most important criteria that money managers and traders would look for in any system," said Sharon S. Schwartzman, editor of Wall Street and Technology magazine.
One Wall Street trader for a large firm who spoke only on condition that neither his name nor that of his company be used said he had seen an early version of MIM and rejected it.
"It was clunky and difficult to use, it didn't have enough functions, and there was no ability to hook up our own data," he recalled. His company decided to develop its own computer-based system, he said. But recently, he added, he had seen the "very impressive" latest version of MIM -- and leased it.
"For the medium-sized shop, it's a must," he said. "We're a larger house, and we have got to have this stuff."

HEADLINE: Networking;
Bringing Laptops Into the Fold

BYLINE: By STEPHEN C. MILLER

 BODY:
People who travel with portable computers are sometimes called road warriors. Like the Mad Max character, they feel alone and surrounded by hostiles. Airport security won't let them on the plane until they can turn on the computer to show it's not an explosive, but the battery just died and no spare is available. The hotel telephone is wired so a modem can't be used. The disk containing that spiffy electronic slide show is damaged and the only copy sits several thousand miles away on the network file server.
While the road warriors are not physically connected to the network, more and more of them are demanding service as if they were. A sales rep who missed a $3 million sale because she couldn't get approval in time from her manager is not mollified by a network manager's explanations about the need for a node address, routing difficulties over a wide area network and the high cost of 56-kilobit data lines. Nor is she interested in a detailed explanation of why she couldn't E-mail the proposed contract changes from the customer's site, the way she would have in the office. 
 Network administrators and their managers who are not planning for the needs of the mobile computer user will find themselves at a competitive disadvantage. The trends are clearly established.
Current portables, weighing less than six pounds, can duplicate most of the power and functions of deskbound machines. Prices, which once were double those of desktop machines, are dropping to a point where they are no longer a significant issue. Sales of portables are increasing at a faster rate than those of desktops.
Manufacturers of portable computers have recognized changes in the needs of the mobile user. Zenith Data Systems, for example, makes a line of laptops with built-in network interface cards that will plug into the network from a branch office wired to the network. The traveling user would instantly be connected to all the usual network services, like E-mail. Other manufacturers have announced similar options.
William Bluestein, senior analyst at Forrester Research in Cambridge, Mass., sees a wider implication for network managers in these "visitor work stations." "You're going to have a lot of people who don't work for your company connecting to the network with a laptop," he said.
Those "casual" users would include important customers, auditors and others who would provide services requiring some network access. It also raises the nagging question of network security.

HEADLINE: The Mall That Ate Minnesota

BYLINE: By NEAL KARLEN

DATELINE: BLOOMINGTON, Minn., Aug. 25

 BODY:
Victor Gruen has been all but lost to history, his name ridiculed when it is remembered. As the designer of Southdale, the world's first fully enclosed shopping mall, Mr. Gruen languishes in the rogues' gallery reserved for those pioneers of plastic consumerism who gave America everything from aerosol cheese to Astroturf.
Yet, Mr. Gruen was thinking more of civics than shopping when he opened Southdale in the Minneapolis suburb of Edina in 1956. To Mr. Gruen, the mall was to have been America's new Main Street, a town common to bring together citizens increasingly displaced from each other by the burgeoning postwar sprawl of freeways and suburbs. 
 This new town square was to have churches and hospitals. One day, he had hoped, the mall would serve as an all-purpose, cross-cultural destination for the new global village.
He was wrong. And today, civic pride being what it is, visitors to the Twin Cities are not taken to see Victor Gruen's still-standing landmark, but to the coffeehouse where Bobby Zimmerman alchemized into Bob Dylan, the purple house where Prince once pranced and the boardinghouse where F. Scott Fitzgerald pounded out "This Side of Paradise."
Then, this month, the Twin Cities were struck with Victor Gruen's Revenge. "Now," said Emily Goldberg, a local film maker, "people visiting me get off the plane and demand to go right to the megamall. The megamall? This is what we're going to be remembered for?"
Perhaps. For this $650 million fully enclosed shopping center is not only the most grandiose mall in American history. Nor is the complex, known officially as the Mall of America, just a pumped-up Paramus-on-the-Prairie, one more cookie-cutter amalgam of stores, frozen-yogurt stands and packs of free-range adolescents in Guess jeans.
It is also a 78-acre full-sensory smorgasbord of consumerism whose indoor vistas, its builders hope, will redeem Mr. Gruen's misbegotten dream. As such, its consortium of developers projects that the megamall, as it is known colloquially, will draw more customers a year by 1996 than Walt Disney World or the Grand Canyon.
Perhaps. Many industry analysts, however, question the sanity of launching a monster mall in the middle of a recession, at a time when studies suggest that Americans would rather do almost anything with their leisure time other than shop.
According to surveys conducted by Chicago's M.A.S. Marketing, the time spent monthly in malls by the average shopper dropped from 12 hours in 1980 to four hours in 1990. And sales per square foot of retail space in malls nationwide have dropped 17 percent since the peak year of 1978, according to studies by Management Horizons of Chicago.
STILL, as they say in Minnesota, the bait seems fresh. Inside the mall, its 4.2 million square feet have been diced into almost 400 specialty stores anchored by huge renditions of rare-to-the-Midwest delicacies like Bloomingdales, Nordstrom and Macy's. Sears, Roebuck fills the last of the corner anchors of the megamall, which, when everything is counted, takes up the equivalent of 88 football fields' worth of space on four floors. Only 41 of those fields are used for retailing; still, it takes almost an hour to briskly cruise the mall, with no timeouts for shopping.
At first sight, the megamall's sheer footage is so intimidating that some local grumps now refer to the shopping center as "megadeath." Yet, vast sums have been spent here to reduce the number of consumers stricken with shopping-mall anomie.
To beat back claustrophobia, the mall's innards have been remade wherever possible to make shoppers feel as if they were outside. The most notable of these efforts is the mall's seven-acre Camp Snoopy. It sits in a central courtyard and rises from ground level to the fourth floor, stocked with 400 trees, 30,000 plants, a mountain and a four-story waterfall.
Upstairs, around the height of the highest peak on Camp Snoopy's roller coaster, are dozens of restaurants, both fast and fancy, as well as an assortment of nightclubs and a 14-screen movie house. When visitors depart, they exit what may be the world's largest parking structure, containing 13,000 free spaces within 300 feet of the mall.
Can it possibly work? "They'll come," said Michael Dorsey, a mall spokesman. "They're coming."
In the years since the first shopping mall, it became clear that Mr. Gruen's new Main Street was not that far removed from the old one in the days of Sinclair Lewis. In Lewis's novel "Main Street," the heroine finds that on her town's central drag: "It was not only the uninspiring, unapologetic ugliness and the rigid straightness which overwhelmed her. It was the planlessness, the flimsy temporariness of the buildings."
Today, this description of downtown Gopher Prairie sounds frighteningly like the blueprint for the malls that America grew up on, where drive-in shoppers browsed in charmless boxes to the accompaniment of Muzak.
Still, mall business remained solid through the 70's. The industry peaked in 1978, when sales per square foot of retail space averaged $197 a year, according to Management Horizons. Then came the downturn, as consumers, experts said, turned off to the shopping monoliths that in time seemed as soulless and over-baroque as a Las Vegas casino. Today, that yield is $163 a square foot.
The fall cannot be attributed solely to the recession. Over the last 10 years, shopping habits have changed. The days of aimless browsing through malls may have passed, as huge department stores are increasingly replaced by one-stop Wal-Mart-like discount chains and mini strip malls. People are increasingly shopping by television and catalogue but, studies show, rarely for pleasure.
And that, said the developers, is where they come in. Looking back to the long-lost vision of Mr. Gruen, they have tried to create a space that can make the megamall a destination, an idealized community, fun.
T HE easy part of that job is simple mall science, knowing, for example, to allow 4.5 parking spaces for every 1,000 square feet of leasable area inside. The hard part is selling the fantasy.
That fantasy is best expressed in the mall's theme song, written by Robin Batteau, New York's famed "Tarzan of the Jingle," whose resume includes lines like Chevrolet's "The heartbeat of America" and Coca-Cola's "Can't beat the feeling." For the mall, he came up with a wickedly memorable hook line played under the words: "There's a place for fun in your life, the Mall of America. You've got to see it to believe it. Who told you you can't have it all?"
To make that magic, the developers had to configure the mall's foreboding caverns to seem somehow inviting. So the complex was chopped into four winding corridors, each with a different theme. Matching their mood to a corridor, shoppers can start, say, in the gazebo- and wooden trellis-lined North Garden, the hallway billed as Main Street U.S.A.
The mix is critical. Because the megamall needs to appeal to the largest cross section of the population, its planners have planted the place with bonbons for every demographic. For each Abercrombie & Fitch, there seems to be a counterbalancing Filene's Basement.
Even shopping music has been democratized. Enlisting 3M, Muzak's arch-competitor, the mall has opted away from elevator music in favor of less somnolent stylings. Light FM, a radio service heavily inflected with Billy Joel and James Taylor, is piped into the mall's common areas, while current rock hits are heard in stores catering to 12- to 25-year-olds.
More than a million people huffed through the mall in the first week after Ray Charles was paid $50,000 to sing "America" during a grand-opening gala on Aug. 10. Other stars also came out. Larry Gatlin, who with his singing brothers owns a country-music show bar in the mall, was so moved by the spectacle that he could not stop repeating his mantra for business success: "Let's move some meatloaf!" he said to all who would listen.
Then there was pizza-pushing Wolfgang Puck, who went to the Mall of America to open his first restaurant outside California. The chef was chastised by an unimpressed woman from suburban Minneapolis who liked the food but was upset that Mr. Puck was eating as he served. "I don't care who he is," she said. "It's gross!"
As the bodies ebbed, flowed and banged up against each other during opening week, shopping proved a near-hallucinatory experience. From the epicenter near Camp Snoopy, it felt like an ecstatic mall rave, the staccato ka-ching of thousands of cash registers serving as the high-frequency techno-beat for hundreds of thousands of Midwest shoppers.
The mood over the megamall was not always so jovial. Ever since plans were first announced in 1985, developers have had to bull past vehement local naysayers. To bolster their case, the builders utilized an inspirational strategy of if-we-build-it-they-will-come. But the mall's first development script appeared to have been taken not from "Field of Dreams" but "The Music Man," with Nader Ghermezian, an Iranian rug merchant turned mall developer, playing Prof. Harold Hill, the silvery-tongued out-of-towner with a bright idea for civic improvement.
"You will have all the shoppers from New York, Rome, Los Angeles and Paris coming here," Mr. Ghermezian told Minnesota doubters at a 1986 news conference. "I bring you the moon and you don't want it?"
Maybe, said Minneapolis civic leaders as they pondered the models, then hauled up to Canada to see what Mr. Ghermezian and his three brothers, doing business as the Triple Five Corporation, had already done. What they found was the $1.1 billion West Edmonton Mall in Alberta, with 5.2 million square feet, still the world's biggest. Nader Ghermezian called his Canadian creation, which opened in 1981, "the Eighth Wonder of the World" -- and Minnesota decided it wanted the ninth.
Land was acquired in 1985 10 miles from Minneapolis in suburban Bloomington, on a site formerly occupied by Metropolitan Stadium, the demolished, much-beloved home of the Minnesota Twins. The Ghermezians originally planned on a mall even bigger than Canada's, but faced with some opposition and with having to refinance the project, they lured the Indianapolis-based mall kings Melvin and Herbert Simon to join the project in 1987, and the plans were scaled back.
On Aug. 11, the doors finally opened. And they came, in hordes nearing 150,000, to see what the developers said was the largest number of retailers ever to open on one day in one place. The Simon brothers, who own shares in the mall with the Ghermezians and several other silent partners, predict that by 1996 the Mall of America will draw 40 million customers a year, a third of them from more than 150 miles away.
BUT the doubters remain unmoved. For in the years since Mr. Ghermezian first pulled into Minneapolis, American shopping habits have changed.
"I hate to say this, but I think it is doomed to fail," said Therese Byrne, a Salomon Brothers real estate analyst. "I think everyone is shocked that the mall's backers actually went through with this. But I guess you can't back off when you're half-pregnant."
What the developers hope they are on to is the good-willed frenzy that seemed to vibrate through the floors of the megamall during its opening week. Those mobs -- as seen from above in a Camp Snoopy roller-coaster car on a weekday afternoon -- seemed as happy, brisk and surreally well choreographed as the June Taylor dancers.
Down below, however, is a suggestion that the mall may be cursed by the ghost of the major-league ball park that used to sit there. Metropolitan Stadium looms in the Midwest imagination the way Ebbets Field is recalled in Brooklyn, and Camp Snoopy sits directly above where home plate used to be.
"Metropolitan Stadium was one of the most pastoral ball parks ever," said Art Simon, a Minnesota native who teaches film theory at New York University and who not related to the Simons. "To pave that paradise is like building on an Indian burial ground: you tempt the gods."
But mall spokesmen seem unworried by the threat of old ball-park voodoo. To other doomsayers, the Mall of America need only point to its opening figures. In the first week of business, during those scarce gorgeous Minnesota summer days, more than a million people, it is estimated, went inside the mall, and 13,000 pounds of chicken were consumed at Camp Snoopy. At BareBones, a medical-supply store, 20,000 purchases were made, 432 screaming fly swatters were sold, and the line of people waiting to go inside often numbered 200. Only three cars were stolen from the parking area, and nobody died anywhere in the megamall.
And what if it all goes bust, if fancy stores go vacant and little stores unbrowsed? Then, experts in shopping-mall sociology say, the mix of tenants will get even wilder. A church, a car dealership, a casino or a hospital might move in, perhaps finally validating in failure Victor Gruen's prophesy of the shopping mall as America's new Main Street.
But right now there will be no talk of that at America's most grandiose shopping center. "The people will come," said Mr. Dorsey, the mall spokesman, repeating his own mantra: "They're coming."

The mall houses Camp Snoopy, the world's largest indoor amusement park, stocked with rides, trees and plants. The developers of Mall of America have tried to create a space that can make the megamall a destination. (Photographs by Steve Woit for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: The Lace Is in the Gloves

BYLINE: By MOLLY O'NEILL

 BODY:
"RIGHT!" barks Tony Canarozzi.
Kristen Connolly, a 25-year-old researcher for the Wall Street firm Tudor Investments giggles and pushes her right arm toward her 24-year-old colleague, Stacy Beecher. Ms. Connolly grins impishly, like a little girl on a playground trying to see just how far she can go. Her punch is tentative, soft, halting. 
 "Jab! Jab!" Mr. Canarozzi yells.
Ms. Connolly exhales into two quick left jabs. She purses her lips and breathes faster.
It's 6:45 P.M. at the Institute for Fitness, a tiny private gym at 76 Beaver Street in the Wall Street area where Mr. Canarozzi is the boxing coach. Four other pairs of professional women have also traded success-dress for cotton shorts and T-shirts. Their hands have been wrapped in strips of protective cotton, their flawless manicures tucked into red Everlast boxing gloves. They're suited up for the latest rage on the fitness frontier: aerobic boxing.
Since the mid-1980's, when Ivy League-educated Wall Streeters started sliding into sweaty gyms to punch bags, jump rope and, eventually, face off in rings, A. J. Leibling's "sweet science" has been popping up more and more in the popular consciousness. There was Mickey Rourke, then "Diggstown." Last month, Allure magazine pictured the actress and model Carre Otis sparring in a gym. Dan Golomb, chairman of the Everlast Sports Manufacturing Company, noticed a sudden surge in the sale of small-size boxer shorts. ("The ones the women typically buy," he said.) Aerobics studios across the nation began offering boxing classes.
"Our clients wanted more than aerobic dance," said Nicole Chevance, the manager of the Jeff Martin Studio on the Upper West Side. "The response to the first classes this summer was amazing. And it was all women."
A typical boxing workout begins with 15 minutes on the Stairmaster, followed by five 3-minute rounds of fast-paced jump rope. Next comes 10 minutes of punching, first a heavy bag, and then a smaller speed bag, followed by 15 minutes of drills in steps, ducks and punches. After that, the would-be boxer faces a partner holding "focus pads" -- cushions meant to absorb punches with 1,000-pound force -- and practices punching in 10 3-minute rounds. A hundred sit-ups and a 3-minute round of fast-paced shadow boxing end the session.
Lilly Rivera, a personal trainer at the private gym of a Wall Street investment firm who began boxing two months ago, estimates that the workout burns 600 to 800 calories, more than most aerobics classes that last as long, and offers better-rounded training. In addition to upper- and lower-body development, she said, boxing teaches coordination, quick reflexes and agility.
The fascination of pugilism for women is already moving beyond the tidy, mirrored confines of aerobics studios. Women are beginning to enter the ring at Gleason's Gym, the Brooklyn boxing center where professionals train.
"There's been a tremendous increase in women, mostly the lawyer and M.B.A. types," said Ira Becker, an owner of the gym. "But we trained Twyla Tharp and more actresses than I can name.
"Initially, they see the value in terms of fitness, but eventually it goes deeper. Eventually they want to spar."
When the first punches connect with the body of another, they make a thunk: a particular, determined, relentless sound; a scary sound that Norman Mailer once described as "a heavy ax in the distance chopping into a wet log." For women learning to box, the ax-into-wet-log sound is just as shocking as that of Ibsen's Nora slamming the door. The sound of a woman landing a punch is the sound of a shattered taboo.
Mr. Canarozzi, a former middleweight who trains women at the Institute for Fitness as well as at Gleason's, protects his prodigies from the shock of the sound. After the first 30 minutes of his workouts, hearts are racing, endorphins surging. The women are sweat-drenched, breathless -- Stepford wives to the sound of his voice. He barks orders for every exercise, every drill, assuming all responsibility for the initiation of violence and removing it from the conscience of his pupils.
"Duck to the right! Duck to the left! Jab, jab! Right! Duck to the right! Duck to the left! Left hook! Right! Right!"
Ms. Connolly's impish grin tightens. She bends, sways and slams into the focus pads that Ms. Beecher is holding. Each punch comes with the kind of gasping grunt that Gestalt therapists dream of. Her teeth are bared. She punches again and again. She punches until Mr. Canarozzi yells, "Stop!"
Immediately, as if awakened from a hypnotic trance, she stops. She giggles. She swings her Breck girl ponytail and stares at her boxing gloves.
"Gee," she says.
Later, Ms. Connolly called boxing a stress reliever. "You're concentrating so much that nothing else matters," she said. "Afterwards, I feel totally clear and calm."
Other women who have entered the ring agree.
"It takes intense concentration and precision, a combination of physical and psychic energy," said Wendy G. Finch, the 33-year-old director of sales for Chanel Fragrances Exclusive in Manhattan, who boxes three times a week at Gleason's. "When I leave here, I am clear, self-confident and peaceful."
THE cultural shadow boxing that draws women into the ring is as arresting as the social underpinnings of any great fight. Dempsey versus Tunney, wrote Elliot J. Gorman, was basically a contest between brutal, lower-class frontierism (Dempsey) and clean-cut Victorianism (Tunney). Women who box symbolize an affluent economy, the blurring of sexual distinctions, a continuing power struggle and, perhaps as much as anything else, a need for utility and precision.
In her 1987 essay "On Boxing," Joyce Carol Oates wrote that since gladiatorial times in Rome, the fascination with boxing has been at its highest in the wealthier, more advanced societies. In the decadent era of Nero, women boxed publicly. In the essay, Ms. Oates wondered whether an effete and indulgent prosperity didn't engender a need for "the outward signs and gestures of 'savagery.' "
Watching women, I kept seeing the same smile. It appeared after the aerobics, after the punching, when the women got inside the coach's focus pads and ran, flailing, into his chest. Mr. Canarozzi conceded that all the several dozen women he trains "have a lot of anger toward men."
But the smile I saw repeatedly wasn't that of the victim; it was the smile of the reassured.
"How often do you come up against a real guy like this anymore?" asked Teri Giorgi, a hair stylist who had just "boxed into" Mr. Canarozzi, getting inside the pads to his chest.
The exercise seemed to solidify the shift in distinctions between the sexes. "Some of the guys I work with can't believe I go to boxing classes," said Judy Salvatore, an administrative manager on a securities trading desk who boxed with bangles of drop pearl earrings from both lobes. "I say, it's 1992. Wake up. If some weirdo comes after me on the subway, I'll deck him. It's 1992. I can take care of myself."
Y OU think differently about yourself after three lessons. Like most women who have taken to the sport, I was overeducated, overexercised and underinterested in punching anybody out. But as soon as I walked into a gym that reeked of human effort and faced Mr. Canarozzi, a barrel-chested old-time coach in a sweatshirt with a whistle around his neck, some kind of invisible gauntlet seemed to have been thrown down. I felt voyeuristic, inside some male citadel and bound to prove that I belonged.
Aerobic boxing made my daily hour on the Stairmaster seem like a walk in the park. By the time the coach tied on my gloves, I was in an altered state of consciousness: "the zone," athletes call it.
The coach ordered punches, and I knew what an attack dog feels like when its owner says, "Sic!" I felt safer than I've felt in years when I boxed into Mr. Canarozzi's chest and he wrapped his bear arms around me in a boxer's embrace.
"O.K., fighter," he said to me. To an assistant he said, "Take her gloves." He told me later that I'd fought like an animal and he didn't want me to hurt anybody. I don't remember that part.
I remember the focus and feeling of purpose in the exercise. After 10 years of aimless aerobics, motion with a mission was a relief. I lost three pounds during each workout, as opposed to the pound and a half I normally lose working out. My shoulders, trapezoids, lateral chest muscles, thighs and calves ached afterward.
I was euphoric after my lessons with Mr. Canarozzi. There was also a nameless sort of longing that I couldn't define until I dropped by Gleason's one night last week. A group of women I'd watched Mr. Canarozzi train on Wall Street had gone there for the first time to train. All professed a deep distaste for violence and had no aspiration, they said, for an appearance in the ring.
But inside the fetid sweat- and bloodstained world of Gleason's, punching bags alongside welterweight and middleweight contenders, all kept looking toward the four rings in the center of the gym. They moved closer to the rings. They touched its canvas mat, tested its ropes. The bell rang every three minutes.
Punches thunked soggily and steadily. Each of the six women who'd been studying aerobic boxing "for the exercise" narrowed their eyes over their raised gloves and focused on the ring.
Mr. Becker, the owner, studied the women and smiled.
"Male or female," he said, "it doesn't matter. They learn the craft, and eventually they're going to say, 'Let's box.' It's inevitable."
