LENGTH: 374 words

HEADLINE: How to Repel Immigrants' Threat

 BODY:
The generally supportive and approving tones describing the local front in the third-world invasion of the United States ["Hispanic Influx Being Felt on East End," July 12] is reprehensible. With the left-wing political culture doing everything it can to discourage assimilation, promote bilingualism and cheapen the value of American citizenship, no one on Long Island or in the nation can afford to be apathetic, let alone passively supportive, of the breakdown of national soverignty which characterizes United States immigration policy.
In an age of increasing technological sophistication, the United States can not afford to continually accept massive immigration by slash-and-burn third-world peasants. The overload of non-Western immigrants threatens the cultural and political traditions which created this republic and made it the envy of the world. 
 The dangers posed by growing communities of illegal immigrants was graphically exposed by the fact that one-third of the rioters in South-Central Los Angeles were illegal aliens, and the Washington Heights riots were orchestrated by drug gangs manned primarily by illegal aliens. The argument that these people are needed is contradicted by good evidence that over the last 20 years immigrants, especially from Latin America, extract more wealth in social services than they create in labor. With unemployment as high as it is among blacks, college and high-school students, it is doubtful that these people are as necessary as some businesspeople contend -- though no doubt they may be cheaper.
The failure of the Suffolk County government to take steps to aid in repelling this invasion of our nation is more than disconcerting. It is despicable. For a Town Councilman to state that "we have no choice but to welcome them" is an admission of governmental and moral bankruptcy. You don't welcome people who have violated our law and threaten our national cohesion and institutions. The correct response for governmental leaders, and indeed all citizens, is to inform the I.N.S. whenever these people are identified, so they may receive the only benefit they have any claim to -- a fast return trip to their country of origin.
 
H. F. HAZELBAKER
Huntington Station

HEADLINE: 'How Stupid Do They Think We Are?'

BYLINE: By Ross Perot; Ross Perot is author of "United We Stand: How We Can Take Back Our Country."

DATELINE: DALLAS

 BODY:
The other day, a plumber stopped me and said, "Ross, there's not a nickel's worth of difference between the two political parties." A construction worker told me, "If I hear one more phony speech about 'family values,' I think I'll throw up!" The most frequently repeated statement by good, hard-working people since the Democratic and Republican conventions has been: "How stupid do they think we are?"
Both parties act as if they bear no responsibility for the $4 trillion debt that burdens our country. But the world financial markets tell the story, as the dollar dropped to new lows against the German mark after the Republican convention. A computer engineer summed it up: "We can't be a superpower unless we are first an economic superpower -- just look at Russia." 
 Sam Walton became America's richest man by standing in the aisles, listening to his customers and taking swift action to meet their needs and desires.
Unfortunately, the parties have lost touch with the American people. Neither party has a Sam Walton. They spend so much time listening to the special interests -- from the foreign lobbyists to the tobacco industry -- that give them the huge amounts of money needed for their campaigns that they can't hear taxpaying citizens.
An interesting thing happened this year. Five and a half million people, who feel they have no voice in the country they own, joined together in a petition signing process to put me on the ballot.
Even after I announced that we could best solve the country's problems if one or both of the political parties would squarely face the issues and deal with them, the people continued getting petitions signed and have now formed a national organization, United We Stand America. The process has been completed in 48 states and should be successfully completed in all 50. Their goal is to influence every Congressional race, as well as the Presidential race, and they are active in every state. At their request, I am actively supporting them.
I am not the centerpiece of this effort. My role as a candidate is incidental. This effort is focused on rebuilding our country. Only the owners -- the people -- can do it.
Earlier this year, the Republicans and Democrats spent several months arrogantly wandering around asking, "Who are these people getting petitions signed?"
I can tell you exactly who these people are. These are people who love their country, work hard, rear good children, play by the rules, love the principles on which this country was founded -- and don't like to see those principles violated. They are disgusted with gridlock government, finger-pointing, check bouncing and neglect of domestic and economic issues. They are concerned about their jobs and their children's futures, as we export entire industries, deindustrializing America and putting our people out of work.
Why should Democrats and Republicans in every political race listen to these people? Here is one good reason: their numbers are so large that they can be the swing vote that determines who wins Congress and the White House in November.
Just to eliminate any confusion by the candidates and political parties about what the people who make up United We Stand America are concerned about -- here is a partial list. I hope the parties will treat it as a wake-up call.
* Rebuild the job base and put our people back to work.
* Stimulate the growth of small businesses by making capital and credit available. Today they are starved for both. Credit can be made available by changing the banking regulations. Capital can be made available by eliminating all capital gains taxes for money that goes directly into the treasuries of small businesses.
* Develop an intelligent, supportive relationship between Government and business.
* Develop strategic plans on an industry-by-industry basis to strengthen and rebuild our large companies. Target the industries of the future and develop specific plans to be the world leader in those industries. Our successful international competitors do this. We don't.
* Build and maintain our manufacturing base. We cannot be a superpower unless we are a world leader in manufacturing. Never forget that if we have to defend our country we will need to be able to make a range of products, from steel and integrated circuits to shoes (remember Valley Forge?), and convert our factories to wartime use.
* Eliminate the deficit. Pass necessary legislation to balance the budget. Put in place a program to pay off the national debt. The President's check-off box is a gimmick. It won't begin to pay for just one year's interest on the debt.
* Get rid of illegal drugs and dramatically reduce crime and violence throughout our country. Talk won't do it.
* Rebuild our cities. Make our "alabaster cities gleam undimmed by human tears."
* Make our public schools the finest in the world. Today, they rank at the bottom of the industrialized world.
* Restructure our health-care system to make it cost effective. Today, it is the most expensive in the industrialized world, but not the best. Minor changes suggested by both parties won't fix the problem.
* Develop a new tax system that is fair, paperless for most Americans and raises the money necessary to pay our country's bills. The parties don't even talk about this.
* Make it a criminal offense for foreign countries, foreign companies or foreign individuals to hire lobbyists or contribute money -- or services -- to political campaigns. Both parties have foreign lobbyists in key campaign roles.
* Pass laws prohibiting cashing in on prior Government service.
* Develop truly fair free-trade agreements. Today we are giving entire industries to other countries.
* Develop an intelligent energy policy. We have no policy. Our country is totally vulnerable in case of war.
* Give the President a line-item veto.
* Pass laws to stop Congress from exempting itself from laws it imposes on the rest of the country.
* Bring the overly generous Congressional retirement plan in line with private-sector plans and get rid of unnecessary perks in Government. Slash staffs in the executive and legislative branches.
* Pass laws requiring the return of all excess political campaign funds to the Treasury.
* Pass laws to reduce the time required for Federal election campaigns, reduce the cost of Federal campaigns and create equal opportunity for all new candidates by providing equal television time.
* Replace the Electoral College with the popular vote.
* Eliminate political action committees and pass laws eliminating all possibilities for special interests to give large sums of money to candidates.
* Hold elections on Saturday and Sunday, instead of Tuesday. Make it easy for working people to vote. Prohibit the release of election information until the polls close in Hawaii.
* Finally, and most importantly, bring us together. United teams win. Divided teams lose. Play to our multicultural strengths. Stop preaching the messages of hate and division in your campaign themes.
And now, a message to both parties. Please remember that those who have participated in the United We Stand America movement are intelligent, thinking, responsible people. They are not unprogrammed robots who can be emotionally swayed by your negative ads or messages of fear and divisiveness. Bluntly, you will have to face the issues to get their votes. Mud wrestling and messages aimed at destroying your opponent and his loved ones won't work.
I love the American people and I am sure that you do, too. I owe them a debt I can never repay and so do you. Today, their Government is a mess, and they want it fixed. By joining together as the owners of this great country, they can solve these problems.
As I've said before, it is time to clean out the barn -- join us -- pick up a shovel. Get to work!

HEADLINE: THE WORLD;
Show of Force on Iraq Signals New Direction

BYLINE: By JUDITH MILLER

 BODY:
The creation of a "no-fly zone" over southern Iraq by the United States and its allies last week was ostensibly aimed at preventing President Saddam Hussein from continuing to terrorize and slaughter the region's large, dissident Shiite Muslim population in the name of quelling its persistent, if ineffectual insurgency. But it also signaled the start of a reversal of Administration policy toward Iraq, which has failed in its implicit goal of ousting Mr. Hussein.
After 18 months of studiously ignoring, first, Mr. Hussein's brutal crushing of the March 1991 Shiite uprising after the Persian Gulf war and more recently, Iraq's repression of the southern Shiites, the Bush Administration is sending condolences to the victims. 
 Though the new policy, in which President Bush demanded that Iraq halt all flights over the 47,500 square miles of southern Iraq, was praised by many analysts and quickly endorsed by Gov. Bill Clinton of Arkansas, its timing prompted questions about political motivation, a suggestion that Mr. Bush and his team heatedly denied last week. Nevertheless, said the Middle East expert Fouad Ajami, who supports the thrust of the new policy, the Administration's "election-eve conversion" made such suspicions unavoidable.
Clearly there are new circumstances. Mr. Hussein only began using fixed-wing aircraft in raids against the Shiites in April. A United Nations report detailing Iraq's human rights abuses of the Shiites was presented only on Aug. 11. And only in recent months has Iraq increased harassment of United Nations inspectors, human rights monitors and relief workers.
Moreover, officials note, the Western and Arab coalition authorized by the United Nations to oust Iraqi forces from Kuwait lacked a specific mandate to protect Iraqi minorities until April 1991, when Mr. Hussein's savage repression of the Iraqi Kurds prompted the Security Council to approve Resolution 688. By then the Shiite uprising in the south had already been put down.
Finally, they say, the Administration needed time to mobilize logistical support for a joint response, and above all, time to determine that such action would not jeopardize the Arab-Israeli peace talks under way in Washington. Those talks are widely seen as the most impressive byproduct of the gulf confrontation. Yitzhak Rabin's election victory over the intransigent Likud Government insured that the talks would progress, as, indeed, they have.
 
Fears of Dismemberment
 Only a year ago, the White House accepted Arab warnings that the creation of a no-fly zone would lead to the effective partition of Iraq, a prospect anathema to Sunni Arabs, the Shiites' bitter historical rivals, and who have traditionally relied on Iraq to counter militantly Shiite Iran. The dismemberment of Iraq, in turn, could lead either to the creation of a Shiite Islamic enclave in southern Iraq, or even worse, they feared, to Iranian influence or domination there. For far too long, officials now acknowledge, they believed that Mr. Hussein would be ousted by the Sunni military men who surround him.
Critics, like Laurie Mylroie, a fellow at the Washington Institute for Near East Policy, argued futilely that the Iraqi Shiites were nationalists, and that they probably lacked the political cohesion needed to form a separate state. The Iraqi opposition, even those based in Iran, were committed to keeping Iraq intact.
But fear of Islamic fundamentalism and of Iran led to inaction. United States officials calculated that it was better to risk keeping a de-fanged Hussein in power than to risk dismembering Iraq.
Now, officials conclude, that assessment was wrong. Perhaps, paradoxically, it was the Kurdish and Shiite rebellions just after the war that led Sunni military officers to rally to Mr. Hussein, but the Iraqi leader has not become more vulnerable. Even worse in an election year, his taunting of United Nations inspectors reminds Americans that he is still around. The value of getting rid of him increased as pro-Clinton bumper stickers began appearing on the cars of unemployed auto workers -- "Saddam Hussein Has a Job. Do You?"
 
The Latest 'Ism'
 The evolution of Administration thinking about militant Islamic fundamentalism also played into the reassessment. In a speech in June outlining Administration policy, Edward P. Djerejian, an Assistant Secretary of State, stressed that America would not seek to portray Islam as the latest "ism" to be fought -- a natural successor to the cold war which in the 1960's prompted the United States, Saudi Arabia and even Israel to support militantly Islamic groups as a bulwark against Godless communism, the same groups that now threaten American interests in the region. Washington, Mr. Djerejian said, has no quarrel with the great Muslim religion, but rather with those who use religious cover to espouse extremism, violence, intolerance and terrorism, and who use democracy to gain power and destroy democracy. In an interview last week he named some culprits: the Shiite group Hezbollah in Lebanon, Sunni Muslim Hamas in the Israeli-occupied territories, and the Governments of Sudan, and, of course, Iran.
Yet despite the Administration's obvious antipathy to Iran's support for Hezbollah, its fears of Iran's designs on southern Iraq (which Iranian officials adamantly deny), Washington, he said, is prepared to engage in a "dialogue" with Iran. It is Iran that has refused to talk.
Absent such a dialogue, the perceived threat of Iranian meddling in southern Iraq has not changed dramatically. And the Administration is obviously still concerned about what might happen there should Baghdad lose effective sovereignty. Administration officials, for example, are known to oppose sending arms and aid to the Iraqi Shiites to fight Saddam by creating an Iraqi Shiite mujahedeen, as they did in Afghanistan.
But absent such support, Ms. Mylroie says, Hussein will cling to power. The Administration, she argues, still has no actual strategy for getting rid of him if he does not rise to this provocation. "What we've had," she says, "is a tactical shift when a strategic one is required."
Nevertheless, the shift reflects a marked change in priorities. Whatever risks inherent in the no-fly zone are now outweighed by the dangers of doing nothing to try to hasten Mr. Hussein's ouster. The Administration's fear of Islamic fundamentalism, justified or not, has taken second place to Mr. Bush's need to appear a man of action. The Administration has found a new, if temporary "ism" to oppose in the Middle East -- Saddam has replaced the Red Menace.

HEADLINE: Conversations/Juzo Itami;
A Director Boasts of His Scars, and Says He Is Right About Japan's Mob

BYLINE: By JAMES STERNGOLD

DATELINE: TOKYO

 BODY:
Drawing his finger along a fresh scar stretching from one end to the other of his famous craggy cheek, and then across the soft flesh behind his ear, which had also been disfigured, Juzo Itami described how three gangsters had toyed with his life.
"They cut very slowly; they took their time," he said. "They could have killed me if they wanted." 
 Like violent runes, the series of pale lines carved professionally in the face of one of Japan's most respected film directors in May spelled out a gruesome message. It was a warning that one should not challenge, as Mr. Itami has in his latest film, the integral role that organized crime groups play in Japanese society. Indeed, for decades few had.
Mr. Itami tried to demonstrate in the movie, to be called "Minbo -- or the Gentle Art of Japanese Extortion," when it is released in the United States later this year, that the mobsters are paper tigers whose ferocity crumbles when confronted with careful resistance. But does the attack prove him wrong?
"The fact that I was attacked proves that what I pointed out in my film was correct," he insisted, in one of his first interviews since the assault on May 22, a week after the film opened. "If I was wrong they wouldn't have reacted so strongly.
"I pointed out the truth, and they wanted to stop this information from spreading," he added. "In a way, it was a very subtle way for them to try and make it seem there was some contradiction."
The attack on the roguishly handsome Mr. Itami, who at 59 years old has pursued successful careers as an actor, screen writer and director, was just one reminder of how deep the problem of organized crime runs today in Japan. A new law took effect earlier this year granting the police new powers in cracking down on the yakuza, which the gangsters have fought. This made Mr. Itami's movie that much more timely.
Then last week, Shin Kanemaru, the kingmaker of the ruling Liberal Democratic Party, admitted he had accepted an unreported 500 million yen cash contribution from the head of a trucking concern with extensive mob connections. One article said Mr. Kanemaru had dined several times with the head of the second-largest yakuza group in the country.
 
Shocking Badge of Honor
 If such signs of influence have made some think twice about challenging the gangs, Mr. Itami is not among them. He boasts of his scars and brandishes a shocking photo of his wounds, taken shortly after the assault, like a badge of honor. It brings out a side to this pre-eminent satirist of modern Japanese ways that is not often in view -- immense pride and a sense that the Japanese ought to do better standing up for themselves as individuals.
Mr. Itami is best known in the West for "Tampopo," a hilarious send-up of Japanese noodle-eating habits, and "A Taxing Woman," which turns the adventures of a tax inspector into a revealing look at the Japanese penchant for tax evasion.
He is nearly 6 feet tall, which meant that his head just about grazed the beams in his sleekly modern office in an apartment building in Roppongi, a popular entertainment district in Tokyo. The office is an elegant mix of contemporary and traditional. The modest-sized room is surrounded by matched grain, natural wood panels, with impressive stereo and video systems resting just behind the straw tatami mats, where he conducted an interview sitting cross-legged at a low Japanese-style table.
But the reminders of his ordeal are never far away. He said that his summer had felt even hotter than usual because he had been unable to escape to his country home on the nearby Izu peninsula, Tokyo's Hamptons.
"It's the police, who secretly watch me all the time,"he said. "They say it would be too dangerous to go to my house."
The yakuza have long been pictured in popular films and novels as principled if earthy characters, who break the laws of mainstream society but follow their own code of honor. The reality, of course, was far different. Everyone knew that their central weapon was violent intimidation and that their strength came from their numbers, not their courage.
But the myth, in a way, provided an excuse for years of tolerance of the yakuza, who co-existed with the police as shadow enforcers in their own world. The recent police crackdown is an attempt to change that, but a series of scandals have shown how the yakuza have entered the economic mainstream in recent years, with connections not only to top politicians but also major corporations.
For Mr. Itami, this power represents not just a loss of control to the underworld, but a loss of self-esteem for the Japanese. He said he resents most the way the yakuza break peoples' spirit and pride to extract money. Their favorite means of earning money is extortion schemes, which generally involve humiliating their victims and demanding payments for fabricated slights and wrongs.
 
Culture of Death
 Mr. Itami said the recent scandals and the highly publicized attack against himself have started to change attitudes. He admitted that the evidence is thin but he said that in a nation of people extremely sensitive to the prevailing winds of opinion there is a growing sense of disapproval of the yakuza.
In his next film, Mr. Itami will be pursuing another theme related to this notion of holding on to one's pride. Death, as he puts it, is the film's central character. The film is his rejection of the way the modern Japanese have tried to banish it from their lives. "Traditionally, when people were about to pass away they stayed at home, surrounded by the people close to them," Mr. Itami said.
"The dying person was the central actor. People had this idea of a gallant death, done with deep bravery. People around them accepted the fact that this person is about to pass away and communicated with them. There was a culture of death.
"People now use science," he added. "Science means the defeat of death. They no longer want to look death in the face. Death is worthless, scary, like something you flush from your house. You take this person to the hospital because you don't want this thing to happen in your house."
Mr. Itami is clearly trying to explore what he regards as some of the more penetrating themes of Japanese society, but he did admit to some discouragement. The film industry in Japan is shrinking. His anti-yakuza movie proves the point.
"The film's timing was very good, and the attack on me made people more interested," Mr. Itami said. "But even with all those elements it still only brought in 1.5 billion yen at the box office. This shows you the decline in the Japanese film industry. A few years ago it would have been 5 billion yen.
"When a Japanese wants to dream, they see Western films or animated films, not Japanese films," Mr. Itami said.
He discussed with some resentment the sums Japanese corporations were spending to buy studios in Hollywood, rather than investing the money in the Japanese film industry. "It's easy to buy your software from another company, but it's risky to buy an American company," he said. "I can't imagine they're capable of managing these companies."

SECTION: Section 4; Page 1; Column 3; Week in Review Desk

LENGTH: 1894 words

HEADLINE: Payments to the Retired Loom Ever Larger

BYLINE: By ERIK ECKHOLM

 BODY:
To the elderly they represent a sacred trust, their rightful due. To critics, "welfare for the well-off." To bureaucrats, they are "non-means-tested entitlements." And to politicians they are instruments of political suicide.
But for anyone who imagines curing the budget deficit, the huge, mostly tax-free benefits that flow to the elderly, mainly via Social Security and Medicare, present unavoidable targets -- money monsters that must be tamed. 
 If, as many economists think, the $300 billion deficit should be Public Issue No. 1, then these soaring payments should be Topic A. That does not mean that they will be discussed meaningfully in this Presidential campaign.
The President has ducked the issue, proposing a cap on entitlement programs without saying who should suffer, and exempting Social Security. Bill Clinton, who pilloried Paul Tsongas during the primaries for suggesting mild curbs on payments to the rich, now seems intent on obscuring his own hesitant notion that those payments should be taxed. Even Ross Perot, who called the deficit "the crazy aunt you try to hide in the basement," flinched when asked about reducing benefits for rich retirees, saying that maybe it should be voluntary.
"If current trends continue, conflict between the generations is inevitable," says Mr. Tsongas, who now is trying to mobilize the public to fight the deficit.
Whatever the politics, "entitlement" programs -- those for which spending is determined by formulas set in law rather than by Congressional appropriations -- are engulfing the budget. They now account for more than half of all Federal spending. Four-fifths of them are not "means-tested," or linked to the incomes of recipients. Dominating the scene are the benefits offered to the elderly.
In 1993, Social Security alone is expected to surpass defense as the largest single item in the Federal budget, paying out $302 billion, a fifth of all Federal spending, Congressional Budget Office projections say. Medicare is expected to cost less -- $146 billion, but its costs are climbing much faster.
No one suggests that affluent retirees are entirely responsible for the deficit, or the entire answer. Rather, it is the sense that these programs are taboo that troubles many. "If you talk of imposing pain on those most capable of bearing that pain, or distributing the sacrifice most equitably, I think you can make a fairly strong case that the upper-income elderly should bear a portion of that sacrifice," said Robert D. Reischauer, director of the Congressional Budget Office.
"From a substantive point of view, there's no question" that benefits for the elderly should be trimmed, said Leon E. Panetta, the California Democrat who runs the House Budget Committee. "But from a political point of view, you're challenging what are probably the toughest constituencies out there." Affluent senior citizens, he noted, "make their presence known."
John Rother, chief lobbyist for the American Association of Retired Persons, says his organization accepts in principle the notion of shared sacrifice, but believes that the elderly are being used as scapegoats and that runaway medical costs are the real problem.
"Deficit reduction won't work if all it does is whack at everything and leave the fundamental causes unaddressed," he said.
But reducing benefits for the affluent elderly, however "affluent" is defined, is no budgetary panacea. In 1991, about 20 percent of all Social Security payments went to people with family incomes over $50,000, according to Federal data analyzed by Neil Howe of the National Taxpayers Union Foundation. Five percent went to those with family incomes over $100,000.
These numbers overstate the security of some recipients who have little income but live with better-off relatives. And over half the recipients have family incomes below $30,000 a year, although some hold substantial assets. Many barely scrape by: in 1990, 29 percent of people 65 or older lived with less than 125 percent of the official poverty line for the elderly -- less than $9,881 for a couple or $7,835 for a single person.
For big savings, new taxes or cuts in benefits would have to reach down to retirees in the middle range, even those with incomes around $30,000.
And even then, this is only a partial solution. The most common proposals for increasing taxation of Social Security benefits, for example, would raise an extra $6 billion to $24 billion in 1994. Not peanuts, but hardly enough to vanquish the deficit.
 
Social Security
Doing Well Right Now, But a Disaster Looms
 Viewed in isolation, Social Security is for now filling rather than draining Federal coffers. For the next two decades or so, until the baby boom generation starts to retire, the Social Security taxes collected will be much larger than the benefits paid out.
Whatever its short-term balance, though, any program that accounts for one-fifth of Federal spending presents a plump target -- and, to the extent that its benefits flow to the well-off, what many see as a fair one.
"You don't have to be part of the problem to be part of the solution," Mr. Reischauer said.
Common sense to many economists, but tinkering with Social Security benefits sounds like betrayal to many elderly people. Plaguing the debate is a wide misunderstanding about how Social Security actually works.
Many, perhaps most of the elderly believe they are simply getting back what they are owed, money they paid in during their careers. In fact, the average person retiring today will get benefits equal to what he or she paid in -- including employer matching contributions and interest -- after about 10 years of retirement, according to the Congressional Research Service. Every cent after that is a subsidy paid by current workers.
 
Only a Paper Surplus
 "Social Security has been sold to the public as if it were a saving scheme," said Benjamin Friedman, an economist at Harvard University. "But people who are now retired are receiving back vastly more than anything that corresponds to a market return on what they paid in." In turn, younger workers can expect to get back less than they contributed during their working years.
For now, taxes are far greater than payments. But when the income and payment lines cross -- an event now expected in the year 2015 as baby boomers retire en masse -- there will be no pot of savings on which to draw. Rather, there will be a pile of i.o.u.'s from the Treasury, because the Social Security trust fund is invested in non-marketable Federal securities. The surplus in the trust fund is only a paper one.
Congress at that point will either have to raise Social Security taxes, use other revenues or cut benefits. By 2025, the annual shortfall will total $60 billion in today's dollars, according the the Congressional Research Service.
Perhaps the most commonly voiced proposal for trimming Social Security is to limit the annual Cost of Living Adjustment, which accounts for a large share of program growth. Some suggest holding the increase below the consumer price index each year, or skipping the increase in some years.
The idea appeals to politicians: How much can someone complain about a percentage point or two? In truth, it hurts the poor the most, and holding the increase just 2 percentage points below the price index over 10 years would mean a huge cut of more than 20 percent.
Fairer and more rational, in the view of most economists and budget experts, would be to make more benefits subject to the income tax, which would protect those with low incomes. Since 1983, individuals with incomes over $25,000 and couples over $32,000 have paid income tax on half their Social Security benefits. Still, middle-income elderly people pay less than half the total taxes of younger families with comparable incomes.
"If you could get most members of Congress in a dark closet, they will tell you that we ought to be taxing more of Social Security benefits than we are today," said Henry Aaron, an economist at the Brookings Institution. "Liberals and conservatives, away from the press, say that is the way to do it."
If Social Security were treated the same way as private pensions -- with the funds that were not previously subject to taxes being taxed at the time they are received -- then about 85 percent of benefits, rather than half, would be taxed.
And if the income threshold below which no benefits are taxed were eliminated, $24 billion would be raised in 1994.
 
Medicare
Doctors' Bills Send Costs Out of Control
 While Social Security costs are rising steadily, those for Medicare and Medicaid are soaring.
Medicare is the health insurance program for everyone 65 and older; it is partly paid for by payroll taxes and premiums paid by recipients, but requires a growing subsidy. Medicaid is the smaller but still gigantic program that covers care for the poor and nursing-home care for many of the elderly; its costs are paid from Federal and state taxes.
Controlling medical costs is today's apple pie issue, but there is little agreement about who will take the hit. Will providers be paid less, will patients pay more, or will services be rationed?
Proposing a cap on Medicare without specifying how to allocate the pain, as the White House has done, "is like saying the waves shall not advance," Mr. Aaron said. "You need a large scale reform of health care financing for the nation as a whole."
Whatever is done to contain medical costs, the more sensitive issue for the elderly is whether those who can afford it should pay more. One proposal is to raise the Medicare premiums for insurance that covers services outside the hospital. These premiums now cover only one-fourth of the real cost. Another approach uses the income tax. Taxing the subsidy value of Medicare would distribute the burdens more fairly, and could raise $15 billion a year by 1997.
Mr. Aaron said that benefits for the elderly may not be as untouchable as they seem. In 1983, he chaired the Federal advisory commission that proposed taxing some Social Security benefits for the first time. "The reaction was overwhelmingly and unanimously, 'Are you crazy?' " he recalled. Nonetheless, the 50 percent tax passed, and Congressional heads did not roll.
Mr. Bush, not wanting his lips read again, has steered clear of any proposals that might be construed as tax increases. But Mr. Clinton, when pressed, has clearly left the door open. He treads a careful, and perhaps intentionally obscure, line. On Aug. 10, for example, in response to a question on "CBS This Morning," he said: "I won't dip into Social Security benefits. I think you pay for them, you're entitled to get them. It's a trust . . . "
But he continued: "I do believe that there are people with high incomes . . . with annual incomes well in excess of $100,000 a year, who are getting Social Security benefits way over what they ever paid for. And only half of that income is subject to taxation. So, for upper income people, I think more of that should be subject to taxation."
To get through Congress, any changes in benefits for the elderly will have to be part of a much broader package, spreading the sacrifices widely. Whether the association of retired persons would cooperate, said Mr. Rother, its chief lobbyist, "depends on what else is there."

HEADLINE: THE NATION;
The Democrats as the Devil's Disciples

BYLINE: By TOM WICKER

 BODY:
During the celebrated trials in Salem, Mass., children fell into writhing fits at the sight of an accused witch. That was, of course, 300 years ago, when the fear of witchcraft was common. It couldn't happen again.
 Or could it? 
 So intense are the political passions of 1992 that the Rev. Pat Robertson, a former and possibly future Republican candidate for President, prophesies that children may again be foaming at the mouth as victims of sorcery in modern America.
Mr. Robertson warned that a proposed equal rights amendment to the Iowa state constitution was part of a "feminist agenda" that he described as "a socialist, anti-family political movement that encourages women to leave their husbands, kill their children, practice witchcraft, destroy capitalism and become lesbians."
President Bush, fortunately, has had no such apocalyptic "vision thing" as that. And Mr. Robertson's jeremiad on witchcraft and infanticide in Iowa was not a product of the Republican National Convention in Houston or issued on behalf of the Republican Party. Some Republicans, moreover, professed to be backing away last week from the "religious war" that Patrick Buchanan proclaimed at the Houston convention.
The evangelist-politician was writing, instead, on behalf of the Christian Coalition, an organization that arose from the hot ashes of his 1988 Presidential campaign. Representatives of the Christian Coalition virtually dominated that convention and the platform Mr. Bush accepted; and the Robertson letter fit snugly into a pattern of dire Republican threats that a Democratic victory would abandon the nation to debauchery and deviltry.
Mr. Buchanan, another former and future Republican Presidential candidate, had the delegates up and roaring with his none-too-subtle suggestion that Democrats are sexually weird -- his charge that "20,000 liberals and radicals" had gathered at the Democratic National Convention in New York "dressed up as moderates and centrists in the greatest single exhibition of cross-dressing in American political history."
In the first days of the campaign, Representative Newt Gingrich of Georgia, a purveyor of brimstone if ever there was one, pounced on the sexual theme. The Democrats, he said, had a "Woody Allen plank" in their platform because "they have no concept of families."
The President later picked up Mr. Buchanan's notion of a "religious war." He told a campaign audience that the Democrats had written thousands of words into their platform while leaving out the letters "G-O-D."
The nation was warned, during and after the convention, that Hillary Clinton is not only a radical feminist but favors children suing their parents; that if her husband is elected President, homosexuals and lesbians will take over the Oval Office and "environmental extremists" the economy; and that Mr. Clinton's past marital problems showed that he could not be trusted, even to defend the country from foreign assault, much less to guard it against the alien manipulations of a "media elite."
The tried-and-true crime issue also might have been resurrected when Vice President Quayle made the bizarre suggestion that Governor Clinton is a closet opponent of the death penalty -- even though the Governor had let several executions go forward in Arkansas, including one in 1992. But he once had said that Gov. Mario Cuomo of New York would be a good choice for the Supreme Court. Mr. Cuomo does oppose the death penalty -- and that was all the evidence Dan Quayle needed to suggest that Mr. Clinton was hiding what might really be soft-on-crime views.
Fear led to the "witch hunt" in Salem in 1692. It might be too strong to call the early Republican campaign of 1992 another witch hunt. But these claims and charges were not only directed at the presumed fears of Americans; these fears seemed actually to reflect the profound convictions of many of those who met in Houston.
If so, fear is a theology as well as a strategy. A strategy largely based on fear worked in 1988, but 1992 is different in at least two ways. One is a slumping economy, now perhaps moving into a "triple-dip" recession. In hard times, most Americans expect the Government, personified by the President, to act. Polls show they expect no less of George Bush in 1992 -- at least that he say more than he has about the economic action he plans to take in a second term.
The other difference is a Democratic Presidential candidate far more willing to fight back than Michael Dukakis was for much of 1988. Whatever else may be thought of Bill Clinton, he already has shown himself to be a tough and resilient candidate, able to hand out punishment as well as take it. And he, too, knows how to play the fear game -- witness his claim that Mr. Bush, having broken his word once and raised taxes, cannot be trusted not to do it again.
No doubt influenced by post-convention polls, Mr. Bush's senior political adviser, Charles Black, repudiated Mr. Gingrich's linkage of Woody Allen's troubles to the Democrats. William J. Bennett, the former Secretary of Education, conceded that the election did not pit "the forces of light against the forces of darkness."
But you'd never have known that if you were watching the televised sessions from Houston. And it's early yet. The roots of fear -- religious, sexual, social -- seem to lie deep in American life and particularly among the so-called conservatives who dominated the Houston convention. If coaxed into malodorous growth in the two remaining months of the campaign, the theology of fear may prove more effective than the strategy.

HEADLINE: AFTER THE STORM;
RISING COMPLAINTS OF PRICE GOUGING

BYLINE: By JOSEPH B. TREASTER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MIAMI, Aug. 29

 BODY:
With supplies of ice, basic foods and building materials scarce and many restaurants and gas stations still closed in the aftermath of Hurricane Andrew, state officials said today that complaints of price gouging were pouring in.
"There are people charging $5 for a bag of ice that normally costs $1," said Robert A. Butterworth, Florida's Attorney General. "They are charging $125 for a motel room that was $35 before the storm." 
 By midday Saturday, the Attorney General's office and the Department of Agriculture and Consumer Services had received nearly 2,500 complaints. Callers reported tuna fish at $8 a can, $15 for a gallon of water, gasoline selling at $2 a gallon, candy bars priced at $4, and $200 chain saws selling for $900.
 
'It's Disgusting'
 "It's disgusting," said Toni Riordan, a spokeswoman for Florida's Department of Community Affairs, which is coordinating the state's response to the disaster.
Mr. Butterworth said he believed that most businesses that have reopened had not raised their prices. He also said many people who have set up roadside businesses in the heart of the disaster zone were selling things at fair prices.
One of those selling items on the road was Jon Kyseth, who owns a used car lot in Charleston, S.C. He had set up shop along a devastated stretch of highway framed by downed power lines and shredded trees. His car is his warehouse, he said, and the car's hood his showcase for flashlights, chain saws, work gloves and bottles of propane gas.
"I went through Hurricane Hugo," Mr. Kyseth said, "so I know people need these things. I drove down here thinking I could make some money but also help these people, give them fair prices and make sure they don't get gouged the way we did in Charleston."
 
Comparing Gougers to Looters
 Mr. Butterworth, who has nearly 40 investigators and lawyers working on the complaints, said, "As far I'm concerned, there's no difference between a looter and a person whose gouging victims of this disaster."
Mr. Butterworth said violaters of Florida's Deceptive and Unfair Trade Practices Act face fines of up to $10,000. He said officials of Dade County, where the worst of the hurricane damage occurred, have made price gouging a misdemeanor punishable by a $500 fine and up to 60 days in jail. The state is intervening, aids to Mr. Butterworth said, because the economy has been disrupted and some merchants have gained an unfair advantage.
Mr. Butterworth's office said more than 75 subpoenas for sales records have been issued so far, including seven to major national companies. No arrests have been made yet.
Most of the business people accused by customers and investigators of price gouging have tried to shift responsibility for the higher prices.
"The retailers tell us it's the wholesalers and the wholesalers say it's the retailers," said Mona Fandel, the Attorney General's Chief of Consumer Affairs. "Everybody blames everybody else. That's why we're issuing subpoenas."
Many business people and those selling items on the roadside insisted they were doing a public service, going out of their way to provide critically needed supplies and equipment. Many of their customers agreed.
In the heavily damaged town of Naranja, Ed McKelvey, the vice president of the M.C.R. Lumber Company, was scrunched down on an office chair in a patch of drying mud, selling roofing paper and nails as fast as he could make change.
Tar paper was selling for $15 a roll, compared with $8 before the storm. "The wholesalers shot the price up," Mr. McKelvey said. "We're adding 50 cents or $1 to the price. We're trying to make as much available to the community as we can. Believe me, for a dollar a roll I don't need to be out here."
Hilda Olvera, who manages a McDonald's Restaurant, was buying tar paper for the first time. She had no idea what it ought to cost. "It seems reasonable to me," she said as a young man filled her trunk.
Many of those selling items on the roadside said they were selling at or below standard prices.

LENGTH: 1799 words

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: Political Memo;
A Contest of 2 Generations, Molded by 2 Different Wars

BYLINE: By MICHAEL KELLY, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 28

 BODY:
With both nominating conventions completed and the fall race begun, the 1992 election has emerged as a generational contest of unusual intensity and scope, a choice between two men of two very different epochs, shaped by two very different wars.
The 68-year-old George Bush may be the last President of the age of World War II, the war in which every President since Harry S. Truman has served, excepting Jimmy Carter, who was a midshipman at the United States Naval Academy during the war. The 46-year-old Bill Clinton, born a year after that war ended, is the first Presidential nominee of the age of the Vietnam war.
 
Peril for Both Candidates
 Over this week and last, the old, unresolved differences between the time of America's good war and that of its failed war have begun to occupy a place at the center of the 1992 race, informing debates on matters ranging from party ideology to personal morals. 
 It is, the events of this week showed, a discussion that holds peril for both sides.
On Tuesday, Mr. Clinton had to stand before the American Legion and offer, as he put it, "one final statement" on how he had come to not go to Vietnam. His incomplete explanation left Republicans gleefully convinced that they have in this issue the perfect means by which to portray the Democrat as a closet hippie and a dishonest man.
 
Cooling on 'Family Values'
 But the "family values" debate that is rooted in these generational politics, and that the Republicans began with a great, angry to-do in their nominating convention in Houston, appeared by this week to have very nearly backfired, repelling as many voters as it had reached. By midweek, senior Bush Administration and campaign officials had called for a cooling of the hot-button talk.
The differences between the 1940's and the 1960's, and between Mr. Clinton's age and Mr. Bush's, shape the way each candidate depicts the choice between them.
In the choice as portrayed by the Democrats, Mr. Clinton is John F. Kennedy, the young, vigorous candidate poised to lead the country into an exciting (but not radical) era of growth and change, a time of bold experiments in leadership. Mr. Bush, in this picture, is an old, stuck-in-his-ways, golf-playing duffer, incapable of seizing the historical moment. "Change" is the keyword of Mr. Clinton's campaign.
In the choice as portrayed by the Republicans, Mr. Bush is Dwight D. Eisenhower, the sober, sensible steward, shaped by the ways of the old, good days, and imbued with the half-mythic values of those days. He does not promise so much to induce change as to manage it, and he does not promise to make things better as much as he warns that his opponent will make them a great deal worse. Mr. Clinton, in this view, does not represent the promise of the early 1960's but the traumas of the decade's later years; he is a secret radical hiding behind the mask of moderatism. "Trust" is Mr. Bush's answer to Mr. Clinton's "change."
Nowhere is the generational contrast between Mr. Bush and Mr. Clinton sharper -- and a cause of greater hope to Republican strategists -- than in the matter of who did what in each generation's war. Mr. Bush, acting in a manner fairly typical of the men of his class and time, was an officer and gentleman-hero of his war; Mr. Clinton, acting in a manner fairly typical of the men of his class and time, was a protester and an avoider of his.
 
Making Their Cases
 On Tuesday, both men stood before the audience at an American Legion convention and made cases for themselves that centered on just this matter.
"You know that I never served in the military," Mr. Clinton told the veterans. "You know that I opposed the war in Vietnam. But I want you to know this: I was never against the heroic men who served in that war. I honored their service then, and I honor it still."
He went on: "If any of you here choose to vote against me because of what happened 23 years ago, that is your right as an American citizen, and I respect it. But I hope that you will cast your vote looking toward the future rather than remaining fixed to the problems of the past."
Mr. Bush began by casting back 20 years before 1960 to, as he put it, "the world we knew," a phrase that evoked remembered youth and old songs. "Fifty years ago this summer, America is at war," he said. "At the age of 18, I went off to fight. Like many of you, I was scared, but I was willing. And I was young, but I was ready. And like so many of you, I had barely lived when I began to watch men die."
 
Clinton Stressed the Theme
 The generational theme began with Mr. Clinton; it has been present since the inception of his campaign, when he spoke in October 1991 of an America "on the threshold of a new era, a new millennium," and used the word "generation" four times in the first six paragraphs.
At the Democratic convention in New York last month, Mr. Clinton imitated Mr. Kennedy in a well-choreographed appearance in the convention hall the night before his acceptance speech, and the biographical movie that preceded Mr. Clinton's speech featured President Kennedy, in a moment heavy with torch-passing symbolism, shaking hands with an awe-struck 16-year-old Bill Clinton.
In their convention, and in the week since, the Republicans have sought to turn this argument for generational change sharply against Mr. Clinton, by reminding voters that he is not really of Mr. Kennedy's generation (Mr. Kennedy shared Mr. Bush's war, not Mr. Clinton's) but Abbie Hoffman's, and to link the Democrat with a decade remembered unhappily by many as a time when liberalism was turned into radicalism.
In a convention speech that opened this effort, Marilyn Quayle said: "Much has been said lately about the need in this country for a new generation of leadership; that the moment has come for a couple of baby boomers to take the helm of this great and complex nation; that the time has come for generational change."
She then said, with Mr. Clinton clearly in mind: "Not everyone joined the counterculture, not everyone demonstrated, dropped out, took drugs, joined in the sexual revolution or dodged the draft. Not everyone concluded that American society was so bad that it had to be radically remade by social revolution."
 
Broadening the Attack
 Other speakers broadened the generational attack. Hillary Clinton, whom friends at Yale recall as a committed leftist, and who has left a trail of legal writings and involvement with advocacy groups that place her clearly closer to the left edge of liberalism than her husband seems to be, was brought into play as a sort of stand-in for Mr. Clinton. The suggestion in the Republican strategy was that she represents the views Mr. Clinton secretly holds, too, but will not admit.
The "family values" assault was generational, too, contrasting the warm appeal of the idealized 1950's family as embodied in "Father Knows Best" with the equally idealized depiction of single motherhood in today's "Murphy Brown."
This approach, on its face, would seem to offer promise for Mr. Bush and peril to Mr. Clinton. It is, after all, rooted in fact. Mr. Clinton is a product of his generation. He did demonstrate against the war, he has (sort of) acknowledged smoking marijuana, and he has been the subject of much sexual gossip. Most important, he did avoid the draft during the Vietnam war.
 
Clinton's Vulnerability
 This last issue, which Republican strategists plan to make much, illuminates the vulnerability Mr. Clinton faces in matters related to his generation.
In his efforts to avoid Vietnam, Mr. Clinton first worked his way through academic deferments. When these were no longer available, and after he had received an induction notice while attending Oxford as a Rhodes Scholar, he promised to join the Reserve Officers Training Corps at the University of Arkansas. In the fall of 1969, after President Richard M. Nixon had announced that draft-eligible students like Mr. Clinton could finish the school year before induction, and proposed a lottery system to select inductees, Mr. Clinton put himself forward for the draft. He drew a high number in the lottery and was never drafted.
Mr. Clinton has said repeatedly that he exposed himself, however briefly, to the draft because he felt it was the honorable thing to do. But on Dec. 3, 1969, two days after the lottery showed he was safe, Mr. Clinton wrote an extraordinary letter to the Army colonel who had supervised the R.O.T.C. program at the University of Arkansas.
In the letter, he thanked the colonel for "saving me from the draft," acknowledged that he had misled the colonel about his intentions and views on the war and said that he had "decided to accept the draft in spite of my beliefs for one reason: to maintain my political viability within the system."
 
Changing Accounts
 Moreover, the statements Mr. Clinton made about his wartime actions when the subject first came up this year do not hang together well. He was quoted in news reports in the spring as saying he had never received an induction notice, and later as saying he was not sure. In his remarks to the American Legion, he acknowledged having received the induction notice before signing up for the R.O.T.C.
Speaking to reporters the day after Mr. Clinton spoke to the American Legion, Charles Black, a senior political adviser to the Bush campaign, identified this area as "an issue of huge vulnerability" that, he cheerfully admitted, he was "trying to stir up interest in."
Mr. Black made it clear that the Republicans regard Mr. Clinton as vulnerable on this issue not because he did not fight, but because Mr. Clinton's maneuverings around the draft and his varied explanations of those maneuverings lend resonance to the core Republican assertion, that he is misrepresenting his generational self, hiding from the voters what he really stands for. "He has to put this draft issue to rest and the credibility issues that surround it, and he certainly didn't do it yesterday," he said.
 
Possible Strategy Weakness
 But the appearances by Mr. Clinton and Mr. Bush before the veterans also illustrates what may be a great weakness in the Republican strategy. Appearing before a group of men who could be expected to be receptive to the Republican's continuing attack, Mr. Clinton received a notably warm reception -- about as warm as that accorded Mr. Bush.
Polls taken in the days since the convention have found a lack of interest among voters in debating such things as the rights of homosexuals and the ways of radical feminists. What the voters did seem to be interested in hearing about was the economy, health care and crime. It may be that most people are finally tired of fighting about who did what in the cultural wars.

PUB-DATE: August 30, 1992

SECTION: Section 1; Page 24; Column 1; National Desk

LENGTH: 929 words

HEADLINE: AFTER THE STORM;
For Red Cross, the Chance To Help and Raise Money

BYLINE: By FELICITY BARRINGER, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 29

 BODY:
Overwhelmed by the scope of Hurricane Andrew's destruction in South Florida and Louisiana, the American Red Cross has begun a national appeal for money to support its relief effort.
Some of the appeal's immediate success -- million-dollar gifts from corporations and foundations -- reflect the dual role of disasters in the Red Cross's existence in highlighting the group's mission and providing a backdrop for fund-raising efforts. This week, for instance, Elizabeth Dole, the president of the American Red Cross, was soliciting money for both the immediate needs of South Florida and the longer-term needs of the Red Cross's disaster relief program. 
 At the same time, emergency officials are publicly grumbling about a Red Cross effort that, they say, plays better in the press releases from national headquarters than it does in the disaster areas.
The criticisms, from people like Kate Hale at the Emergency Operations Center in Dade County, often try to distinguish between the local volunteers, some of whom have worked tirelessly to supply shelter, food and water for the newly homeless, and the national organization.
After an appearance Wednesday night with Mrs. Dole on the CNN program "Larry King Live," Ms. Hale complained to reporters in Miami that the Red Cross chief was exaggerating the number of shelters in operation.
 
Housing 85,000
 But it is hard this early to quantify what the nation's best-known relief organization has done to help the victims of Hurricane Andrew. Of the more than 200 shelters opened by Red Cross around the state in Florida on Sunday, housing at least 85,000 people as Hurricane Andrew neared the coast, 14 remained open Friday, housing 2,071 people, the group said.
The Red Cross is also a conduit for the thousands of volunteers it has on standby in case of disasters. Like those in Dade County who were transformed by the hurricane this week into shelter directors, they are prepared to abandon their jobs until the crisis passes.
"It's a massive responsibility to be ready for a disaster anywhere on American soil," said Mrs. Dole, the former Labor Secretary who has led the Red Cross since 1991, in an interview on Tuesday in Miami. She spoke about her responsibilities: raising money for disaster relief, and allocating it to get the most assistance possible to the greatest number of people.
' "In California alone in the last three years we've had earthquakes, the Oakland fire and the riots in Los Angeles," she said. "Fund-raising has become a big part of what I'm doing."
While a disaster like Hurricane Andrew is both a chance to meet human needs and an instant showcase for the Red Cross's humanitarian mission and need for donations, local government relief agencies reap nothing from disaster but big problems and bigger bills, a situation that can produce frictions.
Such was the situation in the aftermath of the 1989 Loma Prieta earthquake in California. San Francisco Mayor Art Agnos and other officials who had helped raise contributions from local businesses were furious to see some of the $52.2 million earmarked for the earthquake-ravaged areas being appropriated for use elsewhere. The Red Cross later agreed to use the money in the area.
Fund-raising is central to the Red Cross's existence. Private donations are one of the most important sources of funding for the Red Cross. Most of these come regularly from the network of 2,100 local United Ways, which, on average, provide 70 percent of the funding of local Red Cross chapters.
Nationally, the Red Cross and its local chapters have a budget of $1.4 billion. About $800 million is raised and spent in its blood donation program, which is kept separate from the rest of the group's operations, said John Campbell, the vice president for finance at the American Red Cross.
About $150 million of the remaining $600 million goes to national headquarters to pay for donations to international relief efforts, services to the armed forces (such as informing military personnel of births and deaths in their families), management and disaster services. More than 2,600 local chapters use the remaining $450 million to provide services in their communities.
However the money is distributed, the Red Cross said, their resources to meet human needs has been strained by a series of disasters on American soil since Hurricane Hugo. "Just like Hurricane Hugo and the California earthquake, we don't have the funds to fund Andrew," said Mr. Campbell. "We have to fund-raise."
The timing may not be right. Local United Ways chapters are nervous over whether their fund-raising efforts will be hurt by a recent scandal over lavish perquisites for officials in the national office. The economy may also have an effect.
 
Falling Short
 In fiscal year 1990, Hurricane Hugo and the Loma Prieta earthquake in northern California were the big-ticket items in a period when the American Red Cross spent a record $224.2 million on disaster relief. But publicity about the disasters helped fill the coffers of the national headquarters' Disaster Relief Fund, a $30 million reserve for times when the money budgeted for disaster relief fall short.
Since then, however, the number of disasters grew while their visibility decreased. The Red Cross spent $184.4 million in fiscal 1991, which ran from July 1990 through June 1991, for relief of floods in Texas, mudslides in Puerto Rico and floods in American Samoa, without the publicity and contributions produced by a hurricane or earthquake. The disaster relief reserve was depleted.

HEADLINE: AFTER THE STORM;
Ailing Louisiana Now Has to Recover From Storm

BYLINE: By ROBERTO SURO, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: CHARENTON, La., Aug. 28

 BODY:
Driven from their ruined homes -- three dark, damp cabins with freshly punctured roofs -- Leroy Hose, his family and his neighbors sat outside, surveying another calamity in the fields all around them.
Wilting under the heavy heat that follows a hurricane, a sugar cane crop lay all twisted, flailed and muddy. 
 Teen-age girls took turns curling each other's hair while Mr. Hose listened to an old blues song on a tape recorder running off his car battery. The crop does not belong to them. It belongs to the same grower who owns the old, sagging cabins and pays the men $4.80 an hour to work the fields.
 
Post-Hurricane Arithmetic
 Mr. Hose has already calculated the trickle-down economics of a natural disaster. "If Uncle Sam doesn't take care of the boss, the boss doesn't get a profit," he said. "If there's no profit, there's no work, and if I don't work, the kids don't eat."
All along the swath Hurricane Andrew cut through southern Louisiana, people are doing their own arithmetic. Instead of dealing with manifest catastrophes like a house or business in pieces, most of the storm's victims in Louisiana are agonizing over uncertainties, not knowing how quickly their homes will be repaired or how long their livelihoods will suffer.
And for some there are more immediate needs.
Sunlight filters through the rafters of Mr. Hose's cabin. Past a stack of mattresses beginning to mildew and a little pile of stuffed animals bloated with water, the ramshackle kitchen holds a valued cache: a few cans of pork and beans.
"We're getting real short on supplies right now," he said, "I heard help is coming, but how am I going to find it?"
 
Tens of Thousands Displaced
 Unknown to him, at least two relief kitchens were operating in towns less than 20 miles away, but down roads choked with tree limbs and power lines.
In Florida, relief officials have been overwhelmed by the hundreds of thousands of victims packed into areas of concentrated devastation. In Louisiana, where 25,000 to 30,000 people were displaced by the storm, according to Gov. Edwin W. Edwards, the challenge has been to reach victims scattered from the suburbs of New Orleans and Baton Rouge down through tiny, remote settlements deep in the coastal bayou country.
So far, relatively few complaints have been heard here about the overall size or speed of the relief effort, but there is growing impatience with the way it is being distributed.
"I know there are generators up the road, and there are some down the road," said Gus Lipari, Mayor of the small town of Patterson. "If I could get every generator that's been promised to me by one agency or another, I'd light up the state, but I haven't seen one of them."
Like many other communities, Patterson survived the initial storm damage almost solely with hometown efforts. The water was kept flowing with equipment provided by local businesses, and the streets are being cleared by volunteer crews who are fed by a local grocery.
But four days after the storm, worries about the future are beginning to set in. "By now I can see us getting past the emergency stage," said Mayor Lipari. "But I have a hard time seeing what kind of economy we're going to be left with."
 
Economy Already Battered
 Once the heart of a thriving energy industry, the area of south-central Louisiana hardest hit by the storm lost its economic glow when the price of oil fell in the 1980's. More recently it has suffered from a sharp drop in drilling.
This summer, hopes sprang from two directions. A bountiful oyster harvest was under way, and a bumper sugar cane crop was in the fields.
Now oystering has been suspended while state officials determine if the beds have been polluted, and many parts of the seafood industry face severe property damage. Governor Edwards has calculated that half the sugar crop was destroyed, for a loss of $200 million to an industry that has yet to recover from a freeze in 1989.
As Hurricane Andrew swept across the sugar belt, the counterclockwise circulation of its winds left the cane twisted and tangled.
"We like it standing up nice and straight," said Herman Louviere, who farms 2,200 acres of sugar near Jeanerette. "Now we've got to fight it all the way for the harvesting and the planting."
When the hurricane struck, farmers had just begun the planting process: taking cuttings from the current crop that will be laid out in furrowed fields to produce crops for the future. The twisted cane will not produce good cuttings, and when the current crop is harvested, probably in October, the tangled canes will jam harvesting machines and leave a great deal of cane in the fields as waste.
State Senator M. J. Foster, scion of an old land-owning family, captured the bleak mood of the hurricane's victims with a dire prediction. "This could be the end of the Louisiana sugar industry," he said, "and if the sugar goes, I don't know what we'll have left."
He stood on the portico of a fine Greek Revival plantation house built in 1837. With walls 16-inches thick, the mansion had survived with minor damaged, but there was ruin in the gardens for which Oaklawn Manor was named. Tourists by the bus loads have come to see the 40-acre grove of ancient live oaks, and now the long twisting limbs sculptured by nature over centuries lie on the ground in an impenetrable thicket of dead wood.
Mr. Foster ran through a chronology of recent calamities: busts, freezes and floods and now Hurricane Andrew.
"You can only take so much bad luck," he said.

HEADLINE: Entrepreneurs in China Attain the Age of Greed

BYLINE: By NICHOLAS D. KRISTOF, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: SHANGHAI

 BODY:
James Shen is a tall, well-dressed 29-year-old who for the last five years has lived the Chinese dream: He has worked abroad for a Western company, earning a Western salary.
So when Mr. Shen, who sells flame-retardant chemicals for a British company, returned to his native Shanghai this month to visit friends, he had a rude surprise. 
 "I'd worked abroad, so I figured I was rich," he said over breakfast in the $105-a-night hotel where he is staying. "But I came back and discovered that I was poor. There are so many rich people here now, so many millionaires."
A dozen years of some of the highest economic growth rates in the world have nurtured the re-emergence in China of the super-rich, a class that the Chinese masses used to struggle against and now struggle to join. The average monthly salary in China may be less than $35 at the official rate of exchange, but for anyone who thinks of this as a nation of impoverished peasants wading in rice paddies, it is a shock to inspect the gleaming counters of Shanghai's Glorious Clock and Watch Shop.
"With consumer standards rising, we're planning to sell Rolexes soon," said Shi Guanghui, the middle-aged assistant manager of the shop, as he sneaked a disapproving peek at an American visitor's plastic digital watch. "A lot of other shops already have Rolexes."
Mr. Shi said he is already doing a brisk business selling Rado watches from Switzerland that cost as much as $3,000. The Rolexes will cost up to $20,000 each, and Mr. Shi said there would definitely be customers.
A potential customer is Su Yaopeng, who says he is in the real estate business. Mr. Su is doing well: last month he paid $55,000 in an auction for Shanghai license plate Z0518. It is regarded as an auspicious number because it sounds vaguely like the words for "make me rich."
 
No Longer Lean, Just Mean
 Meeting the needs of a changing society, the Chinese language has in the last few years come up with new expressions, "dakuan" and "kuanye," which are equivalent to "fat cats." The concept did not exist in more orthodox Communist days when everyone was supposed to be egalitarian -- and lean.
The dakuan can be found shopping in the boutiques of Nanjing Road, buying Italian suits or French cosmetics despite the huge tariffs that can make lipstick cost up to $50. Every now and then, they take a call on their cellular telephones and make a note to themselves with European fountain pens.
One place to spot the dakuan is in the new brokerage houses around Shanghai, where the elite come to play the stock market. The big players get special rooms where they can relax and watch trading on computer screens as they issue buy and sell orders.
At first, the brokers extended this privilege to anyone with more than $35,000 invested. But there were too many of those, so now an investor must have a stake of nearly $100,000 to get special treatment.
To be sure, most Chinese are still peasants who have never been to Shanghai and are lucky if they have electricity. The average peasant made $132 last year, and some can afford meat only once or twice a year. Yet at the same time, the ranks of the dakuan are growing very rapidly: about four million families have annual incomes of $5,500 or more, and there are 10,000 private cars around the country.
Typical of Shanghai's new dakuan is Gu Desheng, a tall 37-year-old with a high school education. Until 1984 he was a driver for a food company, earning about $10 a month, but then he quit and decided to do business on his own.
After making and selling toys for a few years, Mr. Gu amassed a bit of capital. He traded his apartment for one on the ground floor and last year turned it into an elegant restaurant -- the "We Happy Restaurant," a sign at the entrance proclaims in English, reflecting the fashion for English names.
Mr. Gu and his wife and child live in a tiny loft they built over the restaurant, and they put $20,000 in savings into refurbishing the restaurant. It is now equipped with stereo music, air-conditioning, a bar and a set of aquariums near the door to keep fish and snakes alive and squirming until customers order them for dinner.
With a monthly profit of $600 or more, and growing, Mr. Gu is now considering several other locations for additional restaurants.
"I've never heard of anyone in Shanghai not making money on a restaurant," Mr. Gu said. "Some people quit because they find it a lot of work, but nobody goes bankrupt."
 
Putting Everything on the Tab
 Some of the cash that the dakuan throw around is "heiqian" -- black money -- obtained through illegal means. Bribery, prostitution and smuggling are booming industries in coastal China. There is a black market in Beijing in fake receipts for food and lodging so officials can pad their expense accounts.
Still, most of the money is either honestly obtained or else is huiqian -- gray money -- from practices of uncertain legality. For example, an engineer recently developed a product using the premises of his employer, a state-owned company, and then sold it to a semi-private factory. He was rewarded with a new apartment and nearly $8,000.
"The wealth these days mostly isn't heiqian; it comes principally from business," said Wei Wenyuan, executive director of the Shanghai Stock Exchange. "The Chinese economy isn't very well developed, so people can go into sales and distribution and make money by taking advantage of price differences."
A couple of dozen students at Qinghua University are said to have earned $20,000 or more as sales representatives. One Beijing University senior, Huang Lin, earned enough by selling photocopiers that he reportedly invited some of his classmates out for an evening of karaoke singing -- and unflinchingly paid the $650 bill.
"Some kids have relatives who work in a factory and can arrange huge purchase orders," said a Shanghai art student. "Then the kids can sell a lot and make a mint."
One reason why private enterprise in China often prospers is that the competition comes largely from state-owned companies that are legendary for their sloth and discourteousness. State restaurants, for example, tend to offer cold, overpriced food served by rude waiters.
Zhang Fugen, a 55-year-old entrepreneur wearing a gold Bulova watch, ran a bustling restaurant out of his home by catering to people's tastes -- if they spoke with a Cantonese accent, he served food lightly stir-fried; if they spoke with a Sichuan accent, he added peppers. Mr. Zhang, who has since replaced the restaurant with a craft shop, says he has savings of $40,000.
"I'd say my life has improved a hundred times over," he said. 'Aside from my income going up, I've sent my two sons to study in Japan, and now they've got good jobs there as well."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Zhang Fugen, now able to relax, has converted his bustling Shanghai restaurant into a craft shop. Mr. Zhang made his money in the restaurant by matching dinner orders to his customers' regional accents. (Nicholas Kristof for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Iraq Softens Anger Over Air Quarantine in South

BYLINE: By WILLIAM E. SCHMIDT, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: CAIRO, Aug. 29

 BODY:
Iraq tempered its angry response to American air patrols today and made what seemed to be an appeal for sympathy, conceding that the plan by the United States and its allies to take control of the skies in the south would further weaken the country.
The Government-run newspaper Al Jumhouriya said in an editorial that the United States and its allies had waged a long campaign to weaken Iraq through "wars, economic sanctions and partition plans." 
 The latest strategy, the newspaper said, threatens to put Iraq "in its weakest state" by creating "a southern pocket to dismember Iraq."
Since allied warplanes took to the air over southern Iraq on Thursday, flying reconnaissance patrols and vowing to strike against any Iraqi fighter south of the 32d parallel, Baghdad's air force has made no move to challenge the so-called no-fly zone.
 
Protecting Shiite Rebels
 The United States, Britain and France say the zone is needed to protect Shiite Muslim rebels in the south from assaults by Iraqi warplanes and helicopter gunships.
Though commentary was subdued today, Al Jumhouriya and other Baghdad newspapers insisted that the Iraqi people remained united behind President Saddam Hussein and would never submit to what is described here as a "colonialist-Zionist plot" to break up the country.
A senior official in Cairo said the Iraqi arguments appeared to be addressed mainly to Arab governments that have raised doubts about the allied strategy and refused to endorse it.
Kuwait has publicly supported the plan, though other Arab Gulf states, like Saudi Arabia, have provided extensive logistical support. But other governments, including Egypt, Syria and a number of Gulf states, have expressed concern that a weakened and unstable Iraq could break up and foster instability in the region.
"Iraq knows there is no point in arguing with the coalition, so they are trying to muster more sympathy and support among the Arabs, especially in the gulf," said the senior official here, a foreign affairs specialist.
 
Fears Over Iran
 There is particular concern that Iran's fundamentalist Shiite Government, which Arab governments regard as hostile, will exploit a power vacuum in southern Iraq.
The Iraqis, who have recently used ground forces as well as planes and helicopters to attack the Shiites, have said that they are pursuing rebels and Iranian infiltrators who are trying to overthrow Mr. Hussein's Government.
Unconfirmed reports this week from Iraqi dissident groups suggest that Baghdad may be using its ground forces to control the Shiites while it waits for the American-led allies to relax or end their air patrols.
In Beirut, an official of an Iraqi dissident group said today that the Baghdad Government had rounded up and arrested thousands of Shiite Muslims in the days before the United States and its allies began to enforce the ban on Iraqi flights south of the 32d parallel.
Sheik Abu Maythem al-Saghir, an official of a group that calls itself the Supreme Council of the Islamic Revolution in Iraq, estimated that 10,000 people were arrested earlier this week, mostly at random, in the cities of Basra, Amara, Nasiriya, Diwaniya and Najaf. There was no way to confirm that report independently.
The official Iraqi News Agency reported later that the Cabinet was briefed today in Baghdad on the air operation. The Cabinet vowed to treat the operation "with the disdain it deserves and meanwhile to continue the political and mobilizing activity inside and outside Iraq," the report said.
