TALES OF ROUNDUP IN NORTH
 GUSHTEPE, Iraq, Aug. 29 (AP) -- Drivers in northern Iraq said today that Iraqi security forces had rounded up a large number of Shiites in the region this week and taken them away on trucks as Western allies prepared to begin air patrols in the south.
The reports could not be independently confirmed. The roundup was described by drivers carrying goods and people between Kirkuk, a major Iraqi oil city, and the Kurdish-dominated region to the north. They were interviewed at a Kurdish guerrilla checkpoint near this village, just south of the Kurdish city of Erbil.
All of the drivers spoke on condition of anonymity, saying they feared retaliation by the Baghdad Government.
The first allegations that Shiites had been seized in the north were made on Friday by Kurds interviewed by a London-based television station run by opponents of President Saddam Hussein.
Iraq drew Shiites to Kirkuk from southern Iraq in the 1980's with promises of land and money to "Arabize" the city, which has a mainly Turkmen and Kurdish population.
Iraq's Shiites, who make up 55 percent of the country's 17.5 million people, are concentrated in the south, where Mr. Hussein is trying to suppress a rebel movement. Reports on Friday said security forces, including the elite Republican Guard corps, took Shiites to a military camp at Tob Zawa, just outside Kirkuk, last week. But the latest reports said the roundups took place within the last three or four days.
"The reports, if true, are extremely alarming," said Eric Goldstein of the New York-based human rights group Middle East Watch. He said on Friday that thousands of Kurds who were taken to Iraqi barracks at Tob Zawa during Mr. Hussein's 1987-88 campaign against the Kurds later disappeared.
Today, one driver said he saw 40 to 50 Shiite families removed by truck from Kirkuk. Another driver said he saw about 20 busloads of people whom he believed to be Shiites taken to the village of Mahmoor, about 30 miles southwest of Erbil.

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: The Democrats;
Clinton Calls for an Inquiry Into Delays in Storm Relief

BYLINE: By B. DRUMMOND AYRES Jr.

DATELINE: LITTLE ROCK, Ark., Aug. 29

 BODY:
Gov. Bill Clinton said today that an effort should be made to "look into" why problems have plagued the hurricane disaster-relief effort in Florida and Louisiana.
But he pulled up short of blaming President Bush for any of the troubles, saying he did not want to politicize the issue in an election and that any investigation should be carried out in "as nonpolitical a way as possible." 
 "I do believe an important job of a President is the management of disasters," the Democratic nominee told reporters here at an appearance before a wildlife group. "I think they need to look into it. But I don't want to get into assessing the blame there because I don't know what the facts were. We ought to look into how it can be changed for the future."
The Bush Administration has been hit by complaints over the past several days that it moved ineptly and too slowly to aid the victims of Hurricane Andrew, particularly in Florida.
Today, the Bush campaign said that Mr. Clinton, despite his claims to the contrary, was jumping on criticisms of the disaster relief for political advantage. "He's trying to exploit what is a terrible situation for political gain," said Torie Clarke, a campaign spokeswoman. "He should be ashamed of himself."
 
President Defends Response
 Yesterday, worried that the complaints about the relief effort might further erode his already weak position in the campaign, the President strongly defended his Administration's relief efforts, asserting that Federal disaster teams had responded "within a few hours."
"When we were asked to move," he said, "we moved these massive numbers of forces. But I'm not going to participate in the blame game."
While Mr. Clinton said he did not want to politicize the hurricane relief issue or blame the effort's shortcomings on President Bush, he did not hesitate to attack his opponent on a number of other issues today.
He asserted again this morning, as he had yesterday, that the Bush campaign was deliberately distorting his tax record. But unlike yesterday, he did not charge that his opponent's attacks called into question the "character" of the Bush campaign and constituted "raw cynicism."
The Bush campaign was quick to return the fire in what has become perhaps the most contentious and acerbic feud of the 1992 campaign.
Ms. Clarke, charged that the Governor had "sunk to new lows."
"Bill Clinton and his campaign," she added, "have resorted to name-calling and flat-out lying in an effort to distract the American voters from his tax-increase record and agenda."
 
Finger-Pointing Over Taxes
 The Bush campaign contends that Mr. Clinton has raised taxes and various fees 128 times since he became Governor. Clinton aides say the correct figure is 59. They also say that by their calculations the President not only broke his "read my lips" promise and imposed a tax increase as part of the budget compromise but also imposed other new taxes and fees at least 326 times.
Bush campaign officials said today they could not comment on the Clinton campaign figure of 327 increases but were confident once they added up the various increases, the true figure would be considerably less. They said they would make the figure available next week.
In his speech to the Arkansas Wildlife Federation, Mr. Clinton accused the President of neglecting the environment by putting economic needs before human needs.
And in a second speech today, to wildly cheering partisans attending the biennial Arkansas State Democratic Convention here, he said he was "desperately, desperately" trying to make the 1992 race a positive campaign but was constantly being thwarted by negative campaigning by the President.
"The present political climate has run us down," he said. "It has made us less than we ought to be. Every time we want to reach out or reach up, it diverts us."
Mr. Clinton asserted that in the end, the 1992 contest would come down to "a race of hope against fear."
"It's going to be about the courage to seize the future or whether we just stand with what we've got for four more years and hope we decline more slowly," he said. "I'm tired of seeing children grow up with the prospect of doing worse than their parents."
Actually, Mr. Clinton argued, the future could hardly be brighter in many ways, if only the nation would look beyond despair toward good things that have happened recently. He cited the end of the cold war, the decline of Communism and the tremendously diminished prospect of nuclear war between super powers.
"Just think," he said. "When we tell about all that, our grandchildren won't even know what we're talking about."
The Governor said that if the nation could mesh the interests and efforts of labor, business, education and government, it would be able to "handle" its opportunities.
"There's nothing we can't do," he fairly shouted.
Mr. Clinton's address to the Democratic gathering was something of a nostalgic homecoming for him. He was visibly moved by the tumultuous cheer that greeted his arrival.
"It brought tears to my eyes," he said when quiet returned to the auditorium where he was speaking. "Most of what I know I learned from you. I have done my best to honor the traditions that I learned here in this campaign for President."

HEADLINE: AFTER THE STORM;
Florida Relief Effort Prefers Money to Goods

 BODY:
In the wake of Hurricane Andrew, southern Florida has been swept by another kind of deluge: cars, trucks and vans packed with goods for the storm victims and with people volunteering their services.
Fraternity brothers from South Carolina drove trucks full of canned goods to the Miami area. A paper company sent 20 tons of toilet paper, tissues and paper towels. A beer company sent aluminum cans full of water. 
 But relief organizers in Florida and in national headquarters say that grateful as they are for the flood of donations, they would rather have cash donations. They say the infusion of money is a boon to the hard-hit economy, and transportation and storage costs often make the donations of goods impractical.
"People hear that they have not eaten in a couple of days and want to send packages of food," said Larry Mackler, a spokesman for the Red Cross in New York. "But that is a costly procedure, you have to think of storing and shipping."
 
Climate of Confusion
 The steady stream of deliveries continues, however, in a climate of confusion. Rae Patterson, an assistant director of the Seventh-Day Adventists' charity effort, said that on some days the organization has been asked to send faxes authorizing donors to make deliveries in order to get past the Florida Highway Patrol, which is stopping many trucks entering the state because of packed highways.
"But then we heard that they don't need a fax," she went on.
Mrs. Patterson said her organization was using a church in Fort Lauderdale to insure that every truck arriving has a site from which to distribute goods with volunteers ready to unload the trucks. A volunteer worker said trucks came through yesterday carrying water, canned food, diapers and tents and mattresses.
The general secretary for the Florida branch of the Salvation Army yesterday described how the Florida Highway Patrol was stopping trucks and vans to check whether they had proper paperwork for interstate travel and sending them back if they did not.
The Salvation Army official, Lieut. Col. Doris M. Fizer, said her office in Tampa received frantic calls from donors who lacked a written destination for their deliveries.
Colonel Fizer said: "The trucks can't get through, the highways are jammed. The highway patrol have been calling asking what proof they have that they are making deliveries and ask if we are expecting them. We tell them 'yes.' "

HEADLINE: Turkey Puts on a New Face and Sidles Up to Europe More Coyly

BYLINE: By ALAN COWELL, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: ANKARA, Turkey

 BODY:
Across the long decades of the cold war, this vast land, composed of a sliver of Europe and a slab of Asia, knew exactly where it stood.
As East confronted West, Turkey was the last outpost on the frozen Anatolian steppe; it offered the United States air bases and missile installations, and it guarded the long southeastern flank facing the Soviet Union. It was a loyal and doughty member of the Western alliance that might one day aspire to full acceptance among those its huge army and outmoded tanks were supposed to protect. 
 These days the calculations have changed and Turkey is quietly seeking to redefine its relationship with the Western world, acknowledging that the long hoped-for peace dividend of membership in the European Community seems as remote as ever because its members do not want it to join.
"There is a wind blowing over Turkey," a senior Foreign Ministry official said. "It is coming from Europe and blowing Turkey away from Europe."
 
The New Sales Promotion
 As they review the changes, Ankara's diplomats are seeking new ways to entice the United States and Western Europe.
Instead of offering a bulwark against Communism, they are promoting their land as influential and stable in a region of instability and Islamic fundamentalism that encompasses the new nations in Central Asia and the anti-Western governments of Iran and Iraq.
They are quietly scaling back Turkey's public clamor for integration into Europe -- Ankara's most fundamental goal for decades -- and seeking economic advance to support a longer-range bid for membership in the European Community.
"There's this feeling that Turkey's expectations have been lowered," an official said. "That is a tactic. Instead of knocking on Europe's door every day, we are saying, 'Let's do our homework on the economy.' "
The armed forces of 850,000 are to be modernized and trimmed to 500,000, offered more as a potential peacemaker than cannon fodder before a Soviet advance. Under United Nations auspices, for instance, Turkey says it wants to send peacekeeping troops to Bosnia and Herzegovina, despite the region's memories of occupation by Ankara's Ottoman forebears.
 
Host at Economic Conference
 And by casting itself as a role model of Western-looking, secular democracy to the new Central Asian republics, Turkey is seeking to enhance its value to the West Europeans, both as an integral part of their defenses and a land that offers far more than guns and soldiers.
Turkey sought to underscore its new regional profile by convening an 11-nation gathering in Istanbul this year that brought together leaders from as far apart as Albania and Russia in a declaration of intended economic cooperation in the Black Sea region. It also was host to the 47-nation Islamic Conference Organization at a gathering supposed to marshal Muslim support for Bosnia and Herzegovina.
As much as ever, though, Turkey confronts a deep reluctance among many Europeans and some Americans to share its visions, a reluctance that officials, including President Turgut Ozal, ascribe to Western aversion to Turkey's Muslim culture: while the Ankara Government is unflinchingly secular, 99 percent of its 60 million people are Muslims.
Greece, Turkey's perennial adversary, is blocking any move toward closer integration with Europe until Turkey makes concessions on Cyprus. Both Greece and Turkey are under strong American pressure to advance the stalled dispute, which began with Turkey's invasion of the island in 1974.
 
At Loggerheads Over Kurds
 The European Community has told Ankara that it will have to wait in line behind other lands from both Western and Eastern Europe, and greatly improve its economic performance, before it can even hope for community membership.
While Turkey says it has an "enhanced strategic relationship" with the Bush Administration, the United States Congress is urging that more than $450 million in military aid grants to Turkey be made into credits that Ankara will have to pay for.
As Europe itself ponders its own future defense structures, Turkey fears it could be left on the sidelines. And, most important of all, on the key issues of human rights and the plight of the Kurds of northern Iraq and Turkey, Ankara is at loggerheads with some of those same Western powers it is seeking to court.
The latest Amnesty International report on Turkey accuses the police of routine torture of suspects. Turkish human rights groups have charged the security forces with gunning down scores of civilian demonstrators at a major Kurdish festival in southeastern Turkey on March 21. [More bloodshed followed in August when 50 hours of fighting between rebels and Government forces in the southeastern town of Sirnak sent thousands of people fleeing their homes.]
 
Iraq Is a Sticking Point
 Arguably, though, it is over the issue of how the West should handle Iraq that Ankara is most at variance with its allies.
At the start of the Persian Gulf crisis in August 1990, Turkey was one of the first Western coalition members to enforce sanctions against Baghdad, closing down the pipelines that carried a full half of Iraq's oil for export and that earned Turkey millions of dollars.
And last year Ankara agreed to permit American and British warplanes to fly from Incirlik air base in southeastern Turkey over northern Iraq as a deterrent to further Iraqi onslaughts against the Kurds.
But as tension with Baghdad mounted again this summer, Prime Minister Suleyman Demirel has made clear that his country would not assume the same high profile and would not permit allied warplanes to fly combat missions against Iraq from Turkish bases.
Turkey acknowledges that the allied flights may be preventing Iraqi attacks that would cause a further, unwelcome flood of Kurdish refugees into Turkey. But as the deterrence continues, the Kurds of northern Iraq seem to be taking advantage of the protection to advance toward a Kurdish mini-state, possibly setting an example for Turkey's own Kurdish minority, six million of whom live in southeastern Turkey.
 
War Grows More Vicious
 "The Turks fear that if there's such an entity in northern Iraq, there would be a similar pressure for a Kurdistan in Turkey," a Western diplomat said. Ankara is fighting an increasingly vicious war against separatist Kurdish insurgents in the southeast precisely to prevent that.
On some issues Ankara's interests are more closely aligned with those of Baghdad than those of the West: both want the economic benefit of reopening the oil pipeline; neither wants the region's Kurds to pursue their separatist visions. Ankara pointedly refused to recognize the administration set up by Kurds in northern Iraq earlier this year.
"If Turkey does not want an independent Kurdish state" in northern Iraq, a high official said, "there is no independent Kurdish state."

HEADLINE: Los Angeles Riot Anger Spills Into Competition for Jobs

BYLINE: By SETH MYDANS, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES, Aug. 29

 BODY:
Hammers fell silent last week on the wooden frame of a new shopping center in riot-torn South-Central Los Angeles as workers paused to hear the angry shouts of a small group of intruders.
"You white guys won't give us black people a chance," Jake Bellamy, a member of the Black Carpenters Association, shouted at one of the foremen. "You aren't fair. You don't know nothing about fair. Black men can do this work, too. They need to do this work to give their children a better future. Is your child better than mine?" 
 Three days later, on Aug. 21, more than 100 protesters, including Mr. Bellamy, took over the work site in the latest of a series of demonstrations that have become a new outlet for the anger that erupted into rioting four months ago.
 
Desperate for Work
 The protesters say desperation over finding jobs was one underlying cause of the riots. They say the absence of black workers on some inner-city rebuilding projects is a harsh reminder that these problems have not been resolved.
"It seems to me that the substance of the issue is that, void of buildings burning and ashes smoldering, people really are going back to business as usual," said Danny Bakewell, the leader of a black group called the Brotherhood Crusade.
"We are saying to the people operating in our community, 'You have an obligation and a responsibility to hire the people who live here,' " added Mr. Bakewell, who has organized a number of the demonstrations.
 
Changing Neighborhoods
 But the demographics of South-Central Los Angeles have been shifting rapidly, and the area, traditionally black, is now home to an equal number of Hispanic residents. The work-site demonstrations have aroused new tensions in neighborhoods where competition between black and Hispanic residents for shrinking jobs and services has been increasing sharply.
Hispanic workers were among the crews at some of the projects singled out by the protesters, and some Hispanic leaders have spoken out against Mr. Bakewell's work-site protests. One militant group has threatened to lead counterdemonstrations, arguing that the black-jobs campaign threatens the employment of Hispanic construction workers.
The demonstrations have persuaded some employers to hire black workers, and they have reminded others in the area of the need to integrate their construction crews.
But the roots of the anger run deeper, tapping still-unresolved social problems.
 
'People Are Still Angry'
 "I want to say to you that the riots didn't stop because the rage had ended," said Rodney White, a leader of the Black Carpenters Association, a small private group that was formed to protest a shortage of blacks in construction trade unions. "They stopped because of the National Guard. But the people are still angry down here, very angry, and I'm talking specifically about black males."
At the corner of Florence and Normandie Avenues, where the first serious violence of the riots broke out and where another demonstration this month shut down the rebuilding of a burned-out gasoline station, idle young black men talk angrily about a society that seems to have quickly forgotten their problems.
"The riot is over, and it's back to business as usual: nothing for us to do," said a shirtless young man who displayed across his back the tattoos of the 8-Trey Gangster Crips, a violent street gang. "All I can say is, next time it's going to be worse."
The work-site protests, which include both gang members and men in suits and neckties, have been mostly peaceful, though some violence and vandalism has been reported.
 
Problems Deeply Rooted
 On the site singled out by the black carpenters group, the general contractor, George Inouye, said: "I've been in this business for a number of years, and I do understand the problems that they have. But when people like this come up and scream and yell at me, it's difficult for me to know just what they want."
The economic and racial problems here are so deep that the composition of work crews can amount to little more than a symbol, said James Johnson, director of the Center for the Study of Urban Poverty at the University of California at Los Angeles.
"The seeds of the rebellion are inherent in the restructured economy and the massive job loss between 1978 and 1989, when about 200,000 jobs disappeared from the Los Angeles economy, most of them centered in South-Central Los Angeles," he said in an interview.
"These were high-paying, highly unionized jobs in manufacturing, jobs that were the lifeblood of the communities in South-Central Los Angeles, providing stable employment and livable wages that allowed people to maintain stable families," he said.
 
'The Lethal Combination'
 With the jobs gone and families disintegrating, and with the school system registering an inner-city dropout rate between 60 and 80 percent, Mr. Johnson said, "What you have in South-Central Los Angeles is a large population of mostly males who are neither at work nor in school, and that is the lethal combination."
And with the rapid influx of Hispanic residents into the area, he said, "What is happening is that you find blacks and Latinos competing for resources off the same dry bone."
As their population in South-Central Los Angeles approachs 50 percent, Hispanic residents have claimed a larger share of affirmative-action jobs and have sought a Hispanic balance in reapportionments of city, county and school districts.
They fought for more jobs at Martin Luther King Hospital, which was built after the Watts riot of 1965 to address black needs, and they raised loud complaints when no Hispanic candidates were included in the final selection for a new Los Angeles police chief.
 
Growing Urban Underclass
 The competition has been heightened as various groups, including a newly militant Korean community, have jostled for their share of reconstruction aid and jobs after the riot.
"We can't accept that the city continues to act as if immigrants aren't here," said City Councilman Mike Hernandez at a recent public forum, arguing for more Hispanic representation in rebuilding plans. "They're creating and perpetuating an underclass."
Sergio Munoz, news director of the Spanish-language KMEX television channel, called the workplace demonstrations an act of provocation and said, "Of course there is competition for jobs. We are all here legally, and so we should compete for the jobs. What I see wrong is when some leaders try to take advantage of this to pit one community against the other."
Some Hispanic leaders, like Nick Sandoval, president of the Hispanic Contractors Association, are seeking to calm the post-riot competition, saying it feeds already volatile tensions.
"We are all professionals. We want to share the pie," he said. "We want to go by our percentages and have parity."
But even this approach angers some blacks who see their share of the pie dwindling.
"They seem to feel that the Hispanic population is so large now that a few of us need to come off the scene and make room for them," said Nareshimah Osei, who led the shutdown of the work site at Florence and Normandie Avenues.
But with the riot, he said, "The door has been kicked open for those of us who are willing to stand up and fight for our community."

The competition for jobs among minorities continues to add to the neighborhood's tension. (Jan Sonnenmair for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: China Frets as U.S. Mulls Taiwan Jet-Fighter Deal

BYLINE: By SHERYL WuDUNN, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: BEIJING, Aug. 29

 BODY:
The Chinese Government is worried that the United States and France may soon sell fighter planes to Taiwan, but is finding itself with little maneuvering room to block the sales, according to Chinese and foreign officials and scholars.
Officials in the Bush Administration have said that the President is leaning toward approving a proposed $4 billion sale of about 150 F-16's to Taiwan, a move that would save thousands of American jobs. 
 France must decide whether to sell Mirage 2000-5 fighters to Taiwan, a move that would also help keep production going and save jobs in hard economic times.
For Taiwan, which has been trying desperately for years to buy modern fighter planes, the prospect of having a choice would have been considered unimaginable just a year or two ago. But for China, which regards Taiwan as part of its territory and until now has successfully pressed Western countries to curb military sales to Taiwan, the proposed deals suggest an alarming trend.
Trade between Taiwan and China is soaring and relations are warming. But because Beijing refuses to renounce the use of force to "recover" Taiwan, the Taiwanese Government argues that it needs modern jet fighters to balance the Chinese Air Force, now strengthened by two dozen SU-27's purchased last year from Russia.
In the past, Chinese leaders reacted very strongly to block sales by Western countries to Taiwan, even lowering the status of its diplomatic relations with the Netherlands from an ambassadorial level in the early 1980's after it sold submarines to Taipei. But the Beijing Government appears to have less leverage now.
Many experts say that China understands the difficulties faced by President Bush in his re-election campaign and will be restrained in its reaction if he approves the F-16 sale after a review now under way.
"If the Bush Administration says: 'We have to do this. You know the best friend you've got in the world is Bush, and the best approach to take is a low-key one,' I think that would get some resonance here," said one American official, who insisted on not being identified.
In the past, the pressure to sell weapons to Taiwan came primarily from those who sympathized with its Government and its claims to represent all of China.
But these days the pressure is mainly economic: Taiwan, with more than $80 billion in foreign-exchange reserves, wants to buy weapons at a time when the military industry in many countries is shrinking.
Taiwan is also parceling out billions of dollars in domestic projects in the coming years, and it is likely to reward those countries that also sell weapons.
Although China also offers business opportunities to Americans, the jet sale to Taiwan, by directly saving jobs, could contribute to Mr. Bush's re-election chances.
China has warned France several times against selling the Mirage jets to Taiwan, and a Chinese buying mission recently boycotted France, but the threats have not been particularly harsh. Its tactics toward Washington has been even milder.
"China, of course, is paying attention to this, and it opposes the deal," said a senior Chinese official, in a reference to the proposed American sale. "At the least, China would express its objection.
But he said, "If you sell, there's nothing we can do."
Some American analysts say that China must take particular care in reacting to any deal because it faces a yearly vote in Congress over renewal of its favorable trade status.
Asked what China's response to a sale would be, the Foreign Ministry said the United States should "implement in a practical way the obligations" undertaken in the joint communiques between the countries.
What the Chinese are suggesting is that the sale could violate a communique issued by the United States and China in 1982 that says American arms sales to Taiwan should not exceed "either in qualitative or in quantitatve terms" the level of arms supplied in the previous four years.

HEADLINE: Voices of Hunts Point Talk of Drugs and Decay, but Rebirth Too Voices of Hunts Point Talk of Drugs and Decay, but Reality Too

BYLINE: By JONATHAN RABINOVITZ

 BODY:
On the streets of Hunts Point, young men and women, aimless and bored, hang out in front of storefronts covered with graffiti, abandoned buildings and vacant lots. Dealers brazenly sell drugs, paying children 5 cents to collect used crack vials. A guard at the McDonald's on Garrison Avenue tries to keep prostitutes from using the bathrooms as shooting galleries.
On other streets, children play stickball late into the evening as their parents linger on the sidewalks and stoops to escape hot, humid apartments. A woman on Bryant Avenue grows tomatoes and peppers in her front yard. Government officials from France tour the neat rows of new town houses bearing plaques trumpeting the day they opened. 
 These are the two worlds of Hunts Point in the Bronx -- two conflicting realities that collided early Wednesday morning in a bloody barrage of bullets on Longwood Avenue that wounded 12 people but, miraculously, killed no one.
The shooting, which the police said appeared to involve rival drug gangs trying to expand their turf, underscored the competing visions of a neighborhood, like others in New York City, on the cusp of a rebirth after the urban devastation of the 1970's and 1980's.
"You have stable micro-neighborhoods living next to a war zone," said Mitchell L. Moss, the director of the Urban Research Center at New York University. "How do you resolve this is the great question."
In more than two dozen interviews since the shootout, men and women, young and old, spoke of their different versions of Hunts Point, what is wrong and what would make it better. They spoke of their lives and their families, of fear and despair, of devotion to the neighborhood and hope for its future.
 
Jose Suares
Store Worker, 21 years old
Michael Santiago
Unemployed, 21 years old
 At the corner of Lafayette and Bryant Avenues, a spot that neighbors say is full of drug dealing, Jose Suares and Michael Santiago pointed proudly to a poem they helped spray-paint on the wall of a bodega:
Never forget!
What we may do is wrong, but it is a way of life!
We are forced to survive by any means necessary!
Why?
Because we're just products of our environment.
Created by the getto, born in the getto, also taught in the getto.
All we really are is victims of the getto!!!
"We've memorized it," Mr. Santiago said. "It tells it like it is."
The poem was written by a friend, they said. When they first heard it, they insisted on enshrining it on the wall in an elaborate mural. Next to the poem is a brief "R.I.P." for a friend, Charlie, who was shot to death on the corner last summer. Other friends are memorialized on other blocks of Hunts Point.
Mr. Suares and Mr. Santiago grew up in Hunts Point. They say that they have spent most of the summer hanging out late into the night on this and other corners. Mr. Suares said he has a job that pays $5 an hour at a clothing store. Mr. Santiago is unemployed. Both dropped out of high school. Asked about their plans for the future, both shrugged.
On Thursday night, cars drove by and their passengers called out to friends and others loitering on the corner. Prostitutes sauntered past.
Mr. Suares: "The shooting the other night -- that was typical. I've been here all my life, and it's always been the same."
Mr. Santiago: "That's everyday life here. Stick around here a little longer and you'll hear the gun shots."
Mr. Suares: "There's nothing that can be done about it. Kids grow up and they see everything going on. No one grows up and becomes a drug dealer because they were born with it. They see other people who are getting paid lots of money, and they want to get paid too."
Mr. Santiago: "It's a chain of events. You come and see a 16-year-old guy driving a 300z, a Benz or a Beamer, you want one too. You go to the streets because you make your money faster.
"And you do it for fame. It impresses the girls."

HEADLINE: Fort Dix May Become Federal Prison

BYLINE: By ROBERT HANLEY

 BODY:
The Pentagon and the United States Bureau of Prisons are close to an agreement to convert Fort Dix, the sprawling Army base in central New Jersey that has been stripped of its function as an active duty training center, into the largest prison in the Federal system.
The plan calls for five empty barracks to be converted, by early 1994, to a low security prison complex for 3,200 convicted drug offenders and white-collar criminals, Daniel R. Dunne, a spokesman for the Bureau of Prisons, said in a telephone interview yesterday. The prison would be larger than any of the Government's 67 other prisons, he said. 
 He estimated that the conversion would provide 500 to 700 permanent new jobs to help soften the economic blow of the Army's retrenchment at the 75-year-old base in Burlington County. Nearly 4,000 civilians worked at Fort Dix, one of the county's biggest employers, when the change in its military status was announced a year ago. About 3,100 of them have lost their jobs, leaving about 900 civilian jobs when active duty training ends next month, officials said.
A final agreement on the prison plan has not been reached, but there do not appear to be any major roadblocks, officials said.
 
Community Comment Sought
 "It's really a win-win-win situation for everyone involved," Mr. Dunne said. "It's an excellent opportunity for the taxpayers. We'd move into a facility that's already established. Both organizations see the importance and economic significance this has and are looking to make it work."
Mr. Dunne said a study of the environmental and economic impact of the proposed prison was completed early this summer. Military and prison officials are soliciting reactions to the proposal from local officials and residents in Wrightstown, Pemberton Township and other communities around Fort Dix.
The 30-day period for gathering comment ends on Sept. 7. Afterward, Defense Department and military officials will begin a final review of the plan, Mr. Dunne said.
So far no strong objections have been raised.
 
'It Sounds Good'
 "There's been no groundswell of opposition," said Paul Tuliano, a former member of Pemberton Township's zoning board who has been long active in the community's civic affairs. "Economically, it sounds good."
Mr. Tuliano noted that New Jersey created a state prison at other existing buildings at Fort Dix in the early 1980's and that local officials have routinely favored renewing the lease for that prison.
Last month, the township council in Pemberton Township wrote to the Bureau of Prisons in support ofthe proposed prison and the jobs it would generate, Harold Morin, a council member, said yesterday.
"The people going into it aren't going to be any worse than those in the state prison," Mr. Morin said. "The state prisoners are killers, rapists, everything."
Inmates in low-security prisons are "normally not people who have a history of violence or serious offenses," Mr. Dunne said. Their average sentence is five years, he said. Sixty-four percent of low-security inmates are in a Federal prison for the first time and 67 percent were convicted of drug-related offenses, he said.
The Fort Dix prison, he said, would be surrounded by two 12-foot high chain-link fences separated by coils of razor wire. Each fence would also be topped by the wire coils.The prison would comprise about 209 of the base's 31,065 acres, a few blocks from its main jogging track.
Mr. Dunne said the proposed prison is part of an "extensive expansion" planned by the prison bureau. Its 67 prisons house 71,000 inmates, 44 percent above capacity, and the number of inmates is expected to increase to 100,000 by 1995, he said.
Currently the largest Federal prison, in Atlanta, has about 2,140 prisoners.
 
Economic Impact of Millions
 For months, Government officials, including United States Representative H. James Saxton, whose district includes Fort Dix, have been scrambling for ways to ease the local economic impact of the Army's cutbacks at the base.
Under a nationwide military consolidation plan announced in July 1991 by the Defense Base Closure and Realignment Commission, Fort Dix's historic mission -- training regular Army troops -- is to end on Sept. 30. On Aug. 21, the final 600 recruits of the 3 million trained at the base since it opened in 1917 graduated from boot camp.
Post commanders are now preparing for the base's new role as a base for weekend and summer training of National Guard and Army Reserve units.
The retrenchment will mean a loss in annual earnings of about $125 million, according to a 1991 study by Burlington County's Department of Economic Development. That figure includes the salaries of the dismissed civilian workers at the base, as well as of employees of the base's suppliers who lost their jobs.
A new prison employing 500 to 700 would make up a fraction of the loss. Mr. Dunne, the spokesman for the Bureau of Prisons, said that the annual payroll for the prison that is planned would be $17.8 million. The prison's estimated annual operating budget will be $25.2 million, including about $7.2 million for food, supplies, electricity, water and other utilities.
Of the 500 to 700 new jobs, Mr. Dunne said, 200 to 280 will go to members of the bureau's staff around the county and 300 to 400 would be newly created clerical, administrative, medical, food service, and maintenance positions.
The proposed prison is expected to cost from $5 million to $10 million, considerably less than a new facility. A new low-security prison with 700 beds, less thanone-fourth the size of the one planned, would cost about $50 million, Mr. Dunne said.
Some of the lower cost stems from the planned use of existing medical buildings, gymnasiums and mess halls near the converted barracks.
About 70 percent of the construction cost will be spent on labor and the other 30 percent for materials, Mr. Dunne said. Local companies will be allowed to bid on the project.
The proposed prison would be the Federal system's first low-security facility on a military base and the first in the Northeast.
There are minimum-security prisons, which do not have perimeter fencing, at Seymour-Johnson Air Force Base in North Carolina, Maxwell Air Force Base in Alabama, and Eglin and Homestead Air Force Bases in Florida. The inmates at Homestead were transferred to other Federal prisons before Hurricane Andrew struck the base last week, causing extensive damage.

HEADLINE: California Senate Reluctantly Passes Grim Budget

BYLINE: By ROBERT REINHOLD, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: SACRAMENTO, Calif., Aug. 29

 BODY:
The two-month-long budget crisis in California took a large step toward conclusion early this morning when the State Senate reluctantly passed an austere recession-era budget that cuts deeply into health and welfare services and state support for cities and counties.
The bill, which closely resembles the no-new-taxes plan proposed by the Republican Governor, Pete Wilson, now goes to the fractious State Assembly, where its fate is uncertain. But with the Democratic-controlled Senate having passed it by a solid 33-to-5 vote, the Assembly has been put in the difficult position of either yielding to the Governor or plunging the state still deeper into crisis. 
 Unable to bridge a $10.7 billion budget gap, the state has been paying its bills with i.o.u.'s since July 1, and centers for the retarded and many health programs for the poor and old are on the verge of shutting. Schools are set to reopen in another week or so amid huge uncertainty about how much money they will have.
 
A Few Concessions
 The Senate action came at 1:30 this morning after days of intense negotiations during which Senate leaders extracted a few concessions from Governor Wilson to make the budget a little more palatable to the Assembly. For example, Mr. Wilson agreed to drop his demand to eliminate adult dental care for the poor under the state's Medi-Cal program and to cut welfare grants by 5.8 percent instead of 10 percent.
Still, the President pro tem of the Senate, David A. Roberti, a Los Angeles Democrat, said, the budget was a harsh one for a state that is suffering through a "great recession, almost depression."
It would cut $1.3 billion from the $2.9 billion that the state would have normally given to cities, counties and special fire-fighting and other districts; reduce money for regional centers for the retarded; limit Medi-Cal eligibility for nonresidents; cut support for the University of California and the California State University systems by $402 million; double fees at community colleges, and delay the opening of two new prisons.
And, most controversially, the budget would maintain support for public schools, kindergarten through 12th grade, but only with complex bookkeeping acrobatics devised by Governor Wilson that "recapture" $1.1 billion in excess money paid to schools last year and then "lend" them money from future years for this year.
Under the budget passed by the Senate, the schools would have to repay the money in better times unless revenues are insufficient to maintain per-pupil spending. This measure is bitterly opposed by the education establishment and the powerful Speaker of the Assembly, Willie L. Brown Jr., a San Francisco Democrat, and will be the main focus of debate in the Assembly.
 
'We Have No Choice'
 Senator after Senator rose last night to decry the proposed budget as "anathema," "horrible" and "lousy," but then voted for it in glum resignation.
"We must act; we have no choice but to act," said Senator Roberti, adding later, "It is long since the time when the public will tolerate a philosophical fight between Republicans and Democrats."
One of the few Senators to vote against the bill was Diane E. Watson, a Democrat who represents the South-Central Los Angeles area torn by riots in late April and early May. "We should stand up to the Governor," she said. "He is a mean-spirited guy."
Reflecting the views of many liberal Democrats, Ms. Watson said that the Governor did not really want a budget passed and hoped that the resulting chaos would help him win voter approval of a measure he has put on the November ballot that would enhance his powers over the budget.
An extraordinarily raucous debate is likely in the Assembly. Under the State Constitution, the budget must obtain a two-thirds vote in both houses, possible in the genteel Senate but extremely difficult on the 80-seat Assembly, which has 47 Democrats and 33 Republicans and where it is unclear whether the Governor is more disliked by his fellow Republicans than by Democrats. The budget must obtain 54 votes to pass.
Voting was not expected until Sunday or Monday, when the session officially ends.
On Friday afternoon, Speaker Brown denounced the education part of the package, likening it to "skimming in Las Vegas."
The Governor has said he would veto any budget that raises taxes or rolls over obligations into future years. But if his Senate-amended budget passes, it would mean many new fees for Californians and almost certainly higher taxes as local governments try to replace lost state subventions.

GRAPHIC: Photo: California's budget impasse moved closer to resolution yesterday when the State Senate passed a package that cuts deeply into health and welfare services. Maddy, conferred early yesterday in Sacramento after the budget's approval. (Associated Press)

HEADLINE: Trenton G.O.P. Shifting School Aid to Suburbs

BYLINE: By WAYNE KING, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TRENTON, Aug. 27

 BODY:
After abandoning a plan to amend the State Constitution to cut the underpinnings from a law set up to funnel aid to the state's urban schools, the Republican legislative majority is now preparing to shift money back to suburban districts.
Administration officials agree that something must be done to revise the Quality Education Act, acknowledging that a drop in tax revenues and the strict guidelines of the current aid formula could hurt suburban districts. At the same time they are concerned that the courts might not agree with changes that would slow down a long-term court-ordered plan: to bring school spending in poorer districts closer to that of wealthier districts. 
 "Obviously we are going to have to have an adjustment to the formula, but we want to insure that it is equitable and fair," said Education Commissioner John Ellis.
Thomas Corcoran, the Governor's policy adviser for education, said, "You want to avoid a situation such as occurred in Texas, where the courts took charge of the allocation of state aid to schools," appointing a special master after the legislature was unable to agree on a plan.
 
Courts Are Not Veto-Proof
 Republicans have been at odds with Mr. Florio, Mr. Ellis and the Democrats over the school aid formula ever since it was approved two years ago. Now that they have veto-proof two-thirds majorities in both houses, they can enact whatever legislation they want.
But cuts in the original aid package made by the Democrats because of budget problems are already being challenged in court, and any further reductions are also likely to face a legal challenge.
State Senator John H. Ewing of Bernardsville and Assemblyman John A. Rocco of Cherry Hill, the Republicans who are chairmen of the Senate and Assembly Education Committees, served notice this week that drastic revisions were in store, arguing that middle-class districts would be placed in "educational jeopardy" if nothing is done.
"New Jersey is faced with an unnacceptable choice if we leave the Q.E.A. intact," Mr. Ewing said. "Either middle-class districts will lose $234 million, or the state must raise taxes for school aid."
Either choice, he said, is "unacceptable."
 
'Virtually Meaningless'
 Governor Florio called the aid projections, which the Republicans derived from figures given by the bipartisan Office of Legislative Services, "virtually meaningless," saying that"income growth and property values, especially in these volatile economic times, are impossible to determine so far in advance."
Although the Republican leaders said in a statement that they would "endeavor to insure that there are no losers," any revision in the aid formula seems likely to shift money away from poorer districts, unless more money is found to help the schools.
But urban districts would not get less money than they do now; rather the projected increase, now $205 million, would be reduced. The Republicans said they would release specifics of their plan in September, then seek reaction before introducing a bill.
School financing has been a contentious issue in the state since 1990, when Mr. Florio persuaded the Legislature to increase the state income tax, partly in anticipation of court action on school aid.
 
Aid to Poor Cities Required
 As the Governor expected, the State Supreme Court ruled that year in a long-pending case called Abbott v. Burke that the state had to revise its aid formula to pump more money into 30 districts like Newark, Paterson, Jersey City, Hoboken and others that had substantial poor and minority populations, as well as relatively low incomes and property tax bases.
The Quality Education Act was designed to revise the formula, increasing state aid to those special needs districts each year until the districts reach parity in spending with others in the state.
Of a $2.8 billion tax increase in 1990, $1.1 billion was earmarked for education. But the overall tax increase was the decisive factor in the overwhelming defeat of the Democratic Legislature last November.
The Republicans promptly repealed $608 million of the tax increase by rolling back the 1-cent increase in the sales tax imposed by the Democrats. None of that money was intended for education, which is financed by the income tax, but the action coincided with hard economic times that have hurt tax collections of all kinds.
"The problem is the recession," said Mr. Corcoran. "There is just less revenue than we expected."
 
Taking From Urban Schools
 Compounding that is the differing political realities of Republican and Democratic legislators. Republicans mostly represent more affluent suburban districts, while the Democrats tend to come from urban areas, with bigger school problems and less wealth. So even before the revenue picture grew quite so dark, the Republicans complained that too much money was being spent -- some said "wasted" -- on the urban schools.
The Democrats note that the courts ordered more spending in poor districts. They add that it costs more to educate inner-city children with more social problems.
Last year, the Florio Administration managed to quiet outcries from the Republicans and the more affluent and middle-income school boards by adding $342 million to the school aid package through a revision in the state pension system.
Republicans nonetheless continue to chafe under the school aid formula, and even went so far as to propose an amendment to the State Constitution that would have struck down a provision mandating the state to provide "thorough and efficient education" to all its children.
It was that clause that the State Supreme Court used in ruling in 1990 that the state had to provide more money to poorer districts to bring them closer in spending to the wealthier ones.
Now the Republican majority has abandoned the effort to amend the constitution, at least for this year, partly because an attack on the words "thorough and efficient" seemed to many critics like an attack on educational quality. Instead, they have decided to work on revising the education act itself.

HEADLINE: Minority-Contract Rules Raise Issue of How to Measure Discrimination

BYLINE: By JAMES DAO

 BODY:
In 1990, just a year after making record sales, Bill Howell's Brooklyn-based oil company was cutting staff and costs to stay afloat. Certainly the recession hurt, but to Mr. Howell, the culprit was something less tangible, if more insidious: discrimination.
Arousing his suspicions was the timing of his financial downturn. In July 1990, New York State suspended a program that gave preferences to minorities and women bidding on state contracts. Without the program, Mr. Howell, who is black, lost out on bids for $300,000 in state business -- almost half the annual sales of Howell Petroleum Products. 
 "No one gave us a reason, but I knew," he said. "They no longer had to buy from minorities. So they didn't."
On the battlefield over affirmative action, both sides can claim backing from Mr. Howell's story. To supporters of preference programs, it demonstrates why governments should set aside a percentage of their contracts for minorities and women. To critics, it only shows that Mr. Howell could not compete in the open market.
 
Two Studies, One Result
 In recent months, both New York State and New York City have issued studies of the share of government contracts received by women and minorities. In both, minorities and women represented 25 percent of the qualified contract bidders, but received less than 8 percent of the public money spent in a given year.
Based on its study, the city began a program last month intended to insure that up to 20 percent of its spending goes to businesses owned or controlled by minorities or women. And earlier this month, after releasing its own four-volume study, the state announced that it would restore a similar program it had suspended in 1990 after a United States Supreme Court ruling struck down a minority preference program in Richmond.
The studies are important because they apparently hold the key to whether minority preference programs will pass muster with the courts. In essence, in the Richmond ruling in 1989, the Supreme Court said that state and local governments could operate preference programs -- if they demonstrated "with some specificity" the existence of discrimination.
But to critics of such programs, the studies are fraught with inaccuracies, weak analyses and questionable methodologies. Though they may demonstrate barriers against women and minorities, the critics say, they do not necessarily prove that discrimination has occurred. Opponents have vowed to challenge the state and city programs in court.
 
A Matter of Proof
 At stake are hundreds of millions in public dollars for contracts ranging from paper clips to road signs to prison construction.
The debate over the studies cuts to a deeper question: how can a government actually prove the existence of widespread discrimination?
"The nature of discrimination is such that there is not going to be a lot of documentation of what happened," said Franklin M. Lee, chief counsel with the Minority Business Legal Defense Fund in Washington. "There's not going to be a corpse where you can see the crime has been committed."
Such a question has confronted scores of governments and public agencies since the Court ruled in Richmond v. J. A. Croson.
Since the decision, which did not affect Federal set-aside programs, as many as 236 race- or gender-based preference programs across the country have been suspended or substantially altered, according to the Minority Business Defense Fund. And more than 80 studies have been conducted providing statistical ammunition to defend the programs.
New York State's study cost $360,000; New York City's $300,000.
 
Political Issues Raised
 Underlying the debate are broader political issues concerning the emerging role of immigrants and minorities in city governments. For Mayor David N. Dinkins, who in his election campaign in 1989 promised to give more city contracts to minorities, the issue could come full circle: Rudolph W. Giuliani has promised to attack the city's set-aside program in next year's campaign.
"In a city that is trying to save money to help the poor, we are going to pay 10 percent more to do this," Mr. Giuliani said. "We are creating social friction that is race based."
Dinkins administration officials say their program is merely trying to address long-standing discrimination by leveling the playing field.
Across the country, at least 16 challenges to state or local preference programs -- most brought by white-owned construction companies -- are wending their way through courts. In New York State, two contractors have asked the courts to reinstate suits that were dismissed when the state's minority-preference program was suspended.
City and state officials say their preference programs differ from those of the past in that they set flexible goals, not rigid quotas, although critics question whether there is any difference.
 
Minority Concerns Favored
 Under the state program, contractors who pledge to give a certain -- as yet undetermined -- percentage of work to minority or women subcontractors will be favored. Those who fail to meet their goals could lose contracts, said Lynn Canton, director of the state's division of minority and women's business development.
The city program allows department heads to award contracts to minority and women-owned firms whose bids are up to 10 percent higher than the lowest bids from white-owned firms. In some cases, agency heads can limit bidding only to firms owned by women or minorities.
New York State spends about $7 billion each year for all of its goods and services, and New York City about $2.5 billion, so even a small percentage change in the contracts awarded to minorities and women will translate into millions of dollars.
 
Vigilance Against Fraud
 Before a company can qualify for the programs, it must submit for review by the city or state about a dozen documents showing that minorities or women own or control 51 percent of it.
But officials concede that preventing fraud can be difficult. Past set-aside programs were plagued by scandals in which minority companies were actually fronts for white executives. Since then, New York State has toughened its certification process, said Joseph A. Spinelli, the State Inspector General.
The state has prosecuted two cases of fraud in such programs since 1988. In both, the defendants pleaded guilty to misdemeanor charges of filing false documents and served no jail time.
At the heart of the criticism of the discrimination studies that are being used to justify minority preference programs is the fact that most do not account for factors other than discrimination in explaining why companies owned by women and minorities do not fare well in obtaining government contracts.
Studies that do not compare companies of the same size and age are skewed, they contend.
"A company with 50 employees will not be able to perform on a $500 million contract as well as one with 500 employees," said Michael Kennedy, special counsel to the Association of General Contractors of America, which represents 32,000 construction contractors nationwide.
 
Response to Criticisms
 Critics also question the studies' use of anecdotal information.
"There are generalizations made about government officials or bonding agencies or banks, but there are rarely specifics," said George R. LaNoue, an economist at the University of Maryland who has testified in court cases involving minority preference programs.
Authors of the studies have answers to these criticisms.
Dr. David S. Evans of the National Economic Research Associates, who directed New York City's study, said many companies owned by minorities and women were smaller and younger than their white-owned counterparts precisely because of discrimination. Accounting for the size or age of companies in his study would have "contaminated it with the very thing we want to measure: discrimination," he said.
Mr. Lee of the Minority Business Legal Defense and Education Fund said that while anecdotal information could be unreliable, it should be taken seriously when stories consistently pointed in one direction.
"If there wasn't discrimination, how do you account for the broad cross section who say the same things?" he said.

HEADLINE: Conductor-Sleuths Nose Out Bogus Rail Tickets

BYLINE: Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: MILFORD, Conn.

 BODY:
Fred T. may look like any other conductor on the Metro-North Commuter Railroad, but he has a special undercover assignment: catching counterfeiters.
Usually, he can tell a bogus ticket by touch alone.
In the last few months, he has caught an advertising executive, a civilian employee of the Coast Guard and most recently "a very high-priced lawyer." 
 "There's some sophisticated equipment out there, a laser copier that does a very, very good job," said Fred T., who asked that his full name not be used to protect his undercover status.
Thanks to the advent of color copiers and a tight economy, Metro-North officials say, more people are trying their hand at counterfeiting tickets. So far this year, the railroad has arrested 10 people, compared with nine people in all of 1991, said Inspector John Lynch of the Metro-North police.
 
Stakes Are High
 Railroad officials estimate that fewer than 1 percent of the line's 100,000 riders may be using counterfeit tickets. But with a monthly pass running as much as $200, the stakes for the railroad are high.
One of the major tools against counterfeiting is "punch day," when a conductor punches a hole in tickets after closely examining them. Punch days are becoming increasingly frequent on Metro-North, officials said.
Special conductors like Fred T. are trained to catch even the most sophisticated forgery, running the tickets between their fingers and holding them up to the light.
When counterfeiters are found, they are usually arrested by the Metro-North police at the next stop. The excuses are usually familiar ones. "They say, 'I got it from a friend' or 'I bought it while I was standing in line for my ticket,' " Inspector Lynch said.
 
Broke Up Small Ring
 Fred T. can take credit for breaking up a small counterfeiting ring.
He was grocery shopping with his wife and got a tip that someone was selling bogus tickets nearby at a bar in New Rochelle.
"A guy would go into the bar and the bartender'd say, 'Working in New York? How do you get in, the train? What do those commuter tickets cost now, $114?' " the conductor said.
Then the bartender would say he just happened to have a counterfeit ticket he could sell for $25. "And the guy'd leave another $25 at the bar," said the conductor.
The bartender was convicted of counterfeiting and served part of a one-year sentence. In New York, forging a ticket is a felony, while in Connecticut it is only a misdemeanor. Inspector Lynch said Metro-North is seeking legislation to make counterfeiting a felony in Connecticut as well.
 
Fewer Problems on L.I.R.R.
 Counterfeiting is a lesser problem on the Long Island Rail Road, which conducts five punch days a month. Sam Zambuto, a railroad spokesman, said two people were arrested for counterfeiting tickets last year, and one has been charged so far this year.
Metro-North commuters dashing off the train here said they found the more frequent checking of their tickets annoying, but most said they welcomed the punch days.
"Frankly, I'd prefer they check the tickets closely when I'm paying more than $200 a month for my ticket," said Lauren Williams of Milford, a manager of a computer company in Manhattan who commutes daily. "I don't want to hear a forger's riding for free."
Edward Ohms, Metro-North's assistant director of transit planning, said the railroad is trying to develop a ticket that will outsmart the forgers with their laser technology. In the meantime the railroad is relying on more spot checks.

HEADLINE: BASEBALL;
Cone Gets To Toronto But Can't Find Plate

BYLINE: By JOE SEXTON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: TORONTO, Aug. 29

 BODY:
Home is a hotel room, and so who knows where the heart is. There are moments when David Cone feels his in his throat. There are moments, infrequent but inescapable, when it is hard for him not to feel it in pieces.
"Shellshocked," Cone said of himself. 
 The trade, of course, was what had so irretrievably altered his universe. The trade, sudden and to his mind suspicious, that had sent the right-hander from the Mets to the Blue Jays last Thursday. The trade.
"Sadness is an emotion that is prevalent," said Cone. "The excitement can overwhelm it. Nevertheless, it's there."
The trade, though, had to give way to the game, and the game was always something David Cone could deal with. He'd blocked out scandal to pitch for the Mets. He'd closed his mind to demoralization to win for the Mets. He'd pitched on and on and now, the game was upon him.
"To pitch, to be thrown into the fire so soon, it's the best medicine," said Cone.
The game, though, proved to be an awful tough tonic. Cone, in his debut as the latest principal in Toronto's patented strong-arm tactics for prevailing in the American League East, was wild and erratic and consequently bludgeoned by the Brewers in a 7-2 defeat today. He wasn't a savior. He wasn't even as good as Chris Bosio.
Cone, his mind reeling from the shock of the trade but also in pursuit of a theory to rationalize it, arrived in Toronto Friday night and was assigned a locker in the club's space-age clubhouse. "It's like the Jetsons," said Cone.
The cartoon feel of the night lost its comic edge soon enough. The Brewers mauled the Blue Jays for 31 hits and 22 runs Friday night to pull within four and one-half games of Toronto.
"Didn't get scared off, did you?" Cone was asked by Bob Bailor, the first-base coach for the Blue Jays.
Not much scares Cone, but the right-hander had spent the first half of the marathon of runs and pitching embarrassments charting pitches. Toronto's pitching problems, the cause behind the effect of the trade for Cone, couldn't have been any more starkly dramatized. Jimmy Key, the night's starter who was icing his left shoulder afterward, suggested the long Brewer innings might have left Cone wanting to ice his wrist.
"Jack Morris took the chart away from me after the fourth," said Cone.
What was to be handed Cone, then, was as obvious as the Skydome. The Blue Jays, who will pay slightly less than $1 million for Cone's seven regular-season starts, want quality of the highest sort. Unable to shake the Orioles and Brewers, they were feeling the tremors of panic.
"Coming from where I came from, from the deadness, it's an electric opportunity," said Cone. "Deal with pressure, deal with a big game."
Not today, though. Cone's trademark pitches mutinied. He walked seven, three of whom scored, and he was nearly run over as the Brewers stole seven bases off him.
In perhaps his most painful stumble of the afternoon, Cone, who had given up three runs through five innings, promptly sabotaged the 3-2 game the Blue Jays had made of it with a Joe Carter two-run homer in the sixth. He walked the leadoff man in the seventh and two more before it was over, as the Brewers scored four runs to wrap up the game.
"I started to press in the seventh, and fell apart," Cone said. "It's unnerving. That's what I'm supposed to thrive on. But I'll get better. I'll help this club."
 
Staring at the Tracks
 But for the moment, Cone was left to return to his hotel room at Skydome, the one with the window that looks out on railroad tracks, the one he stares out of as he tries to trace his route to this spot. He was traded for a pair of prospects and with the explanation that it was all executed because of economics.
The Mets, the right-hander learned through the club's news conference transcript, figured the potential free agent would demand a deal of five years and that such a contract didn't figure in the organization's calculus of investment risk. Never mind that they never asked Cone what it would take for the All-Star and National League's strikeout leader to stay on.
"I haven't had time to put it all in perspective," said Cone. "I will always speak my mind, but I may hold my tongue for the moment. I do not want to sound like a scorned, tortured, traded athlete."
In his more vulnerable moments, though, that is what he feels like. He finds the club's "asumption" that he wouldn't sign for fewer than five years disingenuous. The appeal to him of a deal with a shorter term and inflated annual figures is and always has been real. His affection for New York and the Mets remained true to the end.
"Yes, I am paid an enormous amount of money, and, yes, trades are all part of the sport," said Cone. "But I felt like I extended myself to the utmost for the Mets, that I was willing to throw 166 pitches and put myself at risk for the Mets, and that I was let drop without so much as a phone call. Maybe it means nothing to anybody and maybe it shouldn't. But it's hard not to feel unappreciated."
 
Did Mets Bear a Grudge?
 The urge, then, to search for a conspiracy theory is hard to fight. Cone doesn't discount the notion that the club's executives have borne a grudge since Cone abruptly halted talks on a long-term deal with the Mets and went on to win his arbitration case against the club last spring.
"It was a heated case," said Cone. "I don't think they were prepared."
As a result, Cone believes, the Mets might have decided right then that he wouldn't be in their future plans, and that he was used accordingly in 1992.
"I'd joked with coaches all season that they were trying to wring everything out of me, and that I definitely must have been a goner," said Cone. "There was joking back, an awkward dialogue. And when they let me throw 166 pitches, that was the first time I saw the writing on the wall.
"Let me say this," said Cone, who threw 166 pitches in a 1-0 shutout of the Giants on July 17. "I wanted the baseball. If I'd wanted out of that game, I could have asked out. I take responsibility. But it's the manager's decision, too."
 
He Won't Issue Any Excuses
 And so there will be continued questions throughout Canada about the status of Cone's arm, especially after his outing today. Cone said he felt flat, but he attributed that largely to the fact that with the trade and the travel, he hadn't picked up a baseball since Tuesday. He said he had been aware that the Brewers ran, but wasn't prepared for how "all-out" their stealing was.
"But all the excuses," said Cone, "all of it goes out the window."
And for Cone, there is a certain healthy unburdening that has come with his closing the door on the Mets.
"There's relief, I suppose," he said. "And there is a tremendous opportunity for a great experience. There's possibility here, right now. I want to pitch a big game and begin to feel a part of it here."
Cone, with mixed emotions, is still waiting after today. Toronto, with crossed fingers, waits with him.

HEADLINE: NOTEBOOK;
Free Agents May Find Many Closed Wallets

BYLINE: By MURRAY CHASS

 BODY:
The Mets' decision to trade David Cone rather than give him millions of dollars as a free agent did not come as a surprise to at least one general manager. Even while Al Harazin, the Mets' general manager, was saying a couple of weeks ago that he would not trade Cone, his counterpart on another team, during a discussion of the coming free-agent market, remarked, "The Mets may have more constraint because they're so awful this year."
Perhaps that general manager had greater insight into Harazin's thinking than Harazin himself. Whatever, by trading their best pitcher to the Toronto Blue Jays, the Mets have begun what can be considered the Cone constraint. 
 Except the Cone constraint could have ramifications well beyond the Mets, who want to reduce the $44.5 million payroll they have labored under this season. It certainly adds intrigue to what is expected to be the key question of the off season:
With the coming free-agent class potentially the most glamorous ever, are clubs prepared to spend the type of money to which top-flight free agents have become accustomed, or will some players suffer economic shock, discovering that the money they expected isn't there?
 
Plenty of Big Names
 Cone, Doug Drabek, Greg Maddux, John Smiley, Greg Swindell are the most attractive starting pitchers heading for free agency. The hitting part of the potential market includes Barry Bonds, Kirby Puckett, Mark McGwire, Ruben Sierra, Joe Carter and Paul Molitor.
As the salaries soar to superstratospheric levels, more teams drop out of the competition for the superstar types. With so many of those players available, then, will they find enough teams willing to spend the kind of money they seek? After all, how many Kirby Pucketts can one team sign?
"When there have been impact and premium players available, they have always been well-rewarded," Fred Claire, the Los Angeles Dodgers' general manager, said. "I don't know of any exception to that. Of course, we've never had as many potential impact players as far as I can recall."
Bobby Bonilla, who was the most attractive free agent available last winter even though his credentials fell short of some in this year's group, signed with the Mets for $29 million over five years, a record annual average income until the Chicago Cubs gave Ryne Sandberg $28.4 million for a four-year extension that prevented him from joining this year's free agents. Danny Tartabull signed with the Yankees for $25.5 million for five years.
 
Here Comes Reality
 Sandy Alderson, the Oakland Athletics' general manager, said he thought it was possible that some free agents might fall short of their quest for riches.
"I think that came close to reality last winter," Alderson said. "Danny Tartabull came very close not to being shut out but to being surprised by what was available until the Yankees bailed him out. This year, with the sheer number of free agents who will be available in the winter, given an additional year of fiscal evolution -- that is, expenses have risen to a level much closer to or beyond our revenues -- you're going to see some of that."
The San Diego Padres are one of those teams that does not plan to participate in the expensive sweepstakes. Bonds, who lives near San Diego, has expressed a desire to play there, but he would have to take maybe 50 cents on the dollar to interest the Padres.
"The number of teams that can afford them is diminishing," Joe McIlvaine, the Padres' general manager, said of the big-name free agents. "For a team like the Padres, it would be extremely difficult, almost impossible, to sign a player of that magnitude for the bucks they're talking about. We'd almost have to strip our team to pay for them."
Player agents are aware of the possibly shrinking market, but they remain confident that the top layer of free agents will go through the procedure economically unscathed. They aren't so certain about the players just below the top.
"I do have a feeling it will be tough for a lot of guys," said Chuck Berrty, Sierra's agent. "I think that will happen to a degree. There will be some who can get jobs at any level, but at what point does that number stop? Are there three, six, nine spots available? Somewhere down the ladder guys are going to be surprised."
Wade Boggs, whose string of successive .300 seasons is about to end at 10, is expected to be one of those who have a surprise awaiting them. One agent, not his, who always has a good read on the market, referred to Boggs as a player who "has become the quintessential mediocre third baseman." The tarnished Boston star, the agent said, "is going to find no one giving him $4 million a year and losing a draft choice."
Scott Boras, who represents Maddux and Benito Santiago, said he believed that some players having off years will adopt a different strategy.
"Players may decide to use salary arbitration," he said, "and go at it another year where there is less talent available, where there is a better market. I think that plan B will be utilized by a number of players if there is a question of the market. That could be a very viable alternative. If a club doesn't offer arbitration, he can sign wth someone for one year and be a free agent next year."
Tom Reich, an agent with years of free-agent experience, noted that the top pitchers won't have anything to fear. "The economic market is totally different for pitchers and everyday players," he said.
That's one of the reasons the Mets decided to trade Cone. Once the Cubs confirmed Reich's view by offering Maddux a five-year contract (for $27.5 million), Harazin concluded that someone might indeed offer Cone five years, but that it wasn't going to be the Mets. Harazin is not alone in his thinking about placing constraints on that type of offer.
"I was shocked when I read about the Maddux negotiations," the A's Alderson said. "My immediate reaction was the only thing dumber than making the offer was rejecting it."
 
The Skydome Is the Limit
 The David Cone trade carried with it another significant development.
In 1982, when the Yankees traded Tommy John to the California Angels on Aug. 31, the transfer of his salary on that day, when rosters are used to compile average team salaries, catapulted the Angels' average past the Yankees' figure, $423,403 to $411,988. That made the Angels No. 1 after the Yankees had held that spot the first four years the Players Association computed average team salaries.
Cone's move from New York to Toronto virtually assures the Blue Jays the No. 1 spot in this year's salary standing. Although Cone earned $3,362,637 of his salary with the Mets and will earn only $887,363 with the Blue Jays, the entire $4.25 million salary will be included in the Toronto total. That's the way labor and management salary practices work.
The roster changes created by the trade left the Blue Jays with an average salary of $1,517,272, the Mets $1,367,635.
 
An Ace, Without the Bandages
 If Mike Harkey's body ever lets him, he could become one of the best pitchers in the major leagues. The Chicago Cubs wait patiently, if with frustration.
After shoulder and knee injuries deprived him of a chance of pitching in the majors in 1989, Harkey had an outstanding rookie season in 1990, compiling a 12-6 record. An early-season shoulder injury led to surgery May 2 last season and limited him to four starts. The shoulder wasn't right this year, either, and he began the season on the 60-day disabled list.
But he finally was able to pitch again after the All-Star Game break, and he reeled off four victories without a loss and a 1.93 earned run average in his first six starts. It was no coincidence that the Cubs' resurgence occurred once Harkey joined the starting rotation.
The 25-year-old right-hander began a Cubs' four-game winning streak with his first victory, extended a winning streak to four games with his second, ended a four-game skid with his third and started a five-game winning streak with his fourth.
Harkey was scheduled to try to extend the Cub's streak to six last Tuesday, but he aggravated a groin pull he suffered in his previous start and couldn't pitch. The Cubs lost the game. They're grateful that this time they won't lose Harkey for long.

HEADLINE: Washington's Spin on Keiretsu

 BODY:
To the Editor:
Yoshi Tsurumi in "Don't Beat the Keiretsu. Join Them" (Forum, Aug. 2) describes Japanese keiretsu as benign groupings of companies helping each other in adversity and pooling resources to develop new businesses. Because some foreign companies sell to, or have joint ventures with, keiretsu members, he concludes that Americans should join keiretsu rather try to break them up. 
 This facile analysis bears little relation to reality. Of course foreigners sell to keiretsu members. But, as studies have indicated, keiretsu companies buy significantly less from abroad than would normally be expected of an economy the size of Japan. Furthermore, cooperation in the form of a joint venture or other long-term relationship is far different from participation in the organizational structure that binds the keiretsu members so closely. Most importantly, why should a foreign firm feel that it must enter a joint venture relationship in order to obtain access to an "open" market?
When foreign companies attend the regular meetings of keiretsu member presidents and are able to dispatch fast-track employees for two-year details to keiretsu firms, then Professor Tsurumi's view that foreigners can join keiretsu might be a reality. If one were to suggest these ideas to Japanese executives, I expect they would die from laughter rather than from karoshi (overwork).
The United States does not want to "break up" keiretsu; it wants an end to the exclusionary nature of these business groupings.
 
IRA WOLF
Washington, Aug. 10
 
Ira Wolf is assistant United States Trade Representative for Japan and China.
