PUB-DATE: August 30, 1992

SECTION: Section 6; Page 43; Column 2; Magazine Desk

LENGTH: 4851 words

HEADLINE: Wasting Away

BYLINE: By Keith Schneider; Keith Schneider is a national correspondent specializing in environmental reporting for The New York Times.

 BODY:
From the bright sunlight and mesquite of the high plains in southern New Mexico, it's a five-minute elevator ride down to the spectral darkness of the world's first deep geologic repository for nuclear wastes. Nearly 2,200 feet underground, the air is warm and tastes of salt dust stirred up by the fleet of forklifts, carts and debris haulers that turn the wide passages mined from rock salt into thoroughfares as busy as a small-town main street.
In one corridor, light floods from the mouth of a storage room filled with stainless-steel pumps and red digital gauges. Overhead, steel beams, rock bolts and wire mesh support the roof. Down another corridor, the sound of metal pounding on the rock salt walls resonates with the same painful clarity of a jackhammer breaking up concrete. Burly miners run the big machines, their headlamps like bouncing scratches of light against the midnight black. 
 What began in the early 1950's as Project Salt Vault has now become the most elaborate and expensive tomb ever built in the United States. The Department of Energy has been testing a 40-year-old theory in the $1.3 billion waste plant: that prehistoric salt beds might be the best place to entomb radioactive wastes. Salt lies deep in the earth and can easily contain deadly radiation. Salt is also comparatively soft and creeps like ice under intense geologic pressures. Over time, rock salt would gradually flow around the buried radioactive wastes, sealing them in a permanent hard cocoon.
The Waste Isolation Pilot Plant -- or WIPP, as it is popularly known -- is a seven-mile lacework of shadowed passages and murky storage rooms 26 miles east of the town of Carlsbad. It is the closest the Government has ever come to a final solution for ridding the country of the lethal byproducts of manufacturing nuclear arms. With the exception of Sweden, which is testing a deep repository mined from granite, no other nation is close to developing a permanent radioactive waste site of this scale.
WIPP had all the makings of an ideal solution if it were not for a serious flaw. Desperate to find an answer to ridding weapons plants of accumulating radioactive poisons, the Government chose the site in New Mexico not on the basis of carefully analyzed geological data but on economic and political grounds -- specifically, in response to layoffs in the potash industry around Carlsbad.
The nuclear-waste repository is hemmed in by underground pressurized reservoirs of brine that, sometime in the future, could flood the repository, pushing contaminants to the surface to harm future generations. In 1981, crew members drilling a test well at a site known as WIPP 12, about a mile north of the center of the repository, narrowly escaped serious injuries when they punctured a reservoir and the pressurized brine blew out their rig. The incident, which was not widely publicized, illustrates a major weakness in the repository's location. If knowledge of WIPP's existence should fade -- a certainty, given the shortness of human memory and given the repository's design life of more than 240,000 years -- future generations drilling the area for oil or gas or for other uses could penetrate the pressurized brine reservoir beneath the repository.
Evidence of water seeping into a repository designed to be bone dry became apparent as early as 1984, but construction work continued. Government scientists also found that the salt walls were closing in at a rate three times faster than expected. Cracks in the ceilings and walls began to appear in 1989, but were not noted in an environmental- impact statement published that year meant for Congress and the public. A year later, a roof in an experimental room collapsed; five other collapses have occurred since.
For most of its existence, WIPP was managed by the Energy Department as though it were a simple salt mine and not a vital nuclear project that merited full public disclosure and open public debate. As word of the repository's technical problems began to filter out of Carlsbad in the late 1980's, citizens in the state grew restive about its safety. A broad popular movement against it quickly developed in New Mexico.
In 1990, the anti-WIPP forces elected Tom Udall -- the scion of one of the Southwest's most powerful political families -- as state Attorney General. Udall, the son of former Interior Secretary Stewart L. Udall, had run on a platform promising to bar the opening of WIPP until every safety question had been answered and the Government had followed the letter of its environmental laws.
For the 44-year-old Udall, WIPP represented an almost transcendent moment: to press the nuclear-weapons industry to obey state and Federal environmental laws. He knew how much the Energy Department's $5.5-billion-a-year cleanup program meant to reclaiming some of the most beautiful stretches of the West that have been contaminated by the Government's nuclear-arms industry. But he also insisted that permanent nuclear-waste disposal sites be safe.
Facing Udall a continent away was Energy Secretary James D. Watkins, a 65-year-old nuclear engineer and retired admiral who was completing a distinguished career in the atomic-arms industry. Asked by President Bush in 1989 to deal with the chaos in the industry, Watkins quickly seized on WIPP as the best chance not only to solve the industry's most vexing waste-disposal problem but also to make a significant mark on the technology he loved.
Watkins believed the lessons learned in New Mexico might prove invaluable in calming public fears and that WIPP would lead to the construction of a separate repository in Nevada for even more lethal radioactive wastes from civilian nuclear-power reactors. He was adamant that WIPP open in 1991, whether or not he had Congressional approval.
The fate of the nuclear-waste repository, therefore, lay in the hands of two men who have never met and who came from two generations shaped by different views of the Government's responsibility to the land and the people.
"I LOVE WIPP!" JAMES WATKINS shouted to a crowd of Energy Department employees and civic officials in a rare public outburst three years ago in Albuquerque. The occasion was a dinner to boost morale and alert his critics that he was intent on following through on his pledge to President Bush to get WIPP opened.
That morning, dressed in coveralls and wearing a hard hat and miner's lamp, Watkins had taken the long elevator ride down to the salt passages for a tour of WIPP. Energy Department engineers who accompanied him remember how impressed they were by his pointed questions. Here, at last, was a leader who was well versed in engineering and construction and who understood the complexities of managing a project like WIPP. Watkins is a nuclear submariner and former chief of naval operation; his predecessors were two lawyers and one dentist.
Watkins's desk-pounding tirades after receiving bad news or for disciplining aides have become legendary at the Department of Energy. The same intensity and need to gain control of any situation are evident at work and at home. Two years ago, he stopped one of his daughters from marrying a man he considered unsuitable for her.
When Watkins took command of the Energy Department in March 1989, the WIPP program was in turmoil. Six months before, the department's first try at opening WIPP imploded during a Congressional hearing. Under withering cross-examination by Mike Synar, a Democratic Representative from Oklahoma and one of the toughest critics of the department, Federal engineers said they did not know if the repository had been built correctly and could be operated safely.
In many ways, the problems at WIPP represented a collapse in the management, morale and mechanics of the American nuclear-arms industry. A cold-war curtain of secrecy, coupled with inattention by Congress and a succession of administrations, had allowed poorly maintained nuclear reactors and dangerous nuclear processing plants to continue operating without any sort of public scrutiny.
The public was not informed of nuclear accidents at the Savannah River Plant in South Carolina and at the Hanford reservation in Washington State. The industry's lethal radioactive and chemical wastes were spread across miles of open ground in the West, poured into rivers and lakes in the East and dumped where it was most convenient and least expensive throughout the 12 states where there are nuclear-weapons plants and laboratories. When the true extent of the environmental contamination and the dangerous operating conditions were finally exposed in 1988, the Reagan Administration ordered nuclear-arms production plants shut down.
Watkins, who declined to be interviewed for this article, was hired by President Bush to fix a broken industry.
RADIOACTIVE WASTES are the only substances produced in the 20th century that will still be deadly to the inhabitants of the 2,000th century. The quest for a disposal site for civilian and military radioactive wastes began formally on Feb. 28, 1955, when the Atomic Energy Commission, the predecessor of the Department of Energy, asked the National Academy of Sciences to establish a committee of eight scientists to develop "a program of research pertinent to the methods of disposing of radioactive waste materials in geologic structures." On March 11, 1957, the committee completed a report that identified beds of rock salt that lay under New York, Michigan, the Gulf Coast and the Great Plains from Kansas to New Mexico as the most promising sites for permanent disposal.
The Atomic Energy Commission assigned the search for a suitable disposal site to scientists at the Oak Ridge National Laboratory in Tennessee, one of the three original secret nuclear plants where materials for the first atomic bombs were developed in the mid-1940's. In 1962, after being invited by the leaders of Lyons, Kan., to investigate an abandoned 70-year-old salt mine near town, Oak Ridge scientists selected the site for the Government's first attempt to build a deep geologic repository for civilian and military radioactive wastes.
The Government spent $100 million on the Lyons project before abandoning it in early 1971. Chief among the problems was the Government's failure to take into account dozens of unmapped oil and gas drilling holes around the old Lyons salt mine. State geologists argued that if water from underground reservoirs ever filled the bore holes, the surrounding salt would dissolve, the repository could be flooded and radiation flowing to the surface would result in a deadly manmade environmental disaster.
In October 1971, the Atomic Energy Commission scientists proceeded to Carlsbad. They had been invited there by executives of the United States Potash Company, which had closed a mine east of town three years earlier, but which was familiar with the deep salt beds that lay beneath the region's potash reserves. This 2,000-foot-thick seam of salt beginning 850 feet below the desert was the southern edge of the same Permian Basin salt beds that lay under Lyons.
In August 1972, the Atomic Energy Commission announced that southeastern New Mexico was under consideration for a repository. Carlsbad residents welcomed the news. The potash industry, which had employed as many as 3,000 workers in the mines east of town, was in decline as a result of competition from Canadian mines. The town was familiar with deep rock mining and eager for more secure Government jobs.
AT THE FOOT OF the Lost River Range in the desert of eastern Idaho lies an enormous white dome, inflated by air and containing thousands of white barrels and gray boxes of plutonium wastes. Piled outside are thousands more barrels covered with soil and grass to contain radiation and to prevent damage from the elements. In all, this lonely stretch of dry lands at the Idaho National Engineering Laboratory, an Energy Department weapons plant, is home to 129,000 drums and 11,000 boxes of sludge, clothing, glass vials, metal cuttings and countless other pieces of debris contaminated with plutonium.
For 37 years, Idaho had been the Government's nuclear-waste safety valve. Plutonium wastes from the Rocky Flats plant near Denver, which milled plutonium into spheres that triggered nuclear weapons, were shipped by rail north to the desert dump on a weekly schedule. Periodically, the wastes became a public issue. The Energy Department sought to quell concern by assuring Idahoans that it would end shipments and move the wastes to a permanent repository. Some of the promises were 20 years old. In October 1988, after the first attempt at opening WIPP failed, Cecil D. Andrus, the Democratic Governor of Idaho, barred any more nuclear wastes from entering his state. Thus, finding a permanent home for plutonium wastes became a pressing matter.
Seven months later, Watkins began his campaign to open WIPP, preparing for it as though it were a military tactical exercise. First, he established special teams of engineers from the Energy Department to review every document and to develop a new data base on the repository's design, engineering, construction and operations. By mid-1991, the final safety analysis report and the department's operational readiness review were completed. These two documents -- certifying that the repository was safe to operate -- were reviewed and approved by internal and independent committees.
The next task -- understanding how the repository would function over the full length of its 240,000-year life, or the time that plutonium remained radioactive -- proved much more problematical. Nobody had tried to predict the performance of a manmade structure tens of thousands of years in the future, but such information was necessary to gain a permit from the Environmental Protection Agency to operate the repository.
Watkins proposed to open WIPP for a five-year program of experiments involving up to 9,000 drums of wastes. To meet an E.P.A. requirement for the tests, the Energy Department had to find out what was inside each drum. Such data would be useful in determining how the wastes might behave underground, the E.P.A. said.
In Idaho, engineers looked at the barrels of waste sitting in a building commonly used for indoor tennis and scratched their heads. For years, the department's employees used the barrels like wastepaper baskets, throwing in contaminated debris, never imagining that someday someone would want to know precisely what was in them. Finding out was not a simple matter of opening the lids or cutting the drums open. Plutonium dust is highly mobile and in even tiny quantities can be lethal if ingested or inhaled.
So the Energy Department spent millions of dollars to install remote sensing equipment at the Idaho laboratory. The equipment is supposed to be able to chronicle the contents, measure the radioactivity and determine how much gas has formed inside from the decay of organic chemicals and other debris. Such gas formation has turned out to be a critical part of WIPP's long-term safety, since scientists have determined that over thousands of years the gas could build up in the repository and cause an explosion or enough pressure to crack the walls and cause radiation to escape.
There were only two problems with the content-deciphering process: time and cost. According to WIPP's Federal managers, it takes a week for technicians in Idaho to complete the examination of a single drum, at a cost of $100,000 per drum, with each drum accompanied by a 2,400-page report. At this rate, it would take 180 years and $9 billion just to prepare enough wastes to conduct Watkins's experiments.
In October 1991, when Watkins said the plant was ready to open, the Energy Department had just one test bin with four barrels of waste to drop into WIPP. Watkins was well aware of this, says Arlen Hunt, the manager of WIPP. None of the WIPP officials suggested to Watkins that he hold off the opening until more barrels were ready.
A third critical item on Watkins's plan was to gain the legal and legislative authority to open WIPP. This involved the control of the land above the repository, which has belonged to the Department of the Interior since 1850, when New Mexico became a United States territory. The 1983 land order that allowed the Department of Energy to construct a nuclear-waste repository also prohibited the transport of radioactive materials into it. Watkins sought to have the prohibition lifted. Despite clear opposition during nationwide public hearings, Manuel Lujan Jr., Secretary of the Interior and a retired Republican Congressman from Albuquerque, did just that on Jan. 22, 1991, without offering any public explanation.
"This was a temporary withdrawal for experiments," Lujan says. "This is my home state. I was a Congressman for 20 years and during all that time I dealt with nuclear issues. I felt comfortable in approving this decision."
Lujan, however, did not give Watkins complete carte blanche. He directed the Energy Secretary to limit the number of barrels to the amount that could be retrieved in the event WIPP did not become a permanent dump. Lujan also insisted that wastes could not be shipped to WIPP until Watkins could certify that the repository had passed all its technical requirements and was ready to open.
That moment came in September 1991, when the last of the independent panels reviewing WIPP's readiness issued its approval. Faced with the high cost of maintaining the repository -- nearly $500,000 a day -- and demands from the Governors of Idaho, Colorado and Nevada to open WIPP, Watkins had to move. On Oct. 3, he notified Lujan that all the work was finished. Later that day, Watkins alerted New Mexico that the first truck, with just four drums of contaminated glassware, would roll on Oct. 10 from Idaho.
THIS WHOLE subject of the nuclear industry highlights the incredible faults and problems with secrecy and power and arrogance," Tom Udall says late one winter afternoon as he is about to head home from his office in Santa Fe. His Volkswagen Rabbit initially slips on the icy streets, but soon settles down.
"The attitude that existed then, and still exists, is 'We know what's best,' " Udall says. "It's that attitude that has hurt them across the board with their credibility with the public."
Like his father, Tom Udall is first and foremost an environmentalist. He admired his uncle -- Morris Udall, who ran for President in 1976 and served as the chairman of the House Interior Committee for 14 years until he retired last year. But he revered his father, Stewart, who, during his eight-year tenure at the Interior Department under Presidents Kennedy and Johnson, preserved tens of millions of acres of wild lands as national parks and monuments and wrote some of the basic American environmental laws, including the Wilderness Act of 1964.
Tom Udall carries himself with his father's straight-backed assurance and even talks like him. Like his father, he is a lawyer (his law degrees are from Cambridge University and the University of New Mexico), and his first encounter with the nuclear-weapons industry came in 1978, as he was about to become an assistant United States attorney in Albuquerque. That September, the senior Udall asked his son for help in investigating a case in St. George, Utah. The city was east of and directly downwind from the Nevada Test Site, the Government's atomic-bomb testing range.
Radioactive debris from many of the atomic blasts in the 1950's and early 60's had fallen on St. George. By the late 70's, St. George was seeing excessive levels of cancer. In a Mormon population that did not smoke or drink, women were killed by lung, breast and ovarian cancer; men, by bladder, skin and soft tissue cancers; children died of leukemia. The high incidence of cancer in St. George and across the Colorado Plateau would later be confirmed by health researchers from the University of Utah and the United States Public Health Service. But in 1978, the Department of Energy's field officers in Albuquerque and Las Vegas dismissed reports of health problems as the hysterics of an ill-informed public.
The Utah case and several others Stewart Udall brought on behalf of Navajo and uranium miners in the Southwest became landmarks in environmental and public-health law. They were the first to reveal how the Government's nuclear cold warriors had disregarded the safety of their own people.
On Oct. 3, 1991, when word reached Tom Udall that Watkins had announced a shipment of radioactive wastes to New Mexico, he proceeded to mount a counterattack. On Oct. 9, he sent Deputy Attorney General Manuel Tijerina to Washington to file suit in Judge John Garrett Penn's District Court to block the shipment. Three weeks later, Udall also flew to Washington, spending five days preparing his case. On Nov. 15, he walked into Judge Penn's court.
Citing the Interior Department land order of 1983, which prohibited the transport of radioactive wastes to WIPP, Udall characterized the unexplained change of policy in January 1991 as the unlawful breaking of a solemn pact by the Government with the people of New Mexico.
"From the start of this project, this promise was contained in Congressional testimony, in budget documents, in letters back and forth between assistant secretaries in the Department of Energy and the Department of Interior, and in various public land orders. The promise was . . . embodied in the law."
Displaying a photograph of the myriad structural problems he contended still existed with WIPP, Udall singled out the roof collapses. In effect, he argued, once the Energy Department put any radioactive wastes in WIPP, it would not be able to get the drums out at the end of the five years of tests. This was tantamount to a permanent change in the use of the public land, and only Congress, Udall said, had the authority to permanently withdraw Federal land.
Udall's case boiled down to proving that the repository was unstable. And the proof was in the Government's own documents. Pressed by the geological weight of the rock layers above it, WIPP's salt walls are closing in at a rate three times faster than the Government had anticipated. The result is that the first rooms mined in WIPP in the early 1980's began collapsing in 1990.
On June 19, 1990, a team of technicians were stunned to see a pile of rubble lying in the room they had been taking measurements only days before. "We could have been in there when it happened, and thinking about it even now scares the hell out of me," says Tia Mills, a 25-year-old member of the team, who left WIPP last October. "Every time we went into that room, we discussed it among ourselves that we shouldn't be in there, because when that stuff comes down it doesn't give you any warning."
That incident caused a furor at WIPP. Managers were transferred. An investigation revealed that rock gauges were picking up swifter rates of salt movement in the ceiling of the room, but at WIPP no one had read the computer printouts for at least one month prior to the collapse. "We're looking at that data now day by day," says Wendell D. Weart, WIPP's chief scientist and a geophysicist at the Sandia National Laboratories, which is WIPP's technical adviser.
Scientists at Sandia have dismissed the importance of most of the roof collapses. According to Weart, five of the six ceiling collapses were in experimental rooms mined early in the 1980's that had been heated to study the salt's "creep." He insists that formulas have been developed for predicting a collapse -- well before ceilings collapsed and rooms shown to be unstable were sealed off.
Early last year, Watkins appointed a panel of independent mining experts to study the situation. It concluded that the stability of the ceiling in the storage room to be used for the experiments could not be guaranteed before the tests were completed. Some panel members suggested a system of beams and bolts to support the ceilings, which Watkins ordered installed last summer. State and Federal mine inspectors who evaluated the system said they were confident the new ceiling support would keep the room open long enough to conduct the tests.
But Udall wasn't convinced. Jack Parker, a geologist and mining engineer from White Pine, Mich., and a member of Watkins's special panel to evaluate the collapses, said in an affidavit for Udall that the roof supports would not work.
The Energy Department's lawyers argued that Stewart Udall was overstating the problems and the repository was sound and safe. They also argued that Lujan's removal of the restriction prohibiting the transport of nuclear wastes into the area was legal and valid.
On Nov. 26, however, Judge Penn issued a temporary injunction. It declared Watkins's maneuver to be illegal and barred the Energy Department from hauling any wastes to WIPP. The judge agreed with Udall that the Interior Department and the Energy Department had no authority under law to change the land order in January 1991. The Energy Department, further stated the judge, had "presented no convincing evidence that the hazardous-waste materials they seek to introduce in the WIPP site can be retrieved." He concluded that Udall had shown that New Mexicans could suffer harm if radioactive wastes were introduced in a repository that was not ready to accept them.
On Feb. 3, 1992, Judge Penn made the prohibition permanent until the Energy Department did as Udall demanded and gained Congressional approval for the opening of WIPP. The same day, the judge also ruled on separate lawsuits filed by two national environmental groups and two state environmental organizations; he barred Watkins from placing any wastes in WIPP until the Energy Department completed the process of securing a hazardous-waste handling permit from the State of New Mexico.
In March, the Energy Department appealed the rulings. On July 10, the United States Court of Appeals for the District of Columbia Circuit upheld the lower court's decision, affirming the law that said Congress must enact the necessary legislation to open WIPP.
In June, the National Academy of Sciences said in a report on WIPP that most of the experiments planned by the Energy Department for its opening had little scientific merit.
Last month, the House approved its version of a bill that would transfer the land above the repository from the Department of the Interior to the Department of Energy. But the House version included at least five new barriers that could indefinitely prevent WIPP from opening. Among the provisions in the House bill is one that requires the Environmental Protection Agency to establish new standards for the disposal of radioactive wastes before any can be put in WIPP. In 1987, the old standards were struck down by a Federal Appellate Court in Boston for being too lax, and the E.P.A. has yet to complete a draft of the new standards. Another provision in the House measure will require public comment and E.P.A. review of any test proposed for the repository.
The Senate had passed its version of the bill last November, and a conference committee to resolve the differences between the two bills has just begun to meet.
Lately, Watkins and his aides have adopted a glum new motto that is heard frequently in the dark halls of the Energy Department's Washington headquarters: Waste is a terrible thing to mind. The fear among the Government's engineers and their critics is that WIPP's considerable problems may be insurmountable and that a billion-dollar investment may go down the drain.
Although the theory of using salt to emtomb radioactive wastes is a sound one, say many scientists, what WIPP has shown is that in the 1990's, when it comes to nuclear issues, the technical problems are far less formidable than the political ones. Since the Manhattan Project of World War II to build the bomb, the ethos of the industry's leaders has been to build technically sophisticated, one-of-a-kind nuclear facilities under costly crash programs. What opponents of the WIPP program are saying is that the building of these nuclear facilities cannot be decided solely by scientists or the Energy Department: the public has to be involved in every phase of development if such projects are to have any chance of success.
In the meantime, the Energy Department is spending $14 million a month to keep WIPP's staff (now numbering 900) together and the equipment operating. Plutonium wastes slated to be buried at WIPP remain stored in hundreds of thousands of steel drums in California, Nevada, Washington, Idaho, Colorado, New Mexico, Illinois, Tennessee, South Carolina and Ohio. Vast stretches of land contaminated by dangerous nuclear-bomb industry wastes still need to be cleaned up in these and 20 other states as well as in Puerto Rico and several Pacific Islands; if and when that is done, it is anybody's guess whether the mountains of contaminants that will be collected will have a final storage place.

If punctured, a reservoir could push contaminants to the surface while they're still harmful, which they will be for 240,000 years. HEADLINE: Bus Companies Get Ready for School

BYLINE: By PENNY SINGER

 BODY:
IN the wake of a recent fatal charter-bus crash in New Jersey, the approaching new school year brings questions about where school districts get the buses that transport thousands of Westchester students each day, and how they monitor the performance of the drivers and the vehicles.
"Transportation is especially important in the northern part of the county where distances are greater," said Dr. Jeffrey Hansen, deputy superintendent of the Bedford Central School District, which is composed of five elementary schools, a middle school and a high school. 
 "We supply bus service to 3,000 students within a 50-mile radius and that includes special-ed students who must be taken to schools as far away as Manhattan and Nyack," he said. "Our transportation budget is $3 million a year."
This year, the contract that covers about 40 percent of the students in the Bedford district has been awarded to the Bedford Bus Company, a division of the White Plains Bus Company that has been certified to carry students. Dr. Hansen said that Bedford was one of five companies bidding on a three-year, $3.7 million contract.
Dr. Hansen said districts choose bus companies by first sending out specifications to local companies interested in obtaining the business.
 
A Rigorous Process
 "Specifications are very detailed," he said. "We ask them about their financial ability. We tell them the number of mechanics we expect them to have on duty, and we ask them how many backup people they employ. We also demand that they provide us with an on-site safety coordinator. Eventually we contact the Department of Motor Vehicles to check out the backgrounds of their drivers."
Then, in accordance with state law, the contract must go to the lowest bidder. But unlike some small charter bus companies that skirt the law by evading state registration and inspection requirements, chartered school buses must meet stiff state and national regulations before they can carry students.
"They don't go out on the road until they are certified by a State Transportation Department inspector, who makes the inspection in person," Dr. Hansen said. "And the inspectors are very strict. I've seen them fail a bus because a tiny piece of chrome was missing from a bumper."
Running a bus company properly, said John Silvani, the president of the White Plains Bus Company and its Bedford Bus division, requires both a lot of capital and a lot of labor.
Referring to the charter bus companies that are not licensed to carry students, he said: "The way the system is set up is that it relies on owners to see that their drivers are tested, their buses are inspected and that all the information is reported properly to the state. Who knows how many gypsy charter companies are operating illegally. The Department of Transportation doesn't know because they don't have enough inspectors to go out in the field and track down every company that puts an ad in the Yellow Pages."
A new coach costs $250,000, Mr. Silvani said. "Then there's a minimum $5 million liability insurance per vehicle. That's a big investment. The gypsies buy an old Greyhound for $25,000 to $30,000 and they register it improperly. Eventually they drop the insurance and keep the plates and for all practical purposes that bus is invisible -- there are no records of it anywhere until something happens."
 
Operates 200 Buses
 Mr. Silvani, who bought the White Plains Bus Company 18 months ago, has been in the transportation business since 1968, when he began as a part-time driver for the Riverdale Country Day School while he was an undergraduate at Manhattan College in Riverdale.
"Although my father, who is a professor at Fordham, encouraged me to study mechanical engineering, I went into the bus business because I found it so interesting," he said. "I started driving a school bus for a Yonkers company."
Mr. Silvani operates a fleet of 200 school buses. In addition to the Bedford School District, he has contracts with school districts in White Plains, Elmsford and Harrison. The White Plains Bus Company also serves day camps and conducts an active charter bus business.
"This year we have several of the new forward-control school buses," he said, referring to a new snub-nosed model with a wraparound windshield. "They cost $45,000 apiece, which is 20 to 30 percent more than a conventional school bus, but they have superior visibility."
The newer school buses in his fleet have seat belts, but the state has not required that older buses be retrofitted with belts because school officials say it would be too costly and would not necessarily improve safety on the older models.
Drivers earn $12 an hour, with a guarantee of a minimum four hours work a day, or 20 hours a week, he said. Senior drivers can earn $32,000 to $33,000 a year.
Mr. Silvani said that unlike the 1980's, when unemployment was low and recruiting drivers was difficult, "today we are finding both quantity and quality out there."
"We've hired several professionals, including a construction engineer who was laid off last year," he said. "We like drivers who live in the community, retirees and women. About 35 to 40 percent of our drivers are women but we'd like more because women are especially good with the younger children."
New drivers are given a classroom and road-training program before taking the Department of Motor Vehicles test, he said.
 
A Demanding Job
 "They also have to pass a rigorous physical test, which, among other things, measures hand-eye coordination," he added. "Recently we had 60 candidates at the Fox Lane campus in Bedford preparing for the Motor Vehicle test. Among them were a 57-year-old retired janitor and a 50-year-old former president of a division of a Fortune 500 company who lives in Bedford, took early retirement and found he wants to do something -- and that something is driving a school bus."
Mr. Silvani said he works closely with the Bedford School District to coordinate the schedules and design the routes for 400 separate bus runs a day, a total of 5,000 miles.
"A bus ride is part of the whole school experience a child goes through," he said, adding that he believes a bus company's performance affects the educational environment. "But our drivers are trained to keep discipline in accordance with the school's own practices."
Transporting youngsters to school is a recession-proof business, Mr. Silvani remarked. He said profits average 5 to 10 percent a year.
"In this business, building equity is more important than operating profits," he said. "The real profit comes from owning equipment. A $45,000 bus that you can sell for $15,000 nine years later becomes an asset."

HEADLINE: Love for Sale

BYLINE: By David Nasaw; David Nasaw, who teaches history at the Graduate Center and the College of Staten Island of the City University of New York, is the author of "Children of the City: At Work and at Play."

 BODY:

CITY OF EROS
New York City, Prostitution, and the Commercialization of Sex, 1790-1920.
By Timothy J. Gilfoyle.
Illustrated. 462 pp. New York:
W. W. Norton & Company. $24.95. 
 IN January of 1857, the Rev. William Berrian matter-of-factly informed his congregation at Trinity Church, one of New York's wealthiest and most prominent parishes, that during his 50-year ministry he had visited "houses of ill fame" some 10 times. "The congregants, and other New Yorkers, reacted with surprising aplomb," writes Timothy J. Gilfoyle in "City of Eros," a fascinating study of prostitution in 19th-century New York City. "So commonplace and central was prostitution to metropolitan life that even the philanderings of a prominent minister hardly shocked New York."
From 1820 to World War I, prostitutes lived and worked in almost every neighborhood in the city and solicited in bakeries, cigar stores, delicatessens, barber shops, hotels, saloons, theaters and the best restaurants. They were visited by men of every ethnic group, social class and occupation. There were brothels located in Corlears Hook, at the easternmost end of Manhattan, for the laborers and seamen who lived nearby; and brothels on the West Side, convenient to Columbia College and the neighboring College of Physicians and Surgeons.
Mr. Gilfoyle, an assistant professor of history at Loyola University in Chicago, does not simply catalogue the omnipresence of the prostitutes. He situates their trade in the economic life of the city. As he tells us, "the major factor inducing young women to sell their bodies was the low wages for female labor. . . . Like peddling, scavenging and ragpicking, prostitution turned something with little value into something with cash value."
The women were not the only ones who made money -- and certainly not the ones who made the most -- from selling their bodies. The city's landlords, from the aristocratic John R. Livingston to the anonymous corner grocer, profited by renting space to prostitutes at premium rates. Corrupt politicians and policemen demanded and received protection money for looking the other way. Rent money and payoffs together went a long way toward insuring the survival of the trade, no matter how vociferous and well organized the remonstrations of vice crusaders like the Rev. Charles Parkhurst and Anthony Comstock.
Mr. Gilfoyle's story ends rather abruptly in the 1920's, when, according to his account, "commercial sex grew less public and increasingly covert." Though the reformers, the politicians and the police would take credit for the cleanup, the simple truth was that the economic and social forces that had made prostitution a viable proposition for working women now pushed it to the margins. Prostitution would not of course disappear entirely from the city, but it would never again be as public or profitable as it had been.
"City of Eros" is social history at its best, beautifully written, with a mosaic of rich detail that informs but does not overwhelm the narrative line. Mr. Gilfoyle introduces us to dozens of incredible characters and allows them to speak directly to us from the pages of court records, guidebooks, vice commission investigations, newspaper reports and exquisitely stylized descriptions of the New York underworld. One wonders at times if he does not place too much faith in the veracity of his witnesses -- especially the vice crusaders who had a stake in exaggerating the prevalence of prostitution. And while he is to be commended for rescuing these working women from anonymous victimhood, there are moments when Mr. Gilfoyle overreaches his mark and underplays the exploitation, degradation, violence and disease that must have been as much part of prostitutes' lives as the dollars they earned.
Still, in the end one comes away convinced that his account is an honest and accurate one. At a moment in our history when talk of family values is cheaper than ever, it is well worth our effort to ponder, as Mr. Gilfoyle has done, the economic necessities that have played so large a part in shaping our sexual mores, our practices and, yes, even our values.

HEADLINE: All About/Children's Books;
In the Thriving Land of Fairy Tale Sales

BYLINE: By M. P. DUNLEAVEY

 BODY:
Whoever said that fairy tales were really written for adults never anticipated today's children's book market. From sales of E. B. White's classic, "Charlotte's Web," to classics-in-waiting like "Tar Beach," or the best seller "Polar Express," children's books have been enjoying an almost recession-proof growth for five years. Likewise, bookstores, book clubs, wholesalers, publishing houses and even schools have been sharing in the bounty.
As millions of children think about starting school again, the publishing industry is already thinking about the winter holidays. This is when stores start stocking up for the gift-giving season, and though some retailers worry about a downturn, the industry's projected sales figures for 1992 look as upwardly mobile as ever. 
 Since 1986, publishers' net sales of children's books have doubled, to more than $1.1 billion in 1991 from $546 million, and industry studies project that sales this year may rise to $1.3 billion. That may sound like a lot of copies of "The Cat in the Hat," but in fact children's publishing has expanded way beyond its usual horizons thanks to several demographic, social and economic changes in the last decade.
First, the baby boom generation had babies. Generally older and better educated, these parents began buying books not just for pleasure but for preparation.
"Everything is for the children," said Cathy Linsk, owner of Watchung Books in Montclair, N.J., commenting on a trend she says has made parents and schools more focused on children's interests. "We even do a lot of ordering for public schools." And many in the industry say that educators and librarians are now just as likely to buy directly from a local store, often in search of books that are more culturally diverse.
"Having trade books in the classroom has opened up a huge new market," said Diane Roback, children's book editor of Publishers Weekly. Many schools are experimenting with literature-based reading programs that use storybooks instead of traditional texts. As a result, publishers' sales to children's retail stores have soared to about 40 percent from 20 percent a few years ago.
 
A New Story
Big Stores Just for Little Ones
 The most remarkable sign of this new demand is the number of children's bookstores that have sprouted across the country -- from The Treehouse in St. Cloud, Minn., to Books of Wonder in New York and Beverly Hills. Even well-known chains like Barnes & Noble and Waldenbooks now have junior chains for the junior set.
Just this month, B. Dalton Booksellers, a division of Barnes & Noble Inc., opened the first new mall store for children, called P. B. Pages. ("P. B." doesn't stand for anything, the company says.)
According to Book Industry Trends 1992, an industry sourcebook, the 76 percent growth of children's bookstores last year was second only to fast food restaurants. In 1985, there were only 215 independent children's bookstores in the country. Today there are nearly 500.
"There weren't a lot of children's books until we had that mini-baby boom," said Maureen Golden, a Barnes & Noble vice president who has expanded the company's children's departments. "Publishers started to fill the gap and we decided to give our children's sections more of an identity."
In addition to P. B. Pages, Barnes & Noble -- which has total revenues of about $892 million -- has opened 23 vast "superstores" since 1987. These stores all have B & N Jr., a children's store-within-a-store where parents can browse through some 15,000 titles shelved in brightly labeled sections. In some B & N Jr. stores kids can even attend pajama parties.
Waldenbooks, with total revenues of about $1.1 billion, also has a chain of 21 children's stores, called Waldenkids. Since these stores began catering just to children in 1987, sales have increased by about 135 percent, said Carol Baklarz, director of store operations. Both Waldenbooks and Barnes & Noble are publicly traded companies. Tower Books and Toys 'R' Us also have been expanding their children's books departments.
Some independent sellers see such expansion as a threat, though most seem to feel secure in their niche.
"Children's book sales are keeping us alive," said Ms. Linsk, whose Montclair store, which opened in November, also carries some books for grownups. "They're enabling us to maintain a nice adult selection."
Charles and Gertie Geck, an accountant and librarian respectively who live in St. Cloud, Minn., opened 12 years ago what Mr. Geck said was the first children's bookstore in the state. "Kids are definitely reading more," he said. "And they're reading books that have a lot more meat to them."
Children's books are more sophisticated now than they ever have been. The prominence of AIDS and other issues, like divorce, has brought a more open discussion of sexuality and life styles in society in general. "Daddy's Roommate," for example, is about a boy whose parents divorce and whose father then moves in with a gay lover.
 
Clubs for Kids
Spreading the Word By Mail
 Another indication of the sales trend in children's books is the rise in children's book clubs. In the last three years, both the Doubleday Book Club and the Book-of-the-Month Club started children's book clubs.
"The growth of clubs reflects the growth of independent stores," said Steven Geck, senior editor for the Children's Book-of-the-Month Club. These clubs are much like the adult clubs. Unlike the Scholastic Book Club, which is school-based, they are clubs that send catalogues out to individuals about every three weeks and offer discounts of up to 60 percent.
According to the Association of American Publishers, book club sales in 1991 were about $753 million. Though some industry studies suggest that book club sales are slipping, those clubs devoted to children's books say their sales are growing steadily. The clubs target children from the ages 2 to 12, and they typically offer thousands of titles .
"People join book clubs for different reasons than they go shopping," said Miranda DeKay, editor in chief of the Doubleday Children's Book Club, which began in 1989. "Not everyone lives near a good bookstore, and they aren't going to drive 40 miles with two toddlers. Clubs save time and money."
Scholastic is the country's oldest book club, beginning in the 1920's with classroom magazines. It caters to teachers who join the club and bring catalogues of mostly paperback, discounted books to their students. Prices range from 75 cents to $14.95.
"Part of our mission is to offer books that are affordable," said Jean Fiewel, editorial director for Scholastic Book Club and publishing company.
The club's 1991 revenues were about $150 million, about half the company's total. Scholastic publishes 400 books a year, but offers a mix of its own and other companies' books..
 
The Best Sellers
Distributors Who Even Reach Parents
 The creativity and teamwork involved in putting a children's book together, publishing it and distributing it around the country is a separate marvel.
In an industry where consumer advertising is almost unheard of and reviews are infrequent, wholesale distributors have become influential marketeers. The Ingraham Book Company, based in Nashville, is the largest of these, with six distribution centers nationwide. It has about 160,000 titles from more than 2,000 publishers, including 19,000 children's books.
Far from being just a supplier, wholesalers review a client's book needs, provide special catalogues to children's booksellers and even produce "tail spinners" -- brochures for the customers that highlight new titles. Ingraham even offers its customers a computer system for inventory analysis. Another company, Wordstock, gives a bookseller a weekly list of the top 50 best-selling children's books.
Unlike the adult market, children's best sellers typically take years to emerge, and then stay on the list for years. A publisher's backlist of older books is often more profitable than its current titles. "Charlotte's Web" is the No. 1 one best-selling paperback today, according to Publishers Weekly. It was first published by Harper & Row (now HarperCollins) 40 years ago.
 
 
TRANSLATING DR. SEUSS
 Why wait for high school for your child to learn another language when "La Gallinita Roja" (The Little Red Hen) is available now for that brilliant 3-year-old?
Mirasol, the Spanish-language division of Farrar, Straus & Giroux, started a year ago and now has translations of 12 titles from its regular list.
"There are an enormous number of children whose first language is Spanish," said Stephen Roxburgh, publisher of the children's book division who helped to start the imprint. Though several houses publish some translations, Mirasol is the only imprint for Spanish language books.
Random House, the country's largest children's book publisher, publishes many Sesame Street, Dr. Seuss and Berenstain Bear picture books in paperback for Spanish language readers.
"We're doing this in an experimental way," said Janet Schulman, publisher of books for young readers at Random House. "The Spanish-speaking market is one with large purchasing power."
Books in other languages, including French, German and Japanese, have been available for years, but the growth of the children's book market has encouraged publishers to explore different corners of the market.
"A lot of parents adopt children from other countries," said Charles Geck, co-owner of The Treehouse, a children's bookstore in St. Cloud, Minn. Mr. Geck has been surprised by the demand for books in different languages.

A DEVASTATED CITY'S TALE

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: Senate Race;
Where Anita Hill Is the Silent Third Candidate

BYLINE: By ISABEL WILKERSON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: PHILADELPHIA, Aug. 26

 BODY:
Against the low drone of an air-conditioner at a roadside inn near Scranton, a dozen campaign workers sat briefing Senator Arlen Specter, Republican of Pennsylvania, on how a certain woman was threatening his bid for re-election.
One by one, they gave their reports, about how Republican women still empathized with her, about how everyone would have to work hard to convince people, given the situation, that Mr. Specter supported women's issues and how, in fact, some people were beginning to come around. 
 The woman at the top of everyone's agenda was not Lynn Yeakel, the Democratic nominee whose upset primary victory made her a household name, although her candidacy against Mr. Specter was implicit in the discussion.
The woman was Anita F. Hill, the University of Oklahoma law professor whose appearance before the Senate Judiciary Committee last fall galvanized women across the country and forced Senator Specter, the panel's lead questioner, to defend his role in the hearings while asking for people's votes.
 
Accusing Hill of Perjury
 If the now-famous scene of white men questioning a black woman about sexual harassment has come to symbolize the exclusivity of national politics, many would say that Senator Specter has, deservedly or not, come to be seen as the parliamentarian of the men's club.
His interrogation of Professor Hill, his public accusation that she perjured herself, the repeated playbacks of it in Ms. Yeakel's primary campaign commercials and the questions that now still arise about the hearings are referred to in the Specter organization as the "Anita Hill situation."
The hearings are an issue in many women's candidacies this year. But more than in any of the other campaigns, Professor Hill is, in effect, the silent third candidate in a tight race in Pennsylvania, a contest that is the closest of any to a referendum on the hearings.
"Here," said Bonnie DiCarlo, a field consultant in Pittsburgh for the Yeakel campaign, "we can actually get the man who did it."
Indeed on the campaign trail, some women clench their teeth and blurt out words like "vile" and "despicable" when asked about Senator Specter's questioning of Professor Hill. Many women said they believed Mr. Specter's tone of questioning was arrogant and patronizing.
At a meeting of the United Automobile Workers in Harrisburg, a woman asked Mr. Specter if voters could "expect more of this kind of conduct" if they re-elected him. At a reception in Scranton, faithful Republicans whispered, shamefaced, that they believed Professor Hill's accusations that Clarence Thomas had sexually harassed her years before his Supreme Court nomination, when the two worked together in Government agencies.
 
Specter Confronts Issue
 Rather than ignore such anger, Senator Specter attacks it head-on. Whether he is in Pittsburgh or Gettysburg, to mine workers or homemakers, the converted or the skeptical, he brings up the hearings before anyone else can. It is an old prosecutor's strategy of getting the bad news over with.
"What I was trying to do was find out what happened between one man and one woman a long time ago," he says to each of his audiences. "It has candidly been a learning experience for me to see the depth and extent of the raw nerve that was touched by the hearings. I have learned that sexual harassment is much more widespread than I had previously thought."
He asks that he not be judged by the hearings alone. "Whatever anyone thinks of my questioning of Professor Hill, there is nobody in the Senate with a stronger record on women's issues than me."
Senator Specter is indeed viewed as one of the stronger Republican supporters of women's issues. He supports abortion rights and supported the family leave bill.
He says the Anita Hill matter is the only weapon his opponent has against him. "I start with the proposition that I have a tough race on my hands," Senator Specter said in an interview this week. "I feel bad that so many people felt such anguish over the subject."
 
'Omnipresent' Issue
 Some of his supporters says they believe he has taken an unfair beating and do not like to see him doing back flips to please critics. "He's done everything but emasculate himself," said John Minora, a Scranton lawyer and a longtime supporter of Mr. Specter.
Political analysts say he has little choice but to confront the issue. "It's there, it's omnipresent," said G. Terry Madonna, director of the Center for Politics and Public Affairs at Millersville University in Millersville, Pa. "He might as well bring it up. It comes up one way or another."
But this race is much more complicated than whether voters sympathized with Professor Hill or Mr. Specter. The campaign pits a moderate Republican, two-term Senator against a liberal advocate for women's issues who has never held elective office and is riding a national movement.
"People are looking for change," Ms. Yeakel said in a recent interview. "I embody change."
On the campaign trial, Mr. Specter is the master politician, slapping backs and pumping palms, never having to be reminded to acknowledge the state legislator or small-town mayor who arranged the campaign breakfast.
Ms. Yeakel is the eager newcomer who is still getting to know local organizers, still fine tuning some of her positions and who, despite growing up around politicians, still approaches campaigning with a kind of wonder, calling Mr. Specter "the ultimate scary opponent."
"This is like an out-of-body experience," she said in the interview. "It's like being in a movie. It's as if I'm watching myself and saying, 'Who is that person and what is she doing?' "
Mr. Specter's strategy has been to dismiss Ms. Yeakel as a single-issue candidate and emphasize his experience on the Appropriations Committee and his role as a Senate compromiser.
Ms. Yeakel's strategy has been to link Mr. Specter to the Hill-Thomas confrontation and to the Bush Administration, while casting herself as an agent of change in the mold of Gov. Bill Clinton of Arkansas, and she never lets a speech go by without referring to "the failed policies of the Reagan-Bush-Specter era." She rarely mentions Professor Hill; she does not have to.
Both campaigns have their problems. Mr. Specter is carrying the weight not only of the hearings but of a sour economy and voter discontent with incumbents and President Bush. Most analysts say they consider the race close, although among women voters polls show Senator Specter trailing Ms. Yeakel by nearly 2 to 1.
It would not likely help Mr. Specter with women that one of his supporters, Larry Yatch, a former state Democratic chairman, was quoted in The Harrisburg Patriot-News and other newspapers as mocking Ms. Yeakel and other women with the remark, "Here, I've got breasts; vote for me."
It has been a tough few months for Ms. Yeakel as well. She is trying to define herself to voters who know her only for her advertisements linking Mr. Specter to Professor Hill and who have increasingly come to learn about her through negative reports and her own campaign missteps.
Probably her biggest gaffe was at a televised news conference in Erie last month where she stood in front of a shuttered department store and blamed the economic policies of Mr. Specter and President Reagan for its closing, only to find out later that the store had closed when Jimmy Carter was President.
Among damaging developments for Ms. Yeakel are some that could hurt her among black voters, a group that she needs badly to win in November. Senator Specter has done well with blacks in previous campaigns, winning a third of the their votes in 1980 and a fifth in 1986.
 
Specter Tries to Soften Image
 In the last few months, Ms. Yeakel has tried to deflect criticism about her affiliation with an all-white country club, to which her husband belongs and which she says she no longer visits, and segregationist stands taken by her father, Porter Hardy, when he was a Democratic Congressman from Virginia in the 1960's. He opposed the Civil Rights Act of 1964 and the Voting Rights Act of 1965.
Mr. Specter crams his schedule with appearances before women, and he has tried to soften his image by talking more about his childhood during the Depression as the son of Russian-Polish immigrants. He has distanced himself from the Bush Administration and the Republican Party, but he has also reminded audiences of the value of experience in Washington. The other veteran Pennsylvania Senator, John Heinz 3d, died in a helicopter crash last year and was succeeded by Senator Harris Wofford, a Democrat.
The question facing both candidates as they travel the coal country and suburbs and mill towns of Pennsylvania is, will people remember the Clarence Thomas hearings, and if they do, how many votes might it cost the incumbent?
 
'His True Colors Came Out'
 There are a lot of women who feel the way Liz Bettinger, a plastics plant worker who is a ranking official in the A.F.L.-C.I.O., does: they have not been able to forgive Mr. Specter. "You look at him, and you see Anita Hill," Ms. Bettinger said. "You can say you're for women all you want, but his true colors came out."
Then there are women like Lisa Demler, an engineering consultant in Allentown, who see Mr. Specter as a good soldier just following orders. "It was a nasty job, and I wasn't proud of it," Mrs. Demler said. "But looking back, I wouldn't hold it against him."
Supporters of Mr. Specter are acutely aware of the cloud hanging over their candidate. The men and women who sat around a cramped conference table in Scranton gave reports from the front.
"There's a segment out there that, frankly, believes she was telling the truth and they're militant about it," Mr. Minora told the group. "That's the problem."

HEADLINE: THE 1992 CAMPAIGN: Issues -- Tort Reform;
Selling Voters on Bush, As Nemesis of Lawyers

BYLINE: By ALESSANDRA STANLEY

 BODY:
The President's aides have found something they think is even scarier to voters than Willie Horton: lawyers.
George Bush and Vice President Dan Quayle have begun painting Bill Clinton as a captive of a special interest group, "sharp lawyers" in "tasseled loafers" who, as Mr. Bush put it in his speech to the Republican convention, are "running wild," terrorizing doctors and even Little League coaches with personal-injury suits, malpractice suits and other kinds of liability cases. 
 Many trial lawyers said they were insulted right down to their tassel-free footwear. Many, including one lawyer in New Rochelle who is seeking $5 million from Barbara Bush's stepmother in a personal-injury lawsuit, also protested that the President could not possibly have been referring to them.
 
Shoot-Out Over Liability
 The Clinton campaign has countered that it is the President who is the captive of special-interest groups, fighting consumers on behalf of insurance companies and large corporations who want to curb the average American's access to civil court and large damage awards.
As family values become a less prominent campaign issue, passed over for lack of popular demand, a battle over legal values may surface in its place. Complex issues involving the state courts, tort law and the public interest have been drawn as starkly as the plot of an old-fashioned western. But instead of cattlemen and farmers, the combatants are insurance companies and the lawyers who sue them.
Though trial lawyers view themselves as the champions of the little guy, theirs is not the kind of vocation that tugs at the heart strings of voters. "If you gave the average American a bullet and asked whether he would shoot the lawyer or the insurance company man," said James Carville, Mr. Clinton's senior campaign strategist, "you'd create a lot of angst out there."
It is hardly a secret that as a group, lawyers have an image problem. This summer, the Maritime center in Norwalk, Conn., charged visitors $7.50 to view its live shark exhibit, but lawyers were offered free admission as "a professional courtesy."
Recent public-opinion surveys suggest that Americans viscerally dislike lawyers and feel that society is, in the words of one Bush campaign focus-group participant, "sue-happy." Republican strategists say such attitudes make them increasingly confident that "tort reform," the term generally applied to any law designed to dissuade people from suing or juries from handing out generous awards, can be made a partisan campaign issue.
As Fred Steeper, a Bush poll-taker, put it rather gleefully: "Trial lawyers today have the same favorability rating as Richard Nixon in 1974."
Tort reform first surfaced as an issue in state legislatures in the 1970's, when insurance premiums for doctors, schools and drivers began skyrocketing. It crystalized as a Presidential campaign issue last year. Mr. Quayle, who as head of the President's competitiveness council has led the charge, argued that excessive litigation had forced up the cost of insurance, which in turn had forced up the costs of goods and services, causing the United States to lose its competitive edge with other industrialized countries.
 
A State Court Problem
 The Administration has been pushing for changes in Federal tort law, but in fact the vast majority of lawsuits are litigated in state courts. So most reforms lie with state legislatures, and the best any Administration can hope to contribute to the cause is moral authority. Among other things, the Administration has proposed caps on damage awards and forcing a losing plaintiff to pay legal costs.
On the political stage, however, the Bush campaign has developed an election year syllogism worthy of Ionesco: Mr. Clinton is a lawyer. Lawyers are bad. Mr. Clinton is bad.
The Bush campaign has pointed out that lawyers, as a group, have contributed twice as much to Mr. Clinton as they have to Mr. Bush. According to a recent study by the Center for Responsive Politics, Mr. Clinton has received nearly $2.6 million from lawyers and lawyer-lobbyists; Mr. Bush has received $1.3 million.
Clinton aides cite the same study's finding that Mr. Bush received more than $2.6 million from the real estate, financial and insurance industries.
 
Lawyers, Teachers and Labor
 The latest round in the battle occurred Thursday, when Vice President Dan Quayle made public a letter from the president of the Arkansas Trial Lawyers Association praising Mr. Clinton's opposition to tort reform. Mr. Quayle cited the letter as evidence that the Democratic candidate was "totally in the pocket of trial lawyers."
The Republicans have also attacked the American Bar Association as overly liberal, citing as evidence appearances at the A.B.A.'s recent national convention by Anita F. Hill and Hillary Clinton.
"The A.B.A. is a pillar of the Democratic Party, along with teachers and labor," said Jim Pinkerton, a Bush campaign adviser. "The left has an impulse to declare everyone a victim, and lawyers pounce on that."
In 1988, the Bush campaign portrayed the Democratic nominee, Gov. Michael S. Dukakis as a soft-on-crime captive of the American Civil Liberties Union. Mr. Pinkerton said that tort reform could be as potent an issue this year because it could be presented as a cause of the nation's economic troubles.
 
Issue or Diversion?
 "Everyone pays higher insurance costs," he said. "People look at the cost of a stepladder or a dose of insulin and attribute it to liability."
Mr. Clinton's advisers dismiss the talk of tort reform as a diversion. "I don't think average voters think litigation has put a stanglehold on the American economy," Mr. Carville said. "I think people think Mr. Bush's economic policies are responsible for the stranglehold."
And from Park Avenue to Houston, lawyers in wingtips and cowboy boots are defending their honor. "Tort reform is just code for closing the courthouse door to poor people," said Joseph D. Jamail Jr., a Texas trial lawyer. Mr. Jamail is widely known as the King of Torts for winning a $10 billion award for Pennzoil in its suit against Texaco -- 40 times the largest amount previously awarded in an American damage suit.
 
Lawyers May Fight Back
 Robert MacCrate, a partner at Sullivan and Cromwell, and a former president of the American Bar Association, said of the Republican pressure, "It offends me, but I feel the attacks are so specious and distorted that they can only redound to the detriment of the accuser."
The American Bar Association is not so sure. It has hired a Washington consulting firm to help restore its image. "They want to fight back," said Tim Ridley, a consultant with the Communications Company, a division of the political consulting firm, Squier, Eskew, Knapp and Ochs Communications, which is advising Mr. Clinton.
"They want the Administration to know they are not the fifth column of the Democratic Party," Mr. Ridley said, adding that only about a third of the A.B.A.'s members are Democrats, with the rest either Republicans or independents.
Tort reform is not entirely a partisan issue. Senator Daniel K. Inouye, Democrat of Hawaii, has spoken out in favor it. Mr. Clinton supports a few limited measures, such as voluntary alternative dispute resolutions. And despite the opposition of consumer advocates like Ralph Nader, some longtime liberals favor it.
And Charles Peters, editor of the Washington Monthly, while agreeing with Mr. Quayle that "Democrats have sold out to the trial lawyers lobby," also believes that Republicans have taken the right stand for the wrong reasons.
Faulting the President and Mr. Quayle for not demanding concessions from doctors, hospitals and insurance companies, he said sternly: "I consider it completely accidental that the Republicans have embraced the cause of virtue."
