HEADLINE: AFTER THE STORM;
Alien Terrain Replaces What Was Once Home

BYLINE: By CATHERINE S. MANEGOLD, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: HOMESTEAD, Fla., Aug. 30

 BODY:
It was the trees that so many people spoke of.
In the midst of the devastation, the privations and the pain, the people of Homestead last week sometimes stopped, mid-conversation, and remarked on a pine tree that had grown with them, an acacia that was a benchmark for their lives, a towering mangrove that was a testament to their own struggles, achievements and now loss. 
 They spoke of the scent of the leaves, the shape of a particular trunk or crown, the shade from the burning South Florida sun and the stretches of green that had made Homestead special. All gone.
They pointed, disconsolately, at groves of fruit trees -- lime, avocado, mango and star fruit -- that were literally lifted up from fields to the east and deposited, roots skyward, to the west like a lost scene from "The Wizard of Oz."
As they gazed dully at a landscape transformed, they said that they could hardly find their way around. Street signs and shop signs were reduced to scraps of steel and flecks of paint. Even buildings were barely recognizable skeletons of wood and steel. Residents interrupted themselves with a start as they tried to give directions. With looks of befuddlement they would pause and apologize: "Oh. That's gone. My landmarks are gone. I don't know how to tell you where to go."
With so many trees down, the hulks of houses, buildings and other new focal points suddenly stood out against the desolate landscape. Even the animals were wind-shocked, easily spooked, yet drifting close, as though encouraged to find fellow survivors.
 
Lost Parrots and Broken Cars
 Few wars or acts of God have ever had this fury. In a matter of hours, in the dark before dawn, the winds of Hurricane Andrew swept through southern Dade County on Monday and took Homestead off the map. This is the story of the town that was, of what it has lost, and what it may eventually become again as residents, shop owners, retirees, farmers and fruit growers try to piece themselves back together after what may be the most destructive natural disaster in America's history.
Every corner of the Homestead region knew a tragedy. Rising to speeds of 150 miles an hour, the winds wiped out Ken Kwan's $100,000 worth of tropical parrots and drove the door right off its hinges even though five people stood inside, their weight pitched hard against the force.
In Leisure City, a town just northeast of Homestead, the winds blew the aluminum siding from the Air Base hangars and sent them flying like razor blades. Cars were sliced in half, and steel sheets sat the next morning impaled like knife blades in concrete walls and wooden siding. Few could have imagined the force. No one had ever seen anything like it.
In the Redlands, an area of fields and fruit groves north of Homestead, a huge truck lay a distance of two football fields back from the road, tipped on its side in the middle of a bean field.
Over at the Americana Village Estates mobile home park off Krome Avenue at 180th Avenue, residents showed off the littered, sodden remains of new furniture and expensive carpet and pointed to where walls and roofs used to be. Old photographs of better times and well-tended gardens exchanged hands.
They talked of the closeness the neighborhood enjoyed, the park's swimming pool and club house, the Monday night bingo games, the Tuesday ceramic classes and the long morning runs down rural roads that stand like a grid among the groves of the Redlands.
Eva Serrano, a native of Hoboken, N.J., who lived in mobile home lot 265, cried as she spoke of a daughter who had died of brain cancer in 1978. For years, she said, she had saved her daughter's bedroom set as something to remember her by.
It was shredded in the wind when the east wall of her mobile home trailer blew away and the rest of her home was demolished.
"My sister's home is gone," she said flatly. "My mother's home is gone. My brother's home is gone. We can't stay here now, because now there are rats."
Homestead residents started burning the debris from their demolished houses and toppled trees today, and huge plumes of black smoke rose above the town and could be seen and smelled as far away as downtown Miami. Rain teemed for the second straight day, soaking furniture and clothes that residents had tried so hard to dry.
The rain brought another problem, as clouds of mosquitoes rose from the damp ground, bringing with them the threat of disease. Those houses that were left standing had no wire screens to keep the insects out.
For some, the hurricane was only the beginning of a chain reaction of troubles. Carmen Rivera Myers and her husband, William, survived the storm by moving into their daughter's house in Princeton, several miles to the northeast. They went back to Homestead on Tuesday to find the roof of their apartment building demolished but their own home hardly damaged.
Then the rains came.
At 2 A.M. Sunday, in a downpour, they were awakened by the sound of their living room ceiling caving in.
"We got up," said Mrs. Rivera Myers. "We ran to see what the sound was. The ceiling had collapsed. And while we stood there, the bedroom ceiling went, too. Now our place is completely demolished. We have lost everything."
Tonight, Mrs. Rivera Myers was bunking in a stuffy, overcrowded room at Homestead Middle School. Her husband, who has been unemployed since November 1991, stayed in the ruined apartment with a gun, watching over their remaining possessions. Already, bands of thieves had moved through the apartment building, carting off what they could and siphoning gas from cars in the lot.
Settling back on a small cot and hoping to get her first good sleep in six days, Mrs. Rivera Myers said the apartment had become a hazard. Food was rotting everywhere, and the standing water they had left before the storm had already gone bad.
Like many others she saw a reason in the disaster: to cleanse a town she said was already headed down the wrong track. After the hurricane, she said, her son had taken her on a drive through Leisure City. "All he did was point and say, over and over, that is a crack house, that is a crack house," she said. "Maybe this will clean our town up. I pray."
 
A Town of Fertile Land And Fickle Fortunes
 Homestead is a multilingual city of about 27,000 just 30 miles south of Miami. But it is a state of mind, too, for residents who use the name to speak loosely of a broader swath of land that includes the Homestead Air Force Base to the east, the fields and fruit groves of the Redlands inland, to the northwest, and the last-chance hotels and restaurants that are strung along U.S. 1 for tourists heading south to the Keys.
As residents sifted through the rubble that covered the ground for miles in all directions last week, they spoke of the region's charms, its problems and its idiosyncrasies. They said it was just another town along the road, really; a "mudhole" of a town, a town for "rednecks," one that has seen good times both come and go.
Built by immigrants and newcomers from other states who for the last 80 years came for the sun, it has shrunk and grown by turns, following the twists of fortune that attended every crop failure, bad storm or economic slump. Even the Air Force base, the biggest employer and biggest customer around, was destroyed once before by a hurricane, in 1945, and then rebuilt.
Blessed with a climate that nurtures crops of all kinds, Homestead is cursed, too, with the fickleness of that climate and the vagaries of the tourist trade. Even before the hurricane, the persistent recession had already choked off visitors' dollars and left the place waiting for better times.
It was a town for gamblers of one sort or another, with a thriving illegal drug trade, a mosaic of ethnic groups, a deep bitterness toward Washington. People say, perhaps overstating the case a bit, that there are more guns than people here.
Farming, fruit and the cocaine trade made for the largest fortunes. Smaller ones were the work of ingenuity, pluck and imagination. It was a sprawling place with a small-town feel where the rodeo each February had all the government employees going to work in cowboy hats or frilly dresses.
Somewhere in the ruins of what this place once was, there lies a road map of what it might have been. There is a bicycle velodrome on that map, an equestrian park and an Olympic shooting range. "We had a lot of plans," said Maj. Charles Habermehl, a mustached, plain-talking officer with the Homestead Police Department who has been working nearly around the clock as the operations commander for the emergency. "But forget about the velodrome when you don't even have any buildings standing."
Some projects were already completed. Most are now gone or in need of so much repair that they might as well be. The town's community pavilion, once the site of boxing matches, clog-dancing demonstrations, huge family reunions and the rodeo, was blown to pieces. The Homestead Sports Complex, which had recently housed some of the trials for the Olympic baseball team, was badly damaged, too.
All week, residents struggled for a way to describe what they saw. Veterans spoke of war. Others said only a nuclear holocaust could have such impact. Some merely repeated, over and over on the first days, that nothing could have prepared them for this.
The Air Base was virtually totaled. So were entire city blocks. Almost no structure came through unscathed. But the damage was selective, almost capricious. One house might stand intact next to a dozen fragments made of a wall here, a piece of flooring there. Amid a junkyard's worth of debris is a refrigerator, bolt upright, the food rotting inside it. Most areas looked as though they had suffered enemy shelling for weeks. Some, like the Quail Roost trailer park in Redlands, were smashed end to end into uncountable pieces.
 
Under the Palms, Living Was Never Very Easy
 To give homeless families a decent place to rest, Jessie Hanna, a 26-year-old maintenance worker with a Howard Johnson's hotel on U.S. 1, has been cleaning up debris in the ravaged hotel, which has one of the few working phones in the area but still lacks electricity and running water. His own house, an $80,000 home across from the air base, was gone, he said, nothing more than heaps of wood and litter. The only rooms left standing were the laundry and the garage.
"I worked so hard trying to accomplish something, and now I don't have it," he said as he took a break on a water-soaked chair on the terrace of a first-floor room.
Mr. Hanna said that he and his wife, Dorothy, who is 39 and works at a retirement home in Cutler Ridge, would somehow pick up and start over. He was fond of the town. "Our big thing was palm trees," he said. "It was like Hawaii."
Homestead's particular story, like any town's, cannot be told by a mere aggregation of facts and figures. But there are plenty of numbers: an average per capita income of $9,700, compared with $14,600 for the state; 15 percent of the population at work in agriculture, forestry and fishing; another 14 percent working in construction with farmland increasingly giving way to developments; the balance at the air base, unemployed or working in private businesses, real estate, the tourism industry or whatever could be found.
After Eastern Airlines shut down last year and dumped another load of newly unemployed workers into the struggling economy, men and women who once made more than $30,000 a year -- pretty good money here -- were looking for retail jobs paying minimum wage. Something in the town was going sour. Yet the immigrants kept coming and the hardships kept on growing.
Some, like Mr. Hanna, still made a good life and looked forward to the future. "I was feeling good about myself," he said. "But now I have to start all over. I came here today to work. But my mind isn't right. I'm not in the groove for it. I can't get myself back."
Homestead was incorporated in 1913, nine years after a south Florida railroad line tied it to markets to the north. The flatlands were transformed from a backwoods filled with snakes, wildcats and otter into a livable place with the flavor of the frontier.
Though in the past several years Homestead had been spruced up with some expensive homes and new downtown facades, a certain grittiness always remained. For every majestic royal palm on the repaved downtown blocks along Krome Avenue, there were just as many hard-luck neighborhoods. Homestead has always been a little lost between the bright lights of Miami and the breeziness of the Keys. But last week the isolation hurt. Homeowners who had lost everything said with bitterness that the slowness of the disaster relief effort was only to be expected. "They always forget about us," said Major Habermehl.
The major, who emphasized that he was a solid Republican, complained nonetheless that President Bush, in his tour of the area Monday, went no farther south than Cutler Ridge, a heavily damaged town to the north that is more of a Miami suburb.
"We never count on anyone," he said. "It's just that way. But this time, I kind of take it personally."
 
Thoughts of Renewal In a Foreign Landscape
 By midweek, as the first of the relief airlifts arrived and the thwack of helicopters was all around, the farmers and grove owners had walked their acres and assessed the damage. Rosario Strano, a second-generation Italian-American tomato farmer, stood in front of his house and pointed with amazement at a red house down the road.
"All these years," he said, "and I have never seen that house before." The view was clear. Every tree around the place was flat.
At the fruit packing house of J. R. Brooks and Sons Inc. business limped ahead at a bare fraction of capacity. Neal Palmer Brooks, the concern's affable 53-year-old owner, surveyed the damage.
His fields were insured by Lloyd's of London. He is confident that his family's venture will spring back despite losses to most of his groves and most of his packing operation. But he is worried that other growers will give up. Mr. Brooks had already responded to market pressures by expanding his operations into Costa Rica and the Bahamas. "My company will go on," he said. "But if we lose a lot of growers we will lose all their experience, their training, their knowledge and their feel for this. Just look around. The groves blew out of the ground. They blew across the road. They blew down the road. They are stacked up like tumbleweed against the fences. Gone."
Gone, too, from the hidden groves to the once busy downtown, were the signposts of the night. No street lights pierced the darkness, no flickering televisions in living rooms or neon signs. After dark, when the curfew fell and the streets cleared and men stood on porch fronts, guarding whatever they had left with loaded guns, only the red glow of flares set up near National Guard encampments broke a curtain of pure black. The trees were gone, and looking up one saw a glittering canopy of stars, brilliant and mute against the eerie silence down in Homestead.

HEADLINE: California Legislature Passes Budget With Big Cuts

BYLINE: By ROBERT REINHOLD, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: SACRAMENTO, Calif., Aug. 30

 BODY:
The two-month-long fiscal crisis in California has moved closer to conclusion with the passage of an austere recession-era budget package that cuts deeply into health and welfare services and state support for colleges, cities and counties.
The State Assembly passed the main $57 billion budget bill on Saturday evening after an impassioned speech by Speaker Willie L. Brown Jr. urging colleagues to pass the bill even though it closely resembled the no-new-taxes budget favored by his main political rival, Gov. Pete Wilson. 
 The State Senate had passed the measure by a wide margin early Saturday after its Democratic leaders reached an accord with the Republican Governor. The vote in the Democratic-controlled Assembly was much closer: with a two-thirds vote necessary for passage, the budget passed, 54 to 23.
Though the Assembly has not yet acted on the crucial bills that would carry out spending cuts necessitated by the budget, the votes appeared to signal the end of the budget impasse that has all but paralyzed the state government and threatened the poor and disabled with loss of health care and other services. The Assembly planned to take up the bills late today.
 
Cutting Aid to Cities
 Unable to bridge a $10.7 billion budget gap, the state has been paying its bills with i.o.u.'s since July 1, and many health programs for the poor and old are on the verge of closing.
The new budget would cut $1.3 billion from the $2.9 billion that the state would have normally given to cities, counties and special fire-fighting and other districts; reduce money for regional centers for the retarded; cut support for the University of California and the California State University systems by $402 million; double fees at community colleges, and delay the opening of two new prisons.
It would maintain support for public schools, but only with complex bookkeeping acrobatics devised by Governor Wilson that "recapture" $1.1 billion in excess money paid to schools last year and then "lend" them money from future years for this year. The schools would be expected to repay the money in better times. This measure is bitterly opposed by teachers' unions and school administrators.
Isolated by the agreement between the Senate leadership and Governor Wilson, the 80-member Assembly yielded out of frustration and weariness.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Specter of the Storm
Damage from Hurricane Andrew to natural gas operations in the Gulf of Mexico will cut production for several weeks, until repairs are completed. Prices for gas contracts rose sharply on Friday, and analysts predicted that customers in the Northeast and Midwest would see bigger heating bills this winter. In addition, some analysts said Andrew's ravages may have ended a long depression in the natural gas market. [Page D1.]
Top executives of large insurance companies toured Florida municipalities where almost all property had become a casualty of the hurricane. Several said the damage was more intense than anything they had ever seen, and the faces of corporate leaders from Allstate, State Farm, Prudential, Aetna and other companies turned expressionless at the destruction. [D1.] 
 The nation's lime crop was largely destroyed. Most of the trees were stripped of fruit and leaves, and others were toppled. [D4.]
The hurricane devastation in Miami created havoc for television stations. Worst hit was WCIX, which is owned by CBS. [D6.]
 
The Economy
A sure way for the nation to increase its output is to provide more education, a group of often-bickering economists agreed at a weekend meeting in Wyoming. [D8.]
Japan's huge economic rescue package is the type of powerful stimulus that Washington policy makers wish they could adopt. But pressured by the budget deficit, Bush Administration officials feel too hemmed in to embrace a huge pump-priming program that could increase it by $50 billion or more. [D1.]
The nation's retailers report that sales of notepads, sneakers, lunch pails and pens recovered a bit of their old buoyancy as students prepared to head back to school. [D2.]
Machine tool orders fell 2.8 percent in July, reflecting a sharp drop in orders from foreign customers, a trade group said. [D3.]
 
International
 Analysts generally praised the Japanese Government's economic rescue package as a good start in restoring public confidence. But they also say it represents the abandonment of an important pledge: to bring the country's astronomical land prices down. News Analysis. [D8.]
The cost of traveling in Europe is pinching Americans this summer as the dollar's value plunges. [A4.]
 
Companies
 David Fiedler has Kodak's risks from the dollar's jumps and jiggles under enviable control, using financial hedges to make sure that Kodak at least breaks even on currency swings. [D1.]
 
The Media Business
Marketers are still struggling to refine, and in some cases to define, their sales pitches of the 1980's for the more practical, more skeptical consumers of the 90's. Stuart Elliott: Advertising. [D7.]
Several cable channels have adopted aggressive programming strategies aimed at taking even bigger bites out of the small network audience on Saturday night. Bill Carter: Television. [D6.]
More and more, advertisers are using a select group of high-profile models to promote their products. [D6.]
Merrill Lynch has become entangled in a Singapore Government investigation of leaks to the nation's leading financial newspaper. The inquiry is testing the limits of press freedom in the prosperous, tightly ruled city-state. [D6.]
 
Today's Column
Why are investors betting that Ivax stock will fall? The shares have quadrupled since early 1991 on the promise of future earnings, leaving the company little room for error. Market Place. [D4.]
 Nothing is going right for Europe's bond markets. And what is going wrong will become worse if the French say no to a single European currency. Jonathan Fuerbringer: Credit Markets. [D5.]

HEADLINE: London Journal;
U.S. Tourist Dollar Paying a Value-Deleted Tax

BYLINE: By RICHARD W. STEVENSON, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: LONDON, Aug. 30

 BODY:
Robert Hite of Denver sat on a bench outside the Tower of London (admission price: $13), munching on his cheapest meal in days, a quarter-pounder from McDonald's ($3.25), and running over the expenses he has incurred during his family's vacation in England.
"The one that really sticks in my mind was the ice cream," he said, sitting alongside his wife, Joanne, and his son, Cass. "We went into a place in Salisbury and had an ice cream, and it was $6 a bowl. That really got to me." 
 The cost of traveling in Europe is getting to a lot of Americans this summer. The prices of almost everything in Western Europe tend to be sharply higher than in the United States even in normal times, but in recent weeks, as the value of the dollar has dropped precipitously relative to the world's other major currencies, the shock to American travelers has been particularly severe.
In Britain over the last several days, the pound has cost about $2.10 after factoring in the commission charged by most banks and money-exchange centers. That reflects a decline in the dollar's value of about 8 percent in a matter of weeks, and it is a far cry from the mid-1980's, when the value of the pound flirted for a time with $1.
 
$25 for a Pair of Socks
 Those were the days when airlines in the United States advertised weekend shopping trips to London, touting the bargains travelers could find. The other day, seeking not a bargain but just the fun of shopping in one of the world's great department stores, Mrs. Hite visited Harrods.
"I walked right out without buying anything," she said. "I thought I'd buy a pair of socks, just so I could say I'd gotten something there, but it was $25. For a pair of socks!"
There are economic advantages to the United States of a weak dollar, most notably the help it gives to companies that export their products, which become less expensive to foreign buyers. And if the cost of tourism for Americans in Europe is exorbitant, it is extremely attractive for Europeans who want to visit the United States while their pounds, marks and francs have so much purchasing power.
British tour operators have begun offering cut-rate Christmas-shopping trips to New York. British Airways, for example, is offering round-trip air fare and two nights in a hotel for the equivalent of about $580 for people traveling between Nov. 1 and Dec. 12. Some of the offers have been promoted so aggressively that customs authorities here felt compelled recently to issue a statement warning that all purchases brought into the Britain were subject to duties and fees including the 17.5 percent value-added tax, a levy that cuts deeply into any cost savings.
But trade patterns and the vicissitudes of the foreign-exchange markets are not foremost on the minds of most Americans in Europe. For them, there seems to be a simple rule: assume that everything could cost twice as much as in the United States, and do not be surprised if it is even more. While many of them said they had been prepared for the high cost of hotels, they said they were taken aback by the price of food, sightseeing admissions, souvenirs and the other incidentals that can eat away rapidly at their traveler's checks.
 
Fewer U.S. Visitors
 Scott Blake Harris, a lawyer from Washington, brought his wife, Barbara, and their son, Colin, with him to England on a business trip, so many of his expenses were paid for. Still, he said as they stared up at Big Ben, the trip had been "shockingly" expensive.
"The last time I was here it was about $1.25 to the pound, and now the dollar is worth nothing," he said. A quick meal of pizza and spaghetti cost them $35. "It's affecting the way we're eating," he said, only half-jokingly. "Less."
Hotels and tour-bus operators said the number of American visitors this summer appeared to be down significantly, a finding confirmed by a quick stop at any major tourist site, where in the babble of languages, American English can be heard relatively infrequently.
Those who have come are clearly trying to work themselves into a state of fiscal stoicism.
"We knew it was coming and it's still shocking," said Stan Davis of Lawrence, Kan., as he stood outside the gates of Buckingham Palace, where watching the guards remains free. He and his wife and their two children just arrived here for three months while he teaches law at University College, London, and they said they were already adapting to the unexpected costs.
"What's not going to happen is eating out a lot," Mr. Davis said. His wife, Linda, said they would be able to get by without much trouble, but only if they learned where to shop most inexpensively and generally kept their outlays down. "It's like New York with a bad rate of exchange," Mrs. Davis said.
 
No Plans to Return

The best strategy for coping, some tourists said, is trying not to compute the cost in dollars of each expenditure mentally. "Look, if you go on vacation and you have to spend a few more dollars, so what?" said Bertha Kaplan of Manhattan as she stood outside Westminster Abbey. But she could not resist noting that she had just paid $14 for a small guide book.
"This is the opportunity of a lifetime and we're going to enjoy ourselves," said Scott Ripper of Brookfield, Ill., as he stood outside the Tower of London. "Yeah, the Tower costs $13 each, but if you go to Disneyland it costs $30 and you don't get the incredible history you get here."
Still, Mr. Ripper added, "we're not going to be coming back here next year, that's for sure."
Mrs. Hite said that despite the expense they were still seeing all the sights they had planned on, and that the trip had been enjoyable.
"But you know, though we didn't set a budget, we never expected to spend everything we brought with us," she said. "Guess what? We're going to."

(Jonathan Player for The New York Times)

LENGTH: 1200 words

HEADLINE: Economists Put Forth Plans to Stimulate Growth

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: JACKSON HOLE, Wyo., Aug. 30

 BODY:
For two days, an all-star cast of economists gathered in the Rocky Mountains here to search for their Holy Grail: a formula to reverse the trend in which growth in the industrial world has slowed decade by decade since World War II.
If the often-bickering economists were able to forge a consensus on anything, it was that providing more education is a sure way for a nation to increase its output. 
 Time and again, the economists stressed the importance of improving "human capital," meaning that a better-educated, better-trained work force can lift a nation's growth rate.
"Human capital can be important for research and development and for adapting technologies from other countries," said Robert J. Barro, an economics professor at Harvard University.
 
Effect on Output
 If an industrial nation raised the average amount of schooling for its population by one year, he said, output per capita would increase three-tenths of 1 percent a year. If sub-Saharan countries increased the average level of schooling, currently two years, by one year, he said, output would grow by seven-tenths of 1 percent.
Mr. Barro and some 50 other economists, who ranged from hard right conservatives to moderate liberals, came to Jackson Hole for a conference, "Policies for Long-Term Economic Growth," which was sponsored by the Federal Reserve Bank of Kansas City.
During breaks from hiking in the Rockies and rafting down the Snake River, the conferees produced a potpourri of proposals that included tax incentives to increase America's anemic savings rate, a consumption tax to encourage investment over consumer spending, and reintroducing an investment tax credit for machinery and equipment to increase productivity.
 
Most Popular Theme
 But of the many disparate proposals, improving human capital emerged as the conference's most popular theme.
Lawrence F. Katz, another Harvard economist, supported Mr. Barro's conclusions, saying an improved work force is often accompanied by increased physical investment by business, which helps spur productivity and growth. Countries that tend to invest more money in improving their work force than in physical investment, like factories or machinery, he said, tend to have a fairer distribution of income.
"One can view human capital investment as win-win," Mr. Katz said. "It is associated with greater growth and better distribution of the pie."
 
The Case for Machinery
 He noted that people with more education tended to have fewer children and that this helps to raise output and productivity per capita. Children in countries with low fertility rates generally receive more education than those from high-fertility nations.
In another paper at the conference, two other Harvard economists -- Harvard dominated the conference with six professors on hand -- contended that it was important for countries to invest more in machinery and equipment. These professors, Lawrence H. Summers and J. Bradford De Long, said investment in machinery had far greater returns than investments in other areas, like buildings, because machinery helped disseminate new technologies and push workers to upgrade their skills.
"Equipment investment is much more likely to promote technological change and be a carrier of technological change," said Mr. Summers. Mr. De Long estimated that there was a 30 to 40 percent return on investment in machinery, with half going to companies and half to societies.
Several speakers urged the United States Government to subsidize spending on machinery by establishing an investment tax credit. They said this would help offset subsidies that favor investments in areas that do not enhance productivity, like housing.
But Lawrence A. Kudlow, chief economist at Bear, Stearns and a member of the Reagan Administration, attacked these calls for favoring certain categories of investment. "We would be in great error if we go back to a more planned-economy approach," he said, asserting that faster growth would result from lower taxes, less regulation and less Government intervention in the economy.
 
Creating Distortions
 Allan H. Meltzer, an economist at Carnegie-Mellon University, argued that it would be wrong to establish subsidies for machinery, asserting that the wide range of tax subsidies, including mortgage interest deductions for homeowners, distort investment and make the economy less efficient.
He said Washington could spur long-term growth by shifting spending away from areas that do little for productivity. He cited farm subsidies and the Government's spending 2 percent of gross domestic product on medical care for people in the last six months of their lives.
"Our problem is shifting resources from wasteful uses to more productive uses," he said. "The Government spends too much foolishly and not enough wisely."
Professor Meltzer stressed that free trade was crucial for faster growth. He pointed to studies showing that developing countries that embrace free trade gain new technologies faster and develop industries that can better withstand foreign competition.
Numerous speakers called on Government to embrace measures that encourage investments that raise productivity. "In the choice between savings incentives and investment incentives, we should choose investment incentives," said Alan J. Auerbach, an economics professor at the University of Pennsylvania.
He said incentives aimed at spurring savings -- the capital that finances investments -- are an inefficient way to increase investments that enhance productivity. The increased savings resulting from tax incentives, he said, might be invested overseas or used to build housing.
Martin Feldstein, a Harvard economist who is president of the National Bureau of Economic Research, disagreed, saying more needs to be done to raise America's anemic savings rate.
"Our net savings rate is just 2 percent of gross domestic product," he said. "It's off the charts in international comparisons of savings rates. In the long run domestic investment is constrained by low domestic savings."
 
Importance of Inflation
 Several economists said low inflation was important for raising long-term productivity and economic growth, since high inflation distorts prices, worries investors and often slows capital spending. The paper by Professors Summers and De Long pointed to evidence that countries with highly independent central banks -- those willing to shun political pressures and adopt tight money policies to crack down on inflation -- enjoy faster productivity growth than other nations.
Professor Summers and Professor Auerbach said the United States could raise long-term growth by substantially increasing investment. Mr. Auerbach said that raising growth by a full percentage point a year would require a 50 percent increase in investment a year. Vastly increasing investment would require a sharp shift away from consumption spending on everything from camcorders to medical care to baseball game tickets.
"Prosperity tomorrow requires sacrifice today," said N. Gregory Mankiw, another Harvard professor. "It is a rare politician who is willing to be the bearer of such truths."

HEADLINE: Tracking the Back-to-School Bounce

BYLINE: By STEPHANIE STROM

 BODY:
Peppering their cautious assessments with a variety of caveats, the nation's retailers report that sales of notepads, sneakers, lunch pails and pens have recovered a bit of their old buoyancy this month as students prepare to head back to school.
But while they appreciate any sign that sales are regaining at least a small semblance of normalcy, many retailers no longer worry if they fail to get a significant bounce from the back-to-school season. Normalcy, they say, has undergone an evolution. 
 Once the harbinger of the Christmas season to follow, when retailers earn the bulk of their profits, the back-to-school season no longer serves as the merchant's crystal ball. Changes in school calendars and shopping patterns, as well as the ability to replenish inventory in three or four days, have diminished the importance of August on the retail calendar, retailers and analysts say.
 
More Sales in the Fall Now
 "We used to use August as the bellwether of critical fourth-quarter sales, but now August sales are pushed into September and even October," said Janet Magnano, a retail analyst at Burnham Securities. "The business has become more evenly distributed than it used to be."
The sluggish economy is intensifying the shift, as financially strapped consumers are choosing to buy things when they need them instead of on a seasonal basis, said Mike Searles, president of Kids "R" Us, the children's clothing division of Toys "R" Us.
Of course, the weeks before school are still critical for stores like Mr. Searles's, which primarily sell children's clothing or stationery. "This is our Christmas right now," he said.
Retailers also say sales have spread out because students postpone their back-to-school buying until they see what their peers are wearing, pushing sales further into the fall. And as merchants become better able to control their inventory, they buy less, knowing that they can replenish their stocks quickly if the need arises.
Gary Prushansky, director of marketing for the Bic Corporation, which does a booming business in pens this time of year, said retailers placed their orders later this year and bought only for the back-to-school season. In previous years, retailers bought more Bic pens than they expected to sell during back-to-school to take advantage of special pricing promotions Bic has for the season.
Despite retailers' caution, Mr. Prushansky said Bic would record strong sales gains. Instead of keeping stock in the warehouse, retailers have been using computerized technology to replace merchandise.
"Everybody has a way of doing business now that works on having less inventory," Mr. Prushansky said.
Such strict controls on inventory should help retailers avoid the price cuts that have been used during the recession to stimulate sales at the expense of profit margins, analysts say. The prudent strategy may serve them well, given low levels of consumer confidence and income growth.
 
A Later Labor Day
 This year's late Labor Day weekend, which falls a week after it did last year, will further emphasize the new trend. Most back-to-school buying occurs in the week before and the week after the holiday, and many retailers say sales were drowsier than normal in the first two weeks of August.
"More back-to-school spending will fall in September than usual," said Orren F. Knauer, vice president of investor relations at the Kmart Corporation. "But even without the late Labor Day, spending patterns around back-to-school have changed." As a result, he said, Kmart's August sales growth is likely to be somewhat less than the increase it posted in July, traditionally a slow sales month, while September sales will probably surge.
More fundamental changes to the calendar include year-round school in a growing number of school districts, particularly in Florida and California, where several chains have strong presences. And even in school districts that operate on a more traditional calendar, some start before Labor Day while others wait until it has passed. In some school districts in Texas, for example, school started the third week of August, while in Minnesota, schools open after the holiday.
"We're doing well, although probably not as well as we had expected up to this point," said Gary Vasques, senior vice president for marketing at Caldor, a chain of discount stores in the Northeast. "But we feel that with the late Labor Day and lots of schools waiting to open till after that, it's going to come in September."
 
The Safe Staple
 Denim is de rigueur and school supplies with an environmental conscience are staples in every backpack. Products featuring characters on the hit television show "Beverly Hills 90201" are still best sellers, as are spinoffs from movies like "Beauty and the Beast" and "Batman Returns."
Sales of denim, Mr. Vasques says, regardless of color or style, were rocketing far beyond the company's plans, a trend echoed by other retailers.
Blue jeans and denim shirts and anything with Western accents like fringe are best sellers at stores across the country. "When the economy is unstable or a little rocky, Western becomes a fashion force because it's a safe, staple American look," explains Carol Edwards, publicity manager for men's and children's merchandising at J. C. Penney.
Overall sales have increased over last year, but have still fallen short of expectations. "From what I understand, so far August hasn't exactly been a barn-burner," said Kurt Barnard, president of Barnard's Retail Marketing Report, an industry newletter. "It's definitely better than last year, but frankly I think most retailers were anticipating even more than that."
In a survey of 600 mothers by Tactical Retail Solutions, a New York-based retail consulting firm, almost 60 percent said they planned to spend the same amount of money on back-to-school clothes and supplies as they did last year. Almost 30 percent said they would spend more, while 13.6 percent said they would spend less.
"We expect that to translate into moderately positive sales gains among discounters and category killers like Kids 'R' Us," said Isaac Lagnado, principal of the firm. "But traditional department stores are not likely to get much of a bounce from back-to-school spending."

HEADLINE: Hurricane Ruins Much of Lime Crop

BYLINE: By STEVE LOHR

 BODY:
Count the nation's lime crop as another casualty of Hurricane Andrew. An estimated 95 percent of America's limes come from the hard-hit area of Florida's southern Dade County, near the town of Homestead.
State officials and citrus growers who have toured the area say the lime crop is destroyed. Most of the trees were stripped of fruit and leaves, they say, and others were toppled. 
 "It looked horrible, like someone had taken a chainsaw to most of the trees," said Larry Murray, vice president of sales for Indian River Foods, an orange and grapefruit processor, who drove through the Homestead area on Thursday.
 
Some Crops Unharmed
 Still, Florida's big citrus industry was largely spared by the hurricane. The crucial region for orange and grapefruit crops, covering about 700,000 acres, was to the north of Andrew's path.
"It was a tightly wound hurricane in Florida, cutting a 35-mile wide path of destruction," said Bobby F. McKown, executive vice president of Florida Citrus Mutual, a cooperative representing most of the state's citrus growers. "So while it hit the lime crop, it was pretty much a nonevent for the citrus industry as a whole."
In the main citrus-growing regions, Andrew had no more effect than a "normal thunderstorm," Mr. McKown said.
Florida's lime crop is valued at $20.3 million a year. But the bigger financial loss to southern Dade County's agricultural economy will come from the damage to the house-plant industry, said Robert Freie, the Florida statistician for the Federal Agriculture Department. The state's $150-million-a-year house-plant industry is centered in southern Dade County.
 
Mexican Limes Differ
 Lime prices are set to jump, because roughly 60 percent of American consumption is produced in Florida. Most of the imports come from Mexico. Moreover, Florida-grown limes are the larger, seedless Persian variety, while most of the imports are Key limes, which are smaller and have more seeds.
"I expect the price to bounce a couple of dollars a box by next week," said Henry Hubner, manager of the Hahn Food Products Corporation, a fruit wholesaler in the Bronx. Mr. Hubner buys the limes he sells to restaurants and hotels in boxes of about five dozen. He said that last week he paid about $8.50 a box.
Yet higher lime prices, or even scarcity of the preferred variety, should not have too much effect on American consumers. At Gallagher's Steak House in Manhattan, where up to 200 gin and tonics (with lime) are served on a busy night, Leo Bennett, a bartender, said: "It shouldn't affect the price of a drink. And even if they have to switch from limes to lemons, most gin and tonic drinkers will suffer through."
Harold E. Kendall Jr., the vice president of the Kendall Foods Corporation in Gould, Fla., whose products include limes and avocados, said: "We've gone through this before, and we'll be back. After all, we have a solemn duty to provide limes for all the gin and tonics in the world."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Harold E. Kendall Jr., the vice president of Kendall Foods Corporation in Gould, Fla., walking through his ruined lime orchard. "We'll be back," he said. "After all, we have a solemn duty to provide limes for all the gin and tonics in the world." (Lee Romero/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Weak Foreign Demand Depresses Tool Orders

BYLINE: By SETH FAISON

 BODY:
Orders for American-made machine tools fell 2.8 percent in July, reflecting the weakness among foreign customers that plagues several sectors of the United States economy, according to a report to be released today by an industry trade group.
The trade group, the Association of Manufacturing Technology, tallied last month's orders at $205.6 million, down from the $211.5 million of orders in June. But compared with orders a year earlier, the July figure was up 6.4 percent. 
 Domestic orders for machine tools grew by 3 percent in July to $180.6 million, the trade group said, but the month's gain was more than offset by a sharp drop in foreign orders, which slumped 31.5 percent to $25 million.
 
Indicator of Economic Health
 Machine tool orders are monitored by economists as one indicator of the nation's economic health. July's results served as another reminder that a United States economic recovery faces an obstacle in the weak economies of Europe and Japan, where buying of American products is down.
Although the dollar's steady fall this year has made American products more attractive overseas, economists have expressed concern that auto, aircraft and computer exports, among others, would be affected by lower purchasing power abroad.
On the bright side, the association said that so far this year machine tool orders totaled $1.47 billion, up 8 percent from the similar period of 1991. Domestic orders during that time were up 25 percent, to $1.28 billion.
"Every month this year domestic orders have been ahead of the same month in 1991," Albert W. Moore, president of the Association of Manufacturing Technology, said in a statement. "This continued pickup of orders is a positive sign."
Machine tool orders were relatively steady at the start of the year, climbed to $242.8 million in March and then dropped to $219 million in April and to $159.2 million in May. Orders rebounded to a revised total of $211.5 million in June, earlier reported as $208.6 million.
Shipments of machine tools, which are often lower than orders because some orders are canceled, slid 23.1 percent in July to $184.3 million, compared with June. Shipments fell 13.4 percent compared with July 1991. For the first seven months of 1992, total shipments of machine tools fell 5.6 percent to $1.44 billion.
In specific tool categories, orders for metal-cutting tools grew 6.4 percent in July from the previous month, to $154.9 million, and were up 7.7 percent compared with July 1991.
Shipments of metal-cutting tools dropped by 17.3 percent, to $133.4 million, compared with June, and by 10.7 percent compared with July 1991. Shipments of metal-cutting tools for the year to date are virtually even with last year's pace.
Orders for metal-forming machine tools slipped 23 percent to $50.8 million, compared with the previous month, but jumped 6.5 percent compared with July 1991. Shipments of metal-forming machine tools dropped by 35 percent to $50.9 million compared with June, and by 19.7 percent compared with July 1991.

HEADLINE: Tokyo-Style Plan Too Costly for U.S.

BYLINE: By STEVEN GREENHOUSE, Special to The New York Times

DATELINE: JACKSON HOLE, Wyo., Aug. 30

 BODY:
The Japanese Government's huge new economic rescue package, aimed at catapulting Japan out of its worrisome slump, is the type of powerful stimulus package that Washington policy makers wish they could adopt.
But pressured by the $350 billion Federal budget deficit, Bush Administration officials feel too hemmed in to embrace a huge pump-priming program that could increase the deficit by $50 billion or more. 
 "United States fiscal policy has been immobilized by the failure to deal with the budget deficit," said Stanley Fischer, an economics professor at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology. Professor Fischer was in Wyoming this weekend, along with dozens of other leading economists, for a conference organized by the Federal Reserve Bank of Kansas City.
Bush Administration officials and many conservative economists contend that if Washington embraced a large-scale fiscal stimulus program, it would probably push up long-term interest rates because the bond markets are already so worried about the deficit. By hampering home buying and business investment, higher rates would sabotage the stimulative effects of increased deficit spending, they say.
If Administration officials are envious of what Japan is doing, the Tokyo plan does offer some solace: the Japanese package could spur America's exports to Japan by billions of dollars a year.
As Japan's economy grows faster, that will increase its demand for foreign goods; the United States supplies about one-third of Japan's imports. Not only that, faster growth could strengthen the yen, which could also help American exports. This could also help reduce Japan's rapidly growing trade surplus, the source of tensions with Washington.
 
Help for the World
 Indeed, with growth languishing in the United States and Europe, the Japanese package could prove a much-needed locomotive for world growth.
"The Japanese program is likely to help reverse the Japanese recession and it will have important repercussions for the world," said Lawrence H. Summers, a Harvard University economist.
Japanese officials predict their $86 billion economic package will increase economic growth by more than 2 percentage points over the next 12 months, from the modest 2 percent that was anticipated to a robust 4.4 percent. Those numbers must make the Bush Administration jealous because, with Election Day rapidly approaching, it is dogged by high unemployment and stunted growth of less than 2 percent.
The program that the Japanese Government announced on Friday calls for investing $69 billion to build roads, sewers, schools and other public-works projects. It includes spending the other $17 billion on housing assistance and on buying land for future public projects.
Yoshio Suzuki, vice chairman of the Nomura Research Institute, said Japan's stimulus package would make its economy "go back to its former growth track of over 4 percent a year" and would make Japanese manufacturers "as competitive as ever." The package offers tax incentives to encourage companies to invest in automation and energy-saving equipment.
 
Aiding Stocks and Banks
 The program also aims to help Tokyo's shell-shocked stock market by, for example, allowing public pension funds to buy more stock. Another objective is to shore up the troubled banking sector by helping banks dispose of bad loans by propping up real estate prices.
Japan is able to embrace fiscal stimulus, a classic countercyclical tool, because it has a sizable budget surplus, when receipts for its Social Security Trust Fund are included.
"The fundamental point is Japan has a fiscal surplus of 3 percent of gross domestic product," said C. Fred Bergsten, director of the Institute for International Economics in Washington. "Japan is in fact the only major industrial country in a position to launch a major expansionary program."
 
Money for States and Cities
 In contrast, America's budget deficit represents more than 5 percent of gross domestic product. Even so, early this year Robert Solow, a Nobel Prize-winning economist at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology, and James Tobin, a Nobel Prize winner at Yale, headed a group of 100 economists who urged Washington to spend $50 billion more a year on public works to push America's stalled economy forward. They asserted that month after month of slow growth was worse for the nation than a $50 billion increase in the deficit.
Professors Solow and Tobin said the $50 billion should go to states and cities, many of which are in such bad financial straits that they have raised taxes, laid off workers and aborted public-works projects -- moves that further depress the slumping economy. They argue that states and cities would restart public-works projects and rehire workers using the Federal money, giving a quick lift to the economy.
"The continuing weakness of the United States economy makes fiscal expansion look more attractive if it can be done in a way that does not undermine confidence in a long-term solution to the deficit problem," said Professor Summers of Harvard.
To spur growth without pushing up the deficit long term, he proposes that some Federal grant money that is due states in future years be given this year.
 
Administration Arguments
 Administration officials say such proposals may hardly increase growth, asserting that they would push up long-term rates and might not produce additional spending because states might merely cut taxes once they receive the Federal money.
With its emphasis on improving infrastructure, the Japanese package sounds somewhat like Gov. Bill Clinton's proposals to spend $80 billion over four years on building railways, highways and a new telecommunications system. His call for spending money on America's rundown infrastructure has a different emphasis from the Japanese program: the aim of the Clinton program is not to pull the nation out of recession, but to make the economy more efficient.
"Japan is perceived as having a crying need for infrastructural investment," said David D. Hale, chief economist for Kemper Financial Services. "It's not that its infrastructure is run down; it's that it's incomplete."

HEADLINE: Treasury to Auction Only Bills This Week

 BODY:
Financings by the Treasury this week are confined to the regular Monday auction of new three- and six-month bills.
By late on Friday, the rate on the outstanding three-month bill was 3.15 percent, and the six-month bill was 3.24 percent. 
 Government agency issues scheduled for this week include a $1.3 billion package of 5- and 10-year debentures from the Federal National Mortgage Association, which are expected to be priced for sale to the public tomorrow.

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS;
Hurricane Likely to Reshape The TV Market in Miami

BYLINE: By BILL CARTER

 BODY:
Routine television audience measurement was disrupted last week by the absence of the nation's 16th-largest television market from the national Nielsen ratings.
The devastation in Miami caused by Hurricane Andrew created havoc for the city's television stations, two of which are owned by networks. Worst hit was WCIX, the station owned by CBS. The station's broadcast tower was blown over by the hurricane winds, knocking the station off the air for 12 hours. The station managed to resume its signal by relying on a low-power station that CBS owns north of the city, but still managed to reach only about 40 percent of the normal Miami viewing area. 
 That could have led to a severe competitive disadvantage, except for the fact that the lack of electricity in other parts of the city prevented other stations from being seen widely.
But both the NBC-owned station, WTVJ, and the ABC affiliate, WPLG, were able to marshal their resources and put together 24-hour coverage of the storm and its aftermath, while WCIX had far fewer resources to call upon because of damage to its news-gathering equipment.
WTVJ is expected to reap particular benefits from its storm-related coverage once the city returns to normal. The station's weather reporter, Bryan Norcross, was widely credited in Miami last week for providing the best and most comprehensive information throughout the storm. Mr. Norcross was on the air for 22 straight hours, giving out information and even direct advice to many threatened by the storm. It is expected that his performance may ultimately increase his station's news ratings significantly.
 
Chaos for the Ratings
 Johnathan Rodgers, the president of CBS's stations division, said ratings measurement in the market had been thrown into chaos, with no way for any advertiser to know what was being seen. "No one knows who's watching what," he said.
Jack Loftus, a spokesman for the A. C. Nielsen Company, said: "In the days after the storm, the Miami ratings were nowhere. We lost too many homes to do any sort of count."
He said the homes counted in the national people-meter survey, which determines national ratings, were wiped out for one day and reached no more than 20 percent of their normal reporting level any day last week.
"There is no telling when that market will be back to full service," Mr. Loftus said. "We may have to resurvey the whole area. I have a feeling it will be later rather than sooner."
Mr. Rodgers added: "We're used to saying the ratings measure homes with television. Now we're talking about homes with electricity."
It will be weeks before the economic impact on the television market can be assessed. "We haven't even begun to think of the economic consequences of this," said Allen Shaklin, the general manager of WCIX.
The personal consequences have been especially serious for WCIX, with more than half the station's employees -- including Mr. Shaklin -- left homeless by the storm. Many of them moved into the station temporarily. CBS in New York had food and building supplies shipped to Miami in an effort to help them.
Mr. Rodgers said the station would build a new broadcast tower. "That's covered by insurance," he said. "We also have insurance for loss of business. But to what extent that will cover the actual losses, we have no idea right now."

HEADLINE: Firms Wary About Hiring

BYLINE: Reuters

DATELINE: CHICAGO, Aug. 30

 BODY:
Recent hiring trends suggest that the economic recovery will remain sluggish in the fourth quarter, the temporary help contractor Manpower Inc. said today.
Of 15,000 companies surveyed, 21 percent plan hiring increases in the quarter, while 11 percent plan to cut their work forces, Manpower said. 
 The percentages are virtually unchanged from a year earlier, when 21 percent planned to hire more workers and 12 percent planned cuts.
"We see no signs of a significant downturn, but at the same time there is little evidence that robust hiring activity will take place," said Mitchell Fromstein, president of the company, based in Milwaukee.
He said the softness of the economic upturn and concerns over possible changes in the Government after the November election are keeping employers cautious.
"There seems to be a wait-and-see attitude that is likely to prevail for several months," Mr. Fromstein said.
Manpower assesses hiring trends every quarter through its telephone survey of public and private employers in 465 American cities. The survey found that hiring plans were at much the same levels as earlier in the year, except for expected hiring increases in the wholesale and retail sector before Christmas and a downturn in hiring for construction jobs.
The South and Midwest showed the strongest hiring outlooks, and the Northeast and West the weakest.

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS;
That Face, Those Fees, for Many Ads

BYLINE: By JAY E. ROSEN

 BODY:
More and more, advertisers are using a select group of high-profile models to promote their products.
Whereas in the past, many of these so-called super models seemed to be the exclusive property of top fashion houses, today they can be seen hawking everything from one-of-a-kind outfits to Gap jeans and diet soda. 
 Of course, models like Fiona Campbell-Walter, Jean Shrimpton, Verushka, Twiggy and Lauren Hutton have been used in the past to capture consumers' attention. But now, with recession-weary marketers trying to save money by cutting back on the number of advertisements they run, many are taking other steps to insure that their products are noticed.
 
Seeing the Same Faces
 And this can result in a seeming paradox: in tough times, these marketers are willing to spend extra money to use well-known models, rather than less-expensive models who are also far less known.
As such, consumers are increasingly seeing the same faces promoting a wide range of products.
For instance, Christy Turlington can be seen in ads for Anne Klein, Maybelline and Calvin Klein fragrance; Irene Pfeifer in ads for the Gap, Tse Cashmere, Emmanuel sportswear and Incognito perfume, and Cindy Crawford in ads for Pepsi-Cola, Revlon and Capezio bags.
"The impression many people are getting is that there are only five models out there," said John C. Jay, executive vice president of marketing and creative services at Bloomingdale's.
Mr. Jay is critical of the overuse of such models by advertisers who believe they are playing it safe. "To say 'Who's hot? Let's get them,' is not creative," he said. "It's brainless."
 
Providing the Right Image
 Still, some advertising executives said such models were often needed to provide the right image for their products.
Ted Sann, vice chairman and executive creative director of the ad agency BBDO New York, said he decided to use Ms. Crawford for the Pepsi campaign because her personality caught his attention.
"We originally tried other, less-known models, but the magic was missing," he said.
Mr. Sann said research by Pepsi and BBDO showed that the Crawford ad had proved successful in consumer likability, awareness and retention. He said a USA Today poll ranked a Pepsi ad featuring Ms. Crawford and two young boys spying on her as the second favorite commercial during the Super Bowl. Nike's ad, with Bugs Bunny, rated No. 1.
Joe Hunter, executive vice president and part owner of Ford Models Inc. in New York, said the modeling industry had been able to maintain some stability during the recession because of the increased demand for such models and the higher fees that some can command.
 
What Models Can Earn
 The average fee for most models, many of whom do catalogue work, is $1,250 to $3,500 a day, several people in the industry said. But well-known models may receive $3,500 to $6,000 a day. And the superstar models usually negotiate even higher fees.
Ford, like most modeling agencies, receives a 20 percent service fee from the client and a 20 percent commission from the model.
Mr. Jay contends that as the economy improves, there will be more opportunities for models in big-name campaigns. "Different clients will have different needs," he said.
For example, Deryl George is a frequently requested model who currently appears in Versace fragrance ads, as well the fashion pages of GQ magazine. But the 33-year-old model believes the days of seeing the same five or six faces everywhere may soon be coming to an end.
"During the high point of the recession, there was a group of about 10 of us whom I seemed to be bumping into at every assignment," he said. "But lately I'm seeing less and less of them and seeing new faces every day."
 
A Face in the Catalogues
 One such model is Donnie Eichar. With the exception of some editorial work in YM magazine and the European fashion publication Donna, Mr. Eichar, 20, who started modeling a year and a half ago, does mostly catalogue work.
So far, Mr. Eichar does not seem too concerned about not getting that many big assignments from advertisers or magazines. "Those assignments are only a small percentage of the work out there for models, and most models are doing what I am doing," he said.
On the other hand, Gillian Mather, a 27-year-old model from Australia who has appeared in print campaigns for Diet Coke and Bailey's Irish Cream, doubts that most models will benefit greatly even when advertising budgets increase again.
"When you have women ages 12 to 35 from all over the country able to recognize the models in the ads, that's an indication that people love this approach," she said of using the well-known faces.
The modeling industry is also feeling pressure from the increasing use of illustrations, fewer models in advertisements and in some cases the use of no models, or "no-frills advertising," like full-page ads with straight copy or a picture of a pair of headphones sitting atop a stereo instead of somebody's head.
 
Not 'a Knee-Jerk Reaction'
 Mr. Jay insisted that larger advertisers usually did not choose to forgo models to save money. Instead, he said, they sometimes found ads without models more effective. "We don't want to act in a knee-jerk reaction and insist that every ad has to have a model in it," Mr. Jay said.
And Pauline Bernatchez, owner and president of Pauline's Model Management, a midsize New York agency, did acknowledge that ads without models were sometimes a practical way to attract customers. "The words sale and reduced can sometimes be more seductive than a beautiful woman," she said.
Mr. Hunter said almost all areas of modeling were feeling the recession's pinch. He noted that two of his most successful models, who only do hand modeling, were finding less work. "Not only did advertisers stop using complete people, they stopped using body parts as well," he said.

HEADLINE: Fischer and Spassky Ready to Play Chess Not Far From a War

BYLINE: By ROGER COHEN

DATELINE: SVETI STEVAN, Yugoslavia, Aug. 30

 BODY:
Even by his own eccentric standards, the reclusive American chess genius Bobby Fischer has come up with something seriously weird in agreeing to make a comeback against his old rival Boris Spassky at this coastal resort just 70 miles from the carnage of the Balkan war.
Defying United States Government threats of stiff fines or even long imprisonment for breaking the current United Nations sanctions on Yugoslavia, Mr. Fischer has holed up in a medieval fort-turned-hotel overlooking the clear waters of the Adriatic. His heavily guarded suite, No. 118, is known as Villa Sophia because Sophia Loren once stayed here. 
 If Mr. Fischer, famous for flirting with commitments and then backing off, does indeed start the first game as scheduled on Wednesday, it will be the first time he has played in public since he won the world championship from Mr. Spassky 20 years ago in a match that narrowed the cold war to a chessboard and held the world riveted.
Surrounded by olive trees, pines, oleander and palm trees, Mr. Fischer, who is 49 years old, is shielded from even the faintest echo of the nearby fighting in Bosnia and Herzegovina by the encircling mountains. Since arriving a month ago, he has spent most of his time in his rooms preparing for the match, though he did, the hotel staff said, allow himself a respite with his chess-playing, 18-year-old Hungarian girlfriend, Zita Rajcszanyi.
Washington is trying to stop him from playing. American officials in Belgrade said they had written repeatedly to Mr. Fischer's hotel warning him that he could be fined as much as $250,000 or go to prison for up to 10 years if he plays. The match, with total prize money of $5 million, is considered a business transaction by the United States Treasury and so a breach of the sanctions.
The American officials said they had received no response. Mr. Fischer, the Yugoslav organizers of the match say, is not unduly concerned by the views of Washington. "Mr. Fischer can stay here for the next 10 years," said Klara Mandic, a close friend of the Belgrade-based businessman who arranged the encounter. Mr. Spassky, who has French citizenship and whose family lives outside Paris, has apparently not been pressed on the issue by the French Government.
Posters, banners and T-shirts throughout this resort, which was much favored by the former Yugoslav Communist leader Marshal Tito, proclaim the match "the world chess championship revenge match of the 20th century." It is, in fact, merely an exhibition match, albeit an extraordinary one.
 
Refused to Defend Title
 Mr. Fischer forfeited the world title in 1975, three years after his victory over Mr. Spassky in Reykjavik, Iceland, by refusing to defend it against Anatoly Karpov of the Soviet Union. He was the first world champion to give up the title without losing. The current champion, Gary Kasparov, has dismissed the new Fischer-Spassky encounter as a crassly commercial matching of two has-beens.
Others are not so sure. Mr. Fischer, despite his long absence, still has the world's second-highest rating behind Mr. Kasparov. Many chess masters are tantalized by the prospect that, even two decades after his last public games, Mr. Fischer could reproduce something close to the relentless strategic brilliance and devastating precision with which he crushed Mr. Spassky in Reykjavik and insured his status as one of the greatest players ever.
"It's enormously intriguing," said Lothar Schmid, the German grandmaster who refereed the stormy match in Iceland and who will again be the referee here.
Whatever its quality, the match clearly has its shabby side. It is being played for a large sum of money on the fringes of a continuing war in which at least 9,000 people have died in recent months. Neither player has as yet commented on that fact.
The encounter has been organized by a mysterious Serbian trader turned private banker named Jezdimir Vasiljevic, who is apparently as rich as he is ferociously nationalistic. Yugoslav magazines and newspapers have linked him to currency speculation and trafficking of various kinds, including arms dealing, but have produced no proof of their claims. Mr. Vasiljevic, whose recently established Jugoskandic Bank is financing the match, has said he hopes to profit through spinoffs like books and other ventures. He did not respond to a request for an interview.
 
'Anything Could Happen'
 The match contract calls for the winner to get $3.35 million and the loser $1.65 million. The two former world champions are to play until one of them wins 10 games, with draws not counting. If each player wins nine games, the match, to be played in a specially prepared basement room in the waterfront Maestral Hotel about a mile from Mr. Fischer's villa, will be declared a draw and the prize money shared equally.
These are rules Mr. Fischer has long favored despite many players' objections. When a version calling for only six victories was used in the 1984-85 Kasparov-Karpov title match, play was finally stopped after more than five months: both contenders were exhausted and neither had enough victories to win. Usually, match rules provide that draws will count a half-point and that the number of games will be limited to 24.
"Anything could happen," said Dimitrije Bjelica, an international chess master and former friend of Mr. Fischer's. "After so many years, it is very hard to say what the quality of the games will be. It's like trying to do a remake of a great movie classic 20 years after it first appeared. You just don't know how it will come out."
Certainly, much has changed on both the personal and geopolitical level since the Reykjavik encounter, which Mr. Fischer, playing with a devouring accuracy that sometimes verged on the cruel, won by 12 1/2 to 8 1/2 after losing the first two games. His prize money then amounted to $156,430.
In 1972, with both the cold war and the Soviet Union still in existence, bitter, sometimes apparently absurd charges of spying, and even of the use of chemical and electronic warfare, characterized the match. A recurrence of this seems unlikely under the new world order.
At the time, Mr. Spassky was an enormously respected world champion. Since then, living the good life in France, he is said by friends to have let himself go somewhat. He is now, at age 55, tied for 96th to 102d place on the ratings list of the Paris-based International Chess Federation.
Mr. Fischer is also a changed man in some respects. The lean, gaunt figure with hooded eyes, who brooded over the chess board like a predatory bird, has given way to an overweight, balding, bearded figure, unmistakably middle-aged, whose expression sometimes seems strikingly vacant.
Has his genius disappeared with his looks? Reports over the past 20 years have portrayed him as living an unusual life in Los Angeles, at times in his own apartment and at others in cheap hotels where he would check in under pseudonyms.
He has, former friends say, been given to anti-Semitic outbursts even though his mother is Jewish. At one time he became deeply involved in a fundamentalist sect called the Worldwide Church of God, which believed that Christ would return to earth in 1975 after a nuclear holocaust.
It was through this sect that he met a woman named Claudia Mokarow, whom he is said to have addressed as "Mommy." Mr. Fischer has never given interviews and has declined to do so here.
 
Still Formidable?
 To what extent he has kept up his chess during his long period of seclusion has remained a mystery. But it is generally believed that he has assiduously continued to study and follow the game, which became his monomaniacal passion as a child.
Mr. Fischer became a grandmaster at the age of 15 and won the United States championship for the first time when he was 16. His subsequent career was brilliant, but marked by outbursts over playing conditions -- often supported by less-outspoken colleagues -- that made his presence in any tournament unpredictable.
His stormy character has apparently not changed. Organizers said that the table on which the games against Mr. Spassky are to be played had already been altered seven times to Mr. Fischer's requests, and that they were still not clear that he was satisfied. The lighting, also built to suit Mr. Fischer, remains under discussion as well.
But up to now, the American chess giant has shown no sign of pulling out. Surrounded by four burly bodyguards -- one in front, one on either side, and one behind -- he has become a familiar sight on the steep stone alleys of the tiny peninsula on which his hotel is set.
Simonida Djordjevic, an artist with a gallery on the peninsula, said that Mr. Fischer strode in a few days ago and roared "Stop!" to his bodyguards. Then, taking what she described as huge strides, he raced around the works before freezing in front of a painting of a naked woman. Then he dropped to his knees and caressed the canvas, saying how beautiful it was.
On the way out, he asked how much a poster cost. Miss Djordjevic told him it cost nothing, which she said left Mr. Fischer incredulous. He took several. "He is a strange man indeed," Miss Djordjevic said.
Just what has lured Mr. Fischer here and tempted him to come out of seclusion is unclear. Over the past two decades, he has received many lucrative offers but spurned them.
He is known to like Yugoslavia, now reduced to a rump state comprising Serbia and Montenegro about half the size of the country he first visited 30 years ago, and is a friend of the grandmaster Svetozar Gligoric and other Yugoslav players. But his affection for the country appears an insufficient explanation for so unlikely a voyage.
One theory is that Mr. Fischer, a man with a huge ego, has been piqued into returning by two recent humiliations. One was Mr. Kasparov's recent achievement in passing him on the ratings list to become the highest-ranked player in the world. The second was the achievement of the young Hungarian woman, Judit Polgar, in becoming a grandmaster at an earlier age than Mr. Fischer did.
Another theory is simpler. It is that Mr. Fischer might be interested in marrying his new girlfriend and that she has been asking him just what they are going to live on.

HEADLINE: Review/City Opera;
A 'Barbiere' Worthy of Trust With Both Head and Heart

BYLINE: By BERNARD HOLLAND

 BODY:
If the New York City Opera continues to revise and rethink its coverage of the standard repertory, it could do worse than to use its current production of "Il Barbiere di Siviglia" as a model. Rossini's opera returned to the New York State Theater on Friday evening.
Desmond Heeley's set is unusually well suited to the proportions of the company's stage. Its rotating components, turning exteriors to interiors and back, have an elegant simplicity. They are clever but not fussily so. Mr. Heeley's costumes, especially for Rosina, offer what seems to be a calculated tastelessness, one that lends an interpretive weight all its own to Rossini's characters. 
 Christian Smith is the stage director responsible for this year's reinterpretation of the Lotfi Mansouri production. At the opening sequences, one cringed a little for the evening to come. Fiorello (Jeff Mattsey) swamped the serenade scene with hyperactive gesture; Jan Opalach's Bartolo appeared a caricature of a caricature, an inflation of a thousand other Bartolos from a thousand past performances.
Yet gradually, the troubling elements assimilated into a coherence. The double takes assumed a certain subtlety. Choreographed gesture and freeze-frame tableaux introduced the comic element of man-as-machine. By the final ensemble of Act I, the listener was very much with this "Barbiere" and by the end, its firm friend.
Robert Orth's Figaro rescued one's confidence in the performance with his famous opening announcement, "Largo al factotum." Mr. Orth uses the toothy leer to good effect; his baritone is high enough for the part, broad when it needs to be and with an agility that is up to Rossini's athletic requirements.
Both romantic leads are less striking but they served their purposes. As Almaviva, Paul Austin Kelly offered a thin, metallic tenor. It can scrape uncomfortably against the high end of the role's tessitura, but the flexibility is there when it is needed. Mr. Kelly survived in decent health.
Kathryn Gamberoni as Rosina sang with a soprano that can penetrate this big house at climaxes but seems to lack the underlying resonance that creates command and presence at quieter levels. She is a singer in firm control, however; every vocal gesture is intelligently thought out; fioritura is expertly handled. I don't know whose idea her judicious tempos in "Una voce poco fa" originally were, but they made eminently good musical sense.
Mr. Opalach sang with the appropriate weight, and one got more or less used to his histrionics. Harry Dworchak was a splendid Basilio, rich in voice and with a sure sense of understated comedy. Susanne Marsee sang Berta's aria "Il vecchiotto cerca moglie" with bluff emphasis. John Lankston was Ambrogio; William Ledbetter played the Official.
The City Opera orchestra conducted by Scott Bergeson played with a sincerity that translated into rough enthusiasm. The chorus sang together; indeed, communication between stage and pit was generally good. Ken Tabachnick's streaks of light and shadow gave the storm sequence a nice vividness.
